401 of 992 DOCUMENTS


In re Kelly


49168


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


192 A.D.2d 236; 600 N.Y.S.2d 682; 1993 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7535



July 22, 1993, Decided  
July 22, 1993, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant commissioner sought review of the order from the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), which declared invalid an amendment to a regulation of the Department of Social Services regarding eligibility for Emergency Home Relief, permanently enjoined the commissioner from enforcing the invalidated amendment, and set aside prior administrative decisions for respondent applicants and provided requirements for change orders.


OVERVIEW: The applicants brought an a combined declaratory judgment and N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 proceeding against the commissioner seeking a declaration that an amendment to the regulations governing the Emergency Home Relief (EHR) program, N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 18 § 370.3(b)(2), which disqualified households with gross income in excess of 125 percent of the Federal poverty guidelines from eligibility for assistance, was invalid as arbitrary and capricious. The court held that the interpretation given by the agency to the regulation's income requirement, in the manner in which it determined an applicant's eligibility for emergency assistance in paying rent arrears, was fundamentally irrational, and that the within determinations, based on that interpretation, were properly set aside. The court held that eligibility was determined on the basis of gross income, which was reasonably computed to include the time during which the applicant should have been planning for the emergency and the period during which the emergency situation which resulted in the arrears endured.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the commissioner's appeal and affirmed the portion of the Supreme Court's order that remanded the matter to the New York State Department of Social Services either to issue the Emergency Home Relief Grants or, if eligibility issues remained, to provide the applicants with an opportunity to establish eligibility.


HEADNOTES

Social Services - Emergency Assistance - Validity of Income Test for Determining Eligibility for One-Time Allowance for Shelter Arrears An amendment to the regulations governing the "Emergency Home Relief" (EHR) program (18 NYCRR 370.3 [b] [2]), which disqualified households with a gross income in excess of 125% of the Federal poverty guidelines from eligibility for a one-time allowance for shelter arrears, is not invalid on its face in view of the legislative mandate to reduce spending on programs such as EHR and the subsequent enactment of legislation imposing the bright-line income test for EHR applications (Social Services Law § 131-w). However, the Department of Social Services' (DSS) interpretation of the amendment, by which it applies the income test for eligibility to prospective income only as of the time of the application for past rent, rather than to income prior to and during the emergency which led to the arrears, which is the only time during which "planning" could have taken place or the arrears could have been avoided, undermines and frustrates the underlying purpose of the regulation to prevent homelessness and the amendment's "cost containment" goal and is thus fundamentally irrational as applied to plaintiffs and plaintiffs-intervenors. While such a prospective method would be appropriate to determine eligibility for ongoing future social services or monthly payments of public assistance, there is no reasonable basis for using that method for determining eligibility for the type of emergency lump-sum assistance contemplated by the EHR program, which is designed to redress past emergencies which are already over, in distinction to ongoing financial need. Accordingly, the administrative determinations based on DSS's interpretation were properly set aside. On remand, DSS must determine eligibility on the basis of gross income which is reasonably computed to include the time during which the applicant should have been planning for the emergency and the period during which the emergency situation which resulted in the arrears endured.

403 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Medical Soc'y of State v. Sobol


66953


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


192 A.D.2d 78; 600 N.Y.S.2d 177; 1993 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7086



July 8, 1993, Decided  
July 8, 1993, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, state medical society and others, appealed from an order and judgment of the Supreme Court in Albany County (New York), which granted a motion by plaintiffs for summary judgment, declared 1990 N.Y. Laws 190, § 280 to be unconstitutional, and denied summary judgment to defendants, Commissioner of the Department of Education and others.


OVERVIEW: The question presented on appeal was whether 1990 N.Y. Laws 190, §§ 278, 280, which reduced the period of physicians' registration under N.Y. Educ. Law § 6524(8), N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 8, § 59.8, by one year and concomitantly increased the registration fee from $ 240 to $ 330 violated the Contract Clause of the United States Constitution, took physicians' property without compensation, and denied physicians their right to due process. The court held in the negative. Absent some clear indication that the legislature intended to bind the state contractually, a statute was presumed not to create private contractual or vested rights, but merely declared a policy to be pursued until the legislature ordained otherwise. The legislature, in passing N.Y. Educ. Law § 6524(8), as amended, never intended to create private rights of a contractual nature enforceable against the state. While a valid registration was necessary to use a medical license, it was ministerial and financial in nature. There was no property right in the duration of the registration. To the extent 1990 N.Y. Laws 190, § 280, affected the underlying medical licenses, it effected no taking.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order and judgment, denied plaintiffs' motion, granted the Commissioner's motion for summary judgment, and declared that the law at issue had not been shown to be unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law - Contract Clause - Change in Physicians' Registration Period Laws of 1990 (ch 190, § 280), which reduced the period of physicians' registration ( Education Law § 6524 [8]; 8 NYCRR 59.8) by one year and concomitantly increased the registration fee from $ 240 to $ 330, does not violate the Contract Clause of the US Constitution. Absent some clear indication that the Legislature intends to bind the State contractually, a statute is presumed not to create private contractual or vested rights. Before a law may be deemed to be a contract between the State and a third party, examination of the statutory language must show it to be plain and susceptible of no other reasonable construction than that a contract was intended. Here, the challenged fee structure is merely one measure of governing the conduct of this heavily regulated business. The statutes in question are clearly general in scope based upon public policy considerations and cannot be interpreted to manifest a legislative intent to create private rights of a contractual nature enforceable against the State.

Constitutional Law - Taking of Property - No Property Right in Physician's Registration With respect to a claim of an unconstitutional taking, there is no property right in the term of a physician's registration. A medical license is a valuable property right, but a valid registration, while necessary to use a medical license, is ministerial and financial in nature.

Constitutional Law - Taking of Property - Change in Physicians' Registration Period - Effect on Medical Licenses Laws of 1990 (ch 190, § 280), which reduced the period of physicians' registration ( Education Law § 6524 [8]; 8 NYCRR 59.8) by one year and concomitantly increased the registration fee from $ 240 to $ 330, effects no unconstitutional taking (US Const 5th Amend) to the extent that it affects the underlying medical licenses. The limited economic impact, together with the de minimis effort involved in the reregistration process, has, at best, only a remote effect upon, or interference with, the ability of doctors to fully utilize their medical licenses. Further, the revenue gained is used in policing the quality of medical care within the State, the heavily regulated business interest from which the revenue is raised.

Constitutional Law - Due Process of Law - Sufficiency of Notice of Change in Physicians' Registration Period With respect to Laws of 1990 (ch 190, § 280), which reduced the period of physicians' registration ( Education Law § 6524 [8]; 8 NYCRR 59.8) by one year and concomitantly increased the registration fee from $ 240 to $ 330, no procedural or substantive due process violations occurred from the lack of individualized notice of the charge to each physician, and notice of the right to hearings regarding the changes in the registration fee and time periods.

People v. Golley


65743


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


195 A.D.2d 713; 601 N.Y.S.2d 871; 1993 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7093



July 8, 1993, Decided  
July 8, 1993, Entered

 

405 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Crosson v. Regan


66683


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


192 A.D.2d 109; 600 N.Y.S.2d 153; 1993 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7110



July 8, 1993, Decided  
July 8, 1993, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner state court administrator filed an action against respondent state comptroller challenging the state comptroller's act of charging judicially required restitution payments to court employees in the 1991-1992 fiscal year judiciary appropriation. The Supreme Court, Albany County (New York) concluded that the state comptroller properly charged the restitution against the 1991-1992 appropriation. The state court administrator appealed.


OVERVIEW: In implementing a piece of legislation, the state comptroller withheld a portion of wages from certain court employees. The lagged payroll was declared unconstitutional and restitution was awarded to the affected employees. The federal district court that ordered restitution also directed that restitution be paid from the fiscal year 1990-1991 appropriation. In October 1991, restitution plus interest was paid to the employees, and the state comptroller unilaterally charged the sum paid to the judiciary's 1991-1992 appropriation. After the state court administrator filed an action challenging the state comptroller's allocation of the funds to the 1991-1992 fiscal year, the trial court entered a provisional order designed to prevent the lapse of unexpended funds allocated to the judiciary for the 1991-1992 fiscal year. The trial court properly rejected the state court administrator's argument that the restitution could have been charged against the judiciary's 1992-1993 appropriation; charging the restitution to the 1991-1992 judiciary appropriation reflected the correct charge that was required at the time of payment. The state comptroller's action was constitutional and was proper.


OUTCOME: The order holding that the state comptroller properly charged the required restitution against the 1991-1992 judiciary appropriation was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Courts - Unified Court System - Unilateral Transfer of Unexpended Fund Balances within Judiciary Appropriation by State Comptroller - Lagged Payroll Restitution The State Comptroller acted properly and within his constitutional power to audit all claims against the State and "all official accounts" (NY Const, art V, § 1) when he unilaterally charged certain lagged payroll restitution payments to the judiciary's fiscal year 1991-1992 appropriation, where a Federal court action ordered such restitution to be made to judiciary employees; that liability concededly arose in fiscal year 1991-1992, and was paid in that same fiscal year by the Comptroller in compliance with the order of the Federal court. The only task remaining was the performance of the Comptroller's paradigmatic function of accounting for that payment, and at the time the required restitution was actually made, in October 1991, the only valid charge was against the judiciary's appropriation for fiscal year 1991-1992; the appropriation for fiscal year 1990-1991, which gave rise to the lagged payroll scheme, could not be used as that fiscal year closed on March 31, 1991, and even if the liability to lagged court employees had been incurred in fiscal year 1990-1991, the following year's appropriation lapsed as a matter of law on September 15, 1991, before the restitution payments were made (see, State Finance Law § 40 [3]). It was only because of a District Court order, later reversed, that the Comptroller was prevented, at the time restitution was made, from transferring to the general State treasury the lapsed money of the fiscal year 1990-1991 judiciary budget in a special account which had been created as a result of the Federal litigation. Moreover, when the restitution payments were actually made, they could not have been charged against any fiscal year 1992-1993 appropriation because that appropriation had not yet been enacted (see, NY Const, art VII, § 7).

 

406 of 992 DOCUMENTS


McDermott v. Regan


67095


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


191 A.D.2d 47; 599 N.Y.S.2d 718; 1993 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6913



July 1, 1993, Decided  
July 1, 1993, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant State challenged a judgment from the Supreme Court in Albany County (New York), which granted the summary judgment motion of respondents, unions and others, and declared 1990 N.Y. Laws ch. 210, §§ 1-7, which changed the method of employer contributions to retirement funds, unconstitutional. The unions and others had collectively filed actions against the State and the comptroller for a declaration that chapter 210 was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The comptroller, who was the administrative head of certain public employees' retirement system, used the aggregate cost, which varied moderately over time, method for calculating employer contributions to the fund. Then, the Legislature passed 1990 N.Y. Laws ch. 210, requiring the comptroller to use the projected unit credit method, which resulted in necessary contributions becoming more volatile, to calculate employer contributions. The new method resulted in a drastic reduction in employer contributions and depleted the surplus, requiring higher employer contributions. The unions and others then collectively filed three actions to declare the statute unconstitutional. On appeal from a summary judgment for the employers, the court held that: (1) the statute was unconstitutional because it imposed a particular funding method, as opposed to the mere setting of guidelines for selection of a method, and vitiated its members' right to have the benefit of the comptroller's discretion in fixing the amount of contributions needed for the continued stability and security of the systems; and (2) the legislature could not compel the comptroller to carry out dramatic funding changes.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment, which had declared the applicable statute, which had changed the method of employer contributions to retirement funds, unconstitutional, in the action of the unions and others against the State and the comptroller.


HEADNOTES

Civil Service - Retirement and Pension Benefits - Unconstitutional Impairment of Retirement Benefits - Legislative Imposition of Particular Funding Method

1. Laws of 1990 (ch 210, § 1-7), which require the State Comptroller to use particular methods of valuing the funds from which benefits of membership in the public retirement systems are derived and of calculating employer contributions for a given year, are unconstitutional as impermissibly impairing the contractual rights of the retirement systems' members (NY Const, art V, § 7). The imposition of a particular funding method, as opposed to the mere setting of guidelines for selection of a method, vitiates the members' right to have the benefit of the Comptroller's discretion in fixing the amount of contributions needed for the continued stability and security of the retirement systems. Notwithstanding that the funding method imposed may be "actuarially sound", it may work an impairment of the members' right to protection of the sources of funds from which benefits will ultimately be drawn, since the method imposed is less safe than the very safe method heretofore employed by the Comptroller. Moreover, chapter 210 does not simply impose a funding method on the Comptroller; it also dictates the rapid depletion of the surplus funds that currently exist, a further usurpation of the Comptroller's authority to manage the accounts of the fund.

Civil Service - Retirement and Pension Benefits - Unconstitutional Impairment of Retirement Benefits - Legislative Imposition of Method for Revaluing Assets - Advance Notice of Changes in Valuation Method to Division of Budget

2. Laws of 1990 (ch 210) is unconstitutional to the extent it requires the State Comptroller to revalue the assets of the public retirement systems fund for fiscal years 1988 and 1989 using a different "smoothing method" than that chosen for those years upon the advice of his selected actuary, and that he provide the Director of the Division of the Budget with advance notice of any changes made to the method of actuarial valuation, since these requirements strip the systems' members of their right to the Comptroller's independent judgment.

 

407 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Brooklyn Heights Ass'n v. Macchiarola


92-03859


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


192 A.D.2d 22; 599 N.Y.S.2d 930; 1993 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6393


September 8, 1992, Argued  
June 21, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Revised September 13, 1993.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondents, residents and civic organizations, commenced a proceeding pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78, challenging the N.Y. City Council district lines. The Supreme Court, Kings County (New York) granted the petition and directed appellant New York City Districting Commission (commission) to redraw the district lines. The commission appealed.


OVERVIEW: In order to create new city council districts, the commission was formed. Pursuant to N.Y. City Charter § 52 (charter), the commission was given several criteria for creating districts. The residents and civic organizations challenged the district lines drawn by the commission and claimed that certain areas were part of their neighborhood and that the commission had violated the charter by failing to keep their neighborhood intact. The trial court found that the commission's decision not to include certain areas when it redrew the district lines for the neighborhood at issue was arbitrary and capricious. The trial court ordered that the district lines be redrawn. The commission appealed. On appeal, the court upheld the trial court's decision. In reaching its conclusion, the court found that, under the facts of the case, the decision not to split the census tabulation block served no real purpose and did not implicate the concerns underlying the general policy of not splitting census blocks. The court further found that the commission's placement of two piers in a district other than the residents' district also was arbitrary and capricious.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court ordering the commission to redraw certain district lines.


HEADNOTES

Elections - Redistricting Plan - New York City Districting Commission - Mandate to Keep Neighborhoods Intact The New York City Districting Commission violated the requirements of the New York City Charter in promulgating a redistricting plan which, in order to establish a district with a majority of Latinos, created a land bridge between two communities by annexing all of a census tabulation block to the new district, rather than splitting the census tabulation block so as not to annex that portion of the block that has historically been considered part of a nearby neighborhood. Under New York City Charter § 52 (1), the Commission in creating new districts was required to apply several criteria to the maximum extent possible and give preference according to the order in which those criteria were listed. Since splitting the block would not have hampered the Commission's desire to create a Latino district, or violated any of the other districting criteria, the Commission, by neglecting to apply the third criterion of keeping neighborhoods intact, failed to implement City Charter § 52 (1) (c) to the maximum extent possible.

Elections - Redistricting Plan - New York City Districting Commission - Burden of Proof on Challenge to Plan In a proceeding pursuant to CPLR article 78 challenging a determination of the New York City Districting Commission promulgating a redistricting plan with respect to certain districts as violative of New York City Charter § 52 (1) (c), which mandates that district lines shall keep intact neighborhoods and communities with established ties of common interest and association, petitioners were not required to establish beyond a reasonable doubt that the relevant portion of a census tabulation block annexed in its entirety to the new district was part of petitioners' neighborhood, since petitioners claim only that the incidental determination of the Commission in refusing to split the block was unnecessary to the creation or maintenance of any other district and violated the interest recognized in the City Charter of keeping neighborhoods intact.

Elections - Redistricting Plan - New York City Districting Commission - Splitting Census Tabulation Block New York City Charter § 1152 (d) (11) (b), which provides that the New York City Districting Commission "shall utilize the final count results of the [1990] census" did not prohibit the Commission from splitting a census tabulation block in creating a new City Council district so as to keep intact a nearby neighborhood that historically contained a portion of that block. Any interpretation by the Commission of section 1152 must be secondary to interpretation of the criteria contained in New York City Charter § 52, which includes the mandate to keep intact neighborhoods and communities with established ties of common interest and association. Moreover, minor deviations of population equality that result from splitting a census tabulation block are permissible if necessary to allow government to pursue other legitimate objectives. Here, splitting the block would result in a deviation from one district to another of no more than 47 persons, and the City Charter mandate of preserving neighborhood integrity is a legitimate objective.

Elections - Redistricting Plan - New York City Districting Commission - Preservation of County Boundaries A determination of the New York City Districting Commission which placed certain piers in a Manhattan City Council district, rather than keeping the piers in the Brooklyn City Council district containing the neighborhood of which the piers were a part, solely to follow county boundaries, is arbitrary and capricious. In giving more weight to the preservation of county boundaries than to keeping the subject Brooklyn neighborhood intact, the Commission failed to give the City Charter criteria for promulgating a redistricting plan the proper priority (see, NY City Charter § 52 [1]).

Elections - Redistricting Plan - New York City Districting Commission - Effect of Voting Rights Act of 1965 Section 5 of the Voting Rights Act of 1965 (42 USC § 1973c) does not require preclearance of the court-ordered changes in the New York City Council district lines. State courts have the power to decide, as a collateral matter, whether section 5 applies to contemplated changes in election procedures. Moreover, in this case involving a challenge to the portion of a redistricting plan promulgated by the New York City Districting Commission which annexed all of a certain census tabulation block to a newly created district, no voting rights would be affected in any event since the disputed area is virtually unpopulated.

 

408 of 992 DOCUMENTS



City of N.Y. v. 17 Vista Assocs.


47709


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


192 A.D.2d 192; 599 N.Y.S.2d 549; 1993 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6298



June 17, 1993, Decided  
June 17, 1993, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant building owner sought review of an order of the Supreme Court of New York County (New York), which granted plaintiff city summary judgment on its complaint against the building owner for payment on a promissory note, and its motion for leave to file a second amended complaint. The trial court dismissed building owner's counterclaim and affirmative defenses and its cross-motion for summary judgment dismissing the complaint.


OVERVIEW: The building owner had purchased the building intending to demolish it in favor of erecting a business tower. The city had denied the demolition permit alleging a possible application of Administrative Code of City of N.Y. § 27-198(a), and then granted the permit after extracting from the seller of the building and the building owner each payment to the city benefitting the homeless. The building owner executed a promissory note. However, it had never been informed that his permit application had already long been approved. The city then sought payment on the note and the trial court granted it summary judgment. On appeal, the court held that the building owner should have been granted summary judgment and that the city's complaint should have been dismissed as against public policy. The court reasoned that the city's asserted goal, providing housing for low-and moderate-income people, while laudable, could not be achieved by extortion. Because the actions of the city violated public policy, they thereby rendered the agreements void and unenforceable. Citizens had the right to expect that all applicants were treated equally and fairly. The city violated the public trust.


OUTCOME: The court held that the orders in favor of the city should be modified to deny the city's motion for summary judgment, to grant the building owner's cross motion for summary judgment dismissing the complaint and for leave to file an amended counterclaim. The court held that the complaint should be dismissed and that the order and judgment should be otherwise affirmed.



HEADNOTES

Contracts - Illegal Contracts - Public Policy - Agreement to Expedite City's Grant of Demolition Permit An agreement executed in 1985 whereby defendant, a real estate developer, in connection with its plan to construct an office tower on the site of an existing building, agreed to pay $ 500,000 to a charitable trust for low-and moderate-income housing, in addition to the $ 1,000,000 the building owner agreed to contribute to the trust from the proceeds of the sale, if it received a prompt determination from plaintiff City of New York that the existing building was exempt from the moratorium on the demolition of single-room occupancy (SRO) buildings then in effect and was timely issued the necessary permits to begin lawful construction, is void and unenforceable as violative of public policy. The City's action in extracting payments from defendants in exchange for a favorable determination of the building's status, after the Department of Buildings had already determined that the building was not an SRO and had approved the demolition permit application, was fraudulent. The City's otherwise laudable goal of providing housing for low-and moderate-income people could not properly be achieved by extortion, with the City playing the role of adversary rather than administrative authority. The City had a duty to render its determination as to the building's status expeditiously and based solely on a review of the relevant criteria. By accepting money to accelerate its review process and promising a favorable determination, the City abused its governmental power and betrayed the public trust. Citizens have the right to expect that all applicants will be treated equally and fairly and that the safeguards imposed by regulations will be followed. Accordingly, the complaint should be dismissed and defendants granted leave to file an amended counterclaim.

 

409 of 992 DOCUMENTS


American Tel. & Tel. Co. v. New York State Dep't of Taxation & Fin.


48971


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


191 A.D.2d 61; 599 N.Y.S.2d 238; 1993 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6172



June 15, 1993, Decided  
June 15, 1993, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff phone company appealed from a judgment of the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), which denied its motion for summary judgment, granted a cross motion by defendant, department of taxation and finance, for summary judgment, and declared N.Y. Tax Law §186-a(2-a) constitutional. The phone company had sought a declaratory judgment that §186-a(2-a) was unconstitutional and a refund of taxes it paid on account of such provision.


OVERVIEW: The phone company sought a refund of taxes based upon its recalculation of its tax liability applying the deduction for carrier access expense to its apportioned state revenues. The phone company argued that the requirement that the deduction be taken from total unapportioned interstate and international receipts violated the Commerce Clause. The court reversed, and held that because the effect of § 186-a(2-a) was to discriminate against long distance carriers to the extent that they did business outside the state, the statute violated the Commerce Clause. The effect of the law was to give the benefit of a tax deduction to otherwise identically situated long distance carriers in different amounts based solely on, and in direct proportion to, the percentage of their property within the state. The method of allowing a tax deduction for New York access fees violated the Commerce Clause because the amount of the deduction increased or decreased based solely upon the percentage of the long distance carrier's property within or without the state. Therefore, the phone company was entitled to a tax refund.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's judgment and granted the phone company's motion for summary judgment and declared the tax statute unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law - Validity of Statute - "Access Fee" Tax Deduction for Long Distance Carriers

 Tax Law § 186-a (2-a) which, with respect to the flat tax imposed on the adjusted gross receipts of long distance telephone carriers, has the effect of giving a tax deduction to otherwise identically situated long distance carriers in different amounts based solely on, and in direct proportion to, the percentage of their property within the State, discriminates against interstate and foreign commerce and is, therefore, unconstitutional as violative of the Commerce Clause of the United States Constitution (art I, § 8, cl [3]). A State may tax long distance telephone carriers in such a way that a carrier doing most of its business within the State has a lower effective tax rate than a carrier doing most of its business outside the State. However, section 186-a (2-a)'s method of allowing a tax deduction for New York access fees violates the Commerce Clause because the amount of the deduction increases or decreases based solely upon the percentage of the long distance carrier's property within or without the State.

 

410 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Penny Lane/E. Hampton, Inc. v. County of Suffolk


90-07527


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


191 A.D.2d 19; 598 N.Y.S.2d 806; 1993 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6093


September 8, 1992, Argued  
June 14, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Revised August 17, 1993.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought a declaratory judgment that Local Laws, 1985, No. 2 of the County of Suffolk was preempted by N.Y. Penal Law and N.Y.C.P.L.R. 6330.


OVERVIEW: The Suffolk County (New York) Department of Consumer Affairs found petitioner retailer guilty of a violation of Local Laws, 1985, No. 2 of the County of Suffolk. Petitioner sought review and a declaratory judgment that the law was preempted and was unconstitutional on its face and as applied. The conviction arose from petitioner's display of a poster featuring a grossly overweight naked woman and four postcards that depicted a male and several females in various stages of undress. The local law was a statute aimed at the display of obscene material. The court held that the law was invalid and annulled on the grounds of preemption, because N.Y. Penal Law and N.Y. C.P.L.R. 6330 demonstrated an intention to occupy the field of prohibiting obscene material.


OUTCOME: The court found the local obscenity law invalid and annulled, because the state penal law and a certain civil practice provision preempted local governments from acting to prohibit obscene material.


HEADNOTES

Local Laws - Preemption by State - Local Law Prohibiting Display of Obscene Material

1. Local Laws, 1985, No. 2 of the County of Suffolk, which prohibits the display of what is deemed to be obscene material, is invalid as having been preempted by State obscenity laws. In view of the nature of the subject matter that is being regulated, the purpose and scope of the State legislative scheme concerning obscenity (see generally, Penal Law arts 235, 245) and the need for a State-wide standard to be applied in obscenity cases, the Penal Law has preempted this area of the law. Moreover, CPLR 6330, which authorizes local officials to bring actions enjoining retailers from selling obscene materials, also supports a finding of preemption.

Courts - Appellate Division - Authority to Grant Declaratory Judgment in Transferred CPLR Article 78 Proceeding

2. Inasmuch as a proceeding pursuant to CPLR article 78 may be converted into a declaratory judgment action when appropriate, the Appellate Division has full authority to grant a declaratory judgment to petitioner where Supreme Court transferred to the Appellate Division an article 78 proceeding to review an administrative determination, in which petitioner also sought a declaratory judgment, but Supreme Court did not address the declaratory judgment aspects of the case.

 

411 of 992 DOCUMENTS


King v. New York State Div. of Parole


49135


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


190 A.D.2d 423; 598 N.Y.S.2d 245; 1993 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 5474



June 3, 1993, Decided  
June 3, 1993, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent New York State Division of Parole appealed a judgment from the Supreme Court of New York County (New York), which granted petitioner inmate's petition and annulled respondent's parole determinations denying petitioner parole and ordered respondent to release petitioner to parole supervision.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner has been a prison inmate for 22 years, following his conviction of murder, manslaughter, attempted robbery, assault, and possession of a dangerous weapon. He was never indicted for intentional murder but was convicted under the felony murder statute, N.Y. Penal Law § 125.25. After the Parole Board again denied petitioner parole, he brought a proceeding pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 seeking to set aside the decision of the Board. The supreme court granted the petition on the ground that the decision to deny parole was irrational. On the Board's appeal, the court found that the decision made by the Board was based on a fundamental misunderstanding of its role and its power, and was not in accord with statutory requirements. The record clearly revealed that the denial of petitioner's application was a foregone conclusion, and since neither the death penalty nor life imprisonment without the possibility of parole was a part of the state law, neither should have entered into the Board's consideration. The court found that the decision of the Board had been fatally tainted by its abdication of its responsibility to fairly consider all relevant factors.


OUTCOME: The court reversed that portion of the trial court's order requiring the Board to release petitioner to parole supervision and remanded the matter to the Board with directions to provide petitioner with a de novo hearing before a different panel, which was to consider all of the statutory criteria and all available information relevant to whether petitioner should be granted parole.


HEADNOTES

Parole - Denial - Improper Denial Based Solely on Factor That Victim in Felony Murder was Police Officer - Required Consideration of All Relevant Statutory Criteria for Parole Release

The Parole Board acted improperly in denying parole release to petitioner, a prison inmate for 22 years following his felony murder conviction for his involvement in the shooting death of an off-duty, plain-clothes police officer during the robbery of a fast food restaurant in 1970, since the decision of the Board was based on a fundamental misunderstanding of its role and its power, and was not in accord with statutory requirements (Executive Law § 259-i [1] [a]; [2] [c]), including petitioner's educational and other achievements which clearly demonstrate his extraordinary rehabilitative achievements and appear to strongly militate in favor of granting parole. The record reveals that the Board was proceeding on the mistaken assumption that its primary duty was to determine, in the abstract, the appropriate penalty for murder in today's society. The role of the Parole Board is not to resentence petitioner according to the personal opinions of its members as to the appropriate penalty for murder, but to determine whether, as of this moment, given all the relevant statutory factors, he should be released. For the Board to simply decide that any case which involves the death of a police officer, regardless of all of the other circumstances surrounding the crime, automatically necessitates the denial of parole because of the "seriousness of the crime" is a breach of the obligation legislatively imposed upon it to render a qualitative judgment based upon a review of all the relevant factors. Accordingly, respondent is directed to provide petitioner with a de novo hearing before a different panel.

 

412 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Walker v. Town of Hempstead


91-01706, 93-03369


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


190 A.D.2d 364; 598 N.Y.S.2d 550; 1993 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 5455


December 14, 1992, Argued  
June 1, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Revised August 10, 1993.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, an injured citizen, appealed an order of the Supreme Court, Nassau County (New York), which entered summary judgment in favor of respondent town on a claim for personal injuries because the town received no prior written notice of the alleged defect that caused the injury. The citizen argued that N.Y. Town of Hempstead Local Laws, No. 90, § 6-2 (1988), which required the notice, violated N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 50-e[4].


OVERVIEW: An injured citizen filed a claim for personal injuries against a town when he was injured on a paddleball court maintained by the town. The citizen argued that the town's local law requiring prior written notice of a defect in connection with paddleball court accidents, N.Y. Town of Hempstead Local Laws, No. 90, § 6-2 (1988), conflicted with N.Y. Gen. Law § 50-e (4) which forbids the imposition of additional notice requirements. The court held that § 50-e (4) limitations could be superseded by the town only if the town strictly complied with the procedures set forth in N.Y. Mun. Home Rule Law § 22 (1), which requires a municipality invoking its supersession authority to state its intention with definiteness and explicitness. Because this was not done, the notice requirement was invalid to the extent it required the service of a prior written notice of defect.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment, vacated the order dismissing the complaint, denied the town's motion for summary judgment, and reinstated the injured citizen's complaint for damages.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations - Notice of Claim - Validity of Town "Notice of Defect" Law - Town's Supersession Authority

1. The "notice of defect" law of the Town of Hempstead, which requires the service of a prior written notice of defect, in addition to the service of a written notice of claim, as a precondition to municipal tort liability based upon defective conditions alleged to exist upon certain Town properties, including beach and playground areas, is not invalid solely because it is inconsistent with General Municipal Law § 50-e (4), which is applicable to towns by virtue of Town Law § 67 and provides that those municipal corporations which, under law, require notices of claim as a precondition to liability may not also require notices of defect, except as to defects to highways, streets, bridges, culverts, sidewalks or crosswalks, since the Town may supersede State law on matters of local concern, such as the presentation of claims against the Town (NY Const, art IX, § 2 [c] [ii] [5]; Municipal Home Rule Law § 10 [1] [ii] [a] [5]) and the care and management of Town property (NY Const, art IX, § 2 [c] [ii] [6]; Municipal Home Rule Law § 10 [1] [ii] [a] [6]) in the absence of any clear expression of legislative intent prohibiting towns from expanding the class of municipal properties with respect to which notices of defect may be required beyond those specific types of properties mentioned in General Municipal Law § 50-e (4) (Municipal Home Rule Law § 10 [1] [ii] [d] [3]). However, a town that seeks to invoke its supersession authority must substantially comply with Municipal Home Rule Law § 22 (1), which requires a municipality to state its intention to supersede State law with definiteness and explicitness. Since the local law under review does not identify in any way the provision of General Municipal Law § 50-e (4) which it is intended to supersede, it was not enacted in accordance with the Town's supersession powers and is thus invalid to the extent that it requires the service of a prior written notice of defect as a precondition to tort liability based upon defective conditions alleged to exist upon properties which do not constitute highways, streets, bridges, culverts, sidewalks or crosswalks. Accordingly, the complaint premised upon injuries sustained because of an allegedly dangerous paddleball court at a Town beach club is reinstated.

Appeal - Matters Appealable - Appeal from Intermediate Order When Judgment Has Been Entered

2. In an action to recover damages for personal injuries sustained as the result of an allegedly dangerous Town paddleball court, the appeal from the intermediate order dismissing the complaint due to plaintiff's failure to comply with the Town's "notice of defect" law must be dismissed because the right of direct appeal therefrom terminated with the entry of judgment in the action. The issues raised on appeal from the order are brought up for review and have been considered on the appeal from the judgment (CPLR 5501 [a] [1]).
