Fernandez v. New York City Taxi & Limousine Comm'n


48973


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


193 A.D.2d 423; 597 N.Y.S.2d 337; 1993 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4780



May 11, 1993, Decided  
May 11, 1993, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner taxicab driver sought review of a decision of respondent New York City Taxi & Limousine Commission which revoked his taxicab driver's license.


OVERVIEW: The court found that the taxicab driver clearly performed a willful act of omission or commission that was against the best interests of the public when he made sexual comments to and grabbed the breast of a female passenger. The court found that the rules were not unconstitutionally vague as applied against the driver because clearly a person of ordinary intelligence would understand that it was against the public interest for taxicab drivers to sexually molest and sexually harass passengers.


OUTCOME: The court confirmed the determination of the New York City Taxi & Limousine Commission.

 

414 of 992 DOCUMENTS


People v. Thompson


47951


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


190 A.D.2d 162; 596 N.Y.S.2d 421; 1993 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4363



April 27, 1993, Decided  
April 27, 1993, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment from the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), which convicted defendant of criminal sale of a controlled substance in the first degree. The state challenged the imposition of a sentence that was less than the statutory minimum.


OVERVIEW: The trial court convicted defendant of criminal sale of a controlled substance in the first degree and imposed a sentence that was less than the statutory minimum. Defendant contended on appeal that that the evidence failed to demonstrate beyond a reasonable doubt that she was the woman who sold an undercover officer vials containing cocaine. The state brought a cross-appeal of the sentence imposed. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment. The court found that there was ample evidence that the officer had sufficient opportunity to observe defendant closely enough to permit a reliable identification at the trial and that the jury was entitled to credit this identification. The court also found that the testimony of the state's chemist demonstrated that defendant sold two ounces or more of cocaine. The court concluded that imposition of the statutory minimum sentence against defendant under the circumstances would have been cruel and inhuman punishment under N.Y. Const. art. I, § 5 and the Eighth Amendment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment against defendant as to her conviction for criminal sale of a controlled substance in the first degree and the sentence imposed against her.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Sentence - Cruel and Unusual Punishment - Mandatory 15-Year Minimum Sentence

Defendant was lawfully convicted of criminal sale of a controlled substance in the first degree for selling 214 vials containing cocaine to an undercover officer in view of the ample evidence that the officer had sufficient opportunity during the sale to observe defendant closely enough to permit a reliable identification at the trial and in view of the testimony of the chemist who testified for the People as to the procedure he employed in weighing the contents of the vials. In addition, the trial court did not illegally impose a reduced sentence of only 8 years to life instead of the statutorily mandated minimum of 15 years to life (Penal Law § 70.00 [2] [a]; [3] [a] [i]; § 220.43) based upon its finding that the punitive statutory minimum sentence, when viewed against such factors as defendant's age, her lack of a criminal history and the pretrial offer of 3 years to life, would constitute cruel and unusual punishment (NY Const, art I, § 5; US Const 8th Amend) as applied to defendant. This is the type of "rare case" in which it may be found that the otherwise constitutional statutory sentencing scheme would be unconstitutional as applied. A system of justice which mandates a 15-year prison sentence, as a minimum, on a 17-year-old girl, who was not cared for by parents and was under the domination of her uncle, the prime mover in the sale of narcotics, also mandates a lifetime of crime and imposes on the community, upon her release, a woman who may be incapable of anything but criminal activity. Accordingly, the judgment should be affirmed.

 

415 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Saratoga Water Servs. v. Sarasota County Water Auth.


66297


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


190 A.D.2d 40; 596 N.Y.S.2d 872; 1993 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4041



April 22, 1993, Decided  
April 22, 1993, Entered



DISPOSITION: Adjudged that the determinations are confirmed, without costs, and petition dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner water company appealed from a decision by respondent Saratoga County Water Authority (water authority), which condemned the water company's assets and real property under N.Y. Em. Dom. Proc. Law § 207 for the creation of a coordinated public water system. The water company argued that N.Y. Pub. Auth. Law § 1199-eee(5), which the water authority relied upon, was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The water authority concluded that the acquisition of the water company's assets and property was required for the maintenance and operation of a town water system and the creation of a coordinated public water system. The water company filed a lawsuit, contending that under § 1199-eee(5), the method that established just compensation was deprivation without due process; the determination of just compensation was impermissibly vested in the legislature; and that it was unconstitutionally vague. The water company argued that the water authority lacked authority to condemn property that was not regulated by the Public Service Commission and that the record did not support its argument that the acquisitions were for a public benefit, use or purpose. The court affirmed the determinations and dismissed the water company's petition. The court concluded that (1) the condemnation fulfilled a sufficient public use and purpose of providing an adequate water supply to the county; (2) there was no excessive taking because the property was needed to build the best route to water mains; and (3) the water company was not denied due process to comment upon and challenge the proceeding.


OUTCOME: The court confirmed the determinations by the water authority that it needed to acquire the water company's assets and property for the county's water system, and the water company's petition was dismissed.


HEADNOTES

Eminent Domain - Award - Water Authority - Just Compensation

1. Public Authorities Law § 1199-eee (5), which authorizes respondent Water Authority to acquire, by various means, any real or personal property necessary to fulfill its purpose of ensuring an adequate water supply for the future of Saratoga County, does not unconstitutionally deprive petitioners of their property without due process of law by restricting the methods for determining just compensation to the net income capitalization methodology "based on the actual net income as allowed" by the Public Service Commission, nor does the statute impermissibly remove the determination of just compensation from the judiciary and vest it in the Legislature. The statute can rationally be interpreted so as not to abrogate petitioners' rights to receive from Supreme Court a determination of just compensation providing them with the fair market value of the condemned property in its highest and best use on the date of the taking. The statute merely implies a preference for the income capitalization method but does not dictate that such method of computation be used in determining just compensation. Under the statutory scheme the Supreme Court may use any other method of computation in determining the fair market value of the property. Under these circumstances, respondent has to either accept that amount or discontinue the condemnation proceeding.

Eminent Domain - Award - Water Authority - Vagueness of Authorizing Statute 

2. Public Authorities Law § 1199-eee (5), which authorizes respondent Water Authority to acquire, by various means, any real or personal property necessary to fulfill its purpose of ensuring an adequate water supply for the future of Saratoga County, is not unconstitutionally vague for allegedly failing to establish when a taking occurs so that the valuation of the property can be decided. Rather, the statute sufficiently defines when the property vests in respondent and therefore is not open to arbitrary or capricious enforcement. The statute indicates that if Supreme Court values the property according to the income capitalization method, title to the property vests in respondent once the determination of just compensation has been made, a final judgment is entered, the final decree is filed and the appeal process ends either by expiration of the time for appeal or the completion of an appeal. In providing for an alternative computation methodology, the Legislature declined to provide for a different time for vesting. It can therefore be concluded that a similar time for vesting would be allowed when alternative methods are used, allowing for respondent to withdraw the condemnation proceeding within that time frame.

Eminent Domain - Public Use - Water Authority - Excessive Taking

3. Public Authorities Law § 1199-eee (5), which authorizes respondent Water Authority to acquire, by various means, any real or personal property necessary to fulfill its purpose of ensuring an adequate water supply for the future of Saratoga County, does not limit respondent to only condemning property which is regulated by the Public Service Commission. The statutory provision which states that title to condemned property shall vest in respondent when Supreme Court decides that the compensation for the real property shall be determined based on "the income capitalization method of valuation based on the actual net income as allowed by the" Public Service Commission, only relates to when title vests in respondent. The fact that the statute also provides for alternative methodologies to value property is a recognition that not all properties to be acquired would fall under the auspices of the Public Service Commission. Respondent's condemnation of petitioners' assets and real property in this case for the creation of a coordinated public water system must be upheld since the record establishes that the condemnation serves a sufficient public use and purpose. Furthermore, the record does does not support petitioners' contention that there was an excessive taking because of an allegedly alternative and superior route for the acquisition involving the annexation of less of petitioners' property. There was no excessive taking given the fact that respondents' route goes more directly to the water mains, the public purpose to be served by the condemnation and the fact that respondent's proposed route was found not to pose any significant environmental concerns. Lastly, the notice sent by respondent to petitioners sufficiently identified the parcels of property to be affected by the condemnation proceeding to satisfy due process (EDPL 202 [A]) and petitioners were not deprived of a meaningful opportunity to comment upon and challenge the condemnation proceeding.

 

416 of 992 DOCUMENTS


People v. Madera


46646


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


189 A.D.2d 462; 596 N.Y.S.2d 766; 1993 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3858



April 20, 1993, Decided  
April 20, 1993, Entered



DISPOSITION: Order of the Supreme Court, Bronx County, entered February 7, 1992, granting the defendant's motion to suppress physical evidence, is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State appealed from an order of the Supreme Court, Bronx County (New York), which granted defendant's motion to suppress physical evidence in the charge against defendant of criminal possession of a weapon in the third degree. The State contended that suppression was erroneous, in that the police action in pursuing defendant was justified.


OVERVIEW: The State charged defendant with criminal possession of a weapon in the third degree, and defendant's motion to suppress the physical evidence, including a gun, was granted by the trial court. The State appealed, contending that the police pursuit of defendant was justified. The court affirmed the suppression, holding that the police had acted on an anonymously reported incident and had pursued defendant only because he had refused to respond to police inquiry. As such, the court found no predicate for the pursuit of defendant. The court held that the police had no indication that defendant had committed or was about to commit any illegal act, apart from the fact that defendant fled upon being approached by the police for information. The court held that the absolute minimum predicate required to support the seizure of a civilian was a reasonable suspicion that the person to be seized had committed or was about to commit a crime. The court found nothing that would have justified such a reasonable suspicion and accordingly held that suppression was appropriate.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the suppression of physical evidence in an action charging defendant with criminal possession of a weapon in the third degree. The court held that the police did not have any reasonable suspicion that defendant had committed or was about to commit a crime that would have justified seizing him. The court noted that defendant's refusal to respond to police inquiry and his flight, without more, did not justify their pursuit.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Unlawful Search and Seizure - Gun Discarded during Flight from Illegal Police Pursuit

A gun discarded by defendant as he fled when police officers approached him to make inquiry must be suppressed since the police, having returned to the location specified in an anonymous radio run as the place where a man with a knife was taking part in a fight with a large group of people after initially observing no fight and no one with a knife on the scene, had absolutely no reason to believe that defendant or any of the other men with whom he was standing had been involved in any criminal activity and thus had no basis to approach and then pursue defendant. Under the circumstances, the officers' decision to pursue defendant can have been based upon nothing more than the fact that he chose to avoid them. Defendant, however, had a right to refuse to respond to police inquiry and his flight when the officers approached could not, in and of itself, create a reasonable suspicion of criminal activity. Only founded suspicion of a person's involvement in criminal activity will justify police pursuit. A police officer's mere curiosity, even if legitimately piqued, cannot suffice as a basis for seizure and interrogation. It is only if a refusal to respond to legitimate police inquiry in combination with other relevant circumstances gives rise to a reasonable suspicion that a person has committed or is about to commit a crime that a person can be kept from going about his or her business. Accordingly, the order granting defendant's suppression motion based upon the finding that defendant's discard of the gun had been a spontaneous reaction to the illegal police conduct should be affirmed.

 

417 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Anthony v. Brookhaven


91-04088


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


190 A.D.2d 21; 596 N.Y.S.2d 459; 1993 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4014


January 22, 1993, Argued  
April 19, 1993, Decided



DISPOSITION: Ordered that the order and judgment is reversed insofar as appealed from, on the law, with costs, the prior judgment dated May 19, 1989 is vacated, and it is declared that the notice given in this case pursuant to the notice provisions of Brookhaven Town Code § 85-23 met constitutional requirements, and that the rezoning of the plaintiff's property from J-2 business to B residential is not invalid by virtue of inadequate notice to the plaintiff.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant town sought review of the judgment entered by the Supreme Court of Suffolk County (New York) in favor of plaintiff property owner, a decision that declared the notice provisions of Brookhaven Town Code § 85-23 unconstitutional and invalidated the rezoning of certain real property owned by the property owner.


OVERVIEW: The property owner filed an action against the town for its rezoning of the property he owned from business to residential. The property owner argued that the rezoning should be set aside on the ground that he had been unconstitutionally deprived of actual notice and an opportunity to attend the public hearing. The trial court held that the notice provisions of Brookhaven Town Code were are unconstitutional because they did not ensure that actual notice of a proposed rezoning would be provided to the true current property owner in every case. The court found that it was to determine whether the notice of the public hearing concerning the rezoning, notice that was given pursuant to the provisions of the Brookhaven Town Code both by publication and by mail to the address of the last property owner as was reflected in the town's most recent tax assessment roll, was constitutionally sufficient. The court held that it was sufficient. The use of the town's assessment roll in providing notice by mail comported with the requirements of due process. The assessment roll constituted a comprehensive and generally accurate compilation of property ownership.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's judgment and declared that the notice given in the case pursuant to the notice provisions of the Brookhaven Town Code met constitutional requirements. The rezoning of the property owner's property from business to residential was not invalid by virtue of inadequate notice to the property owner.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations - Zoning - Notice of Proposed Rezoning - Constitutionality of Use of Assessment Roll in Providing Notice by Mail to Property Owners

Defendant Town's method of giving notice of a public hearing concerning the planned rezoning of several parcels, including plaintiff's premises, from a business to a residential zone, both by publication and by mail to the address of the last property owner as reflected in the Town's most recent tax assessment roll as provided by the Town Code, met constitutional due process requirements in this case even though plaintiff, who planned to erect a dental office on his property, did not receive actual notice because the assessment roll still reflected the name and address of the previous owner 11 weeks after plaintiff had recorded his deed. The use of the assessment roll in providing notice by mail to property owners comports with the requirements of due process and the Town's use of its current assessment roll was appropriate under the circumstances. Plaintiff's allegation that the information on the roll was not properly updated in this case is without support in the record. Plaintiff did not provide notice to either the Town Board or the Town Clerk that title to the subject premises had changed hands. Furthermore, plaintiff's assertion that he paid property taxes on the parcel and thereby gave the appropriate Town authorities notice of his ownership is not supported by any documentary evidence. In addition, plaintiff has not produced any evidence of a requirement that the assessment roll be periodically updated at specific intervals during the tax year to reflect changes in ownership. Moreover, a balancing of the Town's interests against those of the property owners militates against imposing a duty on the Town to verify property ownership information with the County Clerk's office before sending out notices of proposed rezoning to property owners. Such an obligation to create a time-consuming and costly verification system far exceeds the duty to employ a method of notice which is reasonably calculated to apprise owners of a proposed action, and is not constitutionally required. Finally, there is no reason to conclude that more stringent notice procedures should be required for the rezoning of the property in this case than would ordinarily be necessary in an action to foreclose on property for nonpayment of taxes.

 

418 of 992 DOCUMENTS


East Thirteenth St. Community Ass'n v. New York State Urban Dev. Corp.


47803 [M-5897] & M-6479


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


189 A.D.2d 352; 595 N.Y.S.2d 961; 1993 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3524



April 8, 1993, Decided  
April 8, 1993, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, local tenants and resident, brought action pursuant to N.Y. Em. Dom. Proc. § 207, seeking review of the determination and findings of respondent state urban development corporation, a condemnor.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, who were local tenants and residents of nearby buildings, brought an original proceeding pursuant to N.Y. Em. Dom. Proc. Law § 207 which provides for review of the determination and findings of a condemnor. They sought review of a proposed housing project in their neighborhood. Petitioners contended that respondent state urban development corporation was merely lending its name and extraordinary powers to other parties who realistically had a primary stake and interest in the project greater than respondent's interest, and that this was outside the scope of respondent's authority. The court found that the express language of the statute, creating the respondent agency, did not bar respondent's participation in projects where it played an incidental but necessary role, as long as the clearance of blighted areas and reconstruction and rehabilitation remained the primary purpose of its participation. The court further found that respondent complied with the requirement of N.Y. Em. Dom. Proc. Law § 204 to specify the general effect on the environment of the proposed project, and had no duty under N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. Law art. 8, to undertake its own environmental review.


OUTCOME: The determination and findings of respondent agency were confirmed because respondent did not act outside its authority, and its findings were supported by the record.


HEADNOTES

Public Authorities - State Urban Development Corporation - Condemnation Agent - Construction of Residence for Homeless

1. In a proceeding to challenge the planned construction of a residence for the homeless on land currently owned by a municipality, even accepting as true petitioners' allegations that respondent State Urban Development Corporation (UDC) is merely "lending" its name and extraordinary powers to other parties who realistically have a primary stake and interest in the project greater than the UDC and that that is outside the scope of the UDC's authority, it does not necessarily follow that the UDC is acting in excess of its statutory jurisdiction and authority since the actions complained of are not only proper but precisely the sort of activity for which the UDC was created. The UDC is not limited to projects in which it is the prime mover, and there is no bar to it taking part in projects where it plays an incidental but necessary role, as long as the clearance of blighted areas and reconstruction and rehabilitation remain the primary purpose of its participation. Accordingly, inasmuch as the record amply supports the finding that the area in question is substandard and insanitary, and since there is no showing that the UDC determination under review was made corruptly, irrationally or baselessly, there is nothing for the courts to do about it.

Public Housing - Construction of Residence for Homeless - Office and Community Space - Land Acquisition Costs

2. In a proceeding to challenge the planned construction of a residence for the homeless on land currently owned by a municipality, there is no merit to petitioners' contention that since the project also envisions the construction of office and community space, it entails an illegal use of funds to be diverted from the Permanent Housing for Homeless Families (PHHF) program, since the funding of office and community space in the residential building is within both the spirit and express mandate of the Private Housing Finance Law § 64 (5), which permits office and community space that is "incidental and appurtenant" to the housing. While the construction of the commercial building for the municipality at no cost would appear at first blush to be unconnected, the municipality will accept a nominal sum as consideration for the condemnation by the State Urban Development Corporation; the construction of the building is simply a delayed payment, thereby giving the agency charged with administering PHHF funds adequate grounds for considering the erection of the commercial building as permissible land acquisition costs (see, Private Housing Finance Law § 64 [6]).

Public Housing - Construction of Residence for Homeless - Financial Feasibility - Expiration of Tax Credits

3. In a proceeding to challenge the planned construction of a residence for the homeless on land currently owned by a municipality, there is no merit to petitioners' objection that the plan has not been shown to be financially feasible: even if the desired tax credits are obtained, they will expire in about 15 years, while the project owner is obligated to run the project for 35 years to comply with Private Housing Finance Law § 69 (1). However, while the statute provides for project owners of not-for-profit corporations to be "subject to such terms and conditions for a period of thirty-five years or such longer period as may be provided in the project description", there is no correlative requirement that the project owners must, at the initial stage, provide an exhaustive and completely detailed budget for 35 years covering all contingencies. Moreover, judicial review is limited.

Public Authorities - State Urban Development Corporation - Power to Override Local Laws - Condemnation Power - Construction of Residence for Homeless

4. In a proceeding to challenge the planned construction of a residence for the homeless on land currently owned by the City of New York, there is no merit to petitioners' contention that the exercise of the override powers of the State Urban Development Corporation (UDC) (see, McKinney's Uncons Laws of NY § 6266 [3]) in its resolutions under review, which specifically superceded the City's Uniform Land Use Review Procedure (ULURP), was improper on the ground that UDC was not the lead agency on the project, since the fact that the UDC is not the prime mover behind the project is of no moment, and the fact that it is not the lead agency will not circumscribe its override powers. While the UDC does have the right to override local laws, petitioners' contention that various sections of the City Charter and Zoning Resolutions are treated by the proposed project as void is meritless; thus, the prohibition against the sale of City-owned property, except at auction, is not overridden since the property is not being sold by the City but condemned by the UDC. Similarly, the requirement that City facilities be equitably distributed throughout the City is inapplicable since the facility in question will be constructed and operated by a private not-for-profit corporation. Finally, inasmuch as the building is being erected "as of right" there is no necessity for an "override" of the Zoning Resolution.

Public Authorities - State Urban Development Corporation - Condemnation of Site for Residence for Homeless - Adequacy of Notice of Sale of Site to Not-for-Profit Corporation

5. In a proceeding to challenge the planned construction of a residence for the homeless on land currently owned by a municipality, there is no merit to petitioners' contention that the notice promulgated by the State Urban Development Corporation (UDC), which will condemn the land, of its sale of the site to the not-for-profit corporation which will operate the residence was inadequate because, inter alia, it allegedly failed to fix the price for the sale. McKinney's Unconsolidated Laws of New York § 6256 (1) (c) governs the notice requirements of a sale by the UDC and a notice conforming with the statutory strictures was duly published, identifying the not-for-profit corporation as the transferee and reciting that the consideration was nominal; there were no other "essential" conditions placed upon the sale. Even if there had been some technical violation of the statute, it would not affect the validity of the UDC's condemnation procedure, which is the only issue before the court.

Eminent Domain - Condemnation by State Urban Development Corporation - Satisfaction of Procedural Requirements - SEQRA Review - Involved Agency

6. In a proceeding to challenge the planned construction of a residence for the homeless on land currently owned by a municipality, respondent State Urban Development Corporation (UDC), which condemned the site where the residence will be erected, complied with the mandate of EDPL 204 by setting forth the specifications required as to each relevant paragraph of EDPL 204 (B). Moreover, the UDC, which complied with the requirement to specify the general effect on the environment of the proposed project, had no duty under the State Environmental Quality Review Act (SEQRA) to undertake its own environmental review simply because it was the sole State agency involved in the project having the power to condemn; rather, the UDC's determination and findings correctly noted that the State Housing Finance Agency (HFA), as the project's "lead agency", had issued a "negative declaration", and as an "involved agency", the UDC was entitled to rely upon the determination of HFA, which has been closely involved with various aspects of the project.

Environmental Conservation - Environmental Quality Review - Construction of Residence for Homeless

7. The construction of a permanent residence for the homeless, which will also contain office and community space, is not exempt from the requirements of the State Environmental Quality Review Act (SEQRA) on the ground that there is an emergency need to house the poor and homeless, since the project in question does not qualify as one necessary on a "limited and temporary basis" within the meaning of the implementing regulations. Accordingly, the court must review whether the lead agency satisfied the mandate of SEQRA, i.e., took a "hard look" at the relevant areas of environmental concern and made a "reasoned elaboration" of the basis for its determination.

Environmental Conservation - Environmental Quality Review - Negative Declaration

8. In a proceeding to challenge the construction of a permanent residence for the homeless, the "negative declaration" of environmental significance issued by the project's lead agency is adequately supported by the record which contains a photographic description of the project, a "no impact" letter from the State Office of Parks, Recreation and Historic Preservation, an environmental audit showing no evidence of significant chemical contamination, an environmental assessment form which revealed no significant adverse environmental effects, and statements of support for the project from local officials. Under either the "corrupt, irrational or baseless" standard of review, or the "illegal/arbitrary and capricious/abuse of discretion" standard of review, petitioners, who object to the project, have failed to meet their burden of proof. The lead agency identified the relevant areas of environmental concern, took a "hard look" at them, and made a reasoned elaboration of the basis for its determination.

Public Authorities - State Urban Development Corporation - Unconstitutional Delegation of Legislative Authority

9. There is no merit to the contention that the New York State Urban Development Act (L 1968, ch 174, § 1) unconstitutionally delegates legislative authority to the State Urban Development Corporation (UDC) by allowing it to use its condemnation power to "cut through" not only the "red tape" of petty bureaucratic meddling or inaction, but also basic democratic processes and controls, since the UDC's powers stem from the State Constitution itself, and thus there is no constitutional infirmity in either the creation of UDC or its right to override local zoning laws.

 

419 of 992 DOCUMENTS


In re Dale P.


91-05147


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


189 A.D.2d 325; 595 N.Y.S.2d 970; 1993 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3395


June 11, 1992, Argued  
April 5, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Revised June 3, 1993.



DISPOSITION: Ordered that the order is affirmed, without costs or disbursements.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Commissioner of New York City Department of Social Services (Commissioner) sought review of an order from the Family Court of Queens County (New York), which, in a child protective proceeding under N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 10, denied a motion to vacate a prior order that directed the Commissioner to file a termination of parental rights proceeding or directed her to afford legal representation to respondent foster mother.


OVERVIEW: The Commissioner alleged that she was unable to bring a termination of parental rights proceeding because the foster child was never committed to her care, that there was no legal basis to provide legal representation to the foster mother, and that such a direction placed her in an untenable position because she could oppose the adoption. The court found (1) the foster mother and foster child fit the definitions of foster mother and child under N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 371(19); (2) N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 1055 provided all the statutory authority needed to direct the Commissioner to commence a termination of parental rights proceeding on the foster child's behalf, whether the foster child was placed by the Commissioner or by the court; (3) the N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act allowed for the assignment of counsel to the foster mother because of the nature of the judicial proceedings contemplated and such an assignment furthered the objectives of the N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act; (4) the foster mother could be provided with assigned counsel in order to initiate a termination of parental rights proceeding to pursue an adoption; and (5) there was no ethical bar for the Commissioner to provide the legal services.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the lower court in its entirety.


HEADNOTES

Parent and Child - Abused or Neglected Child - Authority of Family Court to Order Commencement of Termination of Parental Rights Proceeding on Behalf of Child Directly Placed by Court

1. In a child protective proceeding in which the child was directly placed by the Family Court with his mother's friend, Family Court did not exceed its authority under Family Court Act § 1055 in subsequently ordering the Commissioner of the New York City Department of Social Services to commence a termination of parental rights proceeding intended to free the child for adoption by his "foster mother". Family Court had the legal authority to place the child outside his family with such a "suitable person" (Family Ct Act § 1055 [a]). Under the statutory scheme, although no social services agency was responsible for the placement, the child nonetheless became a "foster child" in law as well as in fact, and the "foster mother" became his "foster parent" as those terms are defined in Social Services Law § 371 (19). Thus, the court's direction to commence the termination proceeding was not outside the authority granted to the Commissioner by Social Services Law § 384-b, which concerns actions that may be taken with regard to foster children. For purposes of Family Court Act § 1055 (d), which authorizes the court to "make an order directing a social services official ... to institute a proceeding to legally free the [foster] child for adoption, if the court finds reasonable cause to believe that grounds therefor exist", there is no reason to distinguish between a foster child who is not placed by or with a social welfare agency from one directly placed by the court in a home not certified under applicable law. Since the court has the power to place the child in such an environment under Family Court Act § 1055 (a), and to continue such placement pursuant to subdivision (b), it must also be presumed to have the power to direct the initiation of proceedings to free the child for adoption by a foster parent caring for him in that home. If the court did not have such power, there would be a danger of the "foster care limbo" the statutory scheme is designed to avoid.

Parent and Child - Abused or Neglected Child - Authority of Family Court to Direct Commissioner of Social Services to Represent Foster Parent in Adoption Proceeding

2. In a child protective proceeding in which the child was directly placed by the Family Court outside his family with his mother's friend, Family Court did not exceed its authority in subsequently directing the Commissioner of the New York City Department of Social Services to provide legal representation to the "foster mother" to aid her in instituting an adoption proceeding in the event the Commissioner failed to timely comply with the court's order pursuant to Family Court Act § 1055 to commence a termination of parental rights proceeding. In view of the highly interrelated nature of termination and adoption proceedings, where, as here, the same indigent person is prosecuting both a termination of parental rights proceeding and an adoption proceeding, such person is entitled to assigned counsel pursuant to Family Court Act § 262 (a) (iv) with respect to both the termination of parental rights and the adoption proceedings. To hold otherwise would frustrate one of the central policy goals set forth in Social Services Law § 384-b of finding a normal permanent home for children as early as possible where the natural parent cannot or will not provide such a home. Furthermore, naming the Commissioner as the provider of the legal services in this case instead of assigned counsel will not place the Commissioner in an "untenable position" in the event opposition to the adoption arises. A direction to aid the custodial parent in adopting, like the order to institute proceedings to legally free the child for adoption, does not involve any unconstitutional interference with the Commissioner's exercise of independent judgment or discretion with regard to the child. Finally, the direction to aid the "foster mother" in adopting the child by providing legal representation furthers the objectives of the Family Court Act.

 

420 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Allerton Coops Tenants Ass'n v. Biderman


47965


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


189 A.D.2d 249; 596 N.Y.S.2d 10; 1993 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3305



April 1, 1993, Decided  
April 1, 1993, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended.



DISPOSITION: Order of the Supreme Court, New York County, entered on December 9, 1991, which denied defendants' motion for summary judgment dismissing the first and second causes of action, is reversed, on the law, and the motion granted, without costs or disbursements.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A Supreme Court (New York) denied a motion for summary judgment filed by defendant, the City Department of Housing Preservation and Development (the Department), in an action brought by plaintiff tenants in which the tenants claimed they did not receive proper notice of building renovations and prospective rent increases. The Department appealed.


OVERVIEW: The tenants' landlord applied to the Department for a participation loan pursuant to N.Y. Priv. Hous. Fin. Law § 800 et seq. N.Y. Priv. Hous. Fin. Law § 804 required the Department to restructure the rents when the refurbishment was achieved and N.Y. Hous. Fin. Law § 803 mandated that the tenants be advised of the proposed rehabilitation and the expected rent increases. This was accomplished by letter from the landlord and subsequent reminder of rental increases. The tenants were later told that rehabilitation was substantially complete and that the restructured rents, as previously announced, would become effective. Not until the rental increases were about to become effective did the tenants file the current action objecting to the notification process and rental increases. The court found the tenants' allegations unsupported and held that the tenants were provided due process and had no claim against the Department where they never submitted any objections until after the rehabilitation was substantially completed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court judgment and granted the Department's motion for summary judgment because the Department provided the tenants with adequate notice and an opportunity to be heard on the issue of rental housing increases.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and Tenant - Rent Regulation - Due Process Right to Notice of Rent Increase under Loan Program for Rehabilitated Housing

In an action for declaratory and injunctive relief in which plaintiffs, the tenants of six five-story buildings renovated under a loan program administered by the New York City Department of Housing Preservation and Development (HPD) pursuant to Private Housing Finance Law § 800 et seq., allege that they were not accorded constitutionally adequate notice of HPD's determination that the work was complete nor given an opportunity to challenge that determination and comment on the prospective rent increases, defendants' motion for summary judgment dismissing the first and second causes of action should have been granted. Under the statutory scheme, once the refurbishment has been achieved, rents are initially established by HPD at levels deemed sufficient to pay for the work done and to permit the dwelling to be properly maintained. Rent-controlled apartments are decontrolled and the entire building is then placed in the rent stabilization system. Even assuming that plaintiffs are endowed with a constitutionally protected property interest in the rent control and rent stabilization laws, there is no indication that their right to due process was in any way violated. The tenants herein were informed of the intended renovations and the anticipated rent restructuring two years before the new rents went into effect and were notified of the proposed rent rises at least twice in writing and once in person but never submitted any objections until after the rehabilitation had been substantially completed and they were advised that the new rents would soon go into effect. Under the circumstances, the notice available to the tenants cannot be deemed to be constitutionally defective. Finally, to the extent that the tenants are disputing the propriety of the increases in relation to the adequacy of the rehabilitation work actually performed, HPD's determination should have been challenged in a CPLR article 78 proceeding, and if the tenants are dissatisfied with the quality of their services or condition of their dwellings, they have the option of petitioning the Department of Housing and Community Renewal for a rent reduction or pleading abatement in any rent proceeding commenced against them.

 

421 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Hope v. Perales


45532, 45533-4


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


189 A.D.2d 287; 595 N.Y.S.2d 948; 1993 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2738



March 23, 1993, Decided  
March 23, 1993, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, Commissioner of the New York State Department of Social Services and the Department of Health, challenged a decision of the Supreme Court (New York), which found, on a motion by plaintiffs, women, physicians, and health organizations, that the New York State Prenatal Care Assistance Program (PCAP), ch. 584 of the Laws of 1989, codified at N.Y. Pub. Health Law §§ 2521, 2522, and 2529, was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The PCAP provided pregnant women with assistance with prenatal care if their family incomes were within certain percentages of the poverty level. The health organizations argued that, because the PCAP did not encompass allowances for medically necessary abortions for the same class of women, the statute of unconstitutional. The lower court agreed, and found that it unconstitutionally discriminated against women's right to reproductive freedom because the PCAP impermissibly steered indigent women toward childbirth when it paid all related expenses, but none of those associated with terminating a pregnancy. The trial court ordered that the program be expanded to include funds for medically necessary abortions. This decision was stayed pending appeal. On appeal, the court agreed with the lower court, and found that the legislature violated N.Y. Const. art. XVII, §§ 1, 3 by conditioning aid to this needy class on a standard totally unconnected to health or need. The PCAP also failed to conform to N.Y. Const. art. I, § 6, the Due Process Clause, which encompassed the right to reproductive choice. The court held that the expansion of the program was proper.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision that held the statute unconstitutional and affirmed the expansion of the program to remedy the statute's unconstitutionality. The stay of the order and judgment continued pending any further appeal.


HEADNOTES

Social Services - Medical Assistance - Constitutionality of Prenatal Care Assistance Program

1. The New York State Prenatal Care Assistance Program (PCAP) (L 1989, ch 584, Public Health Law § 2520 et seq.), which provides financial assistance to pregnant women with family incomes up to 185% of the poverty level for prenatal and postpartum care, but which excludes allowances for all abortions, is violative of the State Constitution to the extent that the statute's funding scheme allocates medical funds to needy, pregnant women solely on the basis of whether they choose to carry to term. The legislative classification, which limits the use of money to assure a healthy delivery and recovery, does not withstand strict scrutiny since the State's interest in advancing the cause of healthy mothers bearing healthy babies has no reasonable relationship to the statutory abortion exclusion. The promotion of healthy births is not enhanced by requiring poor women to give birth regardless of the medical implications. PCAP, by excluding funding for therapeutic abortions, has the practical effect of being a measure that supports childbirth, sometimes at the expense of the mother's health, thereby rendering the statute in conflict with the requirement that government actions must be constitutionally neutral in affecting the exercise of fundamental rights. Having acknowledged that pregnant women with family incomes between 100 and 185% of the poverty level are "needy" and require help in obtaining pregnancy-related health services, the Legislature violated the State constitutional obligations to aid, care and support the needy (NY Const, art XVII, § 1), and to promote the health of the State's citizens (NY Const, art XVII, § 3) by conditioning aid to this needy class on a standard totally unconnected to need or health; that is, whether an otherwise eligible woman undergoes an abortion or gives birth. Consistent with the dominant sentiment of the Legislature, the constitutional defect in PCAP is best remedied by expanding the ambit of the program to include funding for medically indicated abortions rather than voiding the law in its entirety.

Social Services - Medical Assistance - Constitutionality of Prenatal Care Assistance Program - Due Process of Law

2. The New York State Prenatal Care Assistance Program (PCAP) (L 1989, ch 584, Public Health Law § 2520 et seq.), which provides financial assistance to pregnant women with family incomes up to 185% of the poverty level for prenatal and postpartum care, but excludes allowances for all abortions, is violative of the Due Process Clause of the State Constitution (NY Const, art I, § 6), which encompasses the right to reproductive choice, an integral part of the right to privacy and bodily autonomy. Inherent in the right to privacy is the right of an individual to make free choices concerning procreation, contraception and sexual activity. Once the State assumed the responsibility of helping needy, pregnant women, it was required to do so in a neutral nondiscriminatory manner that does not coerce poor women into choosing childbirth even at the expense of their health and well-being. Consistent with the dominant sentiment of the Legislature, the constitutional defect in PCAP is best remedied by expanding the ambit of the program to include funding for medically indicated abortions rather than voiding the law in its entirety.

Parties - Intervention

3. In an action to declare unconstitutional the Prenatal Care Program (PCAP) (L 1989, ch 584; Public Health Law § 2520 et seq.), which provides financial assistance to pregnant women, but excludes allowances for abortions, since the proposed intervenors in this case have not demonstrated a real and substantial interest in the litigation or that defendants are unable to adequately protect the interests of those who seek to uphold the validity of PCAP, it was not an abuse of discretion for the trial court to have denied the motions of a hospital and others to intervene.

People v. Martinez


48275


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


191 A.D.2d 306; 595 N.Y.S.2d 39; 1993 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2583



March 18, 1993, Entered

 

423 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Doe v. State


0108


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


189 A.D.2d 199; 595 N.Y.S.2d 592; 1993 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2967; 2 Am. Disabilities Cas. (BNA) 633



March 12, 1993, Decided  
March 12, 1993, Filed



DISPOSITION: Judgment unanimously modified, on the law and facts, and as modified, affirmed, without costs, and matter remitted to Court of Claims for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion by Fallon, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, claimant and husband, sought review of the judgment of the Court of Claims (New York), which awarded the claimant and her husband damages but denied in part the claimant's claim for future economic loss. The state cross-appealed.


OVERVIEW: The claimant was stuck by a needle contaminated with the blood of an AIDS patient as a result of the failure of the correction officers to intervene in the hospital employees' struggle with the inmate/patient. The lower court found that the state breached its duty to provide reasonable security to the hospital staff and that the state's negligence was the sole proximate cause of claimants' injuries. On appeal, the court held that the damages awarded to the claimant were reasonable. However, the court that the lower court erred in limiting claimant's future economic loss to her postinjury life expectancy. The court held that where the proof in the record permits the loss to be ascertained with reasonable certainty, damages attributable to an injured person's lost earning capacity due to injury must be awarded directly to that person during his or her lifetime utilizing a preinjury life expectancy. The court further held that the abolition of the right of a tort victim to receive an award for future damages in a lump sum did not deprive that victim of a right or interest protected by the Due Process Clause of NY Const. art I, § 6.


OUTCOME: The court modified the judgment to provide the claimant with an award for all her future economic loss, and the matter was remitted to the lower court for purposes of determining the amount of the judgment.


HEADNOTES

Damages - Inadequate and Excessive Damages - AIDS - Loss of Consortium

1. In a negligence action in which the State was held liable for the injuries sustained by claimant, a hospital nurse, who became infected with AIDS when she was stuck by a contaminated needle after two State correction officers refused to help restrain an inmate being treated at the hospital, the $ 4,250,000 aggregate award made for claimant's past ($ 750,000) and future ($ 3,500,000) pain and suffering is not excessive (CPLR 5501 [c]) in view of claimant's lessened life expectancy and the suffering that will attend her death from AIDS. In addition, the award of $ 250,000 for past and $ 750,000 for claimant's husband's future loss of consortium properly reflects the emotional, physical and financial contributions that claimant has made during the course of the parties' marriage, including the services she has rendered in caring for the household and assisting with their three children.

Damages - Lost Earnings - Future Economic Loss - Award Based upon Preinjury Life Expectancy

2. In a negligence action in which the State was held liable for the injuries sustained by claimant, a hospital nurse who became infected with AIDS when she was stuck by a contaminated needle after two State correction officers refused to help restrain an inmate being treated at the hospital, the Court of Claims erred in limiting claimant's future economic loss to her postinjury life expectancy and determining that claimant's distributees could be compensated for any loss beyond claimant's reasonable postinjury life expectancy by bringing a wrongful death action. Where, as here, the economic and medical proof in the record permits the loss to be ascertained with reasonable certainty, damages attributable to an injured person's lost earning capacity due to injury must be awarded directly to that person during his or her lifetime utilizing a preinjury life expectancy. Based on the proof in this case, claimant, who was 42 years of age at the time of trial and earning approximately $ 30,000 annually, will likely become completely disabled from AIDS in 1996. Claimant has a reasonable work-life expectancy as a registered nurse through the age of 58. Utilizing the calculations offered by the expert economists and making allowances for lost fringe benefits as a percentage of income and without any adjustment for claimant's personal consumption, the impairment of earnings and lost wage component of claimant's future economic loss is determined to be $ 450,000. Additionally, the value of claimant's services as a homemaker is determined to be $ 275,000. Accordingly, the judgment should be modified to provide for such an award and the matter remitted to the Court of Claims for purposes of determining the amount of the judgment to be entered pursuant to CPLR 5041 (e).

Damages - Structured Judgment - Constitutionality of Statutory Periodic Payment Provision - No Violation of Americans with Disabilities Act of 1990

3. The periodic payment provision of CPLR article 50-B requiring future damage awards in excess of $ 250,000 to be paid over a period of time instead of in one lump sum does not unconstitutionally deprive claimants of their property without due process of law in this negligence action in which the State was held liable for the injuries sustained by a hospital nurse who became infected with AIDS when she was stuck by a contaminated needle after two State correction officers refused to help restrain an inmate being treated at the hospital. Article 50-B was intended to maintain the moderate cost of liability insurance premiums, while assuring adequate compensation for tort victims throughout the entire period of their loss. Since the Legislature was acting in furtherance of permissible State objectives and the means adopted in article 50-B reasonably relate to their accomplishment, the abolition of the right of a tort victim to receive an award for future damages in a lump sum does not deprive that victim of a right or interest protected by the Due Process Clause of the State Constitution (NY Const, art I, § 6). Furthermore, since the term "discount rate" as used in article 50-B can be determined with reasonable certainty, the statute is not unconstitutionally vague. In providing that present value determinations are to be made "in accordance with generally accepted actuarial practices by applying the discount rate in effect at the time of the award" (CPLR 5041 [e]), the Legislature merely provided for a flexible approach to such determinations. Finally, application of article 50-B to claimants' award does not violate the Americans with Disabilities Act of 1990 (42 USC § 12101 et seq.) since, even if the Act applies to a situation such as presented by this case, its provisions are not implicated here because there is no causal nexus between the alleged discriminatory action and the disabled individual's disability.

80-05 Grand Cent. Parkway Corp. v. Commissioner of Fin.


45112


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


191 A.D.2d 239; 595 N.Y.S.2d 401; 1993 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3701



March 9, 1993, Decided  
March 9, 1993, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant corporation was granted reargument of its appeal from the order and judgment of the Supreme Court, New York County (New York) declaring New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § 11-2502(a)(3) (1986) to be a valid legislative enactment in the corporation's declaratory judgment action against respondent Commissioner of Finance of the City of New York.


OVERVIEW: The Commissioner promulgated New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § 11-2502(a)(3) (1986), which imposed a five percent tax on a reduced room rate utilizing the full day rate. The corporation brought an action against the Commissioner challenging the validity of the enactment. The trial court entered judgment declaring the provision to be valid, which the court affirmed in its original opinion. The court granted reargument, and it subsequently recalled and vacated its original opinion and issued a superceding opinion and order. In its superceding opinion, the court reversed the trial court's judgment, ruling that the Commissioner exceeded his authority in promulgating § 11-2502(a)(3). The court held that the enabling legislation contained in 1980 N.Y. Laws 253 specifically authorized a graduated schedule for taxing short-stay occupancies, which was inconsistent with the flat percentage rate established in New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § 11-2502(a)(3) (1986). Further, the 1986 amendment to the enabling act, 1986 N.Y. Laws 219, contained no short-stay tax. Based upon that omission, the court concluded that the legislature intended to exclude such a tax.


OUTCOME: Upon reargument of the corporation's appeal, the court recalled and vacated its earlier opinion and order. In its superceding opinion and order, the court reversed, without costs, the trial court's order and judgment declaring the challenged statutory provision to be a valid legislative enactment in the corporation's declaratory judgment action against the Commissioner. The court declared the provision to be null and void.

 

425 of 992 DOCUMENTS


In re Keith H.


91-03421


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


188 A.D.2d 81; 594 N.Y.S.2d 268; 1993 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 1574


June 4, 1992, Submitted  
February 22, 1993, Decided



DISPOSITION: Ordered that the order is reversed, on the law, without costs or disbursements, the respondent's motion to dismiss the petition is denied, the petition is reinstated, and the matter is remitted to the Family Court, Dutchess County, for further proceedings on the petition.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant school principal sought review of an order of the Dutchess County Family Court (New York), dismissing appellant's petition in a proceeding to declare respondent youth as a person in need of supervision.


OVERVIEW: Appellant school principal petitioned, under the Family Court Act art. 7, to declare respondent youth as a person in need of supervision (PINS). The contentions of the petition were based upon school records. Respondent moved to dismiss, contending that the petition was based upon hearsay statements. The court granted the motion to dismiss, finding that a PINS petition should incorporate a nonhearsay requirement upon due process grounds. On appeal, the court reinstated the petition and remanded. Considering the requirements of procedural due process, the court concluded that a petition based upon hearsay statements could be used to support a PINS proceeding. The Family Court Act's provisions provided reliability and adequate notice to respondent, and the addition of a nonhearsay requirement would not significantly strengthen those provisions but might chill petitioners' efforts to secure assistance for a respondent. Using the rational basis standard of review, the court concluded that respondent's equal protection argument failed because the PINS proceeding and the juvenile delinquent proceeding, which required nonhearsay statements, were different in nature.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order, reinstated the petition, and remitted for further proceedings; due process and equal protection concerns did not require that a "person in need of supervision" petition rest upon nonhearsay statements.


HEADNOTES

Infants - Persons in Need of Supervision - Sufficiency of Petition

A petition based on hearsay allegations is constitutionally permissible in a proceeding under Family Court Act article 7 to adjudicate a person in need of supervision (PINS), even though a juvenile delinquency petition under Family Court Act article 3 must be based on nonhearsay allegations. Family Court Act § 732 requires only that the petition in a PINS proceeding specify "the acts on which the allegations are based and the time and place they allegedly occurred". Due process does not dictate that the allegations in a PINS petition be in nonhearsay form. Even before the petition is filed, respondent has been advised at a mandatory assessment conference of the source of the allegations against him and the particular conduct at issue. The imposition of a nonhearsay requirement might interfere with the State's goal of providing an informal procedure whereby youths at risk of committing more serious acts receive appropriate rehabilitation and treatment; to require a PINS petition to provide sworn statements from others to support the various acts of misconduct specified in the petition would be unduly burdensome and could act as a barrier preventing parents or others unversed in the court system from seeking assistance. Moreover, respondent's right to equal protection of the laws was not violated. No suspect category is involved in the distinction between respondents in PINS and juvenile delinquency proceedings, and the distinction between hearsay and nonhearsay petitions has no effect on a respondent's liberty interest. The discretionary decision to detain a PINS respondent prior to the fact-finding hearing may not be predicated on the hearsay allegations of the petition (see, Family Ct Act § 739), and although the ultimate disposition in a PINS proceeding may involve a deprivation of liberty after a fact-finding hearing, the disposition is not affected by the hearsay nature of the petition. The hearsay form of a PINS petition is consistent with the objective of providing a more informal procedure for those respondents whose conduct does not rise to the level of criminal conduct at issue in a juvenile delinquency proceeding.

 

426 of 992 DOCUMENTS


People v. Davis


61820


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


190 A.D.2d 908; 593 N.Y.S.2d 599; 1993 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 1098



February 11, 1993, Decided  
February 11, 1993, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The County Court of Broome County (New York) convicted defendant upon his plea of guilty of the crime of criminal possession of a controlled substance in the second degree. Defendant was sentenced in accordance with the plea agreement to an indeterminate prison term of eight-and-one-third years to life. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was indicted for the A-I felony of criminal-possession of a controlled substance in the first degree. He subsequently pleaded guilty to the A-II felony of criminal possession of a controlled substance in the second degree. The court held that the sentence was not cruel and unusual punishment because the statute in question was not unconstitutional on its face. The court held that the amount of cocaine involved and defendant's admitted knowing possession did not amount to one of the "rare cases" in which the application of the statute constituted cruel and unusual punishment. The court found that the sentence imposed was not harsh or excessive because defendant admitted to the facts, was allowed to plead guilty to a lesser crime, and agreed to the sentence as a part of a negotiated plea bargain. In vacating the sentence, the court held that the trial court failed to exercise its independent judgment in imposing the sentence. The court held that if the trial court ultimately imposed a more lenient sentence that the one agreed to by the prosecution, the prosecution was entitled to withdraw its consent to the plea.


OUTCOME: The court ordered a modification of the trial court's judgment on the law by vacating the sentence imposed. The court remitted the case to the trial court for resentencing.
