Ruotolo v. State


46655& M-4660


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


187 A.D.2d 160; 593 N.Y.S.2d 198; 1993 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 942



February 4, 1993, Decided  
February 4, 1993, Entered



DISPOSITION: Order of the Court of Claims of the State of New York, entered August 8, 1991, which denied claimants-appellants' motion pursuant to CPLR 2221 for reargument (denominated a motion to renew) and vacatur of the court's prior order entered September 9, 1988, granting defendant's motion for summary judgment dismissing the claim, is reversed, on the law, the motion granted and, upon reargument, the court's order entered September 9, 1988 is vacated, and defendant's motion for summary judgment dismissing the claim denied, the claim reinstated and the matter remanded for further proceedings, without costs. Motion by defendant-respondent to file supplemental brief and appendix granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants estate administratrix and injured police officers challenged the judgment of the New York Court of Claims, which held that their action against respondent State for wrongful death and personal injuries was barred by the "fireman's rule."


OVERVIEW: Three police officers were shot and one was killed when a robbery suspect opened fire on them. The robbery suspect had been released on parole from a manslaughter conviction, had since been arrested several times, and sent back to jail. Upon his second release, the police, prosecutor, court, and parole authorities were aware that he had absconded, but no parole violation warrant was issued. The families of the victims filed an action against the State, and appealed after the trial court's dismissal. While the appeal was pending, the New York legislature repealed the "fireman's rule" and allowed for a cause of action for police officers or firemen injured in the line of duty due to a property owner's negligence. The court held that the new legislation gave the estate administratrix and injured police officers' families a cause of action because at the time the legislation was enacted, the action was still pending and had not been reduced to a final judgment.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment in favor of the State.


HEADNOTES

Statutes - Retroactive Application of Statute - Violation of Due Process

1. In an action against the State to recover for injuries and wrongful death arising out of the shooting of three police officers by a parolee, the State, through the Attorney-General, who takes the anomalous position of attacking the constitutionality of an amendment to General Municipal Law § 205-e providing for the revival of claims such as those at bar, may not be heard to complain that the retroactive law which it enacted is void as a violation of due process.

Statutes - Retroactive Application of Statute - Claim against State

2. In an action against the State to recover for injuries and wrongful death arising out of the shooting of three police officers by a parolee, the State's contention that an amendment to General Municipal Law § 205-e, which revived claims such as those at bar, is inapplicable and that any legislative attempt to revive such claims is unconstitutional since it contravenes the prohibition against the gift or loan of State money (NY Const, art VII, § 8), or the audit, allowance or payment of time-barred claims (NY Const, art III, § 19), is unavailing since the only final determination on the merits of the instant action was made by the Appellate Division when it held that claimants' common-law claim was barred by the "fireman's rule" as applied in Santangelo v State of New York (71 NY2d 393). Although the original 1989 version of section 205-e, which created the cause of action in favor of police officers, was before the Appellate Division, that Court merely held that it could not be retroactively applied in accordance with the general rules of statutory construction. Accordingly, the Court did not reach the question of the statute's applicability to the case at bar. The statute does not constitute a gift or loan of State money, inasmuch as there is nothing specifically addressed to the State, nor does it constitute the allowance of a time-barred claim.

State - Claim against State - Injuries to Police Officers - Revival of Claims

3. In an action against the State to recover for injuries and wrongful death arising out of the shooting of three police officers by a parolee, the enactment of an amendment to General Municipal Law § 205-e, which provided for the revival of claims of police officers which had been created by the original version of the statute, did not violate constitutional proscriptions against gifts or loans of State money or the allowance of time- barred claims (see, NY Const, art VII, § 8; art III, § 19), since the State is no different than any other defendant against whom a claim under the new section could be asserted; nor is it in any different position than it has been since 1935 vis-a-vis firefighters. Merely because a statute establishing a right to recover against the State is retroactive does not render it unconstitutional; indeed the treatment of retrospective laws as a distinct constitutional issue is inappropriate since there is no constitutional prohibition against them except for Federal proscriptions against bills of attainder and ex post facto laws. Rather, there is a strong public interest in bringing police officers into parity with firefighters. The obvious intent of the Legislature was to provide claimants' class with a day in court, and there is no question that claimants' claim was still pending and had not been reduced to final judgment as of the date of the amendment in question.

 

428 of 992 DOCUMENTS


In re Cooper


91-03662


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


187 A.D.2d 128; 592 N.Y.S.2d 797; 1993 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 796


January 4, 1993, Submitted  
February 1, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Revised March 11, 1993.



DISPOSITION: Ordered that the order and decree is affirmed insofar as appealed from, with costs payable by the appellant personally.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant claimant sought review of a decision from the Kings County Surrogate's Court (New York), which granted the application of respondent executrix to dismiss a petition for election against the decedent's will and determined that the claimant was not entitled to exercise a right of election.


OVERVIEW: The claimant, a long-term homosexual partner of the decedent, sought to claim a spouse's elective share of the estate. The lower court ruled in favor of the executrix, holding that the claimant's relationship with the decedent was not a marriage for purposes of the elective share law, N.Y. Est. Powers and Trusts Law § 5-1.1. The court affirmed the judgment, finding that the legislature did not intend to include homosexual partners within the definition of a surviving spouse. In considering the claimant's equal protection argument, the court applied the rational basis standard and found no constitutional violation. The court observed that homosexual activity was not a fundamental right and that the state could classify persons authorized to marry.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision that the claimant was not a surviving spouse and could not exercise a right of election.


HEADNOTES

Wills - Elective Share of Surviving Spouse - Homosexual Relationship

1. The survivor of a homosexual relationship, alleged to be a "spousal relationship", is not entitled to a right of election against the decedent's will, pursuant to EPTL 5-1.1, since the Legislature has expressly defined a "surviving spouse" as a husband or wife, and, even in the absence of such express definition, an interpretation of the statute to the same effect would be warranted, given the fact that the language of a statute is to be construed according to its natural and most obvious sense and in accordance with its ordinary and accepted meaning. Although the Court of Appeals has held that same-sex partners were "family members" for purposes of rent control regulations prohibiting certain evictions, that Court subsequently held that a lesbian partner was not a "parent" under the Domestic Relations Law. Accordingly, the term "surviving spouse" as used in EPTL 5-1.1 cannot be interpreted to include homosexual life partners.

Wills - Elective Share of Surviving Spouse - Homosexual Relationship - Equal Protection

2. The survivor of a homosexual relationship, alleged to be a "spousal relationship", is not entitled to a right of election against the decedent's will, pursuant to EPTL 5-1.1, and the denial of such right of election does not violate constitutional equal protection principles. Purported homosexual marriages do not give rise to any rights pursuant to section 5-1.1 and no constitutional rights are abrogated or violated in so holding. Any equal protection analysis is to be measured by the rational basis standard, i.e., the legislation is presumed to be valid and will be sustained if the classification drawn is rationally related to a legitimate State interest, and the classification in question is neither irrational nor invidious.

 

429 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Grumet v. Board of Educ.


65398


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


187 A.D.2d 16; 592 N.Y.S.2d 123; 1992 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 14666



December 31, 1992, Decided  
December 31, 1992, Entered



DISPOSITION: Ordered that the judgment is affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, a village education board and others (board), appealed a summary judgment from the Albany County Supreme Court (New York). The trial court found in favor of plaintiffs, an association and others, and held that 1989 N.Y. Laws ch. 748 (chapter 748), the law founding the village's school district, effected an unconstitutional establishment of religion.


OVERVIEW: The association filed suit, contending that chapter 748 was unconstitutional because it founded the school district primarily to accommodate the religious beliefs and practices of the handicapped Satmarer Hasidim children whom the district principally served. The trial court summarily declared the statute unconstitutional. On the board's appeal, the court held that: (1) the association and its officers lacked standing to contest the constitutionality of the statute because they did not qualify as citizen taxpayers nor had they suffered any injury in fact; (2) under N.Y. Exec. Law §§ 123-a, -b[1], the individual plaintiffs had standing as citizen taxpayers to challenge the statute; (3) the ostensibly secular purpose of the statute did not survive Establishment Clause scrutiny; (4) under the Establishment Clause, the primary effect of the statute was to advance religion because it created a school district coterminous with the village, which was inhabited by residents of almost exclusively one religious sect; and (5) the findings on secular purpose and primary effect rendered 1989 N.Y. Laws ch. 748 violative of the Establishment Clause of both the federal and state Constitutions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that found unconstitutional the legislation establishing the village school district that the board administered.


HEADNOTES

Parties - Standing - Constitutionality of Law Creating Village School District to Meet Religious Needs

1. Respondents, the New York State School Boards Association and two of its officers in their capacity as representatives of the Association, lack standing to challenge the constitutionality of a law creating a Village School District for a Village of Hasidic Jews in an effort to provide public funding for special educational services for the nonpublic school handicapped children of the Village (L 1989, ch 748) since the Association itself is not a citizen-taxpayer within the meaning of State Finance Law article 7-A nor has it sustained any injury in fact. In addition, the Association has not met the requirements for associational or organizational standing since, as units of municipal government, the Association's member school boards do not have the substantive right to raise constitutional challenges to a State statute, particularly in the absence of any claim that compliance with the statute will force one or more of the member school boards to violate a constitutional proscription. However, the Association's officers have standing in their individual capacities as citizen-taxpayers to maintain this action under State Finance Law § 123-b (1) for declaratory or injunctive relief to prevent the unconstitutional disbursement of State funds. The Village District created by chapter 748 will receive State funding and, therefore, the constitutionality of that statute can be challenged in a citizen-taxpayer action. Furthermore, the fact that the action was discontinued as to the State officials when the two school districts involved intervened as party defendants does not affect the individual plaintiffs' standing since the expenditure of State funds remains an issue and the Attorney-General continues to appear in the action pursuant to Executive Law § 71.

Constitutional Law - Establishment of Religion - Constitutionality of Law Creating Village School District to Meet Religious Needs

2. A law creating a Village School District for a Village of Hasidic Jews in an effort to provide public funding for special educational services for the Village's nonpublic school handicapped children (L 1989, ch 748) is unconstitutional as violative of the Establishment of Religion Clause of the State and Federal Constitutions (NY Const, art XI, § 3; US Const 1st Amend). The challenged statute was designed not merely to provide special educational services to the handicapped children of the Village, but to provide those services within the Village so that the children would remain subject to the language, lifestyle and environment created by the religious community and avoid mixing with children whose language, lifestyle and environment are not the product of that religion. Even if the statute has a secular purpose, its principal or primary effect still impermissibly advances religion. The impermissible effect is the symbolic impact of creating a new school district coterminous with a religious community to provide educational services that were already available in an effort to resolve a dispute between the religious community and the school district within which the community was formerly located, a dispute based upon the language, lifestyle and environment of the community's children created by the religious tenets, practices and beliefs of the community. All of the parents' secular concerns regarding the adequacy of the special educational services offered by the former school district for children with "special psychological vulnerabilities" to "exposure to the outside world" could have been addressed in the administrative and judicial review process for handicapped children (Education Law § 4404).

People v. Garrett


1497


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


188 A.D.2d 1055; 592 N.Y.S.2d 1011; 1992 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 14927



December 30, 1992, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order by the Cayuga County Court (New York), which convicted defendant of assault in the second degree and aggravated harassment in the second degree.


OVERVIEW: Defendant pleaded guilty to assault in the second degree and aggravated harassment in the second degree. As part of the plea bargain, defendant waived his right to appeal. Because defendant did not contend that the waiver was constitutionally defective or that public policy precluded enforcement of the waiver, the court's affirmance was predicated on the absence of any reviewable issues.Defendant's contention that the aggravated harassment statute, N.Y. Penal Law §240.30(3), was unconstitutional was not preserved for review. Further, the court rejected defendant's claim that there was no evidence that he intended to cause serious physical injury or that he caused such injury. Defendant failed to preserve his challenge to the factual sufficiency of the plea allocation by not filing a motion under either N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 220.60(3) to withdraw the plea or under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 440.10 to vacate the judgment of conviction. The court further concluded that defendant had meaningful representation. The court declined to modify defendant's sentence in light of the serious nature of the crime and defendant's admission that he struck the first blow.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment, which convicted defendant of assault and aggravated harassment.

 

431 of 992 DOCUMENTS


People v. Glenn


1543


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


185 A.D.2d 84; 592 N.Y.S.2d 175; 1992 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 14955



December 30, 1992, Decided  
December 30, 1992, Filed



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Cayuga County Court (New York) of criminal possession of a controlled substance in the first degree. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: A police officer pulled over a vehicle. The trunk contained a suitcase. The officer testified that the passenger told the officer he needed to speak with defendant, the driver, to obtain the suitcase's combination lock number. The passenger and defendant fled. The suitcase contained heroin and cocaine. The trial court charged the jury on the presumption of knowing possession, N.Y. Penal Law § 220.25(1) (stating that the presence of drugs in a vehicle created a presumption of knowing possession by all in the vehicle). The court held that admission of the officer's testimony as a statement made by a co-conspirator violated defendant's right of confrontation. The court held that the statement was not reliable. However, the court held that because the proof of guilt was overwhelming, the error was harmless. The presumption was permissible if there was a rational connection between the known fact proven and the ultimate fact presumed. That connection was shown by the facts that the key to the suitcase was in the vehicle, defendant slammed shut the trunk and sped away, a witness saw defendant throw the suitcase into the river, and the suitcase contained a large amount of narcotics.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Harmless and Prejudicial Error - Admission of Codefendant's Statement

1. In a criminal prosecution for possession of controlled substances, arising out of an incident wherein an automobile driven by defendant was stopped by a State Trooper and heroin and cocaine were ultimately discovered in a suitcase which defendant removed from the trunk of the vehicle and threw off a bridge after he and a companion fled from the Trooper who had stopped them, the trial court erred in admitting the statement of defendant's companion to the Trooper that he had to talk with defendant to get the combination to the suitcase. Since the companion had a very strong motive to lie to convince the Trooper that defendant had control over the suitcase, the statement does not bear sufficient indicia of reliability to justify its admission in the absence of cross-examination, and because the admission of the statement violated defendant's right to confrontation, its admission was an error of constitutional dimension. However, in light of the overwhelming proof of defendant's guilt, the admission of the statement was harmless.

Crimes - Controlled Substances - Presumption That Occupants of Automobile are in Knowing Possession of Drugs Found Therein

2. In a criminal prosecution for possession of controlled substances, arising out of an incident wherein an automobile driven by defendant was stopped by a State Trooper and heroin and cocaine were ultimately discovered in a suitcase which defendant removed from the trunk of the vehicle and threw off a bridge after he and a companion fled from the Trooper who had stopped them, the trial court did not err in charging the presumption of knowing possession contained in Penal Law § 220.25 (1). The presumption is not unconstitutional as applied if there is a rational connection between the known fact proven by the prosecution and the ultimate fact presumed. Here, there was such a rational connection, since defendant was driving the vehicle when it was stopped, the lock mechanism to open the trunk was located on the driver's door, and the key to the suitcase was inside the vehicle. Under those circumstances, it was rational to presume that defendant had both the ability and the intent to exercise dominion and control over the drugs in the trunk.

Crimes - Verdict - Sufficiency of Evidence

3. In a criminal prosecution for possession of controlled substances, arising out of an incident wherein an automobile driven by defendant was stopped by a State Trooper and heroin and cocaine were ultimately discovered in a suitcase which defendant removed from the trunk of the vehicle and threw off a bridge after he and a companion fled from the Trooper who had stopped them, the evidence was sufficient to support the verdict and the verdict was not against the weight of the evidence. The Trooper's identification of the suitcase, combined with the evidence that the suitcase was discovered downstream from the bridge, was sufficient to prove that it was the same one that was in the trunk of defendant's vehicle, and the evidence, including the statutory presumption of possession (Penal Law § 220.25 [1]) and defendant's actions in fleeing, was likewise sufficient to support the jury's determination that defendant knowingly possessed the drugs in the trunk.

Crimes - Appeal - Preservation of Issue for Appellate Review - Misconduct of Prosecutor

4. In a criminal prosecution for possession of controlled substances, defendant's contention that certain prosecutorial remarks in the opening statement and during cross-examination deprived him of a fair trial was not preserved for appellate review since defendant failed to object at trial. Although the remarks were improper and the prosecutor's attempts to appeal to the possible racial bias of jurors should not be condoned, the conviction should not be reversed in the interest of justice, since the comments were isolated and did not amount to a persistent pattern of racial references.

Crimes - Jurors

5. In a criminal prosecution for possession of controlled substances, the trial court did not err by allowing court officers to accompany a deliberating juror who lived alone to her home to retrieve heart and cancer medication before returning her to the hotel where the jury was sequestered for the evening.

SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT

550 Cent. Ave. Deli Corp. v. Commissioner of Taxation & Fin.


65166


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


188 A.D.2d 845; 591 N.Y.S.2d 590; 1992 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 14372



December 17, 1992, Decided  
December 17, 1992, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, the tax commissioner and department, challenged the judgment of the Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), which converted an application by respondent taxpayer, brought pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78, into an action for monies had and received.


OVERVIEW: The tax commissioner and department (department) were directed to answer or otherwise move with respect to the taxpayer's converted complaint. The court reversed the judgment and held that the supreme court erred in treating the proceeding as an action for monies had and received. An essential element of such an action, payment of the tax in question under protest or duress, was entirely lacking. The court found that the legislature set forth in unambiguous terms the parameters for seeking refunds of sales and use taxes in N.Y. Tax Law §§ 1138, 1139, 1140. Pursuant to N.Y. Tax Law § 1138(a)(3)(A), a bulk sale purchaser's liability for taxes owed by the seller was finally and irrevocably fixed unless the purchaser applied for a hearing within 90 days of the issuance of notice of liability by the department. The taxpayer was further required to file an application for a refund. The court held that the taxpayer failed to raise a claim that warranted a judgment that excused its failure to exhaust administrative remedies.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment in favor of the taxpayer and held in favor of the department. The court held that the taxpayer failed to exhaust administrative remedies and that it was error to convert the taxpayer's application into an action for monies had and received.

Sobel v. Higgins


47394


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


188 A.D.2d 286; 590 N.Y.S.2d 883; 1992 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 13475



December 1, 1992, Decided  
December 1, 1992, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landlord appealed a decision of the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), which denied her claim that rent control regulations violated her right to withdraw rent controlled housing accommodations from the rental market upon a good faith showing that the landlord did not wish to remain in the business of being a landlord.


OVERVIEW: The landlord was the owner of a four-story row house which, at all times since purchased by her, had two apartments occupied by rent-controlled tenants who had a perpetual right to remain in possession subject to rent control by reason of their age or longevity in occupancy. The landlord alleged that she no longer wished to be a landlord and challenged the constitutionality of § 26-408 of the New York City Rent and Rehabitation Law, New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code, tit. 26, ch. 3, entitled "Evictions", and § 2204.9 of the New York City Rent and Eviction Regulations, 9 N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. §§ 2200-2210, titled "Withdrawal of occupied housing accommodations from rental market." The trial court upheld the constitutionality of the statutes. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that because the landlord could cease doing business under the circumstances set forth in the challenged regulations, or could simply sell the property at any time, the restrictions she complained of were well within the government's traditional police power to adjust landlord-tenant relationships by regulating evictions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court, which denied the landlord's claim that rent control regulations violated her right to withdraw rent controlled housing accommodations from the rental market upon a good faith showing that the landlord did not wish to remain in the business of being a landlord.
