Laks v. Division of Taxation of the Dep't of Taxation & Fin.


1314


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


183 A.D.2d 316; 590 N.Y.S.2d 958; 1992 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 14081



November 18, 1992, Decided  
November 18, 1992, Filed



DISPOSITION: Order unanimously reversed, on the law, with costs, and judgment granted in accordance with the opinion by Green, J. P.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer sought review after the Erie County Supreme Court (New York) denied the taxpayer's motion for summary judgment seeking to declare a tax warrant filed by respondent department null and void. N.Y. Tax Law § 1141 (b), N.Y. Tax Law § 1145, N.Y. Tax Law § 1133 (a).


OVERVIEW: In his capacity as an employee, the taxpayer filed a corporation's November 1982 sales tax return. In August 1983, the department demanded payment of the taxes, plus interest and penalties. The taxpayer filed a petition challenging the department's authority to hold him responsible for the taxes, interest, or penalties. The State Tax Commission dismissed the petition. In 1990, the department filed a warrant against the taxpayer. In 1991 the taxpayer filed a complaint, seeking judgment declaring the warrant null and void. The trial court denied the taxpayer's motion for summary judgment and held that the taxpayer was liable for penalties and interest under N.Y. Tax Law § 1145. The court held that an administrative determination of whether the taxpayer was a "person liable for the tax" was never available and a judicial determination was never sought. Therefore, the department had no legal foundation or authority upon which to issue a warrant pursuant to N.Y. Tax Law § 1141 (b). The department also had no authority to hold the taxpayer liable for penalties and interest. N.Y. Tax Law § 1133 (a) did not extend personal liability for payment of interest and penalties.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order, which denied the taxpayer's motion for summary judgment.


HEADNOTES

Taxation - Tax Liens, Tax Sales and Tax Titles - Tax Warrant - Personal Liability for Unpaid Corporate Sales Taxes

1. In an action seeking a declaration that a tax warrant, filed against plaintiff by defendant State Division of Taxation pursuant to Tax Law § 1141 (b), is null and void on the ground that the Division cannot issue a warrant against plaintiff personally for unpaid corporate sales taxes when it had never been determined in any proceeding that plaintiff was a "person liable for the tax", Supreme Court erred in denying plaintiff's motion for summary judgment, since Tax Law § 1133, 1131 (1) do not provide sufficient authority to permit the Division to issue a warrant against plaintiff as a "person liable for the tax". An administrative determination of whether a person is a "person liable" was never available and a judicial determination was never sought; therefore, the Division had no legal foundation upon which to issue a warrant, and the only "person liable" against which defendant could proceed was the delinquent corporation (see, Tax Law § 1101 [a]). Moreover, to permit the Division to issue a warrant without any legal determination of plaintiff's personal liability raises serious constitutional concerns regarding notice and an opportunity to be heard.

Taxation - Tax Liens, Tax Sales and Tax Titles - Tax Warrant - Personal Liability for Interest and Penalties on Unpaid Corporate Sales Tax Deficiency

2. The State Division of Taxation had no authority under Tax Law article 28 to hold plaintiff liable for penalties and interest on an unpaid corporate sales tax deficiency where there had been no determination that plaintiff was a "person liable for the tax" (Tax Law § 1141 [b]), and Tax Law § 1133 (a), which provides that every person required to collect taxes shall be personally liable for the tax imposed, collected or required to be collected, does not, by its terms, extend personal liability for payment of interest and penalties. Accordingly, plaintiff would be liable only for "the tax", and not for penalties and interest.

 

436 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Treichler v. Niagara-Wheatfield Cent. School Dist.


1321


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


184 A.D.2d 1; 590 N.Y.S.2d 954; 1992 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 14086



November 18, 1992, Decided  
November 18, 1992, Filed



DISPOSITION: Judgment unanimously granted in favor of defendants, without costs, in accordance with the opinion by Balio, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayers sought declaratory judgment relief from the court in the taxpayers' claim alleging that N.Y. Real Prop. Tax Law art. XIX, which allowed defendant Niagra-Wheatfield Central School District (school district) to establish homestead and nonhomestead tax rates, was facially unconstitutional and violated the taxpayers' rights to due process and equal protection.


OVERVIEW: The school district established dual tax rates for the portion of the town of Lewiston within its taxing authority, even though the town of Lewiston had not established dual rates. The school district established dual rates pursuant to N.Y. Real Prop. Tax Law 1901(x), which allowed the district to establish dual tax rates for all parcels within the district, so long as one-third of the parcels within the school district were located within the town of Niagra, which had adopted a dual rate structure. In a declaratory judgment action, the court rejected the taxpayers' claim that there was no rational basis for the legislature's use of the one-third fraction or number of parcels as the criteria that allowed the school district to adopt dual rates on a district wide basis. The court determined that the one-third criteria was designed as a threshold figure to encourage revaluation in cases when fewer parcels had been revaluated and that the number of parcels criteria provided an objective standard that was easy to apply. The court also held that the dual classification was reasonable because it ensured stability, and the taxes imposed across the nonhomestead property class were uniform.


OUTCOME: The court granted judgment to the school district and dismissed the taxpayers' civil rights cause of action.


HEADNOTES

Actions - Improper Form of Action - Action on Submitted Facts - Declaratory Judgment

1. Although an action on submitted facts ( CPLR 3222) was not presented to the Appellate Division in accordance with the proper procedure, the Court shall exercise its discretion to disregard the procedural irregularity and reach the merits of the issues raised in the submission, notwithstanding the facts that the parties commenced actions for declaratory judgment relief in the traditional manner and did not agree upon the "submission" or stipulate to removal of the submission to the Appellate Division until various motions were resolved by Supreme Court (see, CPLR 3222 [b] [3]). Moreover, declaratory relief can be sought in a submission on agreed facts.

Taxation - Assessment - Homestead/Nonhomestead Classification - Rational Basis

2. In an action to declare invalid RPTL 1901 (x), which permits school districts to establish district-wide homestead and nonhomestead tax rates (see, RPTL art 19) where at least one third of the parcels within the district are located within a town which has adopted the homestead/nonhomestead classification, plaintiffs' contentions that using the number of parcels as the qualifying criterion, without consideration of the size, use or value of those parcels, lacks any rational relationship either to implementing a dual tax structure or to raising revenues for school purposes on an equitable tax basis, and that use of the one-third threshold amounts to invidious discrimination because it enables those towns constituting a minority of the parcels within the district to control school district economics, especially considering that nonhomestead taxpayers have no vote in school district elections and receive little or none of school district services, are unpersuasive since the purpose of the challenged legislation is to encourage revaluation of properties and updating of assessment rolls; the legislation, by enabling school districts to impose the same classification and equalized rates throughout the district, "ameliorated" constitutional implications, and, by using the one-third qualifying threshold, encouraged other municipalities to undertake revaluation.

Taxation - Assessment - Homestead/Nonhomestead Classification - Rational Basis

3. Utilizing the number of parcels as a threshold criterion for permitting a school district to establish district-wide homestead and nonhomestead tax rates (see, RPTL 1901 [x]) does not amount to invidious discrimination, but rather facilitates a district's decision to adopt the dual classification since it is an objective standard that is easy to apply. Inasmuch as the underlying purpose of the legislation is to encourage the revaluation of a greater number of parcels, there is a reasonable basis for using that criterion as the threshold for implementing the dual tax rate structure.

Taxation - Assessment - Homestead/Nonhomestead Classification - Invidious Discrimination

4. RPTL article 19, which permits school districts to establish district- wide homestead and nonhomestead tax rates (see, RPTL 1901 [x]) where at least one third of the parcels within the district are located within a town which has adopted the homestead/nonhomestead classification, does not result in invidious discrimination against nonhomestead taxpayers in those towns which have not adopted the dual tax rate structure. The classification in question merely continues the de facto tax structure that developed prior to the decision in Matter of Hellerstein v Assessor of Town of Islip (37 NY2d 1), thereby assuring stability in the relative tax burdens of taxpayers. Moreover, plaintiffs' counsel acknowledged that the tax rates for nonhomestead taxpayers throughout the district were "presumably equalized". The challenged legislation is not unconstitutional on the ground that it allegedly enables the school district to impose a greater tax burden upon nonhomestead taxpayers within the district than upon nonhomestead taxpayers in the same towns but whose property is situated in another school district, since plaintiffs and other nonhomestead taxpayers within the subject school district are not "similarly situated" with nonhomestead taxpayers in other school districts.

 

437 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Medical Business Assoc. v. Steiner


90-00252


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


183 A.D.2d 86; 588 N.Y.S.2d 890; 1992 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12120


October 21, 1991, Argued  
October 19, 1992, Decided



DISPOSITION: Ordered that the order is affirmed insofar as appealed from, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff corporation and defendants, a husband and a wife, both filed motions for summary judgment. The Supreme Court in the County of Westchester (New York) denied both the corporation's motion and the husband's and the wife's cross application for summary judgment, but held that the necessaries doctrine should have been expanded to impose liability upon wives for necessaries furnished to their husbands. The husband and the wife appealed.


OVERVIEW: The husband and the wife contended that the common-law doctrine of necessaries should have been abolished because it was unconstitutional, and that neither the husband nor the wife should have been held liable for medical expenses incurred by the other spouse. The corporation agreed that the necessaries doctrine in its historical form was unconstitutional, but contended that the unconstitutionality of the common-law rule should have been remedied by expanding it to impose liability upon wives for necessaries provided to their husbands. The court found that as traditionally formulated, the necessaries doctrine violated the Equal Protection Clause, and that the unconstitutionality of the necessaries doctrine should have been remedied by extending the common-law rule to both spouses. The court held that it was the spouse who received the necessary goods or services who should have been primarily liable for payment. The court concluded that a creditor seeking to recover a debt against the nondebtor spouse retained the burden of demonstrating that necessaries were furnished on the nondebtor spouse's credit, and that the nondebtor spouse had the ability to satisfy the debt.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court.


HEADNOTES

Husband and Wife - Necessaries - Equal Protection of Laws - Reciprocal Nature of Doctrine of Necessaries

The common-law doctrine of necessaries, pursuant to which a husband is liable to third parties who furnish his wife with such "necessaries" as food, clothing, shelter, and medical care, violates the Equal Protection Clause because, while it makes a husband responsible for certain goods and services provided to his wife, it does not impose a similar obligation upon her. The common-law rule should be extended to both spouses, and it is the spouse who received the necessary goods or services who should be primarily liable for payment; a creditor seeking to recover a debt against the nondebtor spouse retains the burden of demonstrating that necessaries were furnished on the nondebtor spouse's credit, and that the nondebtor spouse has the ability to satisfy the debt. Retaining the common-law rule and applying it in a gender-neutral fashion would encourage the extension of credit to dependent spouses who, in an individual capacity, might lack the ability to purchase necessities or to obtain adequate medical care; the necessaries doctrine has historically facilitated support of the family, and expansion of the doctrine to require spouses to mutually share the obligation of furnishing necessaries is fully consistent with 1980 statutory amendments which make the duty of a wife to support her husband equal to that of a husband to support his wife, and the duty of a mother to support her children equal to the duty of the father to support his children.

 

438 of 992 DOCUMENTS


ILC Data Device Corp. v. County of Suffolk


90-01134


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


182 A.D.2d 293; 588 N.Y.S.2d 845; 1992 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10563; 1992 OSHD (CCH) P29,838


March 25, 1992, Argued  
September 14, 1992, Decided



DISPOSITION: Ordered that the order is affirmed, without costs or disbursements, and the matter is remitted to the Supreme Court, Suffolk County, for the entry of an appropriate judgment.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant county appealed a judgment of the Supreme Court of Suffolk County (New York), which struck down a county law regulating video display terminal equipment usage among plaintiffs, employers doing business within the county, on the ground that the legislature had no authority to enact it.


OVERVIEW: The employers brought their action against the county for a declaratory judgment that a local law was unconstitutional and void and to permanently enjoin its implementation and enforcement. The lower court denied the county's motion to dismiss in its entirety and granted the plaintiffs' cross motion to the extent of striking down the law on the ground that the legislature had no authority to enact it. The county appealed. The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court, finding that the state had preempted the field of regulation of safety in the workplace by its adoption of a comprehensive labor law scheme. Even though the county regulation did not directly conflict with state law, it was inconsistent with the state's overriding interests because it imposed restrictions on rights granted by state law.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that the county legislation was invalid because it was preempted by the state labor law.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations - Home Rule Powers - Regulation of Video Display Terminals in Workplace - Preemption of Local Law by State Law

1. A county law governing certain aspects of video display terminal (VDT) equipment usage among employers doing business within the county is preempted by the New York State Labor Law and, therefore, invalid, since the enacting of a comprehensive and detailed regulatory scheme embodied in the Labor Law and the vesting of broad rule-making and enforcement authority in the State Commissioner of Labor lead to the conclusion that the State Legislature intended to preclude local regulation in the field of employee safety in the workplace (see, Labor Law § 21, 27, 30), to the extent that that field is not already regulated by the Federal Occupational Safety and Health Act of 1970. The intent to occupy an entire field of regulation need not be express, and it is well settled that inconsistency may be found, even in the absence of an express conflict, where the State Legislature has evinced a desire to preempt a given field and thereby to prohibit local regulation of the entire area. In effect, the regulatory scheme set up by the Labor Law with regard to employee health and safety is so comprehensive and detailed as to render said local law "inconsistent" with State law under New York Constitution, article IX, § 2 (c) (ii) and Municipal Home Rule Law § 10 (1) (ii).

Municipal Corporations - Home Rule Powers - Regulation of Video Display Terminals in Workplace - Supersession of State Law

2. A county law governing certain aspects of video display terminal (VDT) equipment usage among employers doing business within the county is not invalid on the ground that it impermissibly "supersedes" either the State Labor Law or Workers' Compensation Law, since the county law contains no statement indicating an intent to supersede any other law, and it does not substantially comply with the procedural requirements for superseding a State statute as set forth in Municipal Home Rule Law § 22. Accordingly, in the absence of such substantial compliance, supersession will not be found and, consequently, the question of whether the county law applies to or affects any provision of the Labor Law or Workers' Compensation Law is irrelevant and need not be reached (see, Municipal Home Rule Law § 11 [1] [f]).

Municipal Corporations - Home Rule Powers - Regulation of Video Display Terminals in Workplace - Local Regulation of Workplace Safety

3. A county law governing certain aspects of video display terminal (VDT) equipment usage among employers doing business within the county is preempted by the State Labor Law and, therefore, invalid, and the fact that Labor Law § 21 (9) provides that the Commissioner of Labor may enforce any lawful municipal ordinance or regulation relating to any place affected by the Labor Law and not in conflict therewith, does not preclude a finding of State preemption of the field of employee safety in the workplace, since, in view of the reference to "any place", section 21 (9) should be construed as meaning only that the Commissioner may enforce local building, sanitation, and fire codes, etc., affecting premises (see also, Labor Law § 200 [4]; § 471).

Municipal Corporations - Home Rule Powers - Regulation of Video Display Terminals in Workplace - Silence of State Labor Law

4. A county law governing certain aspects of video display terminal (VDT) equipment usage among employers doing business within the county is preempted by the State Labor Law and, therefore, invalid, notwithstanding the fact that the Labor Law is silent on this specific subject. Inasmuch as the Labor Law preempts the field of workplace safety, it permits, by reason of its silence, the operation of VDTs without any special regulations or safeguards. Hence, the County law in question impermissibly prohibits what the State law permits by providing that the use of VDTs must be accompanied by a panoply of safeguards which the State Legislature and the Commissioner of Labor have not seen fit to enact. Moreover, the County law does contain several provisions which touch upon matters already governed by the Labor Law, viz., vision examinations, noise, and lighting. Thus, the County law improperly attempts to add another layer of regulation in an area where comprehensive and detailed regulations already exist at the State level.

 

439 of 992 DOCUMENTS


People v. Gerber


91-05412


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


182 A.D.2d 252; 589 N.Y.S.2d 171; 1992 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10593


February 20, 1992, Argued  
September 14, 1992, Decided



DISPOSITION: Ordered that the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was indicted for second degree manslaughter. Defendant filed motions to dismiss the indictment. The Dutchess County Court (New York) denied the motions and convicted defendant of second degree manslaughter upon his guilty plea. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was indicted for second degree murder. After his motions to dismiss the indictment were denied, he pled guilty and was convicted of the charge against him. Defendant appealed and asserted that instances of police and prosecutorial misconduct survived his plea and mandated the dismissal of the indictment. The court found that the guilty plea was valid because the evidence showed that it was made voluntarily, knowingly, and intelligently. The court held that the trial court properly refused to dismiss the indictment because it did not have jurisdiction over defendant's postplea challenges. The court held that defendant's challenges to the alleged police and prosecutorial misconduct were forfeited by his guilty plea.


OUTCOME: The conviction for second degree manslaughter was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Plea of Guilty - Forfeiture of Right to Raise Issues on Appeal - Alleged Police and Prosecutorial Misconduct

1. In a homicide prosecution in which defendant negotiated a guilty plea to manslaughter in the second degree, defendant's contentions on appeal regarding alleged police and prosecutorial misconduct do not involve fundamental defects which deprived the court of jurisdiction or constituted such an egregious violation of basic rights as to effectively render the proceedings a nullity; rather, defendant's contentions come under that broad grouping of claims which are forfeited by a plea of guilty. The claims which have been forfeited by defendant's guilty plea are: eavesdropping by police with respect to a brief portion of a taped interview between defendant and his attorney; the test-firing and consequent destruction of a "live" bullet recovered from the rifle which defendant used to shoot the victim; the purported "staging" of a photograph of the rifle and its submission to the Grand Jury; and the elicitation of opinion testimony from a nonexpert witness during the Grand Jury proceedings.

Crimes - Plea of Guilty - Forfeiture of Right to Raise Issues on Appeal - Police Eavesdropping with Regard to Tape of Conversation between Defendant and Counsel

2. In a homicide prosecution in which defendant negotiated a guilty plea to manslaughter in the second degree, defendant waived his right to contend that the indictment must be dismissed on the ground that eavesdropping by the police with respect to a brief portion of a taped interview between defendant and counsel constituted misconduct so egregious as to constitute a perversion of defendant's fundamental rights, where the record does not support that contention. The police listened to only a two or three minute excerpt of the tape, allegedly because the officer suspected that the tape recorder was voice activated and might have recorded police conversations, and defendant could not establish that he suffered any prejudice as a result of the eavesdropping. Such a showing of prejudice is a prerequisite for dismissal of the indictment. Thus, by pleading guilty, defendant himself foreclosed any possibility of demonstrating that the police misconduct would impair his trial rights or defense strategy. Therefore, defendant's contention cannot be said to constitute such a deprivation of fundamental rights as to survive his guilty plea.

Crimes - Plea of Guilty - Forfeiture of Right to Raise Issues on Appeal - Exhibition of "Staged" Photograph of Rifle to Grand Jury

3. In a homicide prosecution in which defendant negotiated a guilty plea to manslaughter in the second degree, defendant waived his right to contend that the indictment must be dismissed on the ground that the police committed egregious misconduct by "staging" a photograph of the rifle defendant used to shoot the victim and then exhibiting that photograph to the Grand Jury, since defendant's contention is disingenuous in light of the fact that a police investigator merely prevented the ejection of a spent cartridge from the rifle so that a photograph which depicted the actual contents of the weapon could be taken. Moreover, the investigator fully explained the operation of the rifle, including its automatic ejection feature, to the Grand Jury. Hence, the issue was fully developed in the Grand Jury proceedings and there is no basis to conclude that the police or the prosecutor were attempting to mislead the Grand Jury by the introduction of the photograph.

Crimes - Plea of Guilty - Forfeiture of Right to Raise Issues on Appeal - Elicitation of Opinion Testimony from Nonexpert Witness

4. In a homicide prosecution in which defendant negotiated a guilty plea to second degree manslaughter, defendant's contention that a police investigator improperly gave opinion testimony before the Grand Jury is based upon a distorted perception of the facts: while the investigator was not qualified as an expert on firearms, he did state that as a nine-year veteran of the police force, he had received extensive training in firearms and handled them on a routine basis in investigating crime scenes. Additionally, he thoroughly examined the rifle defendant used to shoot the victim and demonstrated a clear familiarity with the manner in which it operated. Accordingly, the investigator's competency to testify regarding the operation of the rifle and to respond to hypothetical questions concerning the use of the weapon are not open to serious challenge. Therefore, it cannot be said that the purported errors so tainted the proceedings as to warrant appellate review notwithstanding defendant's guilty plea.

Grand Jury - Defective Proceeding - Impairment of Integrity

5. In a homicide prosecution involving the allegedly accidental shooting of the victim by defendant with a rifle, defendant's contention that the prosecutor engaged in misconduct during the Grand Jury proceedings by placing a "staged" photograph of the rifle in evidence and by eliciting opinion testimony from a nonexpert witness has been forfeited by his plea of guilty to second degree manslaughter, inasmuch as his allegations clearly do not support the conclusion that the integrity of the Grand Jury proceedings was impaired. No egregious misconduct was involved and any limited police impropriety did not rise to the level of pervasive and fundamental unfairness which would warrant dismissal of the indictment.

Crimes - Plea of Guilty - Forfeiture of Right to Raise Issues on Appeal - Police Tampering with Weapon Used to Shoot Victim

6. In a homicide prosecution in which defendant negotiated a guilty plea to second degree manslaughter, defendant's contention that the indictment should be dismissed owing to police misconduct in test-firing a "live" cartridge from the rifle defendant used to shoot the victim has been forfeited by his guilty plea. Although defendant phrases his argument in terms of a suppression issue, defendant was actually seeking the imposition of a sanction (i.e., dismissal of the indictment or preclusion of evidence regarding the "live" bullet) based upon the alleged improper destruction of the bullet evidence by the police; accordingly, the hearing court's denial of defendant's application is not reviewable pursuant to CPL 710.70 (2), inasmuch as that provision applies only to CPL article 710 motions to suppress. Rather, the hearing court's rejection of defendant's claim amounted to an evidentiary ruling, review of which was forfeited by reason of the guilty plea. Moreover, the People's failure to argue on appeal that the issue was forfeited by defendant's guilty plea does not preclude the Appellate Division from making that determination, since a forfeiture may be enforced by a reviewing court regardless of the prosecution's position on the issue.

Crimes - Disclosure - Destruction of Evidence by Police

7. In a homicide prosecution arising out of the purportedly accidental shooting of the victim by defendant, there was no improvident exercise of discretion in the hearing court's refusal to grant defendant's request to either dismiss the indictment or preclude the prosecution from introducing evidence regarding a "live" bullet, where the police test-fired the "live" cartridge from the rifle defendant used to shoot the victim, since the drastic sanctions sought by defendant were inappropriate. Defense counsel was placed on notice of the existence of the bullet prior to its test firing, and firearms and ammunition used in the commission of crimes are routinely test-fired as a matter of standard police procedure, thus negating any possible suggestion that the bullet was destroyed in an effort to deprive the defense of evidence. Moreover, defendant's failure to show that he suffered any prejudice as a result of the test-firing supports the denial of sanctions. Defendant's elaborate and tenuous claims of prejudice advanced on appeal are irrelevant since they were not presented to the hearing court. Accordingly, the hearing court properly rejected defendant's request for sanctions and ruled that defendant would be free to fully explore the issue at trial.

Crimes - Plea of Guilty - Forfeiture of Right to Raise Issues on Appeal - Defects in Indictment

8. In a homicide prosecution in which defendant negotiated a guilty plea to second degree manslaughter, defendant's claims attacking the adequacy of the indictment are nonjurisdictional and, thus, were forfeited by his plea. Moreover, defendant's contention that the indictment was jurisdictionally defective because it failed to state each and every element of the crime charged lacks merit.

 

440 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Clarkstown v. C&A Carbone


91-07786, 91-08409, 91-09014


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


182 A.D.2d 213; 587 N.Y.S.2d 681; 1992 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10378; 23 ELR 20926


November 21, 1991, Argued  
August 31, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Revised November 10, 1992.



DISPOSITION: Ordered that the appeal from the order dated July 15, 1991 is dismissed; and it is further,


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, operators of a transfer station, sought review of a judgment from the Rockland County Supreme Court (New York), which granted summary judgment in favor of appellee town in an action brought by the town to enjoin the operators from unlawfully disposing solid waste in violation of a local law and denied the operator's claim that the local law was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The operators maintained an interstate transfer station in the town that was permitted as a recycling center. The town began operating its own transfer station and adopted a local law that made it unlawful to dispose of solid waste at a site other than the town operated facility. The operators were observed shipping solid waste from the recycling center and the town filed an action to enjoin the shipment of solid waste from the operator's facility. The supreme court found that the operators were in violation of the town's law and granted summary judgment in favor of the town. The operators claimed on appeal that the town's law violated the Commerce Clause, U.S. Const. art. I, § 8, and that the town's law constituted a taking of property in violation of the Due Process Clauses of the United States and New York Constitutions, U.S. Const. amends. V, XIV, N.Y. Const. art. I, §§ 6-7. The court rejected the operator's claims. The court found that the town's law applied evenhandedly to all solid waste processed within the town, regardless or the point of origin and that the operator's had not overcome the presumption that the town's laws were unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the supreme court's injunction against the operator from violating the town's prohibition against operation of a transfer station at a site other than the town operated facility. The court ruled that the town's law applied evenhandedly to all solid waste and that it did not violate the Interstate Commerce Clause and the Due Process Clauses.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations - Regulation of Solid Waste Disposal - Financial Motivation in Enacting Local Law

1. A local law which mandates that all solid waste processed or otherwise handled within a locality be processed at a designated facility constitutes a valid exercise of the locality's police power, and appellants' claim that the enactment of the local law was financially motivated and thus beyond the scope of its police power, lacks merit. The regulation of solid waste collection and disposal, a function traditionally entrusted to State and local governments, is fundamentally related to the public health and welfare (see, e.g., Town Law § 130 [6]; § 136 [8]; § 198 [9]; § 221; see also, NY Const, art IX, § 2 [c] [ii] [10]), and a concern for the continued economic viability of a solid waste management facility established pursuant to a plan to deal with the problem of solid waste disposal does not negate or detract from, but is a part of, the health, safety and environmental concerns such plan is designed to address.

Constitutional Law - Commerce Clause - Municipal Regulation of Solid Waste Disposal

2. A local law which mandates that all solid waste processed or otherwise handled within a locality be processed at a designated facility constitutes a valid exercise of the locality's police power and does not constitute an unreasonable, discriminatory burden on interstate commerce. Although garbage is an article of commerce within the meaning of the Commerce Clause of the United States Constitution, the Commerce Clause protects the interstate market, not particular interstate firms, such as those run by appellants, who are challenging the local law, which imposes no special fees, taxes, prohibitions or duties on those transporting out-of- State articles of commerce. Rather, the local law applies evenhandedly to all solid waste processed within the locality, regardless of point of origin. Moreover, if the current $ 11 difference between the locality's tipping fee and the fee charged by appellants has any effect on the interstate flow of solid waste, there is no evidence to suggest that what can have nothing more than an incidental effect on interstate commerce is impermissibly burdensome, particularly when the "burden" is weighed against the legitimate and significant public concerns underlying the local law.

Constitutional Law - Due Process of Law - Taking of Property - Municipal Regulation of Solid Waste Disposal

3. A local law which mandates that all solid waste processed or otherwise handled within a locality be processed at a designated facility constitutes a valid exercise of the locality's police power and does not constitute an unlawful taking of property in violation of appellants' due process rights. Although appellants, who are challenging the law and who are in the waste disposal business, have alleged that the law will impair their business, they may still operate a recycling facility at their site within the locality, and there is no claim that the law in question deprives them of all economically viable uses of their site. While appellants may have had some vested interest in their permit to operate a solid waste transfer station, the expiration of that permit renders moot any claim that the local law retrospectively diminished or retroactively invalidated vested rights. In light of the close relation of the law to the promotion of the public health, safety and welfare, the acute public interest in the proper management of solid waste, appellants' obvious knowledge that their business was and would be increasingly heavily regulated, and appellants' heavy burden of overcoming the presumption of constitutionality attaching to the law, there are no issues of fact warranting trial of appellants' due process claims.

Parties - Standing - Challenge to Local Law Regulating Disposal of Solid Waste

4. Appellants, the owners and operators of a solid waste disposal business have no standing to challenge the adequacy of the environmental review pertaining to a locality's handling of the solid waste disposal problem within its borders. Although appellants' economic concerns do not deprive them of standing to also complain of environmental injury, they are aggrieved only economically by the enactment of a local law which mandates that all solid waste processed or otherwise handled within the locality be processed at a designated facility. Neither that economic injury nor appellants' status as property owners within the locality is sufficient to confer standing to challenge the locality's failure to separately issue environmental declarations with respect to that part of the locality's remedial plan requiring all solid waste processed within the locality to be processed at the locality's transfer station.

 

441 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Town of Hoosick v. Eastern Rensselaer County Solid Waste Mgmt. Auth.


65726


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


182 A.D.2d 37; 592 N.Y.S.2d 472; 1992 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10153



August 27, 1992, Decided  
August 27, 1992, Entered



DISPOSITION: Ordered that the judgment is modified, on the law, without costs, by reversing so much thereof as granted plaintiff's cross motion for summary judgment, denied defendant's motion for summary judgment and made a declaration in plaintiff's favor; defendant's motion for summary judgment is granted, plaintiff's cross motion for summary judgment is denied, and it is declared that plaintiff's resolution rescinding and revoking its membership [**2] in defendant is not valid without first obtaining authorization from the Legislature; and, as so modified, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant public benefit corporation, created pursuant to N.Y. Pub. Auth. Law § 2050-cc(4), challenged the judgment of the Supreme Court (New York), which denied the public benefit corporation's motion for summary judgment and granted a cross motion for summary judgment by plaintiff city, holding that the city's resolution to withdraw membership in the public benefit corporation was valid in the city's declaratory judgment action.


OVERVIEW: The city filed a declaratory judgment action seeking a declaration that it had properly withdrawn from the public benefit corporation via a resolution that its town board passed and a certificate that it filed pursuant to N.Y. Pub. Auth. Law § 2050-cc(4). The trial court denied the public benefit corporation's motion for summary judgment and granted the city's cross motion for summary judgment. On appeal, the court reversed, finding that the city's resolution revoking its membership in the public benefit corporation was not valid without first obtaining authorization from the legislature. The court held that the absence of withdrawal language in § 2050-cc(4) was intentional and the legislature alone had the power to modify public corporations. The court noted that the statute demonstrated that the public benefit corporation required at least six members to justify its creation and the legislature would reexamine the situation if members wished to withdraw. The court rejected the claim that N.Y. Pub. Auth. Law art. 8, tit. 13-H was unconstitutional because the creation of public authorities and their powers was within the legislature's purview and not within the city's power.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the grant of summary judgment in favor of the city and ruled in favor of the public benefit corporation in the city's declaratory judgment action seeking to find that it had properly withdrawn from the public benefit corporation via a resolution.


HEADNOTES

Public Authorities - Solid Waste Management Authority - Withdrawal of Member Municipality

1. A town may not unilaterally withdraw its membership in a public authority created by the Legislature for the purpose of dealing with the management of solid waste where the town voluntarily joined the authority, where the enabling legislation provided that unless six municipalities joined the authority by a certain date, the authority's corporate existence would terminate, and where said legislation provided that once defendant included six members it would be perpetual in duration (see, Public Authorities Law § 2050-bb [18]; § 2050-cc [4]), but provided no means whereby a municipality could withdraw from the authority, since the absence of withdrawal language was an intended omission based upon prior precedent and the fact that the Legislature alone has the power to create, modify and dissolve public corporations (see, NY Const, art X, § 5). The absence of withdrawal language was not an oversight, and a municipality may withdraw from the authority only through an act of the Legislature. If members were permitted to unilaterally withdraw, defendant could, contrary to legislative intent, be left as nothing more than a perpetual empty shell, thereby usurping the Legislature's plenary power to create public corporations on its own terms.

Municipal Corporations - Home Rule Powers - Withdrawal of Municipality from Regional Solid Waste Management Authority

2. A town may not unilaterally withdraw its membership in a public authority created by the Legislature for the purpose of dealing with the management of solid waste where the town voluntarily joined the authority and where the enabling legislation provided no means for effecting a withdrawal. The enabling legislation (Public Authorities Law, art 8, tit 13-H) is constitutional and does not interfere with plaintiff town's rights under Municipal Home Rule Law § 10 and NY Constitution, article IX, § 2 (c) (ii) (10). There is no unconstitutional limitation of the authority's powers because the creation of public authorities and their powers is wholly within the Legislature's purview, and plaintiff's powers to adopt laws benefitting its citizens do not extend that far. The State has clearly preempted the field as it relates to public authorities, and, therefore, plaintiff town's resolution attempting to unilaterally change its membership status in the authority is inconsistent with the State's transcendent interest, regardless of whether the terms of the resolution actually conflict with Public Authorities Law § 2050-cc (4).

 

442 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Town of N. Hempstead v. Westbury


92-00944


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


182 A.D.2d 272; 588 N.Y.S.2d 293; 1992 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10017


March 19, 1992, Argued  
August 17, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Revised November 13, 1992.



DISPOSITION: Ordered that the order is reversed insofar as appealed from, on the law, without costs or disbursements, the appellants' motions for summary judgment are granted, the cross motion of the Incorporated Village of Westbury is denied, it is declared that Laws of 1983 (ch 544) and Town of North Hempstead Code chapter [***2] 46 are constitutional and valid, the respondents are permanently enjoined from violating the provisions of Town of North Hempstead Code chapter 46, and the counterclaims of the Incorporated Village of Westbury and the Incorporated Village of Mineola are dismissed; and it is further,


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In their actions to restrain respondents, villages located within appellant town, from delivering or disposing of solid waste in violation of the town's law and the town-village contracts, appellants, town and solid waste management authority (authority), challenged the decisions of the Supreme Court, Nassau County, New York, which, inter alia, denied their motions for summary judgment and granted the villages partial summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: The court reversed in part and affirmed in part. It held that the lower court erred in denying appellants' summary judgment motions. The court declared that the villages failed to carry their burden of showing that 1983 N.Y. Laws ch. 544 (effective July 20, 1983), regulating the disposal of solid waste, violated the Home Rule provisions N.Y. Const. art. IX. Nor was the statute unconstitutional because it provided a funding mechanism for financing the construction of resource recovery facilities. The court held that the town's flow control law, North Hempstead, N.Y., Code ch. 46, was valid because 1983 N.Y. Laws ch. 544 and N.Y. Pub. Auth. Law tit. 13-F, N.Y. Pub. Auth. Law § 2049-a et seq., enabled the town to make and adopt a local law which would be applicable in the incorporated villages with respect to solid waste disposal. In addition, the court found that under the agreements between the villages and the town, the villages expressly agreed to comply with the town's flow control law.


OUTCOME: The court reversed insofar as appealed from, granted appellants' summary judgment motions, and denied the villages' cross motion. The court affirmed insofar as cross-appealed from, and upon searching the record, dismissed the causes of action of the villages which were to recover damages.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations - Home Rule Powers - Town Solid Waste Management Authority - State Interest in Waste Disposal - Alleged Mismanagement of Public Trust

1. Laws of 1983 (ch 544), which authorized the Town of North Hempstead to adopt a local law providing for the management on a town-wide basis of all solid waste generated within the town, is constitutional and valid. The several incorporated villages which have challenged the statute have failed to carry their burden of demonstrating that the State had no interest in the subject matter of the law; rather, the villages alleged that the town has mismanaged the trust imposed upon it by the statute, but the subject of mismanagement is nonjusticiable and does not provide a basis for declaring a duly enacted statute unconstitutional. Moreover, it is clear that the State has an interest in solid waste management, and although the villages were traditionally responsible for their own solid waste disposal, the stated purpose of the statute shows that it is also a matter of State concern. If the subject of legislation is in substantial degree a matter of State concern, the Legislature may act, though intermingled with it are concerns of the locality. Accordingly, the subject law does not violate the Home Rule provisions of article IX of the State Constitution. Nor is the statute unconstitutional because it provides a funding mechanism for financing the construction of resource recovery facilities.

Municipal Corporations - Home Rule Powers - Town Solid Waste Management Authority - Local Law - Objections of Villages

2. A town law, which established a town solid waste management authority and required all solid waste generated within the town to be delivered to the authority, was validly enacted pursuant to Laws of 1983 (ch 544), which authorized the town to provide for the management of solid waste and explicitly permitted the town to "displace competition with regulation or monopoly public service". Said town law is valid and not in conflict with Town Law § 132, which renders town laws inoperative within villages unless "specifically provided by statute", since Laws of 1983 (ch 544) specifically provides that solid waste shall be handled on a "Town-wide basis", and the phrase "Town-wide basis" must be intended to encompass the incorporated villages within the town. To achieve the statutory goal of "maximum resource recovery", a solid waste management facility must be assured a steady stream of waste, and Public Authorities Law § 2049-u empowers the town to adopt any local law requiring the delivery of such waste to its facility. Moreover, assuming the relevant statutes are ambiguous, their purpose, i.e., to eliminate competition and provide a comprehensive framework for solid waste management, requires a construction which would further that end. Furthermore, the villages challenging the validity of the town law expressly agreed to comply with it when they executed contracts clearly calling for the delivery of waste to the town's facility.

 

443 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Empire State Bldg. Co. v. New York State Dep't of Taxation & Fin.


45729-45730


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


185 A.D.2d 201; 586 N.Y.S.2d 597; 1992 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9077



July 23, 1992, Decided  
July 23, 1992, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant New York State Department of Taxation and Finance (state) sought review of a decision of the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), which denied its motion to dismiss the action for plaintiff building company's failure to exhaust administrative remedies, granted the company's motion for a preliminary injunction, granted summary judgment in favor of the company, and denied the state's motion for leave to renew an order.


OVERVIEW: The building company challenged the constitutionality and applicability of certain sales tax assessments. The supreme court denied the state's motion to dismiss for the building company's failure to exhaust administrative remedies, granted the company's motion for a preliminary injunction tolling the 90-day Statute of Limitations for it to administratively challenge the assessments, granted the building company's cross motion for summary judgment, declared that N.Y. Tax Law § 1105(b) was inapplicable to rent inclusion charges for non-metered electricity charges supplied by the building company to its commercial tenants and unconstitutional as interpreted and applied by the state, and denied the state's motion for leave to renew an order. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that the building company was not obligated to exhaust its administrative remedies since it was challenging the tax statute's constitutionality. Moreover, the court held that it was for that reason that the building company was entitled to the toll of the Statute of Limitations. Finally, the court held that the imposition of the sales tax on non-metered electricity charges amounted to double taxation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the supreme court, which denied the state's motion to dismiss, granted the building company's motion for a preliminary injunction, granted the building company's cross motion for summary judgment, declared the tax law inapplicable and unconstitutional as interpreted and applied by the state, and denied the state's motion for leave to renew an order.

 

444 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Intrastate Trucking Corp. v. White


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


185 A.D.2d 697; 586 N.Y.S.2d 65; 1992 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9255



July 14, 1992, Decided  
July 14, 1992, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant truck haulers sought review of an order of the Supreme Court, Monroe County (New York), which granted judgment to respondent, Commissioner of the New York State Department of Transportation, declaring that N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 385(15) and related regulations governing the issuance of divisible load overweight permits under N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 17, § 154 were constitutional.


OVERVIEW: On the truck haulers' appeal of the declaratory judgment that N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 385(15) and related regulations were constitutional, the court affirmed, holding that the regulation of vehicle weight for the purposes of highway and bridge safety and protection was a valid exercise of state power. The court found that the truck haulers failed to meet their heavy burden of demonstrating that the subject regulations were not reasonably related to those purposes or that the regulations discriminated against interstate commerce. Thus, under the circumstances, the regulations did not violate the truck haulers' constitutional due process rights, nor did they impose an undue burden on interstate commerce. The court further found that the establishment of a cut-off date for the issuance of permits or the "grandfathering" of certain vehicles registered prior to that cut-off date did not constitute an invalid classification where, as here, the legislation and regulatory scheme were rationally related to the achievement of the governmental purpose of reducing the overall weight of vehicles traveling on state highways.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the supreme court's declaratory judgment that the commissioner's regulation of vehicle weight was a valid exercise of state power and that related statutes and regulations were not unconstitutional.

 

445 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Modern Landfill, Inc. v. Lewiston


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


181 A.D.2d 159; 585 N.Y.S.2d 921; 1992 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9258



July 14, 1992, Decided  
July 14, 1992, Filed



DISPOSITION: Order unanimously affirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landfill company sought a declaration against defendant town that a local law regulating solid waste landfills was unconstitutional and to enjoin enforcement of the law. A settlement was reached and approved by the town board. The parties filed a joint motion for approval of the settlement. Defendant citizens group intervened and opposed the settlement. The Supreme Court (New York) denied approval and the landfill company appealed.


OVERVIEW: The trial court found it could not arrive at a determination that the settlement was just, reasonable and in the interest of the town. The landfill company argued that although it and the town made a joint application for court approval of the proposed settlement pursuant to N.Y. Town Law § 68, such approval was not required and the trial court did not have subject matter jurisdiction to either approve or disapprove the proposed settlement. The landfill company argued that because its action sought a declaratory judgment, and not money damages, the proposed settlement did not require court approval under § 68. The citizens group argued that the clear and unequivocal language of N.Y. Town Law § 68(1)(a) required a town board to obtain judicial approval of a compromise or settlement of any action or proceeding against a town. The court agreed with the citizens group and affirmed the trial court's denial of the joint application for approval. Nothing in the plain language of § 68, its legislative history, or case law limited the judicial approval requirement to actions seeking only money damages. Section § 68(1)(a) clearly provided for court approval of the settlement.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's denial of the joint application for approval of the proposed settlement.


HEADNOTES

Courts - Jurisdiction - Approval of Settlement of Lawsuit between Town and Corporation

1. Supreme Court has subject matter jurisdiction to either approve or disapprove a proposed settlement of a declaratory judgment action between plaintiff corporation, which operated a landfill, and defendant Town, where plaintiff sought relief from various restrictive provisions of a local law adopted by the Town which regulated plaintiff's landfill, since the clear and unequivocal language of Town Law § 68 (1) (a) requires a Town Board to obtain judicial approval of a compromise or settlement of any "action or proceeding" against a Town, and the "small claims exception" of Town Law § 68 (4) is not applicable to oust the court of jurisdiction to approve a proposed settlement of a declaratory judgment action. While a municipality possesses the inherent right to compromise a claim against it, it may not, under the guise of a compromise, impair a public duty owed by it or give validity to a void claim. Accordingly, the courts act as checks against possible fraud, and nothing in the plain language of the statute, its legislative history or case law limits the judicial approval requirement to actions seeking money damages only.

Municipal Corporations - Home Rule Powers - Judicial Approval of Settlement of Lawsuit between Town and Corporation

2. Supreme Court properly refused to approve a settlement of a declaratory judgment action between plaintiff corporation, which operates a landfill, and defendant Town, where plaintiff sought relief from various restrictive provisions of a Town law which regulated plaintiff's landfill, where the court expressly recognized that it was not to sit in review of the legislative actions of the Town Board, yet refused to "rubber-stamp" the proposed settlement. Rather, the court properly viewed its role as one of parens patriae, similar to its obligation in considering the proposed settlement of an action involving an infant, etc. The court did not consider the proposed settlement in terms of its financial impact on the Town, but rather considered whether the proposed settlement properly protected nonfinancial interests such as the legitimate environmental concerns of the Town's residents. Moreover, in noting that the compromise was approved by a simple majority of the Town Board, the court expressed its concern that the Board was using its authority to settle actions as a vehicle to circumvent the supermajority requirement of the local law in question.

 

446 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Leirer v. Caputo


90-00464


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


181 A.D.2d 119; 586 N.Y.S.2d 976; 1992 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8564


September 13, 1991, Argued  
June 22, 1992, Decided



DISPOSITION: Ordered that the appeal from the judgment is dismissed, as the judgment was superseded by the order made upon reargument and renewal; and it is further,


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner employee filed an action under N.Y. C.P.L.R. § 7803(2), seeking a judgment prohibiting respondent county comptroller from recouping moneys from her paychecks. The employee also sought a declaration that such action was unconstitutional. The Supreme Court of Suffolk County (New York) granted the employee relief in the nature of a prohibition and converted the declaratory request to a plenary action. The county comptroller appealed.


OVERVIEW: The employee was a clerk for the county. Based on reports that the employee was frequently absent from work, the comptroller conducted an audit of the employee's work attendance and sent copies of the audit to the employee and to the county legislators as required by the Suffolk Co., N.Y., Charter art. V. The audit report found that the employee had been absent without authorization and that she had received an overpayment in unearned wages. When the employee refused to sign a confession of judgment as to the overpayment, the comptroller began withholding 10 percent of her salary in every paycheck. The court held that the comptroller possessed the inherent common-law authority to formulate and implement a plan of recoupment as a function of his constitutional and statutory duties. As such, the court held that there was no basis for concluding that wages or salaries were immune from a plan of recoupment which otherwise complied with statutory mandates. The court also held that due process did not require the comptroller to commence a plenary action or to await a judicial determination before exercising his authority.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the appeal from the judgment as superseded by the trial court's order made upon reargument. The court also reversed and vacated the judgment in part and modified it in part.


HEADNOTES

Counties - Comptroller - Power to Recoup Salary Overpayment from Wages of County Employee

Respondent County Comptroller possesses the inherent authority, pursuant to his express duties as the chief fiscal officer of the County and his common-law power and duty to recoup public moneys, to recoup alleged salary overpayments from the wages of a County employee, and due process did not require him to commence a plenary action or to await a judicial determination before exercising his authority. The authority to recoup grows out of the recognized principle that public moneys which are erroneously or mistakenly paid out must be recoverable. The Comptroller has the statutory duty to "certify the correctness of payrolls for the payment of salaries of officers and employees paid from county funds" (County Law § 577 [1] [i]), and the County Charter requires that the Comptroller must examine, audit and verify all books, records and accounts paid from County funds, and audit and approve payrolls and determine their regularity. The provisions of the County Charter fail to support the assertion that the Comptroller failed to comply with its procedural requirements. Finally, while petitioner claims that the existence of a factual dispute regarding the amount of money owed precludes the Comptroller from implementing the plan of recoupment, she was afforded the opportunity to submit evidence of her work attendance to the Comptroller and she in fact attended conferences with and submitted documentation to him in an attempt to resolve the matter prior to the issuance of the disputed audit, and she expressly limited her remedies to relief in the nature of prohibition.

 

447 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Altruda v. Forsythe


64213


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


184 A.D.2d 881; 585 N.Y.S.2d 539; 1992 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7977



June 11, 1992, Decided  
June 11, 1992, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner employees appealed from a judgment of the Supreme Court, Albany County (New York) dismissing a combined N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 proceeding and declaratory judgment action against respondents, state budget director and others, which sought to compel respondents to increase the employees' salaries and sought a declaration that a budget bulletin issued pursuant to 1988 N.Y. Laws 732 was unconstitutional and an abuse of discretion.


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to 1988 N.Y. Laws 732, the state budget director issued a budget bulletin that provided that deputies and senior managers would receive salary increases only up to the current salary of their commissioners or agency heads. It provided that those employees whose salaries currently exceeded the salary of their commissioners or agency heads would receive no raise at all. The employees sought a declaration that the budget bulletin in question violated their constitutional right to equal protection of the law and constituted an abuse of discretion. The court affirmed the lower court's judgment in favor of respondents. The delegation of power contained in 1988 N.Y. Laws 732 was sufficiently broad to authorize respondents' determination to cap salaries in order to maintain appropriate salary relationships among officers or employees of the state. In a previous opinion, the court determined that legislation addressing salary compression and promoting the state's fiscal well-being served legitimate state objections. The state's policy of equal pay for equal work did not need to be applied in all circumstances.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment dismissing the employees' application to compel respondents to increase the employees' salaries and making a declaration in respondents' favor in the employees' action seeking a declaration that a budget bulletin regarding their salaries was unconstitutional.

 

448 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Carlone v. Adduci


64689


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


180 A.D.2d 282; 584 N.Y.S.2d 924; 1992 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8000



June 11, 1992, Decided  
June 11, 1992, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant department of motor vehicles sought review of a decision from the Supreme Court of Albany County (New York), which granted plaintiff purchaser's motion for summary judgment in the purchaser's action for an order that declared that he was the owner of a car.


OVERVIEW: The department confiscated the purchaser's car after it was determined that the vehicle registration number (VIN) was a forgery. Pursuant to N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 423-a, the department refused to return the car to the purchaser. The purchaser brought an action for a declaration that he was the rightful owner of the car. The trial court granted the purchaser's motion for summary judgment. On appeal, the court reversed judgment. The court stated that the purchaser had failed to establish that he was the owner of the car prior to the alteration of the VIN or that he had purchased the car from the one who owned the vehicle before such alteration. The court stated that the department was entitled to hold the car, pursuant to § 423-a. The court stated that the purchaser did not offer sufficient evidence to rebut as a matter of law the statutory presumption that the car was a stolen vehicle.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the grant of the purchaser's motion for summary judgment in the purchaser's action for a declaration that he was the rightful owner of the car.

 

449 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Pokoik v. Ocean Beach


90-06451


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


184 A.D.2d 499; 584 N.Y.S.2d 166; 1992 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7683


April 23, 1992, Argued  
June 1, 1992, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff business owner brought an action against defendant village to declare as unconstitutional an ordinance that prohibited public consumption of food and beverages. The business owner also sought to permanently enjoining enforcement of the ordinance. The Supreme Court, Suffolk County (New York) granted the village's motion to dismiss the complaint. The business owner appealed.


OVERVIEW: The business owner asserted that the ordinance was unconstitutionally vague, that it violated his right to eat and consume beverages in public without restriction, and that his right to carry on a lawful business was unlawfully infringed. The supreme court dismissed the complaint for lack of standing to sue and for failure to state a cause of action. The court affirmed and held that the complaint failed to set forth a justiciable controversy and did not establish the business owner's standing. The business owner's attempt to predicate standing on an unspecified right to eat and drink in public without restriction failed because no such protected interest existed. The alleged injury to the business owner's business, a restaurant, was too remote and incidental in nature to establish standing to demand a judgment declaring the ordinance invalid. The business owner's allegations of harm to his business interests was simply an attempt to predicate standing upon the nonexistent right of the public at large to eat and drink in public places without restriction.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of the business owner's complaint, which sought to declare the village's ordinance unconstitutional.

 

450 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Stallone v. Abrams


45390


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


183 A.D.2d 555; 584 N.Y.S.2d 535; 1992 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7334



May 19, 1992, Decided  
May 19, 1992, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed order of the Supreme Court, New York County (New York) granting plaintiffs' motion for summary judgment and holding that N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 3387 (3), which provided for the seizure of crack cocaine pipes, was unconstitutionally void for vagueness.


OVERVIEW: Defendants appealed order granting plaintiffs' motion for summary judgment and declaring N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 3387 (3) unconstitutional. Law provided for the seizure and forfeiture of any equipment intended for use in administering a controlled substance. Plaintiffs were retail stores that sold glass pipes suitable for freebasing crack cocaine. First, court stated that burden on plaintiffs to establish that statute was unconstitutional was high, especially when it was a civil forfeiture provision and not a penal statute. Court found that statute was rationally related to legislative objective of banning sale of drug paraphernalia. Next, court held that phrase "intended for use" was not unconstitutionally vague because it provided sufficient notice to pass constitutional muster. Court found that an ordinary person would be put on notice by the statute that the displaying of crack pipes in his establishment would subject these items to seizure by the police, and the lack of a legitimate use for crack pipes obviated any question concerning the arbitrary or discriminatory enforcement of the statute. Finding the statute to be constitutional, court reversed order.


OUTCOME: Order granting plaintiffs' motion for summary judgment was reversed. The court found that statute was not unconstitutionally vague when it provided sufficient notice to plaintiffs and could not be discriminatorily enforced.

Schulz v. State


63932


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


180 A.D.2d 42; 583 N.Y.S.2d 549; 1992 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6643



May 7, 1992, Decided  
May 7, 1992, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended June 19, 1992.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant State of New York sought review of a decision of the Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), which, in a combined proceeding pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 and an action for declaratory judgment, denied the state's motion to dismiss respondent taxpayers' complaint/petition challenging the disbursement of state funds.


OVERVIEW: An omnibus revenue measure (act) was enacted that was designed to balance the state fiscal year 1990-1991 budget and that provided for a $ 1.2 billion increase in tax revenues and a $ 489 million increase in one-time revenue resources. The taxpayers commenced the combined proceeding challenging various sections of the act as invalid under the state constitution. Specifically, the taxpayers contended that certain provisions of the act authorizing the issuance of bonds, notes and other obligations for various projects violated N.Y. Const. art. VII, §§ 11, 8, and 5. The state's motion to dismiss the petition/complaint based on laches and lack of standing was denied except that the trial court concluded that the taxpayers lacked standing with respect to those portions of the petition challenging the authorization, sale, execution or delivery of bond issues. On appeal, the court held that the taxpayers lack taxpayer standing with respect to those portions of the complaint/petition challenging sections of the act that also dealt with the extension of state credit and long-term debt without voter approval. Hence, dismissal of the entire petition/complaint was warranted.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment denying the state's motion to dismiss the taxpayers' complaint.
