Schoonmaker Homes-John Steinberg, Inc. v. Maybrook


63743


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


178 A.D.2d 722; 576 N.Y.S.2d 954; 1991 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 16010



December 5, 1991, Decided  
December 5, 1991, Entered



DISPOSITION: ORDERED that the judgment is affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property developer sought review of a judgment from the Supreme Court, Orange County (New York), which dismissed appellant's application in a combined action for declaratory judgment and proceeding, pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78, to review a determination of respondent zoning board that found appellant had no vested right to develop its property in accordance with a prior zoning law.


OVERVIEW: The state supreme court dismissed appellant property developer's application, in a combined action for declaratory judgment and proceeding pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78, to review a determination of respondent zoning board that found appellant had no vested right to develop its property in accordance with a prior zoning law and declared certain local laws valid and constitutional. Appellant sought review and the court affirmed the judgment of the supreme court. The court held that appellants had acquired vested rights to develop its site in accordance with the zoning ordinance in effect prior to November 10, 1986. The court found that there was substantial evidence to support a finding of abandonment. The court also determined that there was substantial evidence that the new zoning ordinance would minimize the problems relating to density, open space, traffic, parking and difficulty in providing firefighting services in times of emergency. The court held that the supreme court was justified in dismissing appellant's petition because the new zoning ordinance would provide an overriding benefit to the public.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the state supreme court that dismissed appellant property developer's application to review a determination of respondent zoning board that found appellant did not have a vested right to develop its property in accordance with a prior zoning law. The court found that there was substantial evidence to support a finding of abandonment.

 

474 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Rokowsky v. State Bd. of Equalization & Assessment


63397


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


172 A.D.2d 93; 576 N.Y.S.2d 913; 1991 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 16047



December 5, 1991 



DISPOSITION: Ordered that the order is affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer filed an action against appellee State Board of Equalization and Assessment (board) and alleged inequality of assessment of his property, pursuant to N.Y. Real Prop. Tax Law § 720. The Supreme Court, Albany County (New York) granted the board's motion for summary judgment dismissing the complaint. The taxpayer appealed.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer filed suit against the board and claimed that his residential property had a disproportionately high ratio of assessed value to full market value in comparison to other property in the same class on the same assessment roll. The taxpayer challenged the board's class ratio methodology and asserted that he had no reasonably effective means of proving his claim because the stratified random sample method, prescribed by N.Y. Real Prop. Tax Law § 720(3)(b)(1), was cost-prohibitive. The trial court held that the taxpayer lacked standing and granted the board's motion for summary judgment dismissing the complaint. On appeal, the court held that it was beyond dispute that individual taxpayers lacked standing to challenge the board's methodology used to calculate equalization rates and that a class ratio was essentially an equalization rate. The court also found that, inasmuch as the stratified random sample method afforded the taxpayer a reasonable alternative to the class ratio for proving ratio, barring the taxpayer from challenging the class ratio methodology did not violate his right to due process.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order that granted the board's motion for summary judgment dismissing the taxpayer's complaint that challenged the assessment of his property.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- Assessment -- Standing to Challenge Class Ratio Methodology

1. An individual taxpayer lacks standing to challenge the class ratio methodology employed by the State Board of Equalization and Assessment since such taxpayers lack standing to challenge the methodology used by the Board to calculate equalization rates, and challenges to class ratios, which are essentially equalization rates computed for a particular class of property in a given special assessing unit ( RPTL 1202 [1] [b]), should not be treated any differently. The fact that class ratios are utilized mainly as evidence in tax certiorari proceedings does not compel the conclusion that they must therefore be more precise; while the Board must "ascertain as near as may be the percentage of full value" in determining equalization rates (see, RPTL 1202 [1] [a]), there is no such requirement with respect to class ratios.

Taxation -- Assessment -- Standing to Challenge  Class Ratio Methodology

2. An individual taxpayer lacks standing to challenge the class ratio methodology employed by the State Board of Equalization and Assessment, and it is not true that taxpayers situated in special assessing units are disadvantaged in that they have no reasonably effective means available of proving inequality vis-a-vis ratio because the alternative, the stratified random sample method, is economically unfeasible. The random sample need only be drawn from within the major type of property in which the assessment under review is classified (see, RPTL 720 [3] [b] [1]), and, thus, the taxpayer need not consider the entire roll in proving inequality. Moreover, since the stratified random sample method affords a taxpayer in a special assessing unit a reasonable alternative to the class ratio for proving ratio, barring the taxpayer from challenging the class ratio methodology for lack of standing does not violate his right to due process. The right to be heard does not include an opportunity to judicially contest the merits of every legislative determination in the taxing statute.

 

475 of 992 DOCUMENTS


In re Gissette Angela P.


43288


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


172 A.D.2d 117; 577 N.Y.S.2d 774; 1991 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 16014



December 5, 1991 



DISPOSITION: Final order, Family Court, New York County, entered June 25, 1990, affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, a minor, sought review of an order of the Family Court, New York County (New York), which adjudicated the minor a juvenile delinquent upon a finding that she committed acts, which if committed by an adult, would have constituted the crime of criminal possession of a controlled substance in the first degree.


OVERVIEW: The minor was observed by a police officer while waiting for a bus bound for Virginia. The minor appeared to be young and she was unaccompanied. The minor was nervous and had a grapefruit-sized bulge under her coat. The officer boarded the bus with the minor and asked her how old she was and whether she was alone. The minor told the officer that she was 18 and that she was traveling alone. When the officer told the minor that he was concerned about the bulge under her coat, the minor unzipped her coat and handed him a plastic bag that contained cocaine. The minor claimed that the encounter with the officer was tantamount to a seizure because she was confined in the bus. The court rejected the minor's claims. The court found that the officer's observation of the minor's nervous behavior and the bulge in her midsection provided and objective, credible reason to make an informational inquiry, that there was no detention of the minor or hint of harassment or intimidation, and that the minor's decision to hand over the bag of cocaine was not the result of coercion.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the family court's order, which adjudicated the minor to be a juvenile delinquent after finding that the minor had committed acts, which if committed by an adult, would have constituted the crime of criminal possession of a controlled substance in the first degree.


HEADNOTES

Infants -- Juvenile Delinquents -- Unlawful Search and Seizure -- Inquiry by Police on Bus

1. In a juvenile delinquency proceeding arising out of appellant's handing over to a police officer a quantity of cocaine after being questioned on a bus, the officer's inquiry of appellant was appropriate in view of the purpose sought to be achieved by Family Court Act § 718 and entirely justifiable where the youthful-looking 14-year-old appellant had been observed waiting nervously to board the bus and had a grapefruit-sized bulge under her coat, since the statute, governing the return of runaway children, requires some inquiry by a police officer prior to detaining a juvenile. The officer, based on his knowledge that the bus station attracts passengers carrying illegal drugs, and on his observation of appellant's nervous behavior and the bulge in her midsection, had an objective credible reason to make an informational inquiry. Moreover, there was no detention of appellant, not even a hint of harassment or intimidation, and her decision to turn over the contraband was not the result of coercion.

Infants -- Juvenile Delinquents -- Aid to Runaways

2. In a juvenile delinquency proceeding arising out of appellant's handing over to a police officer a quantity of cocaine after being questioned on a bus, the officer had the statutory authority and even the duty to approach and question appellant, an unaccompanied, youthful-looking 14-year-old girl who was in a location known to be frequented by truants and runaways and to have a high incidence of drug activity (see, Family Ct Act § 718 [a]). Moreover, even if it were conceded that appellant was detained by the police officers after having boarded the bus, her detention would be authorized under the circumstances by section 718, and there is no dispute that she voluntarily opened her coat and surrendered the contraband to the officer. Although appellant's questioning by the officer was noncustodial in nature, even a custodial detention and a search conducted incident thereto would have been justified pursuant to section 718. Accordingly, appellant's motion to suppress evidence was properly denied.

 

476 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Brady v. State


61777


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


172 A.D.2d 17; 576 N.Y.S.2d 896; 1991 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 15096



November 27, 1991 



DISPOSITION: Ordered that the order entered September 12, 1990 and judgment entered thereon are modified, on the law, without costs, by reversing so much thereof as granted defendants' motion dismissing the cause of action in the complaint challenging the constitutionality of Tax Law § 651 (b) (2) and denied plaintiffs' cross motion on said cause of action; motion denied and cross motion granted on said cause of action, partial summary judgment awarded to plaintiffs, it is declared that Tax Law § 651 (b) (2) is unconstitutional as applied to require the filing of a joint New York income tax return by a nonresident solely because that person's nonresident spouse earned New York [***2] income and the couple filed a joint Federal income tax return, and matter remitted to the Supreme Court for further proceedings not inconsistent with this court's decision; and, as so modified, affirmed. Ordered that the orders entered February 15, 1990 and judgments entered thereon are affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, taxpayers, sought review of the decision of the Supreme Court in Albany County (New York), which found in favor of defendant state in plaintiffs' action challenging the validity of New York's income tax treatment of nonresident taxpayers pursuant to N.Y. Tax Law §§ 651(b)(2) and 601(d) and (e).


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, taxpayers, brought an action against defendant state challenging the validity of N.Y. Tax Law §§ 651(b)(2) and 601(d) and (e). The statutes dealt with the income tax treatment of nonresident taxpayers. The lower court found in favor of defendant and plaintiffs appealed. The court held that there was merit to plaintiffs' due process objection to § 651(b)(2), and their complaint should be modified by reinstating the cause of action challenging the constitutionality of § 651(b)(2) and declaring it unconstitutional as applied to require the filing of a joint income tax return by a nonresident solely because that person's nonresident spouse earned New York income and the couple filed a joint federal income tax return. The court stated that plaintiffs did not demonstrate that, under § 601(e), their income from non-New York sources was used in any respect other than as a measurement of the tax due on New York income. Therefore, plaintiffs' due process objection to § 601(e) failed. The court concluded that the superiority of a class action over other available methods for resolution of the controversy involved in this litigation was not established.


OUTCOME: The court held that the dismissal of plaintiff taxpayers' complaint should be modified by reinstating the cause of action challenging the constitutionality of a tax law and declaring that law unconstitutional as applied to require the filing of a joint income tax return by a nonresident solely because that person's nonresident spouse earned New York income and the couple filed a joint federal income tax return.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- Personal Income Tax -- Taxation of Nonresidents

1. Tax Law § 651 (b) (2), which requires nonresident taxpayers and their nonresident spouses, like resident taxpayers and their resident spouses, to file a joint New York State income tax return, thereby reporting their aggregate incomes, if they filed a joint Federal tax return, violates due process insofar as it may be applied to require a nonresident spouse of a nonresident taxpayer to file a joint State tax return solely by reason of the marital relationship and the filing by the couple of a joint Federal income tax return. Due process requires some definite link, some minimum connection, between a State and the person, property or transaction it seeks to tax, and out-of-State cohabitation with a spouse having New York income and filing a joint Federal income tax return with that spouse cannot constitute the necessary minimum connection for the extraterritorial exercise of New York's taxing power.

Taxation -- Personal Income Tax -- Taxation of Nonresidents

2. Inasmuch as Tax Law § 651 (b) (2) violates due process insofar as it may be applied to require a nonresident spouse of a nonresident taxpayer to file a joint New York State income tax return solely by reason of the marital relationship and the filing by the couple of a joint Federal income tax return, constitutional challenges to the validity of Tax Law § 601 (d), (e) that were based upon the interaction of those sections with section 651 (b) (2) are academic. The language of section 601 (d), (e) is "marriage neutral", and once a nonresident spouse having no New York income is no longer obligated to file a joint return, the remaining sections of the Tax Law at issue are not subject to the objection that they increase a nonresident taxpayer's State tax liability on the basis of the non-New York income of that taxpayer's nonresident spouse.

Taxation -- Personal Income Tax -- Taxation of Nonresidents

3. Tax Law § 601 (e), which provides for a two-step process to determine the income tax due from nonresidents, does not violate due process, and the claim that its inclusion of a nonresident's non-New York income in the initial determination of the tax before applying an apportionment formula is the functional equivalent of a tax on that out-of-State income is unavailing, since the procedure for taxing the New York source income of a nonresident by initially applying a progressive tax rate to the taxpayer's income from all sources and then apportioning to fix the final tax due, is a valid means of using income not itself taxable as part of the measurement of the tax imposed on the nonresident's New York income. Plaintiffs, who are challenging the constitutionality of the law, have not demonstrated that under the statute their income from non-New York sources is used in any respect other than as a measurement of the tax due on New York income, that is, in determining the rate of taxation applied to their New York income.

Taxation -- Personal Income Tax -- Taxation of Nonresidents

4. Tax Law § 601 (e), which provides for a two-step process to determine the income tax due from nonresidents, does not discriminate against nonresident taxpayers in favor of New York residents by counting a nonresident's income from New York sources to determine the appropriate tax rate to be applied to a nonresident's New York income, and, hence, is not unconstitutional, since the applicable tax rate for both residents and nonresidents is derived in the same manner and from the same ultimate source, i.e., Federal adjusted gross income, generally constituting income from all sources. Resident and nonresident taxpayers similarly situated in terms of ability to pay taxes out of all sources of income are treated the same within the parameters of New York's progressive income taxation system. Moreover, it is unpersuasive to assert that discrimination against nonresident taxpayers exists because apportionment under the statutes to fix the final tax due dilutes the value of their deductions and exemptions, as compared to their value for resident taxpayers, since to whatever extent application of the ratio of New York income to total income dilutes the value of exemptions and deductions for nonresidents, it does so in direct proportion to the reduction of the final tax due from nonresidents.

Taxation -- Personal Income Tax -- Taxation of Nonresidents

5. A constitutional challenge by nonresidents to Tax Law § 601 (d), which imposes a tax on unearned income from non-New York sources of nonresidents, is meritless since that statute, by its own terms, does not apply directly to nonresident taxpayers; however, the unearned income of a nonresident does enter into the calculation to arrive at the nonresident's tax liability under Tax Law § 601 (e), because the initial determination of tax is made "as if such nonresident * * * were a resident". Nonetheless, the inclusion of the additional tax on unearned income in the two-step computation performed pursuant to section 601 (e) merely serves to add marginally to the tax rate applied to the nonresident's New York income, and the validity of that use of non-New York income in taxing the New York income of a nonresident taxpayer has been sustained.

Actions -- Class Actions -- Superior Form of Litigation -- Stare Decisis -- Governmental Defendant

6. In an action challenging the constitutionality of various provisions of the Tax Law which pertain to the taxation of nonresidents' income, denial of class action certification was proper where the constitutional issues regarding liability have been resolved and the determination of the invalidity of Tax Law § 651 (b) (2), as applied, will be given stare decisis effect in any future litigation. Moreover, defendant governmental agencies have stipulated to being bound by the determination on all duly and timely filed administrative claims. The remaining unresolved issues for entitlement to monetary relief appear to be primarily personal to the individual members of the class of nonresident taxpayers, i.e., eligibility for a refund and the amount of overpayment. Moreover, no cause of action lies for restitution of an unconstitutionally collected tax that was paid voluntarily and, hence, entitlement to monetary relief will require some initiative on the part of the individual taxpayer, by way of protest or proper claim for a refund. Consequently, the superiority of a class action over other methods of dispute resolution has not been established.

Pyramid Co. of Auburn v. Chu


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


177 A.D.2d 970; 577 N.Y.S.2d 1015; 1991 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 15705



November 15, 1991, Decided  
November 15, 1991, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant company sought review of a decision from the Supreme Court, Onondaga County (New York), which granted the motion of respondent Commissioner of Taxation and Finance of the State of New York to dismiss the company's declaratory judgment action on the ground of failure to exhaust administrative remedies. The company asserted that N.Y. Tax Law § 1115(a)(17) was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: N.Y. Tax Law § 1115(a)(17) exempted a contractor who installed personal property purchased for use in the improvement of real property from collecting the sales tax thereon from the real property owner as would otherwise be required. The company contended that the statute was unconstitutional because it violated the equal protection clause, but the trial court granted the Commissioner's motion to dismiss based on the ground that the company failed to exhaust its administrative remedies. The court reversed the decision, reinstated the complaint, and granted the judgment, holding that the statute was rationally related to the legitimate state purpose of ensuring that the sales tax was efficiently collected without imposing double taxation. Although a non-installing contractor had to collect the sales tax, he was entitled to seek a rebate, thereby avoiding double taxation. The distinction implicit in § 1115(a)(17) had a rational purpose and, therefore, § 1115(a)(17) was constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment dismissing the company's complaint, reinstated the complaint, and granted judgment holding that the statute at issue was constitutional.

481 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Averbach v. Board of Assessors


No. 63206


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


176 A.D.2d 1151; 575 N.Y.S.2d 964; 1991 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 13671



October 31, 1991 



DISPOSITION: ORDERED that the judgment is reversed, on the law, without costs, and matter remitted to the Supreme Court for further proceedings not inconsistent with this court's decision.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owners sought review of a judgment from the Supreme Court, Delaware County (New York), which dismissed for failure to state a cause of action appellants' proceeding pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 to review the determinations of respondent municipal authorities regarding the reassessment of appellants' real property.


OVERVIEW: Appellant property owners commenced a N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 proceeding against respondent municipal authorities challenging their 1989-1990 tax assessment roll and alleging that it violated their constitutional rights because assessments were made pursuant to an illegal "welcome stranger" assessment procedure. The trial court dismissed for failure to state a cause of action. The court reversed the judgment, finding that appellants' proceeding was appropriate because it asserted that the method employed in the assessment involving several properties was unconstitutional. Further, the court found that appellants' challenge was not merely couched in illegality but clearly attacked the method employed in taxing. Additionally, the court found that appellants had sufficiently stated a cause of action because their petition recited the assessment history of each parcel in question and identified similarly situated properties with their assessed values. Thus, appellants provided more than mere conclusory statements supporting their allegations. Finally, the court held that appellants' proceeding was timely because appellants were specifically challenging the 1989-1990 tax roll only.


OUTCOME: The court reversed a judgment that dismissed for failure to state a cause of action appellant property owners' proceeding to review the determinations of respondent municipal authorities regarding the reassessment of appellants' real property. The court found that appellants had sufficiently stated a cause of action because their petition provided more than mere conclusory statements supporting their allegations.

 

482 of 992 DOCUMENTS


People v. Galletta


No. 43741


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


171 A.D.2d 178; 575 N.Y.S.2d 825; 1991 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 13626



October 29, 1991 



DISPOSITION: Judgment, Supreme Court, New York County, rendered March 20, 1989, reversed, on the law, the sentences imposed thereon vacated, and those counts of the indictment charging assault in the third degree and verbal harassment pursuant to Penal Law § 240.25 (2) dismissed, and the case is remanded for a new trial on the remaining counts of the indictment.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Supreme Court in New York County (New York), upon a jury verdict convicting defendant of assault in the third degree, official misconduct, and harassment. Defendant contended that the trial court's dismissal of a juror on the grounds of incapacity pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 270.35 was improper, that an assault charge was unwarranted, and that the verbal harassment charge was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, a plainclothes transit police officer, was charged and convicted of assault in the third degree, verbal harassment, and official misconduct stemming from an incident that arose while detaining a student for jumping a turnstile. The court reversed the convictions, finding that the trial court's abrupt decision to replace a juror did not properly measure defendant's rights in jury selection. The trial court, knowing of the juror's whereabouts, did not attempt to ascertain the success of the juror's appearance the next day. Defense counsel's request for a one-day adjournment to allow the juror's return was reasonable, and the prosecution had not objected. Had the absent juror not appeared the next morning, the trial court then could have discharged him. Second, the stage of the trial and witness availability factors warranted a one-day delay because only the summations and jury charge remained in the three-week long trial. The court dismissed the assault charge because the necessary evidence of "substantial pain" was lacking. Finally, defendant's harassment conviction was reversed and dismissed because the statute had been declared unconstitutionally overbroad.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment convicting defendant of assault in the third degree, official misconduct, and verbal harassment. The sentences were vacated, and the counts of assault in the third degree and verbal harassment were dismissed. The case was remanded to the trial court for a new trial on the remaining counts of the indictment.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Jurors -- Discharge of Juror

1. In a criminal prosecution in which a juror called court staff on Monday morning to advise that he was in Florida attempting to secure a return flight on "standby", the trial court's abrupt decision to replace the juror, after criticizing the juror's actions as irresponsible and deciding that with no assurances as to his availability the next day his discharge was warranted, did not properly measure the defendant's important rights in jury selection since the court had determined the juror's whereabouts, yet no further attempts were made to ascertain the success of his ongoing return flight efforts. Moreover, defense counsel's request for a one-day adjournment to allow the juror's return was reasonable, especially in view of the lack of opposition by the People, and had the absent juror not appeared the next morning, the court then could have proceeded to discharge him. In addition, the stage of the trial and witness availability factors counseled patience since only the summations and jury charge remained, and the better course would have been to adjourn the trial one day before proceeding further.

Crimes -- Assault -- Substantial Pain

2. In a prosecution for assault, an examination of the photographs depicting various bruises and marks on the complaining witness as well as the testimony in the record reveals that neither the bruises depicted nor the victim's description of his pain, coupled with the fact that he did not seek medical attention for his bruises, the worst of which healed in about a week, were sufficient to support an assault conviction. While the bruises depicted undoubtedly caused some pain to the complainant, the necessary evidence of "substantial pain" is lacking and, thus, dismissal of the assault charge is warranted.

Crimes -- Harassment  -- Invalidity of Penal Statute

3. A judgment convicting defendant of harassment pursuant to Penal Law § 240.25 (2) for using abusive and obscene language in a public place must be reversed and the corresponding count of the indictment dismissed since the Court of Appeals has struck down that statute for overbreadth, concluding that the statute, on its face, prohibits a substantial amount of constitutionally protected expression and that its continued existence presents a significant risk of prosecution for the mere exercise of free speech. Inasmuch as the instant case is on direct appeal, the Court of Appeals decision controls retroactively.

People v. Malphrus


No. 58614


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


176 A.D.2d 1073; 575 N.Y.S.2d 381; 1991 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 13473



October 24, 1991 



DISPOSITION: ORDERED that the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment of the County Court of Albany County (New York), which convicted him, upon a verdict, of the crime of vehicle dismantling without a registration in violation of N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 415-a(1).


OVERVIEW: Defendant sought review of his conviction for dismantling a vehicle without a registration. On appeal, the court affirmed the conviction, holding that it was more than reasonable to infer that defendant was in the business of acquiring motor vehicles for the purpose of dismantling them for parts when the investigator found that he had 15 or 20 salvage or junk motor vehicles in his yard, and he and defendant looked at several cars before they found one from which they could remove an alternator, which defendant sold to the investigator. The court determined that defendant's prosecution was not the result of the unconstitutional selective enforcement of the law in violation of the Equal Protection Clause of the United States Constitution because defendant did not sustain his burden of establishing a conscious and intentional discrimination against him, as the record reflected that there were prosecutions in less than one percent of the cases because the offenders agreed to and registered, so the matter was then considered resolved.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment convicting defendant of dismantling cars without a registration.

 

484 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Mitchell v. Kemp


No. 91-05331


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


176 A.D.2d 859; 575 N.Y.S.2d 337; 1991 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 13259


September 24, 1991, Argued  
October 21, 1991 



DISPOSITION: ORDERED that the judgment is affirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant town board sought review of a judgment of the Supreme Court, Dutchess County (New York), which declared Pine Plains, N.Y., Local Laws § 4 (1990) unconstitutional and directed the town board to issue respondent petitioner a building permit.


OVERVIEW: The town board appealed from the trial court judgment that declared an interim moratorium zoning law unconstitutional and directed the town board to issue the petitioner a building permit. The town board claimed that the issue of the constitutionality of the moratorium law was academic, since that law had expired and was replaced by a new moratorium law. The court affirmed the trial court judgment. The court held that interim or stop-gap legislation was permissible in order to maintain the status quo pending new legislation, but that the town board failed to offer any satisfactory reasons for the nearly five-year delay in enacting a zoning ordinance. The court concluded that the trial court properly directed the issuance of a building permit because the petitioner had already received site plan review and approval under the old moratorium law; therefore, review and approval under the new moratorium law was redundant. The court held that the town board's denial of petitioner's request for a variance from the moratorium was arbitrary and capricious because its stated reasons for the denial were either irrelevant or unsupported in the record.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court judgment that declared an interim moratorium law unconstitutional and directed the town board to issue the petitioner a building permit. The judgment was affirmed, with costs.

 

485 of 992 DOCUMENTS


North Hempstead v. Regan


No. 63375


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


171 A.D.2d 165; 574 N.Y.S.2d 851; 1991 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 13061



October 17, 1991 



DISPOSITION: Ordered that the judgment is affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant municipalities sought review of an order from the Supreme Court of Albany County (New York), which dismissed a petition to review a determination of respondent state comptroller that imposed a sales tax on revenues received by municipalities from off-street parking fees pursuant to N.Y. Tax Law § 1105(c)(6).


OVERVIEW: After N.Y. Tax Law § 1105(c)(6) (1990) was implemented the comptroller notified the municipalities that all parking services furnished by the municipalities were subject to a new tax except parking services offered through meters on public streets. The municipalities argued that the statute was unconstitutional as it applied to them. They claimed that the providing of metered municipal parking fields for use by their respective constituents to be a municipal obligation and they were not vendors of services of a kind not ordinarily sold by private persons. On review the court found that the municipalities failed to show that the service was not one also ordinarily provided by public vendors and affirmed. The court noted that private vendors that provided parking served the same public interest but were taxed nonetheless. The court rejected the municipalities' argument that the comptroller acted arbitrarily in excluding municipal parking lots and parking fields from the "on-street" exception.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the supreme court's dismissal of the municipalities' petition to review the determination that imposed a sales tax on revenues received by municipalities from off-street parking fees.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- Sales and Use Taxes -- Revenues Received by Municipalities from Off-Street Parking

1. A determination of the State Comptroller and State Department of Taxation and Finance imposing a sales tax on revenues received by municipalities from off-street parking fees (see, Tax Law § 1105 [c] [6]) is not the result of an unreasonable interpretation of the statute, since section 1105 (c) (6) explicitly provides for such tax on receipts obtained from providing "parking, garaging or storing for motor vehicles", and, notwithstanding the general rule that a tax statute is to be construed in favor of the taxpayer, petitioner municipalities, which concededly qualify as "persons" subject to the statute, have failed to show that the provision of parking facilities is not a service also ordinarily provided by private vendors, who, by making parking available, also serve the same public interest of aiding the free flow of traffic, but are taxed nonetheless. Accordingly, petitioners may be considered "vendors" for purposes of the statute, and the fact that they assertedly do not profit from their parking facilities is of little consequence since there is no requirement in the Tax Law that one who renders a service must make a profit before that service is taxable. Moreover, the operation of municipal parking lots is a proprietary, not a governmental, function.

Taxation -- Sales and Use Taxes -- Revenues Received by Municipalities from Off-Street Parking

2. Tax Law § 1105 (c) (6), which imposes a sales tax on revenues received by vendors of off-street parking, is applicable to municipalities which operate off-street parking facilities, and the argument of petitioner municipalities that respondents acted arbitrarily in excluding municipal parking lots and fields from the statutory exception created for "on-street" metered parking, is unconvincing, since respondents' distinction between off-street and on-street parking is rational in light of the fact that the latter is not ordinarily sold by private persons (see, Tax Law § 1101 [b] [8] [i] [H]; § 1116 [a] [1]), while off-street parking commonly is. As to whether that distinction is equitable, in the application of tax statutes, unlike many others, it cannot be assumed that when the Legislature designed the particular statute it had either a specific or even a general desire to achieve a fair or balanced formula; rather, the objective may well have been the production of optimum revenue. Moreover, the failure of the Legislature to enact "curative" legislation is indicative of its acceptance of respondents' interpretation of the law.

 

486 of 992 DOCUMENTS


People v. Clark


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


176 A.D.2d 1206; 576 N.Y.S.2d 704; 1991 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 13872



October 4, 1991, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The prosecution appealed the order of the Monroe County Court (New York) that disregarded defendant's status as a predicate felon and sentenced her to a term of probation rather than a statutorily-mandated period of incarceration. The trial court had imposed probation in the interest of justice and on the ground that imposition of the mandatory sentence would constitute cruel and unusual punishment.


OVERVIEW: Defendant pled guilty to felony charges on the condition that the trial court disregard her status as a predicate felon and sentence her to a term of probation rather than impose a mandatory period of incarceration. The trial court imposed probation in the interest of justice and on the ground that imposition of the mandatory sentence would constitute cruel and unusual punishment. Defendant, the single mother of infant children, was HIV positive and in the final stage before the onset of AIDS. The court modified on the law, affirmed, and remitted the case for further proceedings. The courts did not possess "interest of justice" jurisdiction to impose a sentence less than the mandatory statutory minimum. In addition, the imposition of the mandated sentence would not be unconstitutional as applied to defendant. The fact that defendant was HIV positive or suffered from AIDS was not a sufficient reason to modify an otherwise lawful sentence of imprisonment. Because defendant's plea was specifically conditioned upon the imposition of probation, defendant would be given the opportunity to withdraw her plea. If defendant chose not to withdraw her plea, she was to be resentenced.


OUTCOME: The court modified on the law and remitted the case for further proceedings, holding that the courts did not possess "interest of justice" jurisdiction to impose a sentence less than the mandatory statutory minimum and the imposition of the mandated sentence would not be unconstitutional as applied to defendant.

 

487 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Avildsen v. Prystay


Nos. 42349, M-3159


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


171 A.D.2d 13; 574 N.Y.S.2d 535; 1991 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11833



September 19, 1991 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended September 19, 1991.



DISPOSITION: Order, Supreme Court, New York County, entered on March 22, 1990, affirmed, without costs. Application by defendant-appellant to direct that plaintiff-respondent pay counsel fees (in the event the appellant prevails on appeal) is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff father and defendant mother agreed on an amount of child support to be paid by the father for the benefit of the parties' child. Eight years later, the mother filed a motion, which sought, among other things, to vacate the court-approved agreement between the parties, to grant interim child support, and to provide a retroactive lump-sum payment. The trial court (New York) denied the motion. The mother appealed.


OVERVIEW: The court addressed one issue, the alleged unconscionability of the bargain, although the trial court did not discuss it. The court noted, however, that the difficulty with invocation of the doctrine to reopen the contract, made by the parties for their child born out of wedlock, was that the presumptive inequality of two such contracting parties was considered and preempted by the legislature in enacting N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 516. In recognition of a well-founded perception that a man and a woman enmeshed in such a relationship were almost never in combat on a level playing field, the statute had crafted some fairly elaborate machinery to restore the balance. The trial court, which reviewed the bargain, found the consideration tendered by the father adequate. Thus, the court held that no tribunal of the state was competent, eight years later, to revise the settlement. The court further reasoned that its conclusion was only strengthened by the subsequent conduct of the parties. When the father had once withheld payments, the mother enforced the father's compliance with a contempt proceeding.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial and dismissal of mother's application to vacate the court-approved agreement between the parties and for related relief.


HEADNOTES

Parent and Child -- Support -- Agreement between Parents -- Vacatur of Agreement Approved by Court -- Unconscionability

An order denying defendant mother's motion to vacate a court-approved agreement providing for the support of the parties' out-of-wedlock child should be affirmed. Although defendant now asserts that the eight-year-old agreement is unconscionable, any judicial examination into the inequality or overreaching of the parties when the agreement was executed is foreclosed by the Legislature's enactment of Family Court Act § 516, which contains elaborate machinery to safeguard the position of each party. The fact that a postexecution paternity test showed plaintiff to be the child's father should not affect the stability of the agreement since the test was within the contemplation of the parties at the time the agreement was made, and the results of that test were not reserved by either party as a future escape hatch; nor was any such suggestion made before the Justice who approved the agreement less than one month later. Therefore, no tribunal is now competent to revise the settlement. Moreover, defendant's subsequent conduct, viz., engaging in litigation to enforce the agreement, clearly indicates that she ratified the agreement.

People v. Sasso


No. 61827


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


176 A.D.2d 410; 574 N.Y.S.2d 116; 1991 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12039


August 1, 1991  
September 19, 1991 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended October 11, 1991.



DISPOSITION: ORDERED that the order is reversed, as a matter of discretion in the interest of justice, motion granted, sentence vacated and matter remitted to the County Court of Albany County for resentencing.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed, by permission, from an order of the County Court of Albany County (New York), which denied his motion, made pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 440.20 to set aside a sentence following his conviction of the crimes of robbery in the first degree and grand larceny in the second degree.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was sentenced as a persistent felony offender to 25 years to life imprisonment. Defendant argued that his sentence was improper because the trial court had relied upon his convictions of Connecticut offenses as predicate felony offenses when it sentenced him as a persistent felony offender. The state frankly conceded that an examination of the elements of the Connecticut crimes which were used to find defendant to be a persistent felony offender disclosed that they did not qualify as felonies under New York law. The court found that the Connecticut crimes were all considered misdemeanor offenses under New York law, and therefore, they could not have served as predicate felonies for the purpose of his sentencing as a persistent felony offender. Accordingly, the court reversed the order of the trial court, granted defendant's motion to set aside his sentence, vacated that sentence, and remitted the matter for resentencing.


OUTCOME: The order of the trial court, which denied defendant's motion to set aside the sentence imposed, was reversed and the motion was granted, the sentence vacated, and the matter remitted to the trial court for resentencing.

Lovelace v. Gross


No. 4781E


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


175 A.D.2d 914; 573 N.Y.S.2d 752; 1991 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11335


November 1, 1990, Argued  
August 26, 1991 



DISPOSITION: ORDERED that the order and judgment is modified, on the law, by adding a provision thereto declaring that Social Services Law § 131-c(2) is not violative of either the New York State nor Federal Constitutions; as so modified, the order and judgment is affirmed, without costs or disbursements.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs sought review of an order and judgment of the Supreme Court, Queens County (New York), which granted the motion of defendants to dismiss plaintiffs' complaint and denied their cross motion for class action certification.


OVERVIEW: N.Y. Soc. Serv. § 131-c(2) required consideration of grandparent's income when the mother receiving aid was a minor and both she and her child lived with the grandparent. The plaintiffs brought an action to obtain a judgment declaring that § 131-c(2) was unconstitutional because it violated the Equal Protection Clauses of the Federal and New York Constitutions and N.Y. Const. art. XVII, § 1 which required the legislature to provide for the needy. The defendants filed a motion to dismiss the complaint, which was granted. The trial court also denied plaintiffs' cross motion for class action certification. The plaintiffs appealed. The court affirmed and held that (1) the state did not violate the Equal Protection Clause merely because the classifications made by law were imperfect, it was sufficient there was a reasonable basis for the classification; (2) the state's decision to apply the provision to both Aid to Dependent Children and Home Relief recipients was a rational one; (3) N.Y. Const. art. XVII, § 1 required care of the needy in such a manner and by such means as the legislature determined; and (4) a class action was inappropriate where government operations were involved.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's dismissal of the action seeking to have N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 131-c(2) declared unconstitutional.

Carpinelli v. Kingston


Nos. 62408, 62527


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


175 A.D.2d 509; 572 N.Y.S.2d 777; 1991 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10437



July 25, 1991, Decided



DISPOSITION: ORDERED that the judgments are affirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In two combined actions for declaratory judgment and proceedings in the Supreme Court, Ulster County (New York), petitioners, Commissioners of Public Works, sought to annul a determination by respondent mayor that rescinded their appointments as members of the Board of Public Works. The trial court found the portion of the city charter the mayor relied on was unconstitutional and found for the Commissioners. The mayor appealed.


OVERVIEW: The mayor claimed the Commissioners had been appointed in violation of a provision of the city charter which provided that not more than two Commissioners could belong to the same political party or be of the same political opinion on state and national issues. According to the mayor, even though one Commissioner was a Conservative and one was a Republican, because they both supported the same candidates in the most recent presidential and gubernatorial elections, they were of the same political opinion. The court affirmed the judgment. The city charter, which provided that not more than two commissioners should be of the same political opinion on state and national issues, was unconstitutionally vague. The provision of the city charter failed to provide any objective standards or criteria in order to determine whether more than two commissioners were of the same political opinion on state and national issues and impermissibly placed complete discretion in the hands of those who enforced the provision to determine who would be disqualified from serving as commissioner. The charter provision was arbitrary and had a discriminatory application, which was deemed unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the Supreme Court granting the Commissioners' declaratory relief motion was affirmed.

Bakery Salvage Corp. v. Buffalo


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


175 A.D.2d 608; 573 N.Y.S.2d 788; 1991 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10128



July 12, 1991, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner business appealed from a judgment of Supreme Court, Erie County (New York), which dismissed the business's petition under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 seeking to prohibit respondents, city and Common Council of the City of Buffalo (common council), from enforcing the Offensive and Noxious Odors Ordinance, Buffalo, N.Y. Ordinance art. IX, ch. 216 and to declare such ordinance unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The common council enacted an ordinance under which commercial or manufacturing establishments that produced offensive or noxious odors were to be regulated. Acknowledging that no reliable scientific objective standard or test for the existence of an offensive or noxious odor existed, the ordinance defined a noxious or offensive odor to be the minimum concentration of odor required to give the first sensation of fetid or noisome odor to a person of average odor sensitivity. A proceeding was commenced against the business pursuant to the ordinance, which was stayed pending determination of the business's petition for review of the ordinance's constitutionality. The court reversed the trial court's order dismissing the petition for review and declared the ordinance to be unconstitutional. The court held that the ordinance could not survive the void-for-vagueness test because a lack of a reasonable degree of definiteness pervaded various provisions of the ordinance, which contained no objective standards by which one could determine the quantum of emissions of odors constituting a "minimum concentration" or by which to determine whether a person was of "average odor sensitivity."


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment dismissing the business's petition for review and constitutionality of the "Offensive or Noxious Odors" ordinance and declared such ordinance to be unconstitutional.

 

492 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Wright v. Town Bd. of Ticonderoga


No. 62858


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


169 A.D.2d 190; 572 N.Y.S.2d 397; 1991 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9715



July 11, 1991 



DISPOSITION: Ordered that the judgment is modified, on the law, without costs, by converting this CPLR article 78 proceeding into an action for declaratory judgment; declare that Local Law No. 1 of 1990 of the Town of Ticonderoga has not been shown to be unconstitutional; and, as so modified, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant petitioner, a World War II veteran, sought review of a decision of the Supreme Court of Essex County (New York), which dismissed his petition to review respondent town board's determination that repealed a local tax law, causing petitioner to lose a $ 5,000 property tax exemption that he had been receiving as a veteran.


OVERVIEW: The petitioner, a World War II veteran, had been receiving a $ 5,000 exemption from payment of real property taxes, pursuant to Real Prop. Tax § 458 (1) and (2). The town board repealed a local law that resulted in the petitioner's loss of his $ 5,000 real property tax exemption. Petitioner sought review of the town board's repeal of the local tax law, contending that the state preempted the issue, and the town board's repeal of the local law unconstitutionally removed a vested right he had in continuing to receive the real property tax exemption. The trial court dismissed the petitioner's request for review. The petitioner appealed the dismissal. On appeal, the court affirmed the trial court's dismissal of the petition but held that the petitioner's challenge to the constitutionality of the local law should have been brought as a declaratory judgment action and thus converted the proceeding. The court concluded that the town board's repeal of the local tax law providing property tax exemptions to veterans was not unconstitutional because § 458 (5) (a) specifically reserved to local governments the right to repeal laws opting out of or reducing the ceiling on such exemptions.


OUTCOME: The court ordered the judgment of the trial court to be modified, on the law, into an action for declaratory judgment, holding that the local law enacted by the town board was not unconstitutional, and affirmed as modified.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- Exemptions -- Repeal of Veteran's Exemption by Locality -- State Preemption

1. A petition seeking review of a determination of a town board which repealed a local law providing for a veteran's exemption from real property taxes was properly dismissed. There is no indication, express or implied, that the Legislature intended to preempt the area of veterans' real property tax exemptions beyond the $ 5,000 exclusion that RPTL 458 (1) and (2) provide, and the statute specifically reserves to local authorities the decision of whether to adjust the exemption proportionally by the amount which the assessed valuation of the property has increased or decreased as a result of the change to full value assessment (see, RPTL 458 [5] [a]).

Taxation -- Exemptions -- Repeal of Veteran's Exemption by Locality -- No Vested Right to Continuation of Favorable Tax Treatment

2. A petition seeking review of a determination of a town board which repealed a local law providing for a veteran's exemption from real property taxes was properly dismissed. Citizens have no vested right in statutory exemptions, and a taxing authority is not foreclosed from changing its view respecting the continued allowance of a tax exemption because the passage of time creates no vested right in a tax exemption.

Taxation -- Exemptions -- Repeal of Veteran's Exemption by Locality -- Authority of Locality to Repeal Exemption

3. A petition seeking review of a determination of a town board which repealed a local law providing for a veteran's exemption from real property taxes was properly dismissed. Local governments have the authority to adopt, amend or supersede laws so long as the "new" legislation is not inconsistent with the provisions of the Constitution or any general law (see, NY Const, art IX, § 3 [b]; Municipal Home Rule Law § 10 [1] [i], [ii] [a] [8], [9]; § 22 [1]), and in light of the similarity of language and purpose of RPTL 458-a (4) (a) and RPTL 458 (5) (a), which authorized local governments to adjust a veteran's exemption in the same proportion as affected by full value assessment, it may be fairly implied that the Legislature similarly intended RPTL 458 (5) (a) to permit local governments to repeal laws enacted pursuant thereto, such as the local law in question, since the Legislature has expressly embraced the concept that localities could reduce or eliminate exemptions created pursuant to RPTL 458-a. Moreover, inasmuch as the local law in question neither prohibits that which State law permits nor permits that which State law forbids, there is no inconsistency with State legislation.

Actions -- Improper Form of Action -- Conversion to Proper Form -- Challenge to Repeal of Tax Exemption

4. A challenge to the constitutionality of a local law repealing a veteran's real property tax exemption should have been brought as a declaratory judgment action. Accordingly, pursuant to CPLR 103 (c), the instant article 78 proceeding is converted to the proper form.

 

493 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Nash v. Assessor of Southampton


Nos. 91-04273 (formerly 602E), 91-04274 (formerly 603E), 91-04275 (formerly 604E)


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


168 A.D.2d 102; 571 N.Y.S.2d 951; 1991 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8396


November 19, 1990, Argued  
June 10, 1991 



DISPOSITION: Ordered the orders and judgments are reversed, on the law, with one bill of costs, and the plaintiffs' respective motions for summary judgment are denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In consolidated actions, appellants, town and assessor, challenged judgments of the Supreme Court of Suffolk County (New York), which found for respondent taxpayers. The town and the assessor argued that the trial court erred in granting summary judgment to the taxpayers because there were factual issues in dispute as to whether the delayed real estate assessment as part of a comprehensive reassessment program was unconstitutional discrimination.


OVERVIEW: The town implemented a comprehensive real estate reassessment program. Delays resulted in the reassessment of some parcels of real estate before others. The taxpayers filed actions against the town and the assessor, claiming that the delayed assessment in some parcels was invidious and unconstitutional discrimination against them, and they claimed refunds for assessments that they had paid. The taxpayers' motions for summary judgment were granted by the trial court. The town and the assessor appealed. In a consolidated action, the court reversed the grants of summary judgment, holding that material facts were in dispute with respect to the constitutional issues raised. A primary question was the nature and extent of the purported inequities. An additional question was how long the inequities would persist. It could not be determined whether any disparate tax treatment arose out of an intentional, invidious, or arbitrary discrimination by the town or the assessor. The record did not reveal whether the town acted in bad faith or that the taxpayers were singled out for selective enforcement of tax laws that applied equally to all similarly situated taxpayers.


OUTCOME: In an action brought by the taxpayers against the town and the assessor, claiming that a comprehensive real estate reassessment program was unconstitutional discrimination against the taxpayers, the court reversed the trial court's grants of summary judgment for the taxpayers.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- Assessment -- Reassessment -- Purported Delay in Completing Comprehensive Reassessment Program -- Constitutionality -- Summary Judgment

In actions seeking a refund of real estate taxes attributable to increased assessments imposed on plaintiffs' properties as a result of a comprehensive reassessment program implemented by defendant town, under which plan every parcel in the town is to be reassessed either when it was sold, subdivided, improved or selected for scrutiny under the town's "land equalization program", summary judgment was improperly granted directing refunds to plaintiffs, since material and unresolved questions of fact exist with respect to whether the purported delay in the complete implementation of the reassessment program amounted to invidious and unconstitutional discrimination against plaintiffs. A tax classification will only violate equal protection guarantees if the distinction between classes is palpably arbitrary. Primary questions requiring resolution are the nature and extent of the purported inequities, how long such inequities will persist, and whether any disparate treatment arose out of an intentional, invidious, or arbitrary discrimination. It cannot be said on the record in this case that defendants acted in bad faith or singled out plaintiffs for selective enforcement of tax laws that apply equally to all similarly situated taxpayers.

 

494 of 992 DOCUMENTS


In re Raquel Marie X.


No. 91-01471 (formerly 2459E)


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


173 A.D.2d 709; 570 N.Y.S.2d 604; 1991 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7877


June 27, 1989, Argued  
May 28, 1991 



DISPOSITION: ORDERED that the order is reversed insofar as appealed from, on the law and the facts, without costs or disbursements, and the matter is remitted to the Family Court, Westchester County, for further proceedings on the petition for adoption in accordance herewith, before a different Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Court of Appeals (New York), reversed the court's order holding that respondent biological father's consent was not necessary to the proposed adoption of his daughter and declared N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 111(1)(e) unconstitutional. On remand, the court was to consider the issue of whether respondent's consent was required under the applicable criteria set out by the appellate court.


OVERVIEW: Three days before the child's mother executed a consent to adoption, respondent biological father commenced a custody proceeding. The family court denied the petition for adoption because respondent's consent had not been obtained. The court reversed and concluded that the cohabitation requirement of N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 111(1)(e) had not been met. The appeals court found § 111(1)(e) unconstitutional and remanded. The court again found that respondent's consent was not required and remanded. The court held that the parental interest asserted by respondent was unworthy of legal protection and rendered his consent to the adoption unnecessary. The court noted that respondent failed to commence proceedings during the pregnancy, that his name was not on the child's birth certificate, and that he consented to the child's placement in foster care. The court found that respondent did not have a genuine desire to assume full custody. Respondent's public acknowledgement of paternity and payment of pregnancy and birth expenses were found inadequate. The court also noted respondent's history of abusive conduct and his failure to financially support his other daughter.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the lower court denying the petition for adoption and remanded. The consent of respondent biological father to the adoption was not necessary. Respondent's interest in the child was not entitled to constitutional protection when the evidence demonstrated a lack of fitness and an unwillingness to accept parental responsibility.

 

495 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Shubert Organization, Inc. v. Landmarks Preservation Com.


No. 42320


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


166 A.D.2d 115; 570 N.Y.S.2d 504; 1991 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7246



May 16, 1991 



DISPOSITION: Order and judgment (one paper), Supreme Court, New York County, entered on February 6, 1990, affirmed, without costs or disbursements.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, theatre owners and trade organizations, sought review of an order from the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), which denied a motion by petitioners for summary judgment, granted summary judgment to respondent municipality, and dismissed the petition and complaint which sought declaratory judgment regarding a landmark ordinance.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, theatre owners and trade organizations, sought to annul the designation of 22 theatres as landmarks, and to declare null and void the underlying landmarks legislation and the anti-demolition provisions of an ordinance. Petitioners challenged an order which denied a motion for summary judgment, granted summary judgment to respondent municipality, and dismissed the petition. On appeal, the court affirmed. It held that the administrative determination about the landmarks was based on substantial evidence, was not arbitrary and capricious, and did not violate the law. The court held that petitioners did not carry their burden of demonstrating that New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § 25(3) as applied to these particular theatres denied petitioners essential use of their property.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed an order granting respondent municipality summary judgment in a case brought by petitioners, theatre owners and trade organizations for declaratory relief regarding theatres that had been designated as landmarks.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations -- Landmarks Preservation -- Designation of Buildings

1. In a proceeding to annul the designation of 22 Broadway theatres as landmarks, the court is circumscribed by the scope of judicial review of administrative determinations, and may not substitute its judgment for that of the appropriate administrative body, viz., the Landmarks Preservation Commission, whose members have particular expertise. Considering the wealth of analyses and reports, as well as anecdotal testimony, provided to the Commission prior to the subject designations, it appears beyond serious challenge that a reasonable basis existed for the designations as to each theatre, upon a consideration of the statutory criteria. There is no basis to argue that the proposals were "railroaded" through the Commission since the preliminary analyses and reports were exhaustive, and the decision making occurred over the course of several years; there is no indication that petitioners were deprived of a meaningful opportunity to be heard prior to the determinations.

Municipal Corporations -- Landmarks Preservation -- Designation of Buildings

2. In a proceeding to annul the designation of 22 Broadway theatres as landmarks, the single roll-call vote of the New York City Board of Estimate approving the recommendations of the Landmarks Preservation Commission was not improper where the Board had received individual reports, as well as planning commission reports, prior to the vote, and where the Board had, since the 1950's, routinely entertained a single vote on similar matters, particularly those involving zoning and landmark designations. Although manipulation of the landmarks law for the purpose of preserving the Broadway theatre industry, rather than individual theatres, would have been improper, the designation proceedings addressed the specific buildings in terms of the criteria of the law. Accordingly, the administrative determination was based on substantial evidence, was not arbitrary and capricious, and did not violate the law.

Municipal Corporations -- Landmarks Preservation -- Constitutionality

3. In a proceeding to annul the designation of 22 Broadway theatres as landmarks, the constitutionality of the landmarks preservation law has previously been upheld and petitioners have not carried their burden of demonstrating that the law as applied to their particular theatres denies them essential use of their property. The law serves a legitimate purpose of saving historical landmarks, and with respect to any claim that petitioners are deprived of any economic use of their property, in the absence of final agency action on applications by petitioners for renovations or alterations, the matter is not ripe for review. There is no prohibition against petitioners receiving economic benefit from continuing to use the buildings as theatres, and transferable development rights inure to the owners of those buildings, which, absent a successful challenge, must be presumed to have economic benefit.

Municipal Corporations -- Landmarks Preservation -- Demolition -- Special Permit

4. In a proceeding to annul the designation of 22 Broadway theatres as landmarks, petitioners' challenge to the provision of the zoning resolution which requires listed theatres to obtain a special permit from the city planning commission for demolition is a zoning, rather than a landmark matter, and petitioners bear the heavy burden of demonstrating the unconstitutionality of the zoning ordinance. Discriminatory effects of a statute will not render it invalid if there exists a reasonable basis, and there is no evidence that the provision in question is not based on a comprehensive plan inasmuch as similar demolition permits are required by the resolution in several other special districts.

Municipal Corporations -- Landmarks Preservation -- Judicial Review -- Hearing

5. In a proceeding to annul the designation of 22 Broadway theatres as landmarks, considering the narrow scope of review available to the Supreme Court, it cannot be said that with the volumes of documentary evidence in the record and supporting affidavits, that there was a need to conduct an evidentiary hearing to examine whether the New York City Board of Estimate's voting mechanism was arbitrary and capricious or in violation of law. Since the court was not empowered to substitute its own judgment for that of the administrative body, the only inquiry was whether the record, as submitted, supported the single roll-call voting procedure and the determination, and it did.

Anderson v. State


No. 61785


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


173 A.D.2d 988; 569 N.Y.S.2d 500; 1991 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7558



May 9, 1991 

 

497 of 992 DOCUMENTS


People v. Maye


No. 57473


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


173 A.D.2d 891; 569 N.Y.S.2d 758; 1991 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 5224



May 2, 1991 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected May 2, 1991. Leave to Appeal Granted August 15, 1991.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed, on the law, and matter remitted to the County Court of Albany County for further proceedings under the March 25, 1986 indictment charging defendant with manslaughter in the second degree.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the County Court of Albany County (New York), which convicted him, upon a jury verdict, of murder in the second degree. Defendant argued that the motion for resubmission to the grand jury was improperly granted, and that his trial for second degree murder subjected him to double jeopardy.


OVERVIEW: The grand jury returned an indictment charging defendant with second degree manslaughter. Defendant pled guilty to the manslaughter charge and successfully appealed the conviction. The People moved to resubmit the case to a grand jury on the charge of second-degree murder because of newly discovered evidence. The lower court granted the motion, and defendant was later convicted of second-degree murder. Defendant appealed his conviction, arguing that the motion for resubmission was improperly granted and that his trial for murder subjected him to double jeopardy. The appellate court reversed the conviction and held that defendant had been unconstitutionally subjected to double jeopardy. The court found that the People initially presented the case to a grand jury on a murder theory and an indictment was returned for manslaughter. The court held that the grand jury clearly rejected the evidence as insufficient for a murder indictment. Defendant was later tried under a different indictment, charging him with a separate and distinct offense from that contained in the original indictment to which he pled guilty, contrary to the express provisions of N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 40.30(3).


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed defendant's conviction for second-degree murder, because defendant had been subjected to double jeopardy. The court found that the People had initially presented its case to the grand jury on a murder theory and an indictment was returned only for manslaughter. Defendant was later tried for murder, after he successfully appealed his conviction on the manslaughter charge, thus subjecting defendant to double jeopardy.

19th Street Assoc. v. State


Nos. 42999, 43000


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


172 A.D.2d 380; 568 N.Y.S.2d 771; 1991 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4978



April 23, 1991 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant State of New York appealed a judgment of Supreme Court, New York County (New York), which denied a motion by the State for dismissal of plaintiff property owners' complaint, converted the motion to one for summary judgment, and declared 1989 N.Y Laws ch. 473 to be illegal, invalid, null, and void.


OVERVIEW: The trial court converted the owners' motion to dismiss into one for summary judgment and declared ch. 473 unconstitutional. The trial court also declared that the owners were not required to offer renewal leases to defendant tenants, declared that the owners had the right to enforce a 1982 consent judgment with the State, and permanently enjoined the attorney general from enforcing ch. 473. In affirming, the court held that (1) ch. 473 violated the owners' right to due process by abrogating a fixed and unreviewable judgment through later legislation; (2) the owners' right to equal protection was violated by imposing a classification whose relationship to the asserted goal of reducing the housing shortage was so attenuated as to render the distinction arbitrary or irrational; (3) ch. 473 functioned as an improper regulatory taking without compensation because it denied the owners the economic value of their property; (4) it was error to conclude that ch. 473 was unconstitutional for lack of a home rule message; (5) ch. 473 was not a physical taking of property without just compensation or a bill of attainder; and (6) ch. 473 did not violate the doctrine of separation of powers.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment denying the State's motion to dismiss the owners' complaint, converted the motion to one for summary judgment, and declared unconstitutional a statute, which had the effect of invalidating a prior consent judgment between the owners and the State.

Deutsch v. Crosson


No. 90-02964


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


171 A.D.2d 837; 567 N.Y.S.2d 773; 1991 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3909


January 22, 1991, Argued  
March 25, 1991 



DISPOSITION: ORDERED that the judgment is modified by deleting the provision thereof which awarded prejudgment interest; as so modified, the judgment is affirmed insofar as appealed from, without costs or disbursements.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff judges instituted an action to have N.Y. Jud. Law §221-e, which set forth the annual salaries to be paid to family court judges, declared unconstitutional in that it violated the judges' rights to equal protection of the laws. Defendant administrator appealed the decision of the Supreme Court, Westchester County (New York), which found that the claims of 17 of the judges were not barred and awarded those judges monetary relief.


OVERVIEW: The state contended that the doctrine of res judicata barred several of the judges' claims and that the monetary relief granted was either improper or should be modified. The court held that res judicata should not be applied to this case because combining this case with another in which the judges were also plaintiffs would not have formed a "convenient trial unit". The court further held that although the Statute of Limitations for the declaratory action at bar was six years, the trial court properly awarded back pay for a period greater than six years because the statute did not circumscribe the number of years for which the ancillary relief of back pay could be awarded. The court concluded that inasmuch as the judges were successful on their constitutional claim, which was cognizable under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983, the trial court properly awarded counsel fees. However, the court concluded that the award of prejudgment interest by the trial court was improper. Accordingly, the court modified the decision so as to delete the award of prejudgment interest and otherwise, the court affirmed the trial court's decision.


OUTCOME: The court modified the decision of the trial court by deleting the provision of the trial court's decision which awarded prejudgment interest in the judges' action. As modified, the court affirmed the trial court's decision awarding the judges back pay.

People v. Poole


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]

171 A.D.2d 1041; 579 N.Y.S.2d 614; 1991 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6773
