501 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Sulzer v. Environmental Control Bd.


No. 41668


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


165 A.D.2d 270; 566 N.Y.S.2d 595; 1991 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2330



February 26, 1991 



DISPOSITION: Order and judgment (one paper), Supreme Court, New York County, entered on December 22, 1989, unanimously modified, on the law, to grant the petition, dismiss the five remaining charges, declare Administrative Code § 10-121 (e) unconstitutional, as applied, and, except as thus modified, affirmed, without costs or disbursements.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner artist appealed from the judgment of the Supreme Court of New York County (New York), which annulled a decision of respondent Environmental Control Board and which had remanded five charges of violating New York City, N.Y., Rules of the City of New York, tit. 10, § 121(e) back to the Environmental Control Board.


OVERVIEW: The artist produced handbills advertising his performances, but he did not post any handbills or give anyone instructions to post the handbills. Before prosecuting people for continuous posting violations, the city had a policy of requiring a second observation of a poster three days after the initial violation. Administratively, the artist was found liable for violating New York City, N.Y., Rules of the City of New York, tit. 10, § 121(e), and a civil penalty was imposed for each violation. The lower court annulled all of the administrative convictions and remanded five back for a new hearing under § 121(e). The court affirmed the lower court with a modification; the court found that the five remaining charges should be dismissed because § 121(e) was unconstitutional as applied. The court ruled that the artist's free speech rights under the First Amendment were violated by holding him responsible for posting when his only connection to the posting was having his names on the posters. The court also held that § 121(e) was unconstitutionally vague because the prohibitions were so unclear that people of common intelligence were required to guess at what was proscribed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's annulment of the charges against the artist. However, the court found that the five remaining charges should be dismissed instead of remanded, and, therefore, the decision below was modified to that extent.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations -- Regulation of Handbill Posting -- Vicarious Liability -- Unconstitutionality of Ordinance as Applied

1. Administrative Code of the City of New York § 10-121 (e), which provides that if handbills are posted on certain public property without permission on a "regular and ongoing basis", then the individual whose name appears thereon shall be deemed to have knowledge of such illegal posting and shall be liable for a civil penalty, is unconstitutional as applied to petitioner, whose name appeared on handbills which were discovered to have been posted without permission, since the only behavior chargeable to petitioner is having his name on posted handbills which he produced and distributed to others -- an activity fully protected by the First Amendment -- without ever instructing them to post the same. To hold petitioner vicariously liable because of someone else's conduct in illegally posting the handbills would offend First Amendment principles and violate due process. If the city wishes to prohibit the posting of handbills without permission, it can pursue its goal by punishing those who actually post the handbills. Mere observance by the distributor of the handbills posted at various locations at some later date does not logically suggest that the distributor took part in the illegal posting.

Municipal Corporations -- Regulation of Handbill Posting -- Constitutionality of Ordinance -- Police Powers

2. A municipal ordinance prohibiting the posting of handbills on certain public property without permission is entitled to a strong presumption of validity, which imposes a heavy burden on anyone challenging its constitutionality. In exercising the power granted by NY Constitution, article IX, § 2 (c) and Municipal Home Rule Law § 10, which vest a city with broad powers in the regulation of the use of its streets and public areas for the general welfare, a city may legitimately place limits on the manner in which public areas are used as a forum for speech and other types of protected expression. Accordingly, a content-neutral antiposting law is an appropriate exercise of municipal police power to regulate aesthetics and promote safety.

Municipal Corporations -- Regulation of Handbill Posting -- Unconstitutionally Vague Ordinance

3. Administrative Code of the City of New York § 10-121 (e), which provides for civil penalties for illegally posting handbills, is unconstitutionally vague since it proscribes a violation that continues "on a regular and ongoing basis". Although a more stringent vagueness test should apply if a law interferes with the right of free speech, greater tolerance is allowed where civil rather than criminal penalties attach, as is the case with the statute in question, because the consequences of imprecision are qualitatively less severe. The law contains no hint as to how much time must pass or what events must occur before a violation becomes "continuing"; furthermore, it is unclear what "regular" adds to the statute. Although statutes should be construed to avoid constitutional problems, and the city does not act against a violator until a handbill has twice been observed, the wait between sightings serves no purpose, since it has no bearing on whether the violator has knowledge that the posting has occurred, given that one may violate the statute by printing and distributing handbills with one's name on them that are illegally posted by others. Moreover, one charged with violating the statute is not given notice during the waiting period.

Municipal Corporations -- Regulation of Handbill Posting -- Unconstitutionally Vague Ordinance

4. Administrative Code of the City of New York § 10-121 (e), which provides that if handbills are posted on certain public property without permission on a "regular and ongoing basis", then the individual whose name appears thereon shall be deemed to have knowledge of such illegal posting and shall be liable for a civil penalty, is unconstitutionally vague since the law seems to make any person whose name appears on the handbill liable, even though that person neither produced, distributed nor knew about the handbill; nor is it clear who is liable if several names are listed. A person of common intelligence cannot know which nonliteral statutory interpretation to follow, and the scope of the law and the discretion it gives to enforcement agents is completely open-ended. The law has the potential for chilling First Amendment freedoms. Moreover, the law may not be deemed valid by construing it as a reasonable regulation of the time, place or manner in which one may engage in protected forms of expression, since the imposition of vicarious liability is unrelated to the time, place or manner of the liable person's conduct.

Municipal Corporations -- Regulation of Handbill Posting -- Unconstitutionally Vague Ordinance -- Overbreadth

5. Administrative Code of the City of New York § 10-121 (e), which provides that if handbills are posted on certain public property without permission on a "regular and ongoing basis", then the individual whose name appears thereon shall be deemed to have knowledge of such illegal posting and shall be liable for a civil penalty, is unconstitutionally vague, and may not be saved as a reasonable regulation directed at commercial speech, since it applies to anyone whose name appears on a posting, commercial or otherwise. Nothing in the statute (see also, Administrative Code of City of New York § 10-119) nor any interpretation by the city indicates that the law is limited to commercial speech. Thus, assuming a regulation such as section 10-121 (e) is proper in the area of commercial speech, it is constitutionally overbroad since it applies to all political, social, cultural or other protected expression found posted in the proscribed areas, and the overbreadth is substantial.

Municipal Corporations -- Regulation of Handbill Posting -- Due Process -- Failure to Charge Violator with Offense for which Ultimately Found Liable

6. Petitioner was improperly found liable of violating Administrative Code of the City of New York § 10-121 (e), which provides that if handbills are posted on certain public property without permission in violation of Administrative Code § 10-119 on a "regular and ongoing basis", then the individual whose name appears thereon shall be deemed to have knowledge of such illegal posting and shall be liable for a civil penalty, where petitioner appealed the finding that he had violated section 10-119 and, on administrative appeal, the charges against petitioner were amended to assert a violation of section 10-121 (e), which creates an offense different from that defined in section 10-119, which renders liable the person who actually did the posting. Section 10-121 contains additional elements and in some respects the penalties differ. The amendment of the charges against petitioner on appeal violated fundamental due process rights, and where there is a denial of the right to be put on notice of the charges made, prejudice will be presumed.

 

502 of 992 DOCUMENTS


People v. De Gaspard


No. 58597


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


170 A.D.2d 835; 566 N.Y.S.2d 667; 1991 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2091



February 21, 1991 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the County Court of Albany County (New York), which convicted him upon his plea of guilty and sentenced him for grand larceny in the second degree and for grand larceny in the third degree.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for grand larceny in the second degree and for grand larceny in the third degree. Defendant's plea agreement was not illegal where it encompassed dismissal of a felony complaint for which defendant had not yet been indicted. It was within the sound discretion of the district attorney to decline to prosecute that offense. Although the plea agreement provided for a sentence beyond the maximum for grand larceny in the second degree as a second felony offender, the trial court corrected the inadvertent misstatement at sentencing. The trial court did not err in refusing defendant a hearing concerning withdrawal of his guilty pleas and his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel where he only proffered conclusory statements of coercion and was afforded sufficient opportunity to protest his counsel during the plea allocution. Defendant was properly treated as a second felony offender where his prior federal felony conviction was proved beyond a reasonable doubt and was comparable to a state felony conviction. The court corrected, however, the sentence imposed on a grand larceny in the third degree conviction to correspond to the plea bargain.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for grand larceny in the second degree and for grand larceny in the third degree because defendant's guilty plea agreement was legal and the trial court did not err in refusing to hear belated claims of coercion and ineffective assistance of counsel. Although defendant was properly treated as a second felony offender, the court corrected his sentence to agree with his plea bargain.

 

503 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Santiago v. Riccio


No. 42173


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


170 A.D.2d 340; 566 N.Y.S.2d 44; 1991 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2108



February 21, 1991 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent, the Commissioner of the New York City Department of Transportation, appealed from an order of the Supreme Court, Bronx County (New York) that reversed the Commissioner's determination not to grant petitioners' request to suspend alternate side of the street parking regulations on St. Patrick's Day and granted the petition.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners filed an application with the Commissioner to suspend alternate side of the street parking in observance of St. Patrick's Day. The Commissioner denied the application, and the trial court reversed. On appeal, the court reversed finding that the trial court was without power to direct the Commissioner to grant petitioners' request because it involved a question of policy that was beyond the scope of judicial review. The court found that the suspension of alternate side parking regulations on designated holidays involved a legislative prerogative upon which courts should not intrude and, further, that other circumstances warranting a suspension of the regulation was an administrative determination that was entrusted by city council to the Commissioner's discretion. The court found that the Commissioner did not abuse his discretion in refusing to extend the suspension of parking restrictions to occasions that were not designated by statute. The court also rejected petitioners' claim that the designation of certain religious holidays in the statute violated the establishment clause and determined that petitioners' remedy was to present their cause before the city council.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order that reversed the Commissioner's denial of the petitioners' application to suspend alternate side of the street parking for one day. The court dismissed the petition.

 

504 of 992 DOCUMENTS


People v. Keta


No. 4480E


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


165 A.D.2d 172; 567 N.Y.S.2d 738; 1991 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2305


April 16, 1990, Argued  
February 19, 1991 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Leave to Appeal Granted March 18, 1991.



DISPOSITION: Ordered that the order is reversed, on the law, the branch of the defendant's omnibus motion which was to suppress physical evidence and statements made by him to law enforcement officials is denied, and the matter is remitted to the Supreme Court, Queens County, for further proceedings consistent herewith.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The people appealed an order of the Supreme Court in Queens County (New York) which granted defendant's motion to suppress evidence, determining that the N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 415-a(5)(a) violated N.Y. Const. art. I, § 12.


OVERVIEW: Defendant owned a vehicle dismantling yard. After a warrantless inspection, police examined defendant's records and found stolen auto parts were not properly inventoried under § 415-a(5)(a). They arrested defendant and charged him with penal law violations. The hearing court determined that § 415-a(5)(a) violated the state constitution. In doing so, it declined to follow the U.S. Supreme Court's conclusion that warrantless inspections were authorized under the statute and were reasonable under the Fourth Amendment. On appeal, the court reversed, upholding the constitutionality of § 415-a(5)(a) because it found no constitutional infirmities. The state could address the major problem of auto theft by both an administrative scheme and through penal sanctions. Neither the applicable policy concerns nor fundamental fairness considerations predominated over the desirability of consistency and uniformity in constitutional construction.


OUTCOME: The order was reversed.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Unlawful Search and Seizure -- Administrative Search -- Constitutionality -- State Constitution -- Automobile Dismantlers

1. Vehicle and Traffic Law § 415-a (5) (a), which provides that vehicle dismantlers must maintain records concerning proof of ownership of motor vehicles and parts which they acquire, and that such records must be made available to authorized inspectors during business hours, does not violate NY Constitution, article I, § 12, which prohibits unreasonable searches and seizures. The statute has previously withstood challenge on Federal constitutional grounds, and the identity of language between the Federal and State Constitutions supports a policy of uniformity in interpretation. Defendant, who has been charged with criminal possession of stolen property, grand larceny and falsifying business records, has failed to show that the right at issue has historically been afforded greater protection in New York than is currently required under the Federal Constitution, that the right is of peculiar State or local concern, or that New Yorkers have "distinctive attitudes" toward that right.

Constitutional Law -- State Constitutional Protections -- Policy-Making Role of Court of Appeals

2. In a criminal prosecution arising out of an administrative search of a vehicle dismantling business (see, Vehicle and Traffic Law § 415-a [5] [a]), the holding of the hearing court, that NY Constitution, article I, § 12 affords defendant greater protection from searches and seizures than the Federal Constitution, impinged upon the policy and rule-making function traditionally perceived as the exclusive domain of the Court of Appeals. Where, as here, "noninterpretative", State-wide policy considerations govern, in part, any inquiry into the existence of enhanced protection by the State Constitution, lower courts must temper their actions with restraint in deference to the Court of Appeals' role as the State's policy-making tribunal; that is particularly so where the hearing court discerns in the State Constitution rights which the Appellate Division has twice declined to recognize when examining the statute in question under the identically worded provision of the Federal Constitution.

Crimes -- Unlawful Search and Seizure -- Administrative Search -- Constitutionality -- State Constitution -- Automobile Dismantlers

3. Vehicle and Traffic Law § 415-a (5) (a), which provides that vehicle dismantlers must maintain records concerning proof of ownership of motor vehicles and parts which they acquire, and that such records must be made available to authorized inspectors during business hours, does not violate NY Constitution, article I, § 12, which prohibits unreasonable searches and seizures. The statute has previously withstood challenge on Federal constitutional grounds, and defendant has not shown that, in upholding the statute, the United States Supreme Court has retreated from previously announced rules, that the right in question has been singled out by the New York Court of Appeals as meriting special protection, as has been done with freedom of speech and right to counsel, or that a separate State rule is justified by concerns peculiar to New Yorkers. The challenged enactment affects only a relatively small number of commercial establishments operating businesses within an industry which the Legislature has found to be influenced by a multimillion-dollar trade in stolen automobiles and parts.

Crimes -- Unlawful Search and Seizure -- Administrative Search -- Constitutionality -- State Constitution -- Automobile Dismantlers

4. Vehicle and Traffic Law § 415-a (5) (a), which requires vehicle dismantlers to maintain and make available for inspection records concerning proof of ownership of motor vehicles and parts which they acquire, and which has already passed Federal constitutional muster, does not violate the State Constitution. No reason has been shown to expand the identical language of the State Constitution to strike down what has been deemed permissible under the Federal Constitution. The United States Supreme Court, in upholding section 415-a (5) (a), did not depart from established principles governing administrative searches, and the statutory scheme at issue, which authorizes searches during business hours, sufficiently defines the scope of any inspection and reasonably limits the discretion of the inspecting officer. The vehicle dismantling industry is pervasively regulated, and other administrative schemes enacted to discourage criminal conduct have been upheld as legitimately regulatory in character.

Crimes -- Unlawful Search and Seizure -- Administrative Search -- Automobile Dismantlers

5. In a prosecution for criminal possession of stolen property, grand larceny, and falsifying business records arising out of an administrative search by police of a vehicle dismantling business operated by defendant, defendant's motion to suppress physical evidence and statements he made to law enforcement officers was improperly granted where the inspectors permissibly requested and examined defendant's "police book" upon arriving at his premises, where they then examined defendant's records to ascertain if any parts had been inventoried in conformity with the regulatory scheme after determining, by random computer check, that certain parts on his premises were listed as stolen, where defendant was arrested only after they determined that parts had not been properly inventoried, and where a search warrant was subsequently obtained pursuant to which a more thorough inspection was conducted. The conduct of the police inspectors amply established the administrative character of the encounter and undermines the suggestion that defendant was subjected to an inspection motivated solely by a desire to uncover evidence of Penal Law violations.

 

505 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Dombroski v. Bloom


No. 61998


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


170 A.D.2d 805; 565 N.Y.S.2d 907; 1991 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 1548



February 14, 1991 



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed, on the law, with costs, and motion denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, police officer and association, appealed a judgment of the Supreme Court in Orange County (New York), which granted a motion by respondents, city and deputy chief, to dismiss petitioners' complaint because they failed to exhaust their administrative remedies before seeking judicial enforcement of their rights.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners filed a complaint alleging that respondents deprived the police officer of his rank of detective when they transferred him to a patrol squad and assigned another officer to his former position. Petitioners further claimed that respondents violated N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 75(1)(e) when they demoted the police officer without a hearing. Respondents filed a motion to dismiss the complaint alleging that petitioners were precluded from seeking judicial relief because they failed to exhaust their administrative remedies under a collective bargaining agreement. The supreme court granted respondents' motion. On appeal, the court reversed. The court held that the grievance and arbitration procedures of the collective bargaining agreement were not intended to supersede the police officer's statutory right to a hearing under N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 75. The court further held that the police officer was not obliged to resort to his remedies under the grievance procedure of the collective bargaining agreement before seeking judicial enforcement of his statutory rights. The court noted that the police officer sought to vindicate a right expressly preserved in the agreement.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment on the law, with costs, and denied respondents' complaint.

 

506 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Islip v. Zalak


No. 4911E


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


165 A.D.2d 83; 566 N.Y.S.2d 306; 1991 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2066


September 24, 1990, Argued  
February 11, 1991 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Revised March 4, 1991.



DISPOSITION: Ordered that the judgment is reversed, on the law, with costs, and the defendant's motion is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff municipality brought an action seeking to enjoin defendant property owner from operating a transfer station/recycling center. The property owner brought a counterclaim seeking a finding that the municipality's transfer station/recycling center permit procedures and zoning regulations were invalid. The Supreme Court in Suffolk County (New York) granted the property owner's motion for summary judgment. The municipality sought review.


OVERVIEW: The property owner argued that portions of Islip, NY Town Code ch. 21, which regulated solid waste disposal, was invalid because it was inconsistent with N.Y. Town Law § 16. The court found no inconsistency between the laws reasoning that Islip, NY Town Code ch. 21 was not a zoning law merely because it touched the use of land. Rather, the court found that ch. 21 should be regarded as having been enacted pursuant to the municipality's specific power to regulate garbage collection contained in N.Y. Town Law § 136(8) and § 130(6). The court found that the property owner's contention that Islip, NY Town Code § 21-6(B)(9) and § 68-338(D) amount to an unconstitutional summary termination of an existing nonconforming use because the municipality had not provided a reasonable amortization period was not ripe for review because the property owner had not exhausted its administrative remedies. The court reasoned that actual harm would occur only if the property owner's currently pending application for a permit was denied. The court found that Islip, NY Town Code § 21-6(B)(9) sufficiently specified the criteria the planning board was to apply in reviewing variance requests.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment and denied the property owner's motion for partial summary judgment.


HEADNOTES

Statutes -- General or Local Laws -- Inconsistencies

1. An order and judgment of the Supreme Court, which declared that certain local laws regulating the disposal of garbage were inconsistent with a State law of general applicability and that the locality in question had failed to comply with the formalities which are required whenever a local government purports to enact a law which is inconsistent with a State law (see, Municipal Home Rule Law § 22), should be reversed where there is no inconsistency. In accordance with its exercise of its police powers, a locality may regulate, or even ban, places where garbage is brought, dumped, sorted, and processed (see, NY Const, art IX, § 2 [c] [ii] [10]; Town Law § 136 [8]; § 130 [6]; ECL 27-0701 [2]; 27-0711). Consequently, even if the provisions of the local laws in question providing for review and approval by the town Planning Board were inconsistent with the Town Law, it would not follow that the substantive requirements concerning permits and two-acre minimum lot sizes pertaining to garbage disposal sites were similarly invalid.

Municipal Corporations -- Regulation of Garbage Disposal Centers -- Local Law Not Zoning Law

2. A local law which regulates garbage disposal sites was not enacted pursuant to Town Law § 261 and, therefore, it is not what is commonly referred to as a "zoning" law. While the local law contains provisions which require, for example, a certain minimum lot size for the sites, that alone does not transform the local law into one governed by Town Law article 16 where it is clear from a reading of the local law as a whole that it is intended to regulate a particular occupation, rather than regulate the general uses of land. Inasmuch as there are enabling statutes which specifically authorize town boards to regulate the collection and dumping of garbage (see, Town Law § 130 [6]; § 136 [8]) and to establish agencies for the enforcement of such laws, the town need not rely on the general zoning provisions of Town Law article 16 as the source of its power either to enact laws governing garbage processing sites or to create administrative agencies responsible for the enforcement of such laws. Since the town need not regard article 16 as the source of its power to regulate defendant's property, on which a garbage processing operation is located, it is not bound by the procedural aspects of that article.

Municipal Corporations -- Zoning -- Procedures -- Regulation of Garbage Disposal Centers

3. A town must comply with State statutory zoning procedures only when its local laws represent an exercise of general "zoning" powers, rather than an exercise of a more specific "police" power. A local law which regulates the operation of garbage disposal centers and applies wherever such activity is carried out in the town, is not a zoning law merely because it touches the use of land. The local law does not suspend or limit the application of the town's zoning laws, nor does it curtail the powers of the town's Zoning Board of Appeals. Accordingly, the delegation to the town Planning Board of the power to vary the standards set forth in the local law is not inconsistent with the delegation to the zoning board of appeals of the power to vary the standards set forth in that portion of the town code which expressly deals with zoning, because the local law regulating garbage disposal is not a zoning law. Inasmuch as the town did not attempt to supersede State law by enacting an inconsistent local law, compliance with Municipal Home Rule Law § 22 was not required.

Appeal -- Grant of Relief to Nonappealing Party -- Challenge to Local Law Regulating Garbage Disposal Centers

4. In an action commenced by a town to enjoin, pursuant to a local law, defendants from operating a garbage disposal center on their property, acceptance of defendants' argument on appeal with respect to their alleged right to continue a nonconforming use would not warrant the relief granted by Supreme Court, to wit, a declaration that the local law is invalid, which was the only relief expressly requested by defendants in their papers for summary judgment and which is the only relief expressly requested by defendants on appeal. Inasmuch as acceptance of defendant's argument would warrant relief altogether different from that which was explicitly requested in defendants' notice of motion, and altogether different from that which was granted by Supreme Court, it is not an argument which may serve as an alternative ground for affirmance of the order and judgment appealed from, since acceptance of that argument would require the granting of affirmative relief to defendants, who are nonappealing parties.

Municipal Corporations -- Regulation of Garbage Disposal Centers -- Judicial Review of Ordinance -- Ripeness

5. A challenge to a local law which regulates garbage disposal centers and which allegedly amounts to an unconstitutional summary termination of an existing nonconforming use because the locality has not provided a reasonable amortization period, is not ripe for judicial review where the challengers' application for a permit under the ordinance is still pending. Accordingly, the challengers have not yet suffered an actual, concrete injury. A claim that a local government's land use laws are unconstitutional as applied, in that they constitute an illegal "taking" of property without just compensation, is not ripe for judicial review until administrative relief has been sought and denied. Inasmuch as the granting of administrative relief would render the complaining landowner's arguments moot, judicial intervention should be reserved until after the variance has been denied. Given the lack of actual harm, which may never occur, no justiciable controversy has been presented.

Municipal Corporations -- Regulation of Garbage Disposal Centers -- Judicial Review of Ordinance -- Failure to Exhaust Administrative Remedies

6. A challenge to a local law which regulates garbage disposal centers and which allegedly amounts to an unconstitutional summary termination of an existing nonconforming use because the locality has not provided a reasonable amortization period, is not judicially reviewable where the challengers' administrative remedies have not been exhausted. The challengers' claim is premature. The failure to exhaust administrative remedies may pose a bar to contending that a legislative enactment is unconstitutional as applied. Exhaustion of administrative remedies is not required where the legislation is challenged on grounds of facial invalidity.

Municipal Corporations -- Regulation of Garbage Disposal Centers -- Invalid Delegation of Legislative Power to Planning Board

7. A challenge to a local law which regulates garbage disposal centers and which allegedly constitutes an invalid delegation of legislative powers to a town Planning Board is without merit where the local law contains an adequate statement in "general terms" of the appropriate standards governing the planning board's exercise of its power in reviewing variance requests. The legislation which establishes a minimum lot size states that that requirement will be based on the scale of operation as measured by the size of buildings proposed for a site, the volume of solid waste handled on a daily basis, and the needs for vehicle parking and movement, or a finding that the operation cannot be adequately screened from adjoining property. Accordingly, those conditions which are to govern the Planning Division's exercise of its power to require a greater lot size are also those which should govern the Board's power to authorize a lesser lot size.

 

507 of 992 DOCUMENTS


In re Jessie C.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


164 A.D.2d 731; 565 N.Y.S.2d 941; 1991 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 1651



February 1, 1991  
February 1, 1991, Filed



DISPOSITION: Order unanimously reversed, as a matter of discretion in the interest of justice, without costs, and petition dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent minor sought review of an order of the Family Court of Monroe County (New York), which found the minor to be a juvenile delinquent and placed him in custody for a period of 12 months. The family court had rejected the minor's motion to dismiss the amended petition. Petitioner presentment agency had filed the amended petition against the minor, alleging that he had violated N.Y. Penal Law § 130.20(1).


OVERVIEW: The presentment agency filed an amended petition against the minor. The amended petition alleged that the minor, a 13-year old male, was a juvenile delinquent in that he engaged in sexual intercourse with a 15-year-old female, and that such act, if committed by an adult, constituted the crime of sexual misconduct in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 130.20(1). The family court rejected the minor's motion to dismiss the amended petition, found the minor to be a juvenile delinquent, and placed him in custody for a period of 12 months. The court reversed the family court's order and dismissed the petition. The court held that (1) although § 130.20(1) was unconstitutionally under-inclusive in violation of the Equal Protection Clause, the appropriate remedy was to gender-neutralize the statute and the minor's successful constitutional challenge to § 130.20(1) did not require reversal of his conviction; and that (2) nevertheless the family court's order should have been reversed in the interest of justice, as the parties had stipulated that no force was employed, the minor was only 13 years old at the time, and the minor was no more at fault for his conduct than the female participant.


OUTCOME: With respect to the presentment agency's amended petition regarding the minor's act of sexual misconduct in violation of a state statute, the court reversed the family court's order that found the minor to be a juvenile delinquent and placed him in custody for a period of 12 months. The court also dismissed the petition.


HEADNOTES

Infants -- Juvenile Delinquents -- Sexual Misconduct -- Gender-Based Discrimination

1. In a juvenile delinquency proceeding in which the petition alleged that respondent, a 13-year-old male, engaged in unforced sexual intercourse with a 15-year-old female in violation of Penal Law § 130.20 (1), which defines the crime of sexual misconduct and discriminates on the basis of sex by providing that only a male can commit the crime, petitioner presentment agency has failed to satisfy its burden of demonstrating that the discriminatory treatment is substantially related to the achievement of an important governmental objective and, hence, not violative of constitutional guarantees of equal protection, where the purported objective is the prevention of teen-age pregnancies, since a female of any age can be a victim. Accordingly, the statute is not specifically related to preventing teen-age pregnancies. Assuming that the prevention of early pregnancy is the governmental objective, petitioner has failed to demonstrate that that goal can be better served by a gender-based law than by a gender-neutral law. Insofar as nonforcible sexual intercourse is concerned, petitioner has failed to present any cogent reason why deterring only the male would further the prevention of pregnancy better than imposing a sanction upon both participants.

Crimes -- Sexual Misconduct -- Gender-Based Discrimination -- Unconstitutional Underinclusiveness of Statute -- Remedy

2. Although Penal Law § 130.20 (1), which provides that the crime of sexual misconduct can be committed only by a male, is unconstitutionally underinclusive, striking the statute is not the proper remedy. The court's task is to discern what the Legislature would have done had it foreseen the court's conclusions as to underinclusiveness. Upon a review of recent legislative activity, it must be concluded that, given a choice between striking the gender bias or striking the statute in its entirety, the Legislature, as revealed by its 1987 action gender-neutralizing the rape statutes, would opt to gender-neutralize sexual misconduct. Therefore, the appropriate remedy is to strike the gender exemption and preserve the statute. Consequently, in a juvenile delinquency proceeding arising out of the nonforcible sexual intercourse between respondent, a 13-year-old male, and a 15-year-old female, respondent's constitutional challenge, albeit successful, does not require reversal of his conviction.

Infants -- Juvenile Delinquents -- Sexual Misconduct -- Respondent as Victim

3. In a juvenile delinquency proceeding in which respondent was found to have committed an act which, if committed by an adult, would constitute sexual misconduct (Penal Law § 130.20 [1]), reversal is not required upon the ground that respondent, a 13-year-old male, was incapable as a matter of law of committing sexual misconduct. Although victims under the age of 17 are deemed incapable of consenting to sexual intercourse (see, Penal Law § 130.05 [3] [a]), and cannot be subjected to penal sanctions for their conduct, respondent is not a victim. Respondent is charged with committing nonforcible sexual intercourse with a 15-year-old female, and his consent is not an essential element of the crime.

Infants -- Juvenile Delinquents -- Sexual Misconduct -- Reversal of Delinquency Finding in Interest of Justice

4. A Family Court order adjudicating respondent a juvenile delinquent upon a finding that he committed an act which, if committed by an adult, would constitute sexual misconduct (Penal Law § 130.20 [1]), should be reversed in the interest of justice where the parties have stipulated that no force was employed and that respondent was only 13 years old at the time. The female participant, who was two years older, could not be charged as the perpetrator by reason of the gender bias expressed in the sexual misconduct statute, which provides that only males can commit the crime. Respondent's act amounted to a crime only because the female could not consent by reason of her age. Accordingly, respondent was no more at fault factually for his conduct than the female participant, and his adjudication as a delinquent solely by reason of a gender-based statute was manifestly unfair.

 

508 of 992 DOCUMENTS


In re Stephen C.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


170 A.D.2d 1035; 566 N.Y.S.2d 178; 1991 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 1782



February 1, 1991, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent father sought review of an order of the Wayne County Family Court (New York), which ruled that N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 111(e) applied to the father such that a surrender of his child for adoption by the child's birth mother, under N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 384, was approved against the father.


OVERVIEW: When the father learned that a pregnancy had occurred and that a child had been born but was in foster care, he sought custody. Although the father did not want the child adopted, he told a caseworker that he did not feel he could raise the child himself and that he wanted his mother or sister to raise the child. Neither the sister nor the mother communicated a willingness to take the child. The father indicated that his "pride" made him want to keep the child. At the time of the hearing on the birth mother's surrender of the child for adoption, the father was in jail awaiting sentencing on a criminal charge. The family court applied N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 111(e) to the case, approving the birth mother's surrender of the child against the father. While the father's appeal was pending, § 111(e) was declared unconstitutional. In affirming the order approving the surrender of the child, the court held that the family court had properly enforced the birth mother's surrender of the child against the father, as he had never indicated a willingness to raise the child.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the family court's order, which enforced the birth mother's surrender of the child for adoption against the father.

 

509 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Fiore v. Oakwood Plaza Shopping Center, Inc.


No. 41213


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


164 A.D.2d 737; 565 N.Y.S.2d 799; 1991 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 845



January 31, 1991 



DISPOSITION: Order, Supreme Court, New York County, entered September 8, 1989, unanimously affirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff sellers filed an action and obtained cognovit judgments against defendant buyers on a purchase-money mortgage and bond and warrant in Pennsylvania. The buyers appealed from an order of the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), which granted the sellers' motion for summary judgment in lieu of complaint, and denied the cross motion of one of the buyers to dismiss the action.


OVERVIEW: The mortgage contract provided for a confession of judgment. The sellers had received notice of the cognovit judgments and belatedly attempted to challenge them in the Pennsylvania action. The court affirmed summary judgment in favor of the sellers and held that (1) there was no bar to the trial court's consideration of the sellers' claim for full faith and credit enforcement of a foreign judgment upon the bond and warrant, (2) N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3213 authorized a motion for summary judgment in lieu of complaint, and (3) under circumstances where the parties were engaged in an arm's length business transaction, were represented by counsel, and the buyers were on actual notice of the entry of the judgments and, in fact, sought to challenge same prior to execution, the cognovit judgments did not amount to a deprivation of property rights without due process.


OUTCOME: The judgments in favor of the sellers were affirmed with costs.


HEADNOTES

Judgments -- Summary Judgment -- Motion for Summary Judgment in Lieu of Complaint

Plaintiffs, who commenced this action for payment of money only alleged to be due and owing under a bond and warrant representing a second mortgage and entered into in conjunction with an agreement of sale and purchase-money mortgage related to the purchase by defendants from plaintiffs of land in Pennsylvania, and obtained cognovit judgments in Pennsylvania courts based upon the purchase-money mortgage and bond and warrant against defendants, are entitled to judgment upon this motion for summary judgment in lieu of complaint. While the bond and warrant, insofar as it incorporated other documents related to the parties' real estate transaction, failed to set forth a liquidated sum and did not qualify as an "instrument for the payment of money only" (CPLR 3213), there was no bar to consideration of plaintiffs' claim for full faith and credit enforcement of a foreign judgment upon the bond and warrant. CPLR 3213 authorizes a motion for summary judgment in lieu of complaint in an action "upon an instrument for the payment of money only or upon any judgment". That plaintiffs sought judgment on a note was set forth in their notice of motion pursuant to CPLR 3213, and while plaintiffs for the first time in their reply claimed reliance upon the Pennsylvania judgments, defendants availed themselves of an opportunity to oppose the claim in their surreply. Hence, consideration of plaintiffs' full faith and credit claim resulted in no prejudice to a substantial right of defendants. Similarly, upon consideration of the facts, the Pennsylvania cognovit judgment on the bond and warrant should be accorded full faith and credit. The Pennsylvania procedure at issue herein provides for the opportunity to vacate a cognovit judgment and to date, defendants have failed to do so. Under the circumstances, where the parties were engaged in an arm's length business transaction, were represented by counsel, and the defendant was on actual notice of the judgment and, in fact, sought to challenge same prior to execution, it cannot be said that the cognovit judgment amounted to a deprivation of property rights without due process.

 

510 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Ba Mar, Inc. v. County of Rockland


Nos. 90-01634, 90-04671


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


164 A.D.2d 605; 566 N.Y.S.2d 298; 1991 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 1023


September 25, 1990, Argued  
January 22, 1991 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Revised March 18, 1991.



DISPOSITION: Ordered that the order in action No. 1 is modified, on the law, by deleting the provision thereof denying that branch of the defendant's cross motion which was for a partial summary judgment dismissing the plaintiff's second cause of action, and substituting therefor a provision granting that branch of the cross motion; as so modified, the order is affirmed, with [***2] costs to the plaintiffs, and the matter is remitted to the Supreme Court, Rockland County, for the entry of an appropriate judgment; and it is further, Ordered that the judgment in action No. 2 is reversed, on the law, with costs, and it is declared that Local Laws, 1989, No. 1 of the County of Putnam, as amended by Local Laws, 1989, No. 9 of the County of Putnam, is invalid.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county sought review of a judgment from the Supreme Court, Rockland County (New York), which held appellant county's local law enhancing mobile home owners' protections was preempted and inconsistent with N.Y. Real Prop. Law § 233. Conversely, appellant park operators sought review of a judgment from the Supreme Court, Putnam County, which upheld respondent county's local law enhancing the protections for mobile home owners.


OVERVIEW: After appellant county enacted a local law shifting in favor of tenants the balance between mobile home park operators and tenants, respondent park operators brought suit, claiming appellant county's local law was preempted and inconsistent with N.Y. Real Prop. Law § 233. In a separate action, appellant park operators also claimed respondent county's local law was preempted and inconsistent with § 233. After appellant county's local law was found to be preempted and inconsistent with § 233, but respondent county's local law was not found to be preempted and inconsistent, appellant county and appellant park operators sought review. Upon consolidating the actions, the court affirmed and reversed, holding that both local laws were preempted by and inconsistent with § 233. Even though § 233 lacked express language preempting the field of mobile home legislation, the court found preemption based on § 233's comprehensive regulatory scheme and the variety of state laws regulating mobile homes, notwithstanding § 233's reference to existing local laws. Moreover, the court held the local laws had been inconsistent with § 233 because the local laws had inhibited § 233's operation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed a judgment holding appellant county's local law that enhanced mobile home owners' protections was preempted by and inconsistent with state law and reversed a judgment upholding respondent county's local law which enhanced protections for mobile home owners because the state had intended to occupy the entire field of mobile home regulation and both local laws had inhibited the operation of state law.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and Tenant -- Mobile Home Parks -- Regulation -- State Preemption

1. Local laws regulating, inter alia, the eviction of mobile home owners and alienation of mobile homes are invalid since the State, by enacting Real Property Law § 233, which comprehensively regulates mobile home parks, has preempted the field of mobile home park regulation. While there is no express provision in section 233 indicating a desire by the Legislature to preempt the field of mobile home legislation, it is concluded that the State intended to preempt the area by enacting a comprehensive and detailed regulatory scheme covering nearly every aspect of mobile home park life. The enforcement provisions of the statute evidence a clear intent to provide State-wide uniformity. Moreover, section 233 is not the only statute regulating mobile home parks and mobile homes. The fact that the statute refers to existing local laws does not reflect a lack of preemption since the State's reference to local regulations merely establishes an adequate means of insuring that ancillary concerns such as safety, fire, and health standards are met, unobtrusive to State goals.

Landlord and Tenant -- Mobile Home Parks -- Regulation -- Inconsistency of Local Laws with State Laws

2. Local Laws regulating, inter alia, the eviction of mobile home owners and the alienation of mobile homes are invalid, since there are numerous inconsistencies between the local laws and Real Property Law § 233, which comprehensively regulates such parks. Overall, the local laws significantly seek to shift, in favor of the tenant, the balance established by the Legislature between owner/operator and tenant. There are numerous provisions in the local laws at issue which are identical with the State Law leading to the conclusion that the local laws, were specifically designed to inhibit the operation of the State's general laws where they differ.

Appeal -- Academic and Moot Questions -- Regulation of Mobile Home Parks -- Inverse Condemnation

3. In an action seeking a judgment declaring invalid local laws regulating mobile home parks, any claim by plaintiff park owner for subsequent damages arising out of an inverse condemnation allegedly resulting from the diminished value of his land has been rendered moot by the judicial declaration of the law's invalidity. To the extent such a claim existed, plaintiff failed to raise any triable issue of fact that the enactment of the local law constituted a taking of property. While the law requires the continuance and renewal of site leases, it made no effort to restrict rent increases and, thus, tenants were not given any economic interest in their site which could be marketed to others. Consequently, no taking occurred.

 

511 of 992 DOCUMENTS


People v. Adams


Nos. 2464E, 2464AE


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


164 A.D.2d 546; 565 N.Y.S.2d 821; 1991 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 464


June 11, 1990, Submitted  
January 14, 1991 



DISPOSITION: Ordered that the judgment and the order are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment from the Supreme Court of Kings County (New York), which convicted defendant of attempted murder, assault, criminal possession of a weapon, and which sentenced defendant as a second felony offender. Defendant challenged the sufficiency of the evidence and his adjudication as a second felony offender based on a prior Canadian conviction.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of attempted murder, assault, criminal possession of a weapon, and was sentenced as a second felony offender. The court affirmed the conviction. The court held that there was legally sufficient evidence to support defendant's conviction, including testimony by a witness that defendant had confessed to him that defendant knew he was shooting at a police officer. The court also held that defendant was properly adjudicated a second felony offender based on a prior Canadian conviction. The court held that the trial court did not err when it looked at defendant's Canadian arrest report after determining that the Canadian crime of breaking and entering was broader than the New York crime of burglary in the third degree. The court held that because violation of the Canadian statute could take place in several different ways, the trial court properly looked at the arrest report to ascertain the specific criminal act committed by defendant.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for attempted murder, assault, criminal possession of a weapon, and defendant's sentence as a second felony offender. The court held that sufficient evidence supported the conviction and that defendant was properly adjudicated a second felony offender based on a prior Canadian conviction.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Murder -- Attempted Murder -- Sufficiency of Evidence

1. In a criminal prosecution arising out of a drug "buy and bust" operation, the evidence was legally sufficient to support defendant's conviction of attempted murder in the second degree, assault in the first degree and criminal possession of a weapon in the second degree, where the evidence showed that a police officer, wearing his shield around his neck, entered a bar and announced that he was a police officer; that the officer was shot by an unseen gunman; that the officer was hospitalized for 14 days owing to his wounds; that a doctor stated that the officer's injuries were readily capable of causing death, and that defendant, while incarcerated, admitted to a fellow inmate that he was the gunman and that he knew he was shooting at a policeman. Although defendant contends that the testimony of the inmate was incredible, resolution of issues of credibility, as well as the weight to be accorded to the evidence presented, are primarily questions to be determined by the finder of fact, who saw and heard the witnesses, and upon the exercise of its factual review power, the court is satisfied that the verdict was not against the weight of the evidence. The inmate's testimony was corroborated by that of a police officer who stated that a revolver was discovered near the bar in the condition as described by defendant to the inmate, and by that of a police ballistics expert who stated that the revolver was the weapon used to shoot the officer.

Crimes -- Appeal -- Preservation  of Issue for Appellate Review

2. In a criminal prosecution in which defendant was convicted of, inter alia, attempted murder and sentenced as a second felony offender, defendant failed to preserve for appellate review on the direct appeal from his judgment of conviction his argument that the Canadian crime for which he was convicted and which served as a predicate felony for sentencing purposes did not constitute a felony in New York and that that conviction was unconstitutionally obtained. Although defendant did raise those issues when he moved to vacate his sentence, he has waived that claim by failing to present any evidence in support of his bald statement that the prior Canadian conviction was unconstitutionally obtained (CPL 400.21 [7] [b]).

Crimes -- Sentence -- Second Felony Offender -- Burden of Proof

3. In a criminal prosecution in which defendant was sentenced as a second felony offender, with the predicate felony being a Canadian conviction for breaking and entering with intent to commit an indictable offense therein, the court did not improperly place the burden on defendant to show, in connection with his CPL 440.20 motion, that the Canadian crime was not the equivalent of the New York crime of burglary in the third degree, since the party challenging the validity of his sentence bears the burden of coming forth with allegations sufficient to create an issue of fact.

Crimes -- Sentence -- Second Felony Offender -- Examination of Foreign Accusatory Instrument

4. In a criminal prosecution in which defendant was sentenced as a second felony offender, with the predicate felony being a Canadian conviction for breaking and entering with intent to commit an indictable offense therein, the court did not err in looking past the Canadian statute and at the defendant's Canadian arrest report, which is analogous to an accusatory instrument, once it had been determined that the Canadian crime was broader than the New York crime of burglary in the third degree, since the Canadian crime could be committed in several ways, some of which would constitute felonies in New York and others of which would constitute misdemeanors. The court was not looking at the accusatory instrument in order to expand the crime charged; rather, it was examining the essential operative allegations comprising the charge actually preferred against defendant in order to ascertain the elements of the crime of which he was convicted. Inasmuch as the Canadian arrest report indicated that defendant broke into a jewelry store and that his conviction was based on that crime, the court properly relied on that document in sentencing defendant as a second felony offender.

 

512 of 992 DOCUMENTS


John E. v. Doe


Nos. 90-01332, 90-09805


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


164 A.D.2d 375; 564 N.Y.S.2d 439; 1990 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 16078


October 1, 1990, Argued  
December 24, 1990 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Revised January 4, 1991.



DISPOSITION: Ordered that the appeal from the order entered November 17, 1989, is dismissed, without costs or disbursements, as that order was superseded by the order entered January 10, 1990, made upon reargument; and it is further, Ordered that the order entered January 10, 1990, is affirmed, without costs or disbursements.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner natural father sought review of the judgment of the Family Court, Rockland County (New York), which first ordered the dismissal of natural father's petition against respondents adoptive parents for custody of his minor child and to stay the adoption proceedings, and then which, in a separate order after reargument, affirmed the first order.


OVERVIEW: A child was born out of wedlock, and was turned over to adoptive parents a few days after his birth as part of a prearranged adoption. Natural father filed a petition for custody, a determination of paternity, and a stay of the adoption proceedings. The trial court conducted a hearing concentrated on the best interests of the child and dismissed the custody petition and directed the adoption proceeding to continue. The trial court held a reargument and affirmed its initial decision. On appeal, the court held that the natural father failed to establish that his consent was required for the adoption to proceed and that his interest in his child deserved constitutional protection because his interest was neither sufficiently prompt nor sufficiently substantial. The natural father's interest in his child was only an effort to block the adoption, but not a willingness to assume custody himself.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the appeal as to the first order entered by the trial court and affirmed the second order entered by the trial court.


HEADNOTES

Adoption -- Consent -- Consent of Natural Father

An order of the Family Court, which dismissed a custody proceeding brought by a child's natural father and directed that the adoption of the child in question proceed, is affirmed where the child's biological parents were never married to each other, where the natural father did not live with the birth mother for a continuous period of six months immediately preceding placement of the child for adoption, and where the child was turned over to the prospective adoptive parents four days after his birth, and has lived with them ever since. The consent of the natural father is not required for the adoption of the child herein.

 

513 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Glod v. Morrill Press Div. of Engraph, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


168 A.D.2d 954; 564 N.Y.S.2d 905; 1990 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 16508



December 21, 1990  
December 21, 1990, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff injured worker sought review of an order from the Supreme Court for Oswego County (New York), which, in an action for injuries sustained as a result of exposure to chemicals and alleged strict products liability, negligence, breach of contract, breach of express and implied warranties, and various statutory violations, dismissed the action as being time barred and for failure to state a cause of action.


OVERVIEW: On review the court found that under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 214-c(2), the three-year period within which an action had to be commences should have been computed from the date of the discovery of the injury by the worker or from the date when, through the exercise of reasonable diligence, such injury should have been discovered by the worker, whichever was earlier. The court held that there was no constitutional impediment to legislation that shortened a period of limitations, provided that a party had a reasonable time to have commenced the action under the shortened period. The court found that the worker had 21 months after the statute was enacted to commence suit. The court held that the supreme court erred in concluding the date the worker should have discovered his injury, holding that a determination of constructive discovery was a mixed question of law and fact. The court held that where it did not conclusively appear that the worker had knowledge the question should have been left to the trier of fact. The court held that the statutory violation cause should not have been dismissed because defendant manufacturers neither answered nor requested a bill of particulars.


OUTCOME: The court reinstated the products liability and negligence actions because the question of constructive discovery was for the trier of fact and reinstated the cause for statutory violations because defendant failed to either answer or request a bill of particulars.

Fine v. Commissioner of Dep't of Consumer Affairs

515 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Long Lake Energy Corp. v. New York State Dep't of Envtl. Conservation


No. 61143


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


164 A.D.2d 396; 563 N.Y.S.2d 871; 1990 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 14817


October 16, 1990, Argued  
December 6, 1990 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected December 11, 1990.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed, on the law, without costs, determinations confirmed and petition dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent corporation sought review of an order of the Supreme Court in Albany County (New York), which granted appellant applicant's request to annul the corporation's determinations and directed it to issue the applicant a certificate to construct hydroelectric power plants.


OVERVIEW: The applicant tried to obtain a license to construct four hydroelectric power plants. It submitted applications to the corporation in order to get certification. The corporation sent the applicant letters stating that it needed additional information. The applicant did not respond to the letters, and it was denied certification. The court reversed the lower court's order and held that the corporation's determinations were rationally based. The corporation was concerned about the site-specific water quality impacts. Its letters indicated that a review was in progress, and more information was required to complete the review. The corporation's study was not arbitrary or capricious. The components of water quality as expressed in the Clear Water Act, 33 U.S.C. § 1251 et seq., included the factors upon which more information was requested and to which the applicant failed to respond.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order annulling the corporation's determination and directing it to issue the applicant a license to construct a hydroelectric power plant.


HEADNOTES

Environmental Conservation -- Water Quality Certification -- Hydroelectric Power Projects -- Limited Role of Department of Environmental Conservation -- Components Comprising Determination of Water Quality Standards

1. The State Department of Environmental Conservation (En Con), acting upon an application for State water quality certification of four hydroelectric projects as a prerequisite to the issuance of a Federal license therefor under the Federal Water Pollution Control Act (see, 33 USC § 1251 et seq.), is solely limited to determining whether applicable water quality standards will be met in both the construction and operation of the projects; En Con is not empowered to base its decision on a balancing of the need for the project against the adverse environmental impact. Accordingly, in a proceeding to review En Con's denial of such certifications, the determinations are confirmed and the petition dismissed since such determinations were made in accordance with law, were not arbitrary and capricious, and had a rational basis where the factors listed in the letters denying petitioner's applications, i.e., "turbidity and temperature change", are clearly matters affecting water quality. En Con expressed its concern to petitioner about "site specific water quality impacts, i.e. temperature and summertime dissolved oxygen", as well as other technical matters, and requested that petitioner submit additional data, which requests petitioner ignored (see, ECL 17-0301; 6 NYCRR parts 701, 704). Consequently, it is clear that En Con had not finally found that the projects would not have an adverse impact upon water quality so as to require its certification.

Administrative Law -- Judicial Review -- Nonfinal Denial of Applications -- Raising Defense for First Time on Appeal

2. In a proceeding to review determinations of the State Department of Environmental Conservation (En Con) denying petitioner's request for water quality certification for four hydroelectric projects, although En Con's contention that Supreme Court lacked jurisdiction to review the denials since they were nonfinal and thus not ripe for judicial review (see, CPLR 7801 [1]) was not raised before or reviewed by Supreme Court, the Appellate Division shall recognize the exception to the general rule prohibiting matters being raised for the first time on appeal when the challenge is made upon jurisdictional objections. While no administrative hearings were conducted, En Con's decision to deny the four applications, albeit without prejudice pending the receipt of additional requested data, had its impact upon petitioner at that point and was final and binding. Other than by reapplication de novo, there was no way for petitioner to prevent or significantly ameliorate the certainty and immediacy of harm by further administrative action. En Con had actually denied the requests for the certificates, which would trigger Federal action, thereby placing petitioner's Federal license application at risk, and giving rise to an immediate, actual, and concrete injury to petitioner.

 

516 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Rent Stabilization Ass'n v. Higgins


Nos. 41330, 41331


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


164 A.D.2d 283; 562 N.Y.S.2d 962; 1990 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 14744



December 4, 1990 



DISPOSITION: Order, Supreme Court, New York County, entered April 20, 1990, reversed, on the law, the facts and in the exercise of discretion, and the injunction vacated, with costs. Appeal from order of said court entered on April 10, 1990, is dismissed as moot, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant state proposed an emergency rule extending lease succession rights to non-traditional family members of leaseholders. Plaintiff landlords of rent stabilized buildings brought a declaratory judgment action and obtained a preliminary injunction against the implementation of the rule. The injunction was extended by the New York Supreme Court to cover permanent regulations issued by the state. The state appealed.


OVERVIEW: The court found that the trial court erred in granting a preliminary injunction against the implementation of the state's rule. A party's entitlement to a preliminary injunction required that the party had a likelihood of success on the merits. It was unlikely that the landlords would succeed on the merits of the declaratory judgment action seeking to hold the rule expanding the definition of "family" invalid. The expanded definition of "family," which was originally propagated by the court, had been adopted for rent controlled residences, so it was anomalous for rent stabilization rules to hold the definition was incorrect. The expanded protection of residents was desirable. The statute was not impermissibly vague because it provided information by which a person of ordinary intelligence could make a determination as to whether the residents were a "family." The laws did not unconstitutionally impinge on the landlords' property rights, because it did not expand the occupancy of the residences beyond a family, so the residences were not removed form the landlords' control. Finally, the laws did not constitute a taking because occupancy was limited to the life of the family.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order, enjoining, on the suit by the landlords, the state's expanded lease succession rule, which included the expansion of the definition of "family" for rent stabilized residences.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and Tenant -- Rent Regulation -- Administrative Regulations Governing Lease Succession Rights and Antieviction Protections Set Out by Court of Appeals -- Preliminary Injunction

1. A preliminary injunction enjoining the implementation of permanent regulations issued by the State Division of Housing and Community Renewal (DHCR) which codified the Court of Appeals decision in Braschi v Stahl Assocs. Co. (74 NY2d 201) holding that the term "family" as used in the New York City Rent and Eviction Regulations (9 NYCRR 2204.6 [d]) includes "two adult lifetime partners whose relationship is long term and characterized by an emotional and financial commitment and interdependence", and a subsequent Appellate Division decision ( East 10th St. Assocs. v Estate of Goldstein, 154 AD2d 142), extending the Braschi decision from rent control to rent stabilization, should be vacated since there is little likelihood that plaintiff will succeed on the merits. The regulations are a response to a recognized public need to protect tenants and were engrafted onto succession provisions which are remedial in nature and which should be liberally construed; hence it is unlikely that the challenged regulations violate the policy of the statute which created the DHCR or exceed the delegated authority of that agency. Moreover, it is clear that plaintiffs' challenge based upon the provision of the Omnibus Housing Act of 1983 (L 1983, ch 403, § 9), which provides that no provision of the Rent Stabilization Code shall impair any right granted to any party by law, will not ultimately succeed.

Landlord and Tenant -- Rent Regulation -- Administrative Regulations Governing Lease Succession Rights and Antieviction Protections Set Out by Court of Appeals -- Conflict with Real Property Law

2. Regulations which codified the Court of Appeals decision in Braschi v Stahl Assocs. Co. (74 NY2d 201) holding that the term "family" as used in the New York City Rent and Eviction Regulations (9 NYCRR 2204.6 [d]) includes "two adult lifetime partners whose relationship is long term and characterized by an emotional and financial commitment and interdependence" do not conflict with Real Property Law § 226-b, which governs the assignment of leases, or with Real Property Law § 235-f, which makes it "unlawful for a landlord to restrict occupancy of residential premises, by express lease terms or otherwise, to a tenant or tenants or to such tenants and immediate family." The regulations deal with succession to leasehold rights and with "family members" whether or not related by blood or law who have always treated the apartment in question as their family home, as opposed to "roommates".

Landlord and Tenant -- Rent Regulation -- Administrative Regulations Governing Lease Succession Rights and Antieviction Protections Set Out by Court of Appeals -- Conflict with Domestic Relations Law

3. Regulations which codified the Court of Appeals decision in Braschi v Stahl Assocs. Co. (74 NY2d 201) holding that the term "family" as used in the New York City Rent and Eviction Regulations (9 NYCRR 2204.6 [d]) includes "two adult lifetime partners whose relationship is long term and characterized by an emotional and financial commitment and interdependence" do not conflict with Domestic Relations Law § 11 requiring solemnization of a marriage, since the regulations do not remotely attempt to equate the special relationship defined therein as spousal, and specifically distinguish "husband" and "wife" from that relationship. The regulations do not attempt to confer any other protection or privilege than the protection from eviction upon the death or departure of the tenant of record.

Landlord and Tenant -- Rent Regulation -- Administrative Regulations Governing  Lease Succession Rights and Antieviction Protections  Set Out by Court of Appeals -- Separation of Powers

4. The promulgation of regulations issued by the State Division of Housing and Community Renewal (DHCR) which codified the Court of Appeals decision in Braschi v Stahl Assocs. Co. (74 NY2d 201) holding that the term "family" as used in the New York City Rent and Eviction Regulations (9 NYCRR 2204.6 [d]) includes "two adult lifetime partners whose relationship is long term and characterized by an emotional and financial commitment and interdependence" does not violate the doctrine of separation of powers, since they were enacted in response to the pronouncements of the Court of Appeals, in an area within the particular expertise of the DHCR and do not contain any exceptions but, rather, uniformly advance the policies of the regulatory scheme according to the mandate given the agency by the Legislature.

Landlord and Tenant -- Rent Regulation -- Administrative Regulations Governing Lease Succession Rights and Antieviction Protections Set out by Court of Appeals -- Vagueness

5. Regulations codifying a Court of Appeals decision holding that the term "family" as used in the New York City Rent and Eviction Regulations (9 NYCRR 2204.6 [d]) includes "two adult lifetime partners whose relationship is long term and characterized by an emotional and financial commitment and interdependence" are not impermissibly vague. The regulations set out eight specified factors to be employed in determining whether a family relationship exists, and those factors are sufficiently definite to give a person of ordinary intelligence a reasonable opportunity to make that determination. The question of when such a relationship commences is a relatively straightforward factual one which can be determined in terms of those aforementioned eight factors. Aspects of the relationship from the date intermingling and joint ownership of assets began, to when the two individuals began a close relationship with each other's relatives, can be examined to determine when the "family relationship" truly commenced.

Landlord and Tenant -- Rent Regulation -- Administrative Regulations Governing Lease Succession Rights and Antieviction Protections Set out by Court of Appeals -- Unconstitutional Taking of Landlord's Property without Just Compensation

6. Regulations codifying a Court of Appeals decision holding that the term "family" as used in the New York City Rent and Eviction Regulations (9 NYCRR 2204.6 [d]) includes "two adult lifetime partners whose relationship is long term and characterized by an emotional and financial commitment and interdependence" do not unconstitutionally deprive a landlord of the use of his property indefinitely, without just compensation, since the regulations do not fundamentally change the nature of rent control or rent stabilization, which has been upheld as constitutional. Plaintiffs' fear that the nature of the nontraditional relationship, as defined in the regulations, will permit permanent and multigenerational occupancy by strangers is unfounded since the permanency of the occupancy by the nontraditional family member is no greater than that of a traditional family member, and the definition of the relationship provided in the regulation is not so nonspecific as to allow for abuse. Successors to a tenant's lease rights will not be mere "third-parties" or "strangers" to the tenancy. The regulations do not effectively extinguish a landlord's expectation of a reversionary interest in the apartment, since they specifically define a narrow class of individuals entitled to succession, and the occupancy in question is necessarily limited by the lifetime of the succeeding "family member", which is no greater than that of any other traditional relation.
