Gambardella v. County of Nassau


No. 428E


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


168 A.D.2d 421; 562 N.Y.S.2d 709; 1990 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 14975


November 9, 1990, Argued  
December 3, 1990 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Revised December 13, 1990.



DISPOSITION: Ordered that the order is affirmed, with one bill of costs, and the matter is remitted to the Supreme Court, Nassau County, for the entry of a judgment declaring the challenged supplemental agreement constitutional.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff employees appealed an order of the Supreme Court, Nassau County (New York), which granted the motion for summary judgment of defendants, union and employer. The employees' action sought a judgment declaring a supplemental agreement to a collective bargaining agreement (CBA) between the union and the employer unconstitutional as violative of the Equal Protection Clause of the U.S. Const. amend. XIV.


OVERVIEW: The supplemental agreement deleted a provision of the existing CBA that required medical center x-ray technicians to work mandatory overtime hours in exchange for a salary increase. The employees, who did not work at the medical center but at other county health centers, did not receive a similar salary increase. The employees alleged that they had equal capabilities and qualifications as the medical center technicians. On appeal, the court affirmed, finding that the disparity in wages paid to the two different classifications of technicians by the county was supported by a rational basis where the employer, a county, saved a substantial amount of money by the salary increase. Moreover, it was clearly established that the duties and responsibilities of the medical center technicians significantly differed from those performed by the employees. Accordingly, the challenged supplemental agreement was not constitutionally infirm. Also, the union did not violate its duty of fair representation by bargaining in good faith for the benefit for one class of its employees while being unsuccessful in its attempts to obtain the same benefit for another class of employees.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court, which granted the union and employer's motion for summary judgment against the employees. The matter was remitted to the trial court for the entry of a judgment declaring the challenged supplemental agreement constitutional. The court also awarded one bill of costs.

 

518 of 992 DOCUMENTS


People v. Smith


No. 40955


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


166 A.D.2d 385; 561 N.Y.S.2d 189; 1990 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12994



October 30, 1990 



DISPOSITION: Judgment which, after a jury trial, convicted defendant of attempted burglary in the second degree and possession of burglar's tools and which sentenced defendant to concurrent terms of imprisonment of 3 1/2 to 7 years and one year, respectively, is unanimously affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from Supreme Court, New York County (New York), which convicted defendant of attempted burglary in the second degree and possession of burglar's tools.


OVERVIEW: Police officers arrested defendant when they observed him attempting to jimmy open an apartment door. In the course of the arrest, the officers recovered two metal strips from or near defendant's person. At trial, a prosecution expert on burglar's tools could not be found. Apparently as a matter of efficient courtroom management, inasmuch as defendant had made known his wish to testify on his own behalf, the trial court directed defendant, over his counsel's objection, to proceed with his testimony without requiring the prosecution to rest. The prosecution expert was subsequently permitted to testify. The court found no error in the trial court's departure from the normal order of trial set out in N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 260.30, observing that the statutory framework delineating the order of trial did not foreclose the trial court's exercise of its common-law power to vary the order of proof in its discretion and in furtherance of justice. Moreover, defendant had been informed of the content of the expert's proposed testimony and was not tactically disadvantaged thereby.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction.

 

519 of 992 DOCUMENTS


In re Klein


No. 60390


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


164 A.D.2d 9; 563 N.Y.S.2d 132; 1990 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12026


September 11, 1990, Argued  
October 18, 1990 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected November 27, 1990.



DISPOSITION: Decision affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant claimant challenged a decision of the Unemployment Insurance Appeal Board (New York), which ruled that the claimant was ineligible to receive unemployment insurance benefits and charged her with a recoverable overpayment of benefits.


OVERVIEW: The claimant had been employed as a teacher at a religious high school. After her employment there ended, the claimant filed for and initially received unemployment insurance benefits. She was later found ineligible for benefits because she had been employed by a religious organization that was statutorily exempt from making unemployment benefit contributions. At a hearing, the claimant unsuccessfully argued that the employer exemption violated the Establishment Clause of the First Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. I. The court affirmed the overpayment determination, holding that N.Y. Lab. Law § 563(2) did not violate the Establishment Clause because it also encompassed certain nonreligious employment. Moreover, the statute did not cause excessive entanglement between the state and religious establishments.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision that charged the claimant with a recoverable overpayment of benefits.


HEADNOTES

Unemployment Insurance -- Benefits -- Exemption for Religious Establishments -- Secular Purpose of Statute

1. Labor Law § 563 (2) (c), which excludes from unemployment insurance coverage services rendered for a nonprofit organization by a person employed at a place of religious worship as a caretaker or for the performance of duties of a religious nature, does not unconstitutionally favor religious establishments over nonreligious establishments; the purpose of enacting Labor Law § 563 as a whole was to provide certain nonprofit organizations with an exemption from unemployment insurance contributions, and other nonreligious employment is encompassed within the legislation. The actual primary effect of the statute does not unconstitutionally advance religious establishments, and although one effect of the statute permits religious organizations an exemption from paying contributions for unemployment benefits, any religious benefit is incidental. The statute's primary effect is secular, i.e., the efficient administration of the Labor Law, attained in part by maintaining the concept of the State's "benevolent neutrality" toward all religion.

Unemployment Insurance -- Benefits -- Exemption for Religious Establishments -- Lack of Excessive Entanglement

2. Labor Law § 563 (2) (c), which excludes from unemployment insurance coverage services rendered for a nonprofit organization by a person employed at a place of religious worship as a caretaker or for the performance of duties of a religious nature, does not violate the Establishment Clause of the US Constitution, since there is no "excessive entanglement" between the statute and religious establishments; the State need not unconstitutionally involve itself in determining an employer's religious status to implement the statute and properly allow the exemption, since legitimate good-faith characterizations of status by the religious establishment itself are sufficient. Moreover, without the exemption religious establishments would be exposed to increased State intervention through the collection of contributions and the attendant enforcement; therefore, the statute actually effectuates a greater separation between the State and religion.

 

520 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Linda R. v. Richard E.


Nos. 90-00199 (formerly 1439E), 90-01642 (formerly 1440E)


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


162 A.D.2d 48; 561 N.Y.S.2d 29; 1990 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11863


March 22, 1990, Argued  
October 1, 1990 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended October 17, 1990. As Revised November 8, 1990.



DISPOSITION: Ordered that the appeal from the order dated November 22, 1989 is dismissed, as no appeal lies from an order denying a motion to supplement and amend a memorandum decision; and it is further, Ordered that the judgment is modified, on the law and the facts and as a matter of discretion,  [***2] by (1) deleting the second decretal paragraph thereof which granted the defendant custody of the parties' infant children, and substituting therefor a provision awarding the plaintiff custody of the parties' children, (2) deleting the third decretal paragraph thereof concerning the plaintiff's visitation with the children, (3) deleting the fourth decretal paragraph thereof granting the plaintiff child support, (4) deleting the fifth decretal paragraph thereof directing the plaintiff to transfer title to the marital home to the defendant, (5) deleting the sixth decretal paragraph thereof awarding the defendant exclusive possession of the marital residence, and (6) deleting the seventh decretal paragraph thereof awarding the plaintiff a distributive share of the marital residence; as so modified, the judgment is affirmed insofar as appealed and cross-appealed from, and the matter is remitted to the Supreme Court, Nassau County, for a new trial to be had with all convenient speed before a different Justice on the issues of visitation, child support, and maintenance, as well as the occupancy and equitable distribution of the marital residence; and it is further, Ordered that the plaintiff [***3] is awarded one bill of costs; and it is further, Ordered that pending a new award of child support, the defendant shall continue to pay the plaintiff $ 130 per week child support.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant former wife sought review of a decision of the Supreme Court of Nassau County (New York), which awarded custody of the parties' two children to appellee former husband.


OVERVIEW: The trial court awarded custody of the parties' two children to appellee former husband. On review, appellant former wife contended that the proof at the trial established that she was the appropriate custodial parent and that the trial court, in weighing the parental capabilities of appellant and appellee, failed to apply gender neutral standards. The court reversed the trial court's award of custody to appellee. The court held that the weight of the evidence supported an award of custody to appellant, because there was no support for the trial court's conclusion that appellant was a "remote control" mother, there was no credible evidence that supported the trial court's assertion that appellant had extreme emotional problems that affected her ability to raise her children, the trial court failed to properly apply the best interests of the child standard set forth in N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 240(1), and that the trial court set forth no convincing reasons for its discounting of the psychiatric evaluation of all family members.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's award of custody of the parties' two children to appellee former husband. The court held that the evidence supported an award of custody to appellant former wife, as there was no credible evidence that supported the trial court's assertion that appellant had extreme emotional problems that affected her ability to raise her children and the trial court failed to apply the best interests of the child standard.


HEADNOTES

Parent and Child -- Custody -- Sound Basis for Award

1. In a matrimonial action, the trial court improperly awarded custody of the parties' twin daughters to the father since the determination lacked a sound and substantial basis in the record; accordingly, upon the authority of the Appellate Division to make a determination on the record, it is concluded that the weight of the credible evidence supports an award of custody to the mother. There is no foundation for the trial court's conclusion that the mother was a "remote control" parent who left the actual child rearing to the father and a housekeeper; rather, the evidence indicated that the mother participated substantially in the rearing of the children. Moreover, there was no credible evidence that the mother has extreme emotional problems; she did not terminate treatment irresponsibly, but continued therapy. Her problems would not affect her custodial capacity, and the court failed to consider that the father also suffered from emotional problems. Accordingly, the custody determination did not have the firm expert confirmation upon which it claims to have relied to justify the result.

Parent and Child -- Custody -- Evidence of Mother's Relationship with Another Man

2. In a matrimonial action, the trial court improperly awarded custody of the parties' twin daughters to the father; the court allowed extensive testimony regarding a relationship between the mother and "her lover", concluding that it disserved the children by disordering the mother's priorities. However, the evidence does not justify the conclusion that the mother's alleged relationship resulted in her absenting herself from the children when they were in stress; rather, the evidence indicated that while the children were aware of the parties' problems, they were well adjusted emotionally. There was no credible proof that the mother's alleged relationship with another man affected the children in any significant way; the sexual behavior of a litigant in a custody dispute is only relevant if and to the extent that the children are thereby affected. Moreover, when the mother's attorney tried to question the parties' housekeeper and crossexamine the father about parallel activities on his part, the court improperly found the questions objectionable.

Parent and Child -- Custody -- Gender-Neutral Standards

3. There is no prima facie right to the custody of a child (see, Domestic Relations Law §§ 70, 240), and the role of gender in making custody determinations has no place in current law; accordingly, in a custody dispute the trial court improperly imposed a more rigorous standard upon plaintiff wife with regard to employment outside the home, and by not imposing a corresponding standard upon the husband, the court was not free of the appearance of having imposed a more onerous burden on the wife. A wife who is employed should not suffer in her chances for custody any more than should the husband, and a custody-seeking mother who works outside the home should not be penalized for her employment, by being characterized as undevoted to her child, any more than should the father. Custody determinations should not be based on any unfounded assumption that a "working mother" is a less satisfactory parent or any less fully committed to the care of the child than is a "working father".

Parent and Child -- Custody -- Psychiatric Evaluations

4. In a custody dispute, the trial court improperly awarded custody of the parties' twin daughters to defendant father; the court set forth no convincing reasons for discounting the court-requested psychiatric evaluation of all family members, as well as the evaluation by the county Probation Department, recommending that the mother be granted custody. A review of the record leads to the conclusion that the mother would better advance the emotional and intellectual development of the children in the long run and that she would better promote familial harmony, since she would be less likely to continue an ongoing competition with her former spouse or attempt to alienate the children from him.

 

521 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Barrett v. State


No. 3967E


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


161 A.D.2d 61; 560 N.Y.S.2d 302; 1990 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11336


May 17, 1990, Argued  
September 17, 1990 



DISPOSITION: Ordered that the order is reversed insofar as appealed from, on the law, with costs, the State's motion which was to dismiss the cause of action to recover damages for conscious pain and suffering is denied, and that branch of the claimant's cross motion which was to dismiss the State's fourth affirmative defense is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant administratrix filed an action against respondent state hospital (state) on behalf of her deceased infant. The state filed a motion to dismiss the adminstratrix's claim to recover damages for conscious pain and suffering as untimely, and the administratrix filed a cross-motion to dismiss the state's defense. The Court of Claims (New York) granted the state's motion, and the administratrix appealed.


OVERVIEW: The state argued that N.Y. Ct. Cl. Act § 10(5) precluded a deceased infant's estate from asserting the statutory disability toll. The court reviewed the plain language of § 10(5) and determined that under the plain language of that statute, the administratrix's claim for conscious pain and suffering, which belonged to the infant and survived her death, could have been submitted on behalf of the infant's estate within two years after her death. Further, the court held that the court of claims' resort to the legislative history of § 10(5) was unnecessary because the language of the statute was unambiguous.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the court of claims and held that the tolling provisions applied such that the administratrix had until two years after her infant's death to bring an action for conscious pain and suffering.


HEADNOTES

State -- Claim against State -- Disability Tolling Period

1. In an action arising out of alleged medical malpractice, the personal representative of a deceased infant may assert the two-year tolling provision for infancy set forth in Court of Claims Act § 10 (5) in filing a claim on behalf of the infant's estate to recover damages for the conscious pain and suffering experienced by the infant prior to death, since the plain language of the statutory toll permits its assertion by the deceased infant's personal representative on behalf of the estate; at the time the claim accrued, the deceased was an infant and, therefore, was under a legal disability. The claim for conscious pain and suffering which belonged to the infant and survived her death (see, EPTL 11-3.2 [b]), may be timely presented on behalf of her estate within two years after her death, and to conclude otherwise would place the deceased infant's estate at a distinct procedural disadvantage, for while the infant, if living, would have two years from the date of reaching the age of majority within which to file a claim for conscious pain and suffering, the estate of that same infant, solely by reason of the infant's untimely death, would at best be relegated to a 90-day period from the date of death within which to seek the identical relief.

State -- Claim against State -- Disability Tolling Period

2. In an action arising out of alleged medical malpractice, the personal representative of a deceased infant may assert the two-year tolling provision for infancy set forth in Court of Claims Act § 10 (5) in filing a claim on behalf of the infant's estate to recover damages for the conscious pain and suffering experienced by the infant prior to death, since the plain language of the statutory toll permits its assertion by the deceased infant's personal representative, and the unambiguous language of that statutory provision renders resort to its legislative history unnecessary. Accordingly, the disability toll may be asserted not only by a living claimant who was formerly under the legal disability of infancy, but also on behalf of a deceased infant's estate.

State -- Claim against State -- Disability Tolling Period

3. In an action arising out of alleged medical malpractice, the personal representative of a deceased infant may assert the two-year tolling provision for infancy set forth in Court of Claims Act § 10 (5) in filing a claim on behalf of the infant's estate to recover damages for the conscious pain and suffering experienced by the infant prior to death; claimant is not receiving a longer limitations period to bring suit against the State than she would have received if the action had been brought between citizens of the State (see, NY Const, art III, § 19). While Court of Claims Act § 10 (5) gives claimant an additional two years after the removal of the disability within which to bring suit, and CPLR 210 (a) permits a maximum toll of only one year when the person entitled to bring suit dies, CPLR 210 (a) is a tolling provision for the death of the aggrieved party which has no counterpart in the Court of Claims Act; conversely, Court of Claims Act § 10 (5) provides a toll for legal disability not death and has a counterpart in CPLR 208 which provides an infant or incompetent with an additional three years after cessation of the disability within which to commence suit. Accordingly, the State is not unconstitutionally subjected to a less favorable limitations period than an ordinary citizen.

 

522 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Emanuel S. v. Joseph E.


No. 1347E


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


161 A.D.2d 83; 560 N.Y.S.2d 211; 1990 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11339


May 21, 1990, Argued  
September 17, 1990 



DISPOSITION: Ordered that the order is reversed insofar as appealed from, on the law, without costs or disbursements, and the proceeding is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent parents appealed an order of the Nassau County Family Court (New York) that awarded petitioner grandparents limited, unsupervised visitation with their grandson under N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 72.


OVERVIEW: The grandparents saw and visited their grandson during the first few months of his life, after which the parents denied them any contact. The court reversed the order awarding the grandparents visitation. The grandparents had no common law or constitutional right to visitation with their grandson. Although the grandparents could seek visitation under § 72, they could only be awarded visitation if they established that their established relationship with the child was disrupted because of a change in the status of the nuclear family (divorce of the parents), interference with a derivative right of visitation that arose after the death of one of the child's parents, or abdication of parental responsibility. The grandparents' claim that they were entitled to visitation because they could provide their grandson with love and affection was insufficient to entitle them to visitation.


OUTCOME: The order awarding the grandparents visitation with their grandchild was reversed and their petition for visitation was dismissed.


HEADNOTES

Parent and Child -- Visitation Rights of Grandparents -- Intact Nuclear Family

Grandparents are not legislatively authorized (see, Domestic Relations Law § 72; Family Ct Act § 651) to seek judicial intervention as an aid in efforts to visit a grandchild where both of the child's natural parents object and neither parent has abdicated or forfeited parental responsibility; accordingly, the motion of the parents to dismiss the visitation proceeding should be granted and the issue of the best interest of the child should not be reached. Grandparents enjoy no common-law or constitutional right to visit with their grandchildren, and a petition for an order authorizing visitation pursuant to Domestic Relations Law § 72, which provides for grandparent visitation, must demonstrate the existence of some circumstance or condition, such as untoward disruption of an established grandparent-grandchild relationship because of, e.g., a change in the status of the nuclear family, or interference with a "derivative" right, or some abdication of parental responsibility, before judicial examination of the best interests of the child with its attendant trauma, increased animosity, and financial drain is to be undertaken. The State may not interfere in family life to the extent sought; petitioners' status as grandparents and their love and affection are an insufficient premise for judicial scrutiny.

 

523 of 992 DOCUMENTS


People v. Guce


No. 2664E


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


164 A.D.2d 946; 560 N.Y.S.2d 53; 1990 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10824


June 26, 1990, Argued  
August 27, 1990 



DISPOSITION: ORDERED that the judgment is affirmed, and the case is remitted to the Supreme Court, Queens County, for further proceedings pursuant to CPL 460.50(5).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Supreme Court, Queens County (New York), convicting him of rape in the first degree, sodomy in the first degree, sexual abuse in the first degree, incest, and endangering the welfare of a child, upon a jury verdict, and imposing sentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed from a judgment convicting him of rape, sodomy, sexual abuse, incest, and endangering the welfare of a child, upon a jury verdict, and imposing sentence. Defendant contended that the use of live, two-way, closed-circuit television for the child witnesses violated his constitutional right to confrontation. U.S. Const. amends. VI and XIV; N.Y. Const. art. VI. The court on review found N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law article 65 to be facially constitutional. The court additionally found no violations to defendant's right to confrontation in the application of the procedures set forth in N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law art. 65. Expert testimony was clear and convincing in establishing that five of the 12 factors set forth in N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 65.20(9) were established. Most importantly, the expert gave a detailed opinion of the psychological harm that the children would likely suffer if they were required to testify in open court. The trial court was justified in concluding that there were extraordinary circumstances present to justify the use of the closed-circuit television for the children's testimony.


OUTCOME: The judgment convicting defendant of the crimes was affirmed. There was clear and convincing evidence that the child witnesses were vulnerable and likely to suffer severe emotional harm if compelled to testify in the presence of defendant. Accordingly, the record amply supported the use of live, two-way, closed-circuit television.

 

524 of 992 DOCUMENTS


New York State Soc. of Enrolled Agents v. New York State Div. of Tax Appeals


No. 2271E


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


161 A.D.2d 1; 559 N.Y.S.2d 906; 1990 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10577


November 28, 1989, Argued  
August 13, 1990 



DISPOSITION: Ordered that the order is affirmed, with costs, and the matter is remitted to the Supreme Court, Westchester County, for entry of an appropriate judgment declaring Tax Law § 2014 constitutional.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, a voluntary association of enrolled agents authorized to represent clients in tax disputes and one of its clients, appealed the decision of the Supreme Court (New York), which granted the cross-motion of defendant, New York State Division of Tax Appeals, for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: The voluntary association of enrolled agents authorized to represent clients in tax disputes and one of its clients (association) brought an action for a judgment declaring N.Y. Tax Law § 2014 unconstitutional and enjoining its enforcement by the New York State Division of Tax Appeals (division). The lower court found that the association failed to meet its heavy burden of proving that § 2014 was unconstitutional either as an invalid exercise of the state's police power or as violative of equal protection. The court concluded that, giving § 2014 the presumption of validity to which it was entitled, there was a reasonable connection between the decision to exclude enrolled agents from being retained by taxpayers in state tax proceedings and the promotion of the welfare of society. The court also concluded that the statutory classification met the rational basis test because the state had a legitimate interest in limiting the classes of professionals who could represent clients in state tax disputes to those over whom the state could exercise regulatory and disciplinary control. The court remitted the matter for the entry of a judgment declaring § 2014 constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court remitted the matter to the lower court for the entry of an appropriate judgment declaring the challenged statute constitutional because the association failed to sustain its burden of establishing the unconstitutionality of the challenged statute beyond a reasonable doubt. The court noted that the lower court should have made a declaration that the provision in question was constitutional rather than dismissing the complaint.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- Tax Appeals Tribunal -- Limitation on Taxpayer's Right of Representation -- Constitutionality -- Police Power -- Enrolled Agents

1. Tax Law § 2014, which eliminated the right of enrolled agents, i.e., persons authorized by the United States Department of the Treasury to represent clients before the United States Internal Revenue Service, to represent clients on State tax appeals, is not an unconstitutional exercise of the State's police power, since the Legislature, when it overhauled the tax dispute adjudicatory process in order to remove any perception of bias, presumably recognized the need in State tax disputes to limit representation of taxpayers to professionals who are licensed by the State, i.e., attorneys and public accountants, and over whom the State could exercise regulatory power to achieve the legitimate objective of effective representation of taxpayers by competent professionals; moreover, nowhere in the Federal regulations is there any indication that licensing by the Treasury Department precludes the individual States from establishing different requirements for those who may be retained by taxpayers in State administrative or quasi-judicial proceedings. In light of the presumption of validity enjoyed by the statute, there is a reasonable connection between the elimination of representation by enrolled agents and the promotion of the welfare of society.

Taxation -- Tax Appeals Tribunal -- Limitation on Taxpayer's Right of Representation -- Constitutionality -- Equal Protection -- Enrolled Agents

2. Tax Law § 2014, which eliminated the right of enrolled agents, i.e., persons authorized by the United States Department of the Treasury to represent clients before the United States Internal Revenue Service, to represent clients on State tax appeals, is not an unconstitutional denial of equal protection; inasmuch as neither a fundamental right nor a suspect classification is in issue, the enrolled agents' equal protection claim need not be subjected to strict judicial scrutiny, but is to be measured by the "reasonable basis" test, which merely requires the governmental classification to bear a fair and substantial relation to some conceivable and legitimate State interest. Accordingly, in light of the State's legitimate interest in limiting the class of professionals who may represent clients in State tax disputes to those over whom it may exercise regulatory and disciplinary control, i.e., attorneys and public accountants, the limitation promotes and encourages a high level of professional competence in tax proceedings and, thereby, protects taxpayers in quasi-judicial administrative proceedings.

Taxation -- Tax Appeals Tribunal -- Limitation on Taxpayer's Right of Representation -- Constitutionality -- Impairment of Contract -- Enrolled Agents

3. Tax Law § 2014, which eliminated the right of enrolled agents, i.e., persons authorized by the United States Department of the Treasury to represent clients before the United States Internal Revenue Service, to represent clients on State tax appeals, is not an unconstitutional impairment of a taxpayer/client's right to contract, since the State's interest in ensuring adequate and effective retained representation of taxpayers in State tax matters is an important public purpose; the legislation enacted to serve that objective may not be stricken as unconstitutional even though the method of accomplishing the State's purpose may impair the right to contract or abridge existing contract rights, since the State policy may override private contract rights.

Declaratory Judgments -- Form of Relief

4. In an action seeking a declaratory judgment that a statute is unconstitutional, the Supreme Court, which granted summary judgment to defendant, should have made a declaration that the statute in question was constitutional, rather than dismissing the complaint; accordingly, the matter is remitted for entry of an appropriate judgment declaring the statute constitutional.

 

525 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Davidson v. Rochester Tel. Corp.


No. 60737


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


163 A.D.2d 800; 558 N.Y.S.2d 1009; 1990 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8939



July 26, 1990 



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff telephone service customer sought review of an order from the Supreme Court in Albany County (New York), granting defendant telephone company's motion for summary judgment dismissing plaintiff's complaint that had challenged the legality of defendant's practice of collecting state and local sales taxes on "end user common line" charges to recover costs which plaintiff contended were interstate in nature.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff telephone service customer commenced an action against defendant telephone company for reimbursement of taxes collected on "end user common line" charges, contending that the charges were interstate in nature and exempt from sales tax. The trial court denied plaintiff's motion for class certification, granted defendant's cross motion for summary judgment, and dismissed the complaint in its entirety on the merits. The trial court dismissed plaintiff's claim based on the State Tax Commission's formal advisory opinion, upon which defendant relied, finding that it correctly mandated the collection of sales tax on "end user common line" charges as a matter of law. On appeal, the court affirmed the order. Defendant was designated by law to collect the taxes and as such it had no right to question the underlying validity of the tax. The claim that defendant's collection of sales taxes was improper, unnecessary, unauthorized by law, and an unjust enrichment, were claims that had to be raised in an administrative proceeding against the taxing body. As plaintiff had failed to exhaust his administrative remedies, he was not entitled to maintain the instant action.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order denying plaintiff telephone service customer's motion for class certification, granting defendant telephone company's cross motion for summary judgment, and dismissing the complaint in its entirety. The court found that plaintiff's remedy in contesting the validity of taxes collected on "end user common line" charges was against the taxing body and plaintiff had failed to exhaust his administrative remedies.

 

526 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Davis v. Rosenblatt


Nos. 60999, 61000, 61001, 61002


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


159 A.D.2d 163; 559 N.Y.S.2d 401; 1990 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8612


June 4, 1990, Argued  
July 19, 1990 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended September 11, 1990.



DISPOSITION: Order and judgment in action No. 1 modified, on the law, without costs, by reversing so much [***4] thereof as granted the Rochester plaintiffs' motion for prejudgment interest from October 1, 1978 and deferred on the issue of counsel fees; motion for prejudgment interest denied and matter remitted to the Supreme Court, Monroe County, for further proceedings not inconsistent with this court's decision; and, as so modified, affirmed. Order and judgment in action No. 2 modified, on the law, without costs, by reversing so much thereof as granted the Buffalo plaintiffs' motion requesting a declaration that the legislation permitting the salary disparity between the Buffalo plaintiffs and Yonkers City Court Judges to be unconstitutional, awarded judgment for that differential and denied the Buffalo plaintiffs' request for counsel fees; judgment awarded for the differential between the salary of the Buffalo plaintiffs and that of Utica City Court Judges and matter remitted to the Supreme Court, Erie County, for further proceedings not inconsistent with this court's decision; and, as so modified, affirmed. Order and judgment in action No. 3 modified, on the law, without costs, by reversing so much thereof as awarded prejudgment interest; matter remitted to the Supreme Court, Niagara [***5] County, for further proceedings not inconsistent with this court's decision; and, as so modified, affirmed. Order and judgment in action No. 4 modified, on the law, without costs, by reversing so much thereof as found that none of the Syracuse plaintiffs' claims were barred by the Statute of Limitations, granted the Syracuse plaintiffs' motion seeking a declaration that legislation establishing a salary disparity between the Syracuse plaintiffs and Yonkers City Court Judges to be unconstitutional and awarded back pay commensurate with the Yonkers salary, together with prejudgment interest, and denied the Syracuse plaintiffs' request for counsel fees; complaint of plaintiff Mary H. Fahey dismissed, judgment awarded for the differential between the salary of the remaining Syracuse plaintiffs and that of Utica City Court Judges and matter remitted to the Supreme Court, Onondaga County, for further proceedings not inconsistent with this court's decision; and, as so modified, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee state challenged the judgments of the Supreme Courts in Monroe, Erie, Niagara, and Onondaga Counties (New York) in favor appellant judges. The judges' action against the state alleged disparity between their wages and those of judges in the cities of Yonkers and Utica.


OVERVIEW: In four proceedings in different county trial courts, the judges sought an order declaring that the disparity between their wages and the wages paid to the City Court judges of the cities of Yonkers and Utica pursuant to N.Y. Jud. Law § 221-i violated their rights to equal protection of the laws under N.Y. Const. art I, § 11 and U.S. Const. amend. XIV, § 1. They requested an award of back pay, related ancillary relief, and counsel fees. In all of the actions, the trial courts found in favor the judges. On appeal, the court held that the state did not met its burden of establishing that a rational basis existed for the disparity in salaries between the judges and the Utica City Court Judges. The court found that the disparate treatment was unjustified since there was a true unity of judicial interest indistinguishable by separate geographic considerations. However, the court found that in comparing the information in the record contrasting the situations of the judges and their Yonkers counterparts, the state's contention that a rational basis existed for the salary disparity was sufficiently supported.


OUTCOME: The judgements in favor the judges were modified where they provided for prejudgment interest. The judgments were reversed where they declared that the legislation permitting the salary disparity between the judges and Yonkers City Court judges was unconstitutional and allowed an award of back pay commensurate with the Yonkers salary. In all other respects, the judgments were affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Judges -- Salaries -- Disparities Based on Geographic Location -- Statute of Limitations

1. In actions challenging the constitutionality of the disparity in pay based on geographic location among City Court Judges within the State (see, Judiciary Law § 221-i), the Statute of Limitations is not a bar to those plaintiffs who are either still on the Bench or who have left it less than six years before the commencement of the action, since it is clear that the only appropriate limitation period is the six-year residuary Statute of Limitations provided by CPLR 213 (1) and, with respect to current Judges, their claims for declaratory relief invalidating the statutory scheme cannot be barred because a cause of action for a continuing harm continuously accrues.

Judges -- Salaries -- Disparities Based on Geographic Location -- Constitutionality -- Rational Basis -- Upstate Judges

2. In actions challenging the constitutionality of the disparity in pay between certain City Court Judges within the State (see, Judiciary Law § 221-i), the record does not support the defense that a rational basis exists for the differences in salary between such Judges in Buffalo, Rochester, Syracuse and Niagara Falls, and City Court Judges in Utica where the duties and responsibilities of plaintiff Judges are equal to those of Utica City Court Judges, where the average caseloads are either roughly comparable, or greater with respect to Rochester and Buffalo Judges, where the population and cost of living are greater in Rochester, Buffalo and Syracuse than in Utica, where the difference in population and cost of living between Utica and Niagara Falls is not significant, and where all of the cities in question are located in upstate counties which comprise the Fourth Judicial Department, thereby presenting a true unity of judicial interest indistinguishable by separate geographic considerations.

Judges -- Salaries -- Disparities Based on Geographic Location -- Constitutionality -- Rational Basis -- Upstate Versus Downstate Judges

3. In actions challenging the constitutionality of the disparity in pay between certain City Court Judges within the State (see, Judiciary Law § 221-i), it is apparent that a rational basis exists for the salary differential between Judges in Buffalo, Rochester, Syracuse and Niagara Falls and Judges in Yonkers, and as long as any conceivable statement of facts will support a classification by the Legislature, it cannot be held to be violative of equal protection; although the duties, functions and responsibilities of the Judges in question are similar, and although the population of Yonkers is less than Rochester and Buffalo, albeit greater than Syracuse and Niagara Falls, the State has submitted data which indicate that the cost of living in Yonkers is greater than the cost of living in the upstate cities in question. An examination of the data shows that a rational relationship has been established between higher New York City area salaries and the State's objective of ensuring that the limited resources available for courts are allocated according to need and utilized effectively. Although plaintiffs dispute the accuracy of the State's data, they have failed to provide alternative information. Moreover, since plaintiffs' courts are all located in upstate counties within the Fourth Judicial Department, and the City of Yonkers is located in the Second Judicial Department, it cannot easily be concluded that a true unity of judicial interest has been established.

Judges -- Salaries -- Disparities Based on Geographic Location -- Unconstitutionality -- Remedy -- Counsel Fees

4. Inasmuch as Judiciary Law § 221-i, which perpetuated a salary disparity between the Judges of the City Courts of Buffalo, Rochester, Syracuse and Niagara Falls and the Judges of the Utica City Court, is unconstitutional insofar as it denies the former Judges equal protection in relation to their Utica counterparts, the former Judges should be awarded back pay and pension base adjustments so as to achieve parity with the Utica Judges; however, the back pay awards should not include prejudgment interest pursuant to CPLR 5001. Moreover, inasmuch as plaintiffs have successfully contended that they have been denied their right to equal protection, a claim cognizable under 42 USC § 1983, the actions must be remitted to the respective Supreme Courts so as to allow defendants an opportunity to demonstrate whether special circumstances exist which would bar an award of counsel fees.

Engoren v. County of Nassau


No. 3271E


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


163 A.D.2d 520; 559 N.Y.S.2d 342; 1990 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8815


January 22, 1990, Submitted  
July 18, 1990 



DISPOSITION: ORDERED that the judgment is affirmed, without costs or disbursements.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner former employee (employee) filed a proceeding pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 to review a determination of respondent former employer terminating the employee's employment or ordering her reinstatement. The Supreme Court, Nassau County (New York) denied the application and dismissed the action. The employee appealed.


OVERVIEW: The employee took an examination for an original appointment in another department. She resigned her permanent position in order to accept the new appointment, which required a probationary period. She was terminated during probation and sought review of the employer's termination decision or in the alternative, an order requiring the employer to reinstate her to her former position. The trial court denied review and dismissed the proceeding. On appeal, the court affirmed the trial court's judgment, finding that there was no evidence of any discriminatory reason for the employee's termination. The court held that the employer's good faith reason that the probation was terminated for performance reasons was supported by a letter from the employee acknowledging that her work was suffering due to stress. The court further determined that the employee was not entitled to reinstatement to her former position pursuant to New York City, N.Y., Rules of the City of New York, tit. 4, § 4.5(d) because she was not promoted but instead had accepted a position in another department.


OUTCOME: The trial court's order denying the employee's application for review of her termination or seeking reinstatement and dismissing the proceeding was affirmed.

People v. Dozier

Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department

Defendant appealed, arguing that he was improperly sentenced as a second violent felony offender because a period of time that he was incarcerated could not be used to toll the period of limitation under N.Y. Penal Law §70.04(1)(b)(iv).

529 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Historic Albany Foundation, Inc. v. Coyne


No. 60451


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


159 A.D.2d 73; 558 N.Y.S.2d 986; 1990 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8415


June 4, 1990, Argued  
July 12, 1990 



DISPOSITION: Order modified, on the law, with costs to plaintiff Historic Albany Foundation, Inc., by reversing so much thereof as granted defendants' motion to dismiss and denied plaintiff's motion for summary judgment; summary judgment awarded to plaintiff; and, as so modified, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff historical foundation appealed an order from the Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), which granted defendants' motion to dismiss the complaint seeking to enjoin the county's demolition of houses without compliance with a city ordinance.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff historic foundation brought this action seeking declaratory and injunctive relief to prevent defendant county from proceeding with its plan to demolish a county-owned block of houses without first complying with the provisions of the city's Historic Resources Commission Ordinance, Code of City of Albany §§ 1-126 - 1-136. The complaint was dismissed on the ground that the ordinance violated the constitutional prohibitions against the taking of private property without just compensation in N.Y. Const. art. I, § 7. Historic district and landmark preservation legislation commonly regulated and restricted demolition of buildings in protected districts, and had been authoritatively upheld against constitutional attacks under the Takings Clause. Dismissal of the complaint was reversed because defendant had shown nothing more than that it might be subjected to some as yet unknown expense of new development before it could demolish the property if Code of City of Albany § 1-135 (b)(2) was enforced and, this alone was insufficient to establish a taking without just compensation.


OUTCOME: Dismissal of plaintiff's complaint seeking injunctive and declarative relief to prevent the county from demolishing houses was reversed because the historical preservation ordinance was not unconstitutional as the trial court did not have the proper facts before it to properly construe the statute and its application to the facts.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations -- Historic Preservation District -- Constitutionality of Ordinance Regulating Demolition of Buildings

1. Inasmuch as historic district and/or landmark preservation legislation commonly regulates demolition of buildings in protected districts and has been authoritatively upheld against constitutional attacks under the "takings" clause, a provision of a municipal ordinance keying demolition to a showing either that the building in question is not of historical, archeological or aesthetic value, or that its owner will suffer hardship by being required to repair or maintain property incapable of yielding a reasonable return, is constitutionally valid since it virtually replicates regulatory framework which has sustained constitutional attack. In light of the fact that Supreme Court determined that the subject ordinance was unconstitutional on the basis of a separate provision governing new development, and the fact that the ordinance contains a severability clause, plaintiffs, which seek to bar defendants, who concededly never complied with the provision in question, from demolishing a building until they have complied with the ordinance, are entitled to an injunction restraining the demolition until defendants have complied with the valid and unchallenged portion of the ordinance which remained unaffected by Supreme Court's ruling.

Municipal Corporations -- Historic Preservation District -- Constitutionality of Ordinance Regulating Demolition of Buildings -- Showing of Economic Hardship

2. In an action to prevent defendants, the owners of a historic building, from demolishing the building until they have complied with the strictures of a municipal ordinance regulating the demolition of historic structures, Supreme Court's determination that a portion of the ordinance which required owners of historic buildings to have plans for the new development of the site approved by a municipal commission prior to demolishing the building is unconstitutional, was error where defendants never demonstrated the economic impact of complying with the ordinance; consequently, the court did not have specific facts before it to determine whether the restrictions imposed on defendants' right to demolish were so severe as to amount to a "taking". Moreover, defendants did not even attempt to demonstrate that the requirement in question was not reasonably necessary to effectuate the clearly legitimate purposes of the legislation; indeed, permitting demolition without assurance that there will be some form of development would create a risk of the site becoming overgrown and decayed, a result antithetical to the aesthetic purposes of the ordinance. The requirement in question substantially advances the legitimate municipal purpose underlying the legislation, and there is a direct nexus between the requirement and the purposes underlying the restrictions on demolition.

Municipal Corporations -- Historic Preservation District -- Constitutionality of Ordinance  Regulating Demolition of Buildings

3. In an action to prevent defendants, the owners of a historic building, from demolishing the building until they have complied with the strictures of a municipal ordinance regulating the demolition of historic structures, defendants have failed to show that the requirement of submission and approval of a suitable plan of new development as a condition for permitting demolition deprives them of all economically viable use of the subject property; restricting the right to demolish, which is only one strand in an owner's bundle of property rights, may diminish the value of the property or prevent its most profitable use, but those do not constitute a "taking" in terms of impact upon the property as a whole. Moreover, the ordinance contains provisions for administrative waiver of its provisions and defendants have failed to avail themselves of seeking such relief, thereby obviating their constitutional objections. Defendants have merely alleged that they will be subject to an unknown expense, and that is insufficient to establish an unconstitutional "taking". Accordingly, summary judgment should be granted to plaintiffs, and the various factual issues raised should be resolved in a proceeding before the municipal commission established by the ordinance.

Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


163 A.D.2d 94; 556 N.Y.S.2d 925; 1990 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8339

Kindlon v. County of Rensselaer


No. 61127


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


158 A.D.2d 178; 558 N.Y.S.2d 286; 1990 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8127


June 7, 1990, Argued  
July 5, 1990 



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed, on the law, without costs, and it is declared that 22 NYCRR 822.4 is invalid insofar as it pertains to applications pursuant to County Law § 722-b.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner attorney appealed a judgment from the Supreme Court of Rensselaer County (New York) that dismissed the attorney's action to compel respondent county to pay his assigned counsel fees and expenses awarded by order of the County Court of Rensselaer County, under N.Y. County Law § 722-b, and in which the attorney sought a declaration that N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 22, § 822.4 (rule 822.4) was invalid.


OVERVIEW: The attorney was appointed to serve as counsel to an indigent defendant in a criminal action in the County Court of Rensselaer County. Upon application by the attorney following sentencing, the county court ordered payment of counsel fees in excess of the statutory maximum, under N.Y. County Law § 722-b. Thereafter, the county sought review of the excess compensation by the presiding justice of the court, under rule 822.4. The administrative action was stayed and the attorney brought a combined N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 proceeding and declaratory judgment action to compel payment and to have rule 822.4 declared invalid. The supreme court dismissed the action for failure to exhaust administrative remedies. On appeal, the court ruled that the attorney was not required to exhaust his administrative remedies because the attorney had challenged, on constitutional grounds, the presiding justice's authority under rule 822.4 to administratively review the attorney's award of fees. Moreover, the court ruled, rule 822.4 was invalid insofar as it pertained to applications under § 722-b, because there was no valid delegation of the authority to the presiding justice to review such fee awards.


OUTCOME: The court reversed a judgment, on the law, without costs, that dismissed the attorney's petition to recover assigned counsel fees. The court also declared that the challenged administrative rule governing such fees was invalid insofar as it pertained to applications such as the attorney's.


HEADNOTES

Administrative Law -- Failure to Exhaust Administrative Remedies -- Administrative Review of Assigned Counsel Fees

1. In a proceeding seeking an order compelling payment of the compensation awarded by a County Court to an attorney who acted as assigned counsel for a criminal defendant, and a declaration that 22 NYCRR 822.4, which provides for review by the Presiding Justice of the Appellate Division, Third Department, of fees in excess of the limits contained in County Law § 722-b, is in irreconcilable conflict with section 722-b, which provides for compensation of assigned counsel, petitioner was not required to exhaust his administrative remedies as a prerequisite to legal action, since petitioner has challenged the very authority of the Presiding Justice to administratively review the award of counsel fees, asserting that the court rule is unconstitutional and illegal. Moreover, it cannot be seriously argued that the rule does not conflict with the statute, inasmuch as it affords respondent county officials an avenue of administrative review not provided for in the statute; thus, the rule, inconsistent with the general State-wide procedure provided by the County Law, must yield to the statute, unless the State Constitution provides authority for its promulgation.

Attorney and Client -- Compensation -- Assigned Counsel -- Administrative Review of "Excess" Fees by Appellate Division

2. A rule of the Appellate Division, Third Department (22 NYCRR 822.4), which provides for administrative review by the Presiding Justice of an award of a fee to an attorney who acted as assigned counsel where the fee is in excess of the limits set by County Law § 722-b, is invalid insofar as it pertains to applications pursuant to the statute; although NY Constitution, article VI, § 28 grants the Chief Judge and the Chief Administrator of the Courts the ultimate authority to administer the court system, including the power to review awards of compensation to assigned attorneys, the rule in question must fall since there has been no delegation of the authority to review such fee awards to the Presiding Justice of the Appellate Division. The rule was not continued in effect as a rule of the Chief Administrator by virtue of the savings provision of 22 NYCRR 103.1, since 22 NYCRR 822.4 was not in effect in its present form when 22 NYCRR 103.1 was promulgated, and, thus, could not have been included in the adoption provision of 22 NYCRR 103.1.

People v. Rossi


No. 60693


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


163 A.D.2d 660; 558 N.Y.S.2d 698; 1990 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8140



July 5, 1990 



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment from the Supreme Court of Broome County (New York), which convicted defendant of two counts of manslaughter in the second degree, assault in the second degree, and vehicular assault in the second degree.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was involved in an automobile accident that killed two boys and injured their mother. After the accident, defendant was glassy eyed, had trouble standing, was unresponsive to questions, and admitted to drinking beer and taking medications. Blood tests detected 6 nanograms per milliliter of methamphetamine and 13 nanograms per milliliter of amphetamine. Toxicologists testified that the amount of drugs detected was sufficient to detrimentally affect defendant's driving ability. Defendant claimed on appeal that the blood test results should have been suppressed because there was no probable cause to believe that he was driving in violation of N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law former § 1192, that there was an insufficient foundation for the blood test results, and that the amount of drugs was insufficient to warrant criminal liability. The court affirmed the trial court and rejected defendant's claims. The court found that there was probable cause for defendant's arrest, that the tests were done in an acceptable manner, and that the evidence supported defendant's convictions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for two counts of manslaughter in the second degree, assault in the second degree, and vehicular assault in the second degree.

Miriam P. v. New York


Nos. 40197, 40197A


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


163 A.D.2d 39; 558 N.Y.S.2d 506; 1990 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8098



July 3, 1990 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant hospital appealed an order from the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), which adhered to its original determination sustaining plaintiff complainants' causes of action based on violations of 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 and false imprisonment, and denying defendant's motion to dismiss.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff complainants brought an action against defendant hospital, alleging intentional infliction of emotional distress, violations of 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983, and false imprisonment. A lower court sustained plaintiffs' causes of action based on violations of § 1983 and false imprisonment, and denied defendant's motion to dismiss. Defendant appealed. The court filed an order dismissing plaintiffs' complaint. The court agreed with defendant that plaintiffs' complaint failed to state a cause of action. The court found that defendant was not acting under color of state law for § 1983 purposes in caring for a child pending the completion of an investigation by Special Services for Children. The court concluded, therefore, that the lower court erred in denying defendant's motion to dismiss the § 1983 cause of action. The court determined that defendant's actions were taken in good faith, and that it was entitled to the statutory immunity under N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 419.


OUTCOME: The court filed an order dismissing plaintiff complainants' action against defendant hospital for civil rights violations and false imprisonment, concluding that a lower court erred in denying defendant's motion to dismiss because plaintiffs' complaint failed to state a cause of action where defendant was not acting under color of state law in caring for a child pending the completion of an investigation by a social services agency.

MacIsaac v. Poughkeepsie


No. 60773


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


158 A.D.2d 140; 558 N.Y.S.2d 667; 1990 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7900


May 9, 1990, Argued  
June 28, 1990 



DISPOSITION: Order and judgment affirmed, with costs to plaintiff.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought review of the decision of the Supreme Court, Dutchess County (New York), which granted a motion by plaintiff individual and a cross motion by defendant fire department for summary judgment and declared Local Laws, 1988, No. 1 of the City of Poughkeepsie (N.Y.) to be unconstitutional. The supreme court enjoined the city from appropriating or spending any of the tax proceeds until the proper application was resolved.


OVERVIEW: The city adopted a local law amending its charter laws. The new local law authorized the city's chief fiscal officer to spend the tax proceeds generated by N.Y. Ins. Law §§ 9104, 9105 for acquisition, construction, and maintenance of firehouses and the purchase and care of the requisite vehicles and equipment necessary for the operation of a municipal fire department. The individual commenced an action seeking a judgment declaring the local law unconstitutional. The supreme court granted the individual's motion and the fire department's cross motion for summary judgment, denied the city's cross motion, and declared the local law invalid and unconstitutional. The court affirmed and held that the tax proceeds generated by §§ 9104, 9105 were intended for the personal use and benefit of municipal fire department members and could not be used for general purposes that were normally considered municipal charges, such as those set forth in the local law. Further, the legislature did not delegate any rule-making authority and instead, preempted the field. Thus, the court held that because the local law was inconsistent with the legislative intent, it was invalid.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the supreme court's decision holding the local law unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

Parties -- Proper Parties -- Appeal

1. A party which is in default in pleading cannot be considered a party to an appeal despite its participation before Supreme Court.

Judgments -- Res Judicata -- Full and Fair Opportunity to Litigate

2. In an action testing the constitutionality of a local law which purported to authorize the spending of the tax proceeds generated by Insurance Law §§ 9104 and 9105 on maintaining and purchasing equipment for a municipal fire department, a prior judicial decision is not dispositive of the case on res judicata grounds since the municipality did not have a full and fair opportunity in the prior case to litigate the issue of whether it could designate the manner in which the tax proceeds could be expended; the central issue in the prior matter was whether the tax proceeds should be divided between paid and volunteer firemen.

Municipal Corporations -- Fire Departments -- Use of Proceeds Generated by Tax on Insurance Companies

3. The proceeds of taxes paid by foreign and alien fire insurance companies pursuant to Insurance Law §§ 9104 and 9105 are intended for the personal use and benefit of municipal fire department members and cannot be used for general purposes that are normally considered municipal charges; accordingly, a local law, which purported to authorize the spending of the tax proceeds on maintaining and purchasing equipment for a municipal fire department, is invalid as an unconstitutional exercise of power (see, NY Const, art IX, § 2 [c]). It was not the intent of the State Legislature that the tax proceeds be used for such general municipal purposes as the construction, maintenance and repair of firehouses or other fire equipment; rather, the legislation is clear that the tax proceeds must be expended solely for the personal benefit of the fire department members. The local legislation need not be reviewed in the context of two time frames, i.e., before and after recent amendments to the two statutes, since the intent of the Legislature did not change and the amendments were merely procedural clarifications.

Municipal Corporations -- Fire Departments -- Use of Proceeds Generated by Tax on Insurance Companies

4. The proceeds of taxes paid by foreign and alien fire insurance companies pursuant to Insurance Law §§ 9104 and 9105 are intended for the personal use and benefit of municipal fire department members and cannot be used for general purposes that are normally considered municipal charges; accordingly, a local law, which purported to authorize the spending of the tax proceeds on maintaining and purchasing equipment for a municipal fire department, is invalid since the State Legislature has preempted any local exercise of police power by the passage of and amendments to the statutes, which specifically prohibit contrary municipal enactments. The Legislature's intent to preempt may be implied from the nature of the subject matter being regulated, and the purpose and scope of the State legislative scheme; it is clear that the State has evidenced its intent to occupy the field.

 

535 of 992 DOCUMENTS


New York v. State


No. 40666


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


158 A.D.2d 169; 557 N.Y.S.2d 914; 1990 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7899



June 28, 1990 



DISPOSITION: Order, Supreme Court, New York County, entered on or about May 22, 1990, unanimously affirmed, without costs and without disbursements.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Supreme Court in New York County (New York) denied a motion by plaintiff city for summary judgment declaring 1989 N.Y. Laws ch. 773, as amended by 1990 Laws ch. 17, unconstitutional, and granted a cross motion by defendant state for summary judgment declaring the law constitutional. The law provided for a referendum for the voters of one borough to vote on secession from the city. The city appealed.


OVERVIEW: The law was designed primarily to inform the legislature as to the extent of secessionist sentiment in the borough and the manner in which the residents would have governed themselves if permitted to do so; not the least concern was shown for public sentiment in the other four boroughs. Rejecting the ripeness argument, the court determined that to say that this issue was not ripe was like saying that events were not ripe in the harbor of Charleston, South Carolina, in April 1861. The court added that a question of equal protection was raised: why should only one borough have been given the franchise to stop secession? Affirming the decision, the court answered that while the residents of the other four boroughs would have felt an impact, the effect upon the borough seceding was of a significantly more substantial and direct character. Only they would have been subject to the general governmental powers of the new government. The court held that this, and the state's apparent interest in facilitating that community's self-determination, amply justified a classification that prevented the residents of the other four communities from exercising a veto power over secession.


OUTCOME: In the suit brought by the city against the state requesting that the law permitting one borough to vote on the idea of secession be ruled unconstitutional, the court affirmed the grant of summary judgment declaring the law constitutional.


HEADNOTES

Courts -- Advisory Opinions -- Ripe Controversy -- Constitutionality of Legislation Providing Mechanism for Part of City to Secede

1. In an action seeking a declaration as to the constitutionality of Laws of 1989 (ch 773), which set forth a process by which the Borough of Staten Island may be separated from the City of New York and established as a new city, the issue of whether the State can carve a new city out of the territory of one already existing without a home rule message from the already existing city is ripe for determination since the legislation in question, which authorizes a series of referenda upon which only residents of Staten Island may vote, is not purely advisory in nature: it is only in the event of affirmative votes that further legislation will be required to effect a secession, and a negative vote in either referendum will stop the process cold. Staten Islanders are thereby given a veto power over secession denied the residents of the other New York City boroughs; the question of why should Staten Islanders be given the franchise to stop secession and other New Yorkers not, raises a question of equal protection ripe for adjudication.

Municipal Corporations -- Home Rule Powers -- State Legislation Providing Mechanism for Part of City to Secede -- Lack of Home Rule Message

2. Laws of 1989 (ch 773), which provide a process by which the Borough of Staten Island may be separated from the City of New York and established as a new city, and which provide that only residents of Staten Island may vote on a series of referenda pertaining to secession, is not unconstitutional; although the legislation makes no provision for a home rule message from the City of New York (see, NY Const, art IX, § 2 [b] [2]), the State is free to legislate matters of municipal boundaries unfettered by home rule constraints (see, NY Const, art IX, § 2 [a]; art X, § 1), since legislation dealing with matters of State concern, albeit of localized application and having a direct effect on the most basic of local interests, does not violate the Constitution's home rule provisions.

Municipal Corporations -- Home Rule Powers -- State Legislation Providing Mechanism for Part of City to Secede -- Restricting Franchise on Referenda -- Equal Protection

3. Laws of 1989 (ch 773), which provide a process by which the Borough of Staten Island may be separated from the City of New York and established as a new city, and which provide that only residents of Staten Island may vote on a series of referenda pertaining to secession, is not unconstitutional; in a "single-shot" referendum, if the classification between those who are specially interested and those who are not is reasonably precise, the latter class may be validly excluded from the franchise where the issue to be submitted will have a disproportionate impact on an identifiable group of voters. Given the extraordinarily wide latitude that States have in the creation of various types of political subdivisions, the State can legitimately adopt a geographic classification based upon the boundaries of a proposed new political subdivision to be created if approved by the electorate of the smaller, albeit significant, separating community. Although the residents of the other boroughs of New York City would bear an impact from the proposed separation, the impact upon Staten Islanders would be significantly more substantial and direct; accordingly, that disparate impact and the State's interest in facilitating that community's self-determination, justify a classification that prevents the residents of the other boroughs from exercising a veto power over the question of secession. The interests of the residents of the other boroughs are adequately represented by their State legislators.

 

536 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Anderson v. Eli Lilly & Co.


No. 59943


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


158 A.D.2d 91; 557 N.Y.S.2d 981; 1990 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7901


May 8. 1990, Argued  
June 28, 1990 



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, without [***2] costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Both plaintiffs and defendant drug manufacturers challenged an order from the Supreme Court, Greene County (New York), which granted motions dismissing certain claims, including those for emotional distress and plaintiff spouse's loss of consortium action. A motion by defendants to preclude application of collateral estoppel against them was granted.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff alleged that her exposure to the drug diethylstilbestrol (DES) while she was in utero caused abnormalities in her reproductive system, which resulted in a hysterectomy. She brought an action for damages against a number of defendant manufacturers. Plaintiff's husband asserted a loss of consortium claim because plaintiff was infertile. The lower court dismissed the husband's claim and held that collateral estoppel did not apply. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that the loss of childbearing opportunity, standing alone and unconnected to any other injury to the marital relationship, was not an element of loss of consortium. Moreover, the revival provision of the toxic tort statute did not create a cause of action where none otherwise existed. Collateral estoppel was inapplicable in this case because plaintiff's mother ingested DES in 1950, and the time difference from that in Bichler v. Lilly & Co., 55 N.Y.2d 571 (1982) related to a difference in the foreseeability issues.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of plaintiff's claim for emotional distress and plaintiff spouse's claim for loss of consortium. The grant of defendant manufacturers' motions to preclude application of collateral estoppel against them was affirmed, and the denial of plaintiffs' motion for summary judgment based on collateral estoppel against defendants was also affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Damages  -- Loss of Consortium -- Inability of Wife to Bear Children -- Exposure to Toxic Substance

1. In an action to recover damages allegedly caused by plaintiff's exposure to the drug diethylstilbestrol (DES) while she was in utero, the cause of action for loss of consortium advanced by plaintiff's husband, seeking damages for the loss of the ability to have natural children of the marriage, was properly dismissed since consortium represents the marital partners' interest in the continuance of the marital relationship as it existed at its inception, not some guarantee that the marital partners are free of any preexisting latent injuries; recovery may not be permitted on the ground that plaintiff's preexisting injury was undiscoverable at the time of the marriage and was caused by a toxic substance, since public policy would not be served by extending loss of consortium liability upon speculation that plaintiff's husband would not have entered into the marriage had he known of plaintiff's injuries. Moreover, not only did the allegedly actionable conduct not occur during the marriage, loss of the ability to have natural children is not an element of loss of consortium, which includes such elements as love, companionship, society, sexual relations and solace, none of which was particularly pleaded by plaintiff's husband.

Damages -- Loss of Consortium -- Inability of Wife to Bear Children -- Toxic Tort Revival Statute

2. In an action to recover damages allegedly caused by plaintiff's exposure to the drug diethylstilbestrol (DES) while she was in utero, the cause of action for loss of consortium advanced by plaintiff's husband, seeking damages for the loss of the ability to have natural children of the marriage, was properly dismissed, since loss of the ability to bear children is not an element of loss of consortium; the fact that a toxic substance is involved does not require a contrary result, and the revival provision of the toxic tort statute (L 1986, ch 682, § 4), which is a purely procedural device reviving an otherwise time-barred remedy, did not create a substantive cause of action where none otherwise exists.

Judgments -- Collateral Estoppel -- Toxic Torts

3. In an action to recover damages allegedly caused by plaintiff's exposure to the drug diethylstilbestrol (DES) while she was in utero a jury finding in a prior DES case, which resolved questions concerning whether a reasonably prudent drug manufacturer should have foreseen in 1953 that DES might cause damaging side effects and, if so, whether a reasonably prudent drug manufacturer would have performed certain tests before marketing the drug, and whether those tests would have shown the side effects, shall not be given collateral estoppel effect where the instant plaintiff's mother ingested DES in 1950, since the three-year time difference in the cases and the possibility of scientific advances during those three years raises the question of whether that which was foreseeable and discoverable in 1953 could have been foreseen and discovered in 1950.
