Oak Beach Inn Corp. v. Babylon


No. 3631E


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


162 A.D.2d 689; 557 N.Y.S.2d 404; 1990 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8156


April 10, 1990, Submitted  
June 25, 1990 



DISPOSITION: ORDERED that the order is affirmed insofar as appealed from, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In consolidated actions of plaintiffs, town and others, to recover damages for breach of a stipulation of settlement and for specific performance of the stipulation, defendants, corporation and others, appealed an order of the Supreme Court, Suffolk County (New York), which denied their motion to set aside the stipulation as void and for summary judgment dismissing the complaints.


OVERVIEW: Under the stipulation of settlement, the town had an option to purchase town-owned land upon which the corporation was located. Also, the town was to build a parking lot, and the corporation was to reimburse it for expenses. Defendants filed a motion for summary judgment setting aside the stipulation. The trial court denied summary judgment. On appeal, the court affirmed. It found that the terms of the stipulation did not exceed the town's constitutional or statutory authority, nor were they contrary to public policy. The town's option to purchase the land, the court found, did not constitute an improper delegation of the town's authority to settle claims because the stipulation contained a procedure by which the fair market value could be determined for the land. It further found that the parking lot arrangement did not constitute an unconstitutional pledge of the town's credit or a town undertaking for a private purpose because the town properly exercised its governmental function in agreeing to provide more parking facilities given the traffic problems attendant to inadequate parking facilities.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's denial of defendants' motion to set aside the stipulation and its denial of defendants' motion for summary judgment.

People v. Watson


[No Number In Original]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


162 A.D.2d 1015; 557 N.Y.S.2d 803; 1990 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9785



June 22, 1990  
June 22, 1990, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Leave to Appeal Granted October 4, 1990.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a judgment from the Wyoming County Court (New York), which convicted defendant of promoting prison contraband in the first degree, pursuant to N.Y. Penal Law § 205.25(2).


OVERVIEW: While defendant was an inmate at a correctional facility, defendant was found to have been in possession of heroin and was also convicted of criminal possession of a controlled substance in the seventh degree, pursuant to N.Y. Penal Law § 220.03. On appeal of defendant's conviction for promoting prison contraband in the first degree only, the court held that the trial court properly convicted defendant. Specifically, the court rejected defendant's argument that heroin was not "dangerous contraband" within the meaning of N.Y. Penal Law § 205.00(4). Instead, the court noted that the evidence demonstrated that even a small amount of heroin could have been potentially dangerous to the user and to others, and that its presence in a facility which housed large numbers of prisoners who were known to have used the drug could have resulted in disruptive and dangerous behavior among the inmates. In addition, the court held that N.Y. Penal Law § 205.25 was not unconstitutionally vague because the statute was sufficiently explicit to inform a reasonable person of the nature of the offense and what it was that the law prohibited or commanded.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment convicting defendant of promoting prison contraband in the first degree.

 

539 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Allied Grocers Coop., Inc. v. Tax Appeals Tribunal


No. 60106


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


162 A.D.2d 791; 557 N.Y.S.2d 707; 1990 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7195



June 14, 1990 



DISPOSITION: Determination confirmed, and petition dismissed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner former licensee had been licensed as a wholesale cigarette dealer and stamping agent until it pleaded guilty to class B misdemeanors, arising out of a pricing scheme. Pursuant to legislative amendments, including N.Y. Tax Law § 480(2)(b), the former licensee's application for relicensing was rejected. Respondent Tax Appeal Tribunal (New York) affirmed. The former licensee appealed.


OVERVIEW: The former licensee argued that the refusal to relicense was an impermissible retroactive application of the amendments, that the amendments constituted an unconstitutional ex post facto law, and that the refusal to relicense violated its due process rights. This court affirmed and rejected all of the former licensee's arguments. First, the former licensee's license to act as a wholesaler and stamping agent created no vested right, but was merely a privilege extended by the state subject to alteration by the imposition of reasonable restrictions. The legislative determination to condition relicensing on the absence of a criminal conviction for violating the cigarette tax laws was reasonable. Second, the amendments were not retroactive when made to apply to future transactions, merely because such transactions related to and were founded upon antecedent events. Third, there was no due process violation because the former licensee had no vested right in its license and the amendments were intended for legitimate public purposes such as increased competition in the industry and enhanced cigarette tax receipts.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the rejection of the former licensee's application for relicensing.

Hawes v. State


No. 1485E


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


161 A.D.2d 745; 556 N.Y.S.2d 101; 1990 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6690


May 3, 1990, Argued  
May 29, 1990 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended November 15, 1990.



DISPOSITION: ORDERED that the order is affirmed, with one bill of costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff owner appealed from an order of the Supreme Court, Suffolk County (New York), which granted the separate motions to dismiss the complaint of defendant state and others and denied the owner's cross motion or partial summary judgment in an action for a judgment declaring that the moratorium on development along a creek was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The owner possessed an undeveloped parcel of realty abutting a creek. Soon after the owner had obtained conditional preliminary subdivision approval from the town, the state legislature imposed a moratorium on development along the creek in order to study the area for possible inclusion in the state's Wild, Scenic and Recreational Rivers System Act. The moratorium was to expire in five years or when the creek was added to the state's wild, scenic and recreational rivers system, whichever occurred first. On appeal, the court affirmed and held that the owner failed to file a complete application to the New York Department of Environmental Conservation (EC) for a permit exempting him from the moratorium and thus, there still had been no decision rendered by the EC. The court held that by first resorting to the courts instead of exhausting available administrative remedies, the owner had acted prematurely and was not entitled to any relief.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order, which granted defendants' separate motions to dismiss the complaint and denied the owner's cross motion or partial summary judgment in an action, for a judgment declaring that the moratorium on development along a creek were unconstitutional.

 

542 of 992 DOCUMENTS


People v. Maxam


No. 58218


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


161 A.D.2d 961; 557 N.Y.S.2d 534; 1990 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6407



May 24, 1990 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order of the County Court of Warren County (New York), which found defendant guilty of misconduct in making a false statement on an election petition pursuant to N.Y. Elec. Law § 17-122 and imposed a $ 500 fine.


OVERVIEW: A petition was filed that designated defendant as the Republican Party candidate for the office of town supervisor, but it was later discovered that defendant signed the "statement of witness" on the petition although she was not present at the time of several of the other signatures. Defendant appealed her conviction for making a false statement, asserting that she was not guilty because she signed the statement without reading it on the last day for the filing of designating petitions and because the signatures were genuine and not forged. The court disagreed and affirmed, holding that: (1) § 17-122(7) provided that any subscribing witness to an election petition who made a false statement thereon was guilty of a misdemeanor; (2) defendant signed the petition's statement of witness but did not witness the signatures as stated in the petition; and (3) the misdemeanor required proof of only general, not specific, intent, which was established by her willful act. The purpose of the act was to avoid fraudulent practices, confusion, and threats to the integrity of the system, which could not be defeated by later proving that the signatures were genuine.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order that found defendant guilty of misconduct in making a false statement on an election petition and that imposed a $ 500 fine.

In re Claim of Sortina

Willett v. Dugan


No. 58603


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


161 A.D.2d 900; 557 N.Y.S.2d 465; 1990 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 5796



May 17, 1990 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner taxpayer appealed a determination of respondent Tax Appeals Tribunal (New York), sustaining an unincorporated business tax assessment imposed under former N.Y. Tax Law art. 23n.


OVERVIEW: The court found that the tax tribunal's determination that the taxpayer was not engaged in the practice of a profession within the meaning of former N.Y. Tax Law § 703 (c) was supported by substantial evidence. The taxpayer was afforded due process in that she was given adequate information concerning compliance with the Tax Law. Published rules and regulations, as well as decisions of the State Tax Commission were available to her. The reversal of a prior stipulation was of no consequence to the taxpayer because it was only binding in the earlier proceeding and could have no effect on the current proceeding. Further, there was no evidence that two members of the tribunal were prejudiced by the taxpayer's previous disputes with the Commission. The Legislature's repeal of former N.Y. Tax Law art. 23 was not a recognition by the Legislature that the law was unconstitutional. The fact that different occupations were afforded different tax treatment under former art. 23 did not constitute the invidious discrimination prohibited by the Equal Protection Clauses of the Federal or State Constitutions.


OUTCOME: The court confirmed the tax tribunal's determination.

Turtoro v. Board of Educ. Retirement System


No. 3882E


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


161 A.D.2d 650; 555 N.Y.S.2d 813; 1990 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6023


April 24, 1990, Argued  
May 14, 1990 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner employee challenged the judgment of the Supreme Court of Kings County (New York), which denied his petition to compel respondent board of education to recompute the employee's accident disability retirement allowance.


OVERVIEW: The employee was hired by the board and joined the board's retirement system. Upon sustaining an injury in the line of duty, the retirement system approved his retirement for accident disability. His allowance was calculated by applying N.Y. Retire. & Soc. Sec. § 444, as originally enacted. The employee argued that his allowance should have been calculated under the amended § 444. The disagreed and determined that the board correctly determined that the statutory scheme and the legislative purpose of § 444, as amended, required an interpretation that would reduce the maximum retirement benefit of its members. Therefore, the board did not act unreasonably or in an arbitrary or capricious manner in applying the more beneficial earlier version of § 444. The court affirmed the judgment.


OUTCOME: The dismissal of employee's petition that sought to compel the board to recalculate his disability retirement allowance was affirmed.

 

546 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Prue v. Hunt


[No Number In Original]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


157 A.D.2d 160; 558 N.Y.S.2d 1016; 1990 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9081



May 11, 1990 



DISPOSITION: Judgment unanimously reversed, on the law, with costs, and matter remitted to respondent for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion of Lawton, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner police officer sought review of a decision of the Supreme Court in Onandaga County (New York), which denied his petition to review a determination of respondents, police chief and commissioner, that terminated the officer from his position. The petition also sought reinstatement of the officer with back pay. The trial court stayed the removal of the officer from his employment pending determination of the appeal.


OVERVIEW: The officer was injured in an accident that was outside of work and he had used up his sick leave and personal days. The officer, after completing an extended paid sick leave, demanded reinstatement. His request was supported by a letter from his physician that stated he could return to work at a desk job only. The application for reinstatement was rejected and the officer was terminated pursuant to N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 73. The officer was offered a hearing within five days of receipt of the termination notice. The officer commenced a N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 proceeding contesting his termination and demanding reinstatement with back pay. The trial court affirmed respondents' motion to dismiss and stayed the removal of the officer from his employment pending determination of the appeal. On appeal, the court reversed the judgment and held that in instances such as Civ. Serv. Law § 73 where termination was discretionary, a tenured civil employee had to be afforded a pretermination hearing and that a posttermination hearing was insufficient to satisfy the due process requirements of the Fourteenth Amendment. The court remitted the case to respondents for a hearing.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's decision to affirm respondents' motion to dismiss and remitted the case to respondents for a hearing on whether the officer should be terminated.


HEADNOTES

Civil Service -- Police -- Termination from Employment -- Hearing -- Due Process

1. In a proceeding to review a determination terminating petitioner from his position as a police officer and to order petitioner's reinstatement with back pay, the judgment denying the petition is reversed, the determination annulled and the matter remitted for a hearing on whether petitioner should be terminated pursuant to Civil Service Law § 73, where petitioner was not afforded a pretermination hearing as is his right as a tenured public employee (see, Cleveland Bd. of Educ. v Loudermill, 470 U.S. 532); notwithstanding the fact that petitioner's termination was justified, petitioner should have been afforded an opportunity to be heard on why the discretion provided respondent under section 73 should not have been exercised in his favor, and to be heard before termination with respect to his ability to perform light duty and when he would be able to return to full duty, where the reason for petitioner's termination arose from his failure to submit required medical documentation to establish that he was able to perform the duties of his position following his extended sick leave. Respondent's offer of a posttermination hearing, which petitioner refused, did not satisfy the requirement of due process.

Civil Service -- Police -- Termination from Employment -- Hearing

2. In a proceeding to review a determination terminating petitioner from his position as a police officer and to order petitioner's reinstatement with back pay, the judgment dismissing the petition is reversed, the determination annulled and the matter remitted for a hearing on whether petitioner should be terminated pursuant to Civil Service Law § 73, where petitioner was not afforded a pretermination hearing as is his right as a tenured public employee (see, Cleveland Bd. of Educ. v Loudermill, 470 U.S. 532); although petitioner was on unpaid leave status at the time of his termination and section 73 grants him preferential rights to be rehired, petitioner was still deprived of a substantial property right when he was afforded merely a posttermination hearing, since his property rights existed by reason of his employment, not solely by reason of the pay he received while working. Petitioner was entitled to other employee benefits during the period he was on nonpay status for an extended sick leave, and those benefits would have terminated upon his discharge; moreover, once petitioner was terminated, his rights to reinstatement were much more limited.

Statutes -- Construction -- Constitutionality

3. In a proceeding to review a determination terminating petitioner from his position as a police officer and to order petitioner's reinstatement with back pay, the judgment dismissing the petition is reversed, the determination annulled and the matter remitted for a hearing on whether petitioner should be terminated pursuant to Civil Service Law § 73, where petitioner was not afforded a pretermination hearing as is his right as a tenured public employee; there is no need to exercise discretion and convert the proceeding into a plenary action to declare section 73 unconstitutional per se, since it is sufficient to interpret the statute to include the requirements of pretermination notification and an opportunity to be heard.

In re BABY GIRL B. APPEAL NO. 1


[No Number In Original]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


161 A.D.2d 1201; 558 N.Y.S.2d 875; 1990 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9225



May 11, 1990, Filed

 

548 of 992 DOCUMENTS


520 East 81st Street Assoc. v. Lenox Hill Hosp.


No. 38553


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


157 A.D.2d 138; 555 N.Y.S.2d 697; 1990 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 5308



May 10, 1990 



DISPOSITION: Order and judgment (one paper) of the Supreme Court, New York County, entered on June 15, 1988, unanimously reversed, on the law, without costs and without disbursements, plaintiff's motion for partial summary judgment granted, a declaration made that defendant Lenox Hill is not entitled to renewal leases with respect to the subject apartments and that Lenox Hill employees who occupied said apartments as their primary residences at the expiration of the March 9, 1982 leases are so entitled, and the cross motion denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff lessor appealed from an order and judgment of the Supreme Court in New York County (New York), which granted a cross motion by defendant lessee for summary judgment, dismissing plaintiff's complaint seeking a declaratory judgment that it was not required to offer renewal leases to defendant, as a not-for-profit hospital and a named tenant, under the rent stabilization laws.


OVERVIEW: Defendant lessee was a not-for-profit hospital that subleased its apartments to its nurses and wanted to take advantage of the rent stabilization laws, which provided that defendant was exempt from the primary residence requirement. Plaintiff lessor brought an action against defendant, seeking a declaration that defendant was not entitled to renewal leases because it had not maintained the apartment as a primary residence. Plaintiff challenged the constitutionality of a statute which amended existing law and specifically provided an exception to the nonprimary residence exemption in the rent stabilization law where a not-for-profit hospital was the lease's named tenant. The lower court granted defendant's motion for summary judgment because defendant, as not-for-profit hospital, was the tenant that was to be protected by the corrective legislative amendment. Plaintiff appealed the judgment. The court reversed, holding that rent stabilization, as applied to defendant, constituted a taking because it granted a perpetual tenancy to defendant due to the fact that defendant would always exist and be able to fulfill vacancies.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's grant of summary judgment for defendant lessee, in an action by plaintiff lessor for a declaratory judgment that plaintiff was not required to offer renewal leases to defendant, as a not-for-profit hospital and a named tenant, under rent stabilization laws. Plaintiff's partial motion for summary judgment was granted.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and Tenant -- Rent Regulation -- Renewal Lease -- Not-for-Profit Hospital

1. A not-for-profit hospital, which is the named tenant on expired leases for rent-stabilized apartments in a building converted to condominium ownership, is not entitled to renewal leases for the subject apartments pursuant to Laws of 1984 (ch 940), which amended the Rent Stabilization Law to provide that such hospitals are entitled to renewal leases where they sublet the apartments to "affiliated" personnel (see, Administrative Code of City of New York § 26-504 [a] [1] [f]) and permitted such subletting without the consent of the landlord, since the statute constitutes an unconstitutional taking of the landlord's property; however, inasmuch as statutes should be interpreted to harmonize with the Constitution, chapter 940 shall be interpreted as requiring the landlord to offer a renewal lease to those hospital employees who occupied the subject apartments as a primary residence at the expiration of the previous leases. Such an interpretation results in the continued occupancy of those apartments by persons occupying them as their primary residence for as long as they continue to do so, and inasmuch as the renewal lease is offered to persons, rather than to the hospital, an institution with a virtually limitless existence, the landlord has a reasonable expectation of a vacancy at some point, whereupon it may exercise the prerogatives of ownership.

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Notification of Attorney-General -- Role of Court

2. In an action challenging the constitutionality of Laws of 1984 (ch 940), the lower court erred in not considering plaintiff's argument on the ground that the Attorney-General had not been notified that the constitutionality of a statute was in issue, since both CPLR 1012 (b) and Executive Law § 71 require that it is the responsibility of the court, and not the parties, to give such notice; that the court failed to so notify the Attorney-General or direct plaintiff to do so poses no obstacle to appellate review, since the Attorney-General has been notified that the issue is being raised on appeal and served with a copy of plaintiff-appellant's brief.

Landlord and Tenant -- Rent Regulation -- Renewal Lease -- Constitutionality of Statute Mandating Renewal Leases to Not-for-Profit Hospitals which Sublet Apartments to Employees -- Physical Taking

3. Laws of 1984 (ch 940), which amended the Rent Stabilization Law to provide that not-for-profit hospitals are entitled to renewal leases where they sublet rent-stabilized apartments to "affiliated" personnel (see, Administrative Code of City of New York § 26-504 [a] [1] [f]) and permitted such subletting without the consent of the landlord, is an unconstitutional taking of the landlord's property since it results in the permanent physical occupation of apartments leased to the hospital by transferring the landlord's reversionary interest in all the apartments covered by the leases to the hospital, the existence of which is, to all intents and purposes, perpetual; thus, the landlord is effectively precluded from regaining possession of its property. Moreover, inasmuch as the apartments have been converted to condominium ownership, the landlord has also been deprived of the opportunity to sell the apartments free and clear of tenancies since the perpetual existence of the hospital means that no vacancies will ever occur. Although the hospital must sublet the apartments to employees who must use them as their primary residence, that requirement merely places a minimal restriction on the hospital's indefeasible right to possession.

Constitutional Law -- "Takings" Clause -- Applicability  to States

4. The "Takings" Clause of the Fifth Amendment of the US Constitution applies to the States through the Fourteenth Amendment.

Landlord and Tenant -- Rent Regulation -- Renewal Lease -- Constitutionality of Statute Mandating Renewal Leases to Not-for-Profit Hospitals which Sublet Apartments to Employees -- Regulatory Taking

5. Laws of 1984 (ch 940), which amended the Rent Stabilization Law to provide that not-for-profit hospitals are entitled to renewal leases where they sublet rent-stabilized apartments to "affiliated" personnel (see, Administrative Code of City of New York § 26-504 [a] [1] [f]) and permitted such subletting without the consent of the landlord, is an unconstitutional regulatory taking of a landlord's property since it forces a landlord alone to bear a public burden which in all fairness and justice should be borne by the public as a whole, and fails to advance the purpose of the Rent Stabilization Law. By requiring a landlord to offer renewal leases to an institution whose existence is for all practical purposes perpetual, chapter 940 would increase the dislocation of long-term tenants, thereby contravening the purpose of rent stabilization, viz., to protect residential tenants and their families during an acute housing shortage; dislocation would be increased by requiring the hospital to remove long-term occupants if they terminate their employment with it.

Landlord and Tenant -- Rent Regulation -- Renewal Lease -- Constitutionality of Statute Mandating Renewal Leases to Not-for-Profit Hospitals which Sublet Apartments to Employees -- Regulatory Taking

6. Laws of 1984 (ch 940), which amended the Rent Stabilization Law to provide that not-for-profit hospitals are entitled to renewal leases where they sublet rent-stabilized apartments to "affiliated" personnel (see, Administrative Code of City of New York § 26-504 [a] [1] [f]) and permitted such subletting without the consent of the landlord, is an unconstitutional regulatory taking of the landlord's property, notwithstanding the fact that the statute might promote the public welfare by enabling such hospitals to continue to provide adequate health care services by offering low-rent apartments to its employees, since the Legislature has never declared a health care emergency and landlords should not be required to assume the burden resulting from corrective legislation when fairness dictates that the burden should be borne by society at large. Moreover, the Rent Stabilization Law was enacted to ameliorate a declared housing emergency, not to further a policy of promoting better health care, and it is difficult to accept the notion that the availability of the 39 apartments in issue alleviates a health care emergency to any discernible degree.

Landlord and Tenant -- Rent Regulation -- Renewal Lease -- Constitutionality of Statute Mandating Renewal Leases to Not-for-Profit Hospitals which Sublet Apartments to Employees -- Deprivation of Economically Viable Use of Property

7. Laws of 1984 (ch 940), which amended the Rent Stabilization Law to provide that not-for-profit hospitals are entitled to renewal leases where they sublet rent-stabilized apartments to "affiliated" personnel (see, Administrative Code of City of New York § 26-504 [a] [1] [f]) and permitted such subletting without the consent of the landlord, is an unconstitutional regulatory taking of the landlord's property, since it deprives the owner of an economically viable use of his property by expropriating his expectation of a residual/reversionary interest in the apartments, which would be lost if an institution with a near perpetual existence is entitled to renewal leases; even in rent-regulated apartments, a landlord has a reasonable expectation at some point of a vacancy, which will result in a reversion of possession to him, but by interpreting chapter 940 as reposing a permanent tenancy in the hospital, the landlord has been denied his reversionary interest and the resale value of the apartments, which have been converted to condominium ownership.

Landlord and Tenant -- Rent Regulation -- Renewal Lease -- Constitutionality of Statute Mandating Renewal Leases to Not-for-Profit Hospitals which Sublet Apartments to Employees -- Compensation to Landlord

8. Laws of 1984 (ch 940), which amended the Rent Stabilization Law to provide that not-for-profit hospitals are entitled to renewal leases where they sublet rent-stabilized apartments to "affiliated" personnel (see, Administrative Code of City of New York § 26-504 [a] [1] [f]) and permitted such subletting without the consent of the landlord, is an unconstitutional regulatory taking of the landlord's property, and the fact that the statute provides for a 15% vacancy surcharge if there has been no vacancy increase within a prior seven-year period does not refute the fact that there has been an unconstitutional taking, since there is a vast difference between the profits to be realized from sales of the units, the apartments having been converted to condominiums, and those which would accrue from continued rental; the expectation of profits on sales of the apartments is defeated by the creation of a tenancy in perpetuity in favor of the hospital. Moreover, a taking cannot be refuted merely by pointing out that the statute purportedly provides for some compensation.

Landlord and Tenant -- Rent Regulation -- Renewal Lease -- Not-for-Profit Hospitals which Sublet Apartments to Employees -- Notice of Nonrenewal

9. In an action challenging the constitutionality of Laws of 1984 (ch 940), which amended the Rent Stabilization Law to provide that not-for-profit hospitals are entitled to renewal leases where they sublet rent-stabilized apartments to "affiliated" personnel (see, Administrative Code of City of New York § 26-504 [a] [1] [f]) and permitted such subletting without the consent of the landlord, a hospital is not entitled to a renewal lease as a matter of law on the ground that its landlord failed to serve a 120-to-150-day notice of nonrenewal since the notice requirement of section 60 of the former Code of the Rent Stabilization Association of the City of New York, Inc. is inapplicable to a constitutional takings claim, and chapter 940 has been held to constitute an unconstitutional deprivation of a landlord's property.

 

549 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Waste Stream Management, Inc. v. St. Lawrence County


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


156 A.D.2d 111; 555 N.Y.S.2d 213; 1990 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4720; 1990-2 Trade Cas. (CCH) P69,174



April 26, 1990 



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, engaged in the business of collecting and hauling of wastes, appealed the decision of the Supreme Court in St. Lawrence County (New York), which converted a proceeding pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 into an action for declaratory judgment and granted defendants' motion for summary judgment. Plaintiff had been denied exceptions related to processing of recyclable materials by the St. Lawrence County Solid Waste Disposal Authority.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff, engaged in the business of collecting and hauling recyclable materials, was denied certain exceptions to the standards adopted by the county waste disposal authority. Plaintiff commenced an action seeking to invalidate the authority's resolutions and to invalidate the local law creating the authority. The trial court converted the action to a declaratory judgment action and granted summary judgment for defendants, county and authority. Plaintiff appealed. The court stated that county local laws were entitled to a presumption of constitutionality and that administrative standards were considered legitimate as long within the delegated authority of the administrative body. The court held that the delegation of power to the authority was not improper, that standards developed by the authority were within the legislatively delegated mandate, and that their exercise had been neither arbitrary nor capricious. The judgment of the lower court was affirmed.


OUTCOME: Recognizing the presumption of constitutionality of legislative enactments and the delegation of power to the administrative body, the court held that the delegation of power to the authority was not improper, that the standard for granting exceptions was within the authority's powers, and that the authority's actions were neither arbitrary nor capricious. The court affirmed the ruling of the lower court.


HEADNOTES

Public Authorities -- Solid Waste Disposal Authority -- Delegation by County of Powers to Solid Waste Disposal Authority

1. The enactment by a county of a law providing that all solid waste generated within the county be delivered to a solid waste management facility within the county, and that the solid waste disposal authority charged with operating the facility could make exceptions to the aforementioned delivery requirement "as it from time to time may determine to be in the public interest" was not invalid; the penultimate sentence of Public Authorities Law § 2048-s provided express authority for the county's adoption of the challenged law, and the county does not lack the power to grant exceptions. In view of the close nexus of the authority to the county (see, Public Authorities Law § 2048-c [1], [5], [6] [b]; § 2048-g) and broad powers in waste management matters (see, Public Authorities Law § 2048-e [4], [5], [7]), the county's delegation of the power to grant exceptions to the authority was not an improper subdelegation of powers; rather, there has been a delegation to a body most capable of effectively performing the task.

Public Authorities -- Solid Waste Disposal Authority -- "Public Interest" Standard for Granting Exceptions from Waste Delivery Requirements

2. The enactment by a county of a law providing that all solid waste generated within the county be delivered to a solid waste management facility within the county, and that the solid waste disposal authority charged with operating the facility could make exceptions to the aforementioned delivery requirement "as it from time to time may determine to be in the public interest" was not invalid, and the "public interest" standard for granting exceptions from waste delivery requirements is not inadequate; inasmuch as the public interest standard is directly related to and limited by the general purposes of Public Authorities Law title 13-E, the enabling legislation pursuant to which the authority was established, and the policy expressed in ECL 27-0101, there is a sufficient framework to establish the parameters for agency action. In view of the complexities involved in the field of solid waste management, the standard is prescribed in as much detail as is reasonably practicable; accordingly, the public interest standard is adequate to channel the exercise of the authority's power.

Public Authorities -- Solid Waste Disposal Authority -- Discretionary Denial of Request that Not All Waste be Delivered to Facility Operated by Authority

3. The decision by a solid waste disposal authority to deny a request by a refuse hauler for an exception to a local law requiring all solid waste generated within the county serviced by the authority to be delivered to a facility operated by the authority was not irrational and is upheld; inasmuch as the decision was an exercise of discretion, specific findings of fact were not required. The authority made a reasoned decision since it thoroughly evaluated plaintiff's request, including their environmental and economic effects, before reaching its determination, and denied only so much of the request as failed to document the hauler's ability to successfully recycle the materials it sought not to deliver to the facility; by granting a generic exception, the authority effectively granted some of the hauler's requests (e.g., with respect to paper, glass and cans) and at the same time provided recycling opportunities to the private sector on a broader, more equitable basis. Moreover, the authority is maximizing resource recovery by committing to pursue a goal of recycling 40% of the county's waste by 1997 and by promoting the recovery of thermal and electrical energy (see, ECL 27-0701 [4]; Public Authorities Law § 2048-b [11]; the authority attempted to balance the county's need for short-term and long-term solid waste disposal management in conformity with State policy of resource recovery and the encouragement of recycling.

Monopolies -- Solid Waste Disposal Authority -- Restraints of Competition -- State Policy

4. A local law which requires all solid waste generated within a county to be delivered to a solid waste management facility operated by a county authority does not violate General Business Law § 340, which prohibits arrangements in restraint of competition; even assuming that a legislative act of the county could be considered a contract, agreement or arrangement within the purview of section 340, Public Authorities Law § 2048-s, which allows reasonable restraints and which is part of the authority's enabling legislation, superseded the provisions of section 340 (see, Public Authorities Law § 2048-w). Moreover, because of the State enabling legislation, there is an exception from the Federal antitrust laws.

Environmental Conservation -- Solid Waste Management Facility -- Recyclable Material

5. In a proceeding in which a refuse hauler challenged a county law requiring all solid waste generated within the county to be delivered to a solid waste management facility operated by a county authority, plaintiff's contention that recyclable material is not solid waste (see, Public Authorities Law § 2048-b [13]) does not present a real controversy because plaintiff has not identified the specific types of recyclable material which it contemplated purchasing or hauling; the issue is nonjusticiable since plaintiff has not sought adjudication of any specific dispute and there is no present prejudice to plaintiff. In any event, recyclable materials are included within the definition of "solid waste", but are segregated from the waste stream by means of "source separation" or "resource recovery" (see, Public Authorities Law § 2048-b [11], [16]).

 

550 of 992 DOCUMENTS


People v. Henderson


No. 96E, Ind. No. 4774/85


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


156 A.D.2d 92; 554 N.Y.S.2d 924; 1990 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4513



April 16, 1990 



DISPOSITION: Ordered that the judgment is reversed, on the law and the facts, and a new trial is ordered.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment of the Supreme Court (New York), which convicted defendant of sodomy, sexual abuse, and endangering the welfare of a child. Defendant contended that the court's employment of the procedures authorized under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 65, which permits the taking of the testimony of a child witness through the use of a live, two-way closed-circuit television, had been improper.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of sodomy and sexual abuse involving two young children. On appeal, defendant challenged the court's employment of the procedures authorized under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 65, which permits the taking of the testimony of a child witness through the use of live, two-way closed circuit television. The trial court permitted the two children to testify through the use of such a system based on a social worker's testimony that the children would benefit from the use of such a system because of what had happened to them and because they were fearful of defendant. On appeal, the conviction was reversed and a new trial ordered because the court found that defendant had been deprived of her U.S. Const. amend. VI right to confrontation by virtue of the manner in which § 65 was applied. The court concluded that the trial court's finding of vulnerability with respect to the children was not supported by the record because no evidence had been established that the children would have suffered severe mental or emotional harm if they had been required to testify without the closed-circuit system.


OUTCOME: The conviction was reversed and remanded for new trial because the children witnesses were allowed to testify via closed-circuit television despite the absence of a showing of exceptional vulnerability.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Right of Confrontation -- Child Victims -- Testimony Through Live Two-Way Closed-Circuit Television -- Showing of "Vulnerability"

1. In a sex crimes prosecution involving child victim witnesses, CPL article 65, which permits the taking of the testimony of a child witness through the use of live two-way closed-circuit television in certain circumstances, was applied in such a manner as to result in a deprivation of defendant's right to confrontation under the Sixth and Fourteenth Amendments to the U.S. Constitution and NY Constitution, article I, § 6, since the finding of the trial court that the two child victim witnesses were "vulnerable" within the meaning of CPL 65.10 (1); 65.20, which provide that a "vulnerable" child witness is one who will suffer severe mental or emotional harm if required to testify at a criminal proceeding in the same room with defendant, is not supported by the record; in making its finding of vulnerability, the court relied primarily on the testimony of a social worker who was of the opinion that all child witnesses in sexual abuse cases are "vulnerable". The testimony of the social worker lacked anything that would indicate why the two children in the instant case would suffer severe and emotional harm if forced to testify in open court; there is nothing extraordinary in the instant case which would warrant making an exception to the irreducible literal meaning of the Confrontation Clause.

Crimes -- Right of Confrontation -- Child Victims -- Testimony Through Live Two-Way Closed-Circuit Television -- Particularized Situations

2. CPL article 65, which permits the taking of the testimony of a child witness through the use of live, two-way closed-circuit television in certain circumstances, is not facially violative of a criminal defendant's right to confrontation (U.S. Const 6th, 14th Amends; NY Const, art I, § 6), since it contains sufficient safeguards to prevent the use of indiscriminate televised testimony; neither the State nor the Federal Constitution provides a defendant with the absolute right to total eye-to-eye confrontation in the defendant's physical presence, and where an appropriate individualized showing of necessity is made (e.g., that the child will suffer severe emotional harm if forced to testify in open court) and the infringement on the defendant's confrontation rights is kept to a minimum, the use of closed-circuit television technology is not constitutionally prescribed.

Crimes -- Trial -- Testimony of Children

3. In a sex crimes prosecution involving child victim witnesses, the trial court did not improvidently exercise its discretion in permitting the children to testify under oath (see, CPL 60.20 [2]) since the record established that they understood that they were obligated to tell the truth and that they understood the nature of the oath. Moreover, the trial court did not improvidently exercise its discretion by permitting a social worker to testify at trial as an expert witness with respect to whether the children suffered from sexually abused child syndrome.

 

551 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Simmons v. Popolizio


No. 39126


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


160 A.D.2d 368; 553 N.Y.S.2d 422; 1990 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3985



April 12, 1990 



DISPOSITION: Judgment entered dismissing the petition as untimely pursuant to CPLR 217, unanimously affirmed, without costs or disbursements.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner resident challenged the judgment of the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), which dismissed her motion to vacate a default judgment as untimely pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 217.


OVERVIEW: The resident moved into her grandmother's apartment to care for her. After her grandmother died, the resident sought remaining family member status. The application was denied, and a grievance hearing was scheduled. After the resident failed to appear for a hearing, a default was entered for respondents, chairman and housing authority. The decision denied the resident's grievance on the basis of her default and failure to pay use and occupancy. The resident sought, 17 months later, to vacate the default. The hearing officer denied the request. A N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 proceeding was dismissed as untimely. The court affirmed. The resident argued that the trial court improperly computed the statute of limitations. The court found that under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 217, a proceeding against a body or officer must be commenced within four months after the determination to be reviewed became final and binding. The resident waited 17 months. It was well established that an application to reconsider an administrative determination did not extend the four-month statute of limitations. Further, there was no support to a claim that the resident's constitutional rights were violated.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision to deny the resident's petition seeking to vacate a default judgment entered against her by the housing authority.

McComb v. Greenville


No. 3513E


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


160 A.D.2d 779; 553 N.Y.S.2d 825; 1990 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4208


Argued March 22, 1990  
April 9, 1990 



DISPOSITION: ORDERED that the judgment is affirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff property owner filed an action pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 against defendant town in the Supreme Court, Orange County (New York), which was converted to an action for a declaratory judgment with respect to Greenville, N.Y., Local Law No. 2 (1987). The trial court declared the law valid and upheld the town's determination that the owner's property was a public nuisance, for which the owner sought review.


OVERVIEW: The town's building and fire inspectors determined that the storage of tires on the property represented a fire and health hazard so the town's board notified the owner that his property was a "dangerous property" that had to be made safe within 60 days. The plaintiff acknowledged receiving the notice but did not attend the public hearing. Following the hearing, the board directed the building inspector to prepare contingency plans for a cleanup of the property in the event that the owner did not comply with the notice. The owner argued on appeal that the procedures in Local Law No. 2 deprived him of due process and that the law was invalid. The trial court held that the record established that the board's action was not arbitrary or capricious. The court held that: (1) because statutes were presumptively valid, the owner had the burden of establishing the unconstitutionality of the local law beyond a reasonable doubt, which he did not do; (2) the law was a valid exercise of the town's authority to regulate unsafe properties; and (3) the law provided notice and an opportunity to be heard before the town took any direct action, which was all that due process required.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision.

Jensen v. Old Westbury


Nos. 3433E, 3434E


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


160 A.D.2d 768; 553 N.Y.S.2d 820; 1990 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4225


Submitted March 19, 1990  
April 9, 1990 



DISPOSITION: ORDERED that the order dated November 18, 1988, is reversed, without costs or disbursements, and the motion is denied, and it is further,

ORDERED that the appeal from the order dated March 3, 1989, is dismissed, without costs or disbursements, as no appeal lies from an order denying reargument.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an action for a judgment declaring that a provision of the Village of Old Westbury, N.Y., Zoning Ordinance (ordinance) was unconstitutional, plaintiff landowner challenged an order from the Supreme Court, Nassau County (New York), which granted a motion by defendant village to dismiss the landowner's complaint on the grounds of res judicata.


OVERVIEW: The landowner made applications for a variance with regard to frontage requirements under the ordinance. N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 proceedings followed the denial of each application. Both proceedings were dismissed, with the second being dismissed based on the res judicata effect of the first determination. The landowner brought this declaratory judgment action seeking to challenge the constitutionality of the ordinance, but the supreme court dismissed the complaint on res judicata grounds, citing the earlier proceedings as bars to this proceeding. The landowner brought this appeal, arguing the constitutionality of the ordinance was not previously raised, and could not have been properly raised, in the earlier N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 proceedings. The court found the earlier dismissals of the variance applications did not have res judicata effect on the landowner's constitutional challenge. The administrative proceeding could not have been converted to a declaratory judgment action because the village was not a party in the previous actions and the constitutional argument was not previously raised. Moreover, until the variance was denied, the constitutional issue was not ripe.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order, which had granted the village's motion to dismiss the landowner's declaratory judgment complaint.

 

554 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Messina v. Sobol


No. 59697


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


159 A.D.2d 916; 553 N.Y.S.2d 529; 1990 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3583



March 29, 1990 



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, a traffic safety education instructor, appealed the judgment from the Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), which granted the motion of respondent, state Commissioner of Education, to dismiss for failure to state a cause of action and for lack of standing the petition filed by the instructor under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 challenging the commissioner's failure to mandate a specific driver and traffic safety education curriculum.


OVERVIEW: The instructor, along with six others, filed a combined N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding and action under N.Y. State Fin. Law § 123-b, which challenged the commissioner's alleged failure to mandate a specific driver and traffic safety (DTSE) curriculum. The commissioner filed a motion to dismiss the petition for failure to state a cause of action and for lack of standing. The trial court granted the motion, and the instructor appealed. The court held that the instructor, as a full time DTSE instructor, lacked standing, because he was unable to show that the commissioner's actions had actually harmed him and that the interest that he asserted was arguably within the zone of interest to be protected. Further, the instructor could not show standing as a citizen taxpayer pursuant to § 123-b, since he had not demonstrated the involvement of state funds. Finally, because each individual school district was free to elect whether to offer DTSE and, if it was offered, whether it would be offered within or outside the regular school schedule, the instructor's claims lacked even colorable merit. The trial court's judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of the action.

Sisario v. Amsterdam Memorial Hosp.


No. 59397


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


159 A.D.2d 843; 552 N.Y.S.2d 989; 1990 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2937



March 22, 1990 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff appealed the decision of the Supreme Court in Montgomery County (New York), which granted the motions of defendants, a hospital and an additional individual, to dismiss the complaint in plaintiff's medical malpractice action.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff commenced a medical malpractice action against defendants. Following joinder of issue, defendants brought a motion to dismiss the complaint principally due to plaintiff's failure to comply with N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3012-a, which required a complaint in a medical malpractice suit to be accompanied by a certificate of merit. To avoid dismissal, the trial court ordered plaintiff to, within 30 days of its order, file a certificate of merit and pay each defendant a specified sum. Plaintiff appealed that decision, and the court affirmed. However, plaintiff never complied with the trial court's order and, consequently, defendants again each brought a motion for a final order of dismissal. In response, plaintiff argued for the first time that N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3012-a was unconstitutional. The trial court rejected plaintiff's constitutional claim on the merits and granted defendants' motions for a trial order of dismissal. Plaintiff appealed. The court affirmed because the state's objective in enacting the statute passed the rational basis test as it involved the health and welfare of the community. Thus there was no violation of plaintiff's equal protection rights.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's grant of defendants' motions for dismissal in plaintiff's medical malpractice action.

 

556 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Enright v. Eli Lilly & Co.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


155 A.D.2d 64; 553 N.Y.S.2d 494; 1990 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2944; CCH Prod. Liab. Rep. P12,429



March 22, 1990 



DISPOSITION: Order modified, on the law, with costs to plaintiffs, by reversing so much thereof as granted defendants' motions dismissing the third cause of action in the complaint; said motions [***2] denied and, as so modified, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, infant and her parents, sought review of a decision of the Supreme Court of Chenango County (New York), which dismissed all causes of action against defendant drug manufacturers that sought to recover damages for plaintiff infant's various physical disabilities. Plaintiffs had alleged causes of action against defendant that sounded in negligence, strict products liability, breach of warranty, and fraud.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, infant and her parents, brought an action versus defendant drug manufacturers that was related to plaintiff infant's various physical disabilities alleged to have arisen from plaintiff infant's grandmother's exposure to the drug diethylstilbestrol (DES). Plaintiffs alleged causes of action against defendant that sounded in negligence, strict products liability, breach of warranty, and fraud. The lower court dismissed all causes of action against defendant that sought to recover damages for plaintiff infant's various physical disabilities. The lower court held in part that New York did not recognize preconception tort liability. The court reversed the lower court's judgment with respect to its determination on the validity of plaintiff infant's strict products liability cause of action. The court held that there was no sound basis for denying plaintiff infant her day in court because plaintiff infant was no less a victim of the devastation wrought by DES than was her mother. As modified, the remainder of the judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's order and allowed an action by plaintiffs, infant and her parents, against defendant drug manufacturers with respect to the preconception strict products liability theory advanced by plaintiff infant. The court held that there was no sound basis for denying plaintiff infant her day in court because she was no less a victim of the drug diethylstilbestrol than was her mother. The order was affirmed as modified.


HEADNOTES

Products Liability -- Strict Products Liability -- Toxic Torts -- Preconception Tort -- Ingestion of DES by Plaintiff's Grandmother

1. An infant plaintiff, who was born with various physical disabilities, has a cause of action in strict products liability against the manufacturers of the drug diethylstilbestrol (DES), which was allegedly ingested by plaintiff's grandmother while pregnant with plaintiff's mother and allegedly caused certain physical abnormalities in the mother's reproductive system, which, in turn, prevented plaintiff's mother from carrying her to full term, thereby causing plaintiff to develop severe disabilities which will affect her for her entire life; in light of the Legislature's policy of favoring a remedy for DES-caused injuries (see, L 1986, ch 682, § 4 -- the toxic tort revival statute) and the absence of the necessity of establishing manageable bounds for liability under a strict products liability theory, the infant plaintiff has a cause of action for injuries caused by her mother's exposure to DES prior to plaintiff's conception. Plaintiff is no less a victim of DES exposure than her mother, and there is no sound basis for denying plaintiff her day in court; plaintiff's burden of proving proximate cause provides an adequate boundary of the manufacturers' legal responsibility for birth defects caused by DES.

Appeal -- Preservation of Issue for Review -- Abandoned Issues

2. In an action sounding in strict products liability the only disputed issue to be resolved on appeal concerns preconception tort liability; since plaintiffs have not briefed the question of whether parents may recover damages for emotional distress resulting from the birth of an impaired child, plaintiff's appeal from the dismissal of that cause of action is deemed abandoned, as is the dismissal of the cause of action of plaintiff's father seeking damages based upon the inability to have a natural healthy child from his marriage.

 

557 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Newsday, Inc. v. Goodman


No. 2175E


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


159 A.D.2d 667; 552 N.Y.S.2d 965; 1990 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3016; 17 Media L. Rep. 1725


Argued March 16, 1990  
March 21, 1990 



DISPOSITION: ADJUDGED that the petition is denied, and the proceeding is dismissed, without costs or disbursements.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, a newspaper that was covering a criminal trial (newspaper), sought review pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78, of an order by respondent judge that denied the newspaper's request for disclosure of the names and address of the sworn jurors in the criminal case.


OVERVIEW: The judge submitted questionnaires to the members of a petit jury in a homicide case pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 270.15(1)(a). The newspaper requested copies of the completed questionnaires. The judge originally denied the request in toto, but modified the ruling by releasing an edited version of the questionnaires. The released version deleted the names of the jurors and any information that would have enabled the public to learn the jurors' identities. The judge's ruling was based upon the daily increasing tense emotions among the members of the victim's family and the defendant's family and reported threats against defense counsel. The newspaper filed an action pursuant N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 to compel the judge to make the entire questionnaires public. The newspaper claimed that the restricted public access to the voir dire was unconstitutional. The court disagreed with the newspaper's claim. The court ruled that the judge's deletion of a very small fraction of the material in the written questionnaires was justified in order to allow the jurors to serve without fear of intimidation or harassment, and that the newspaper's constitutional rights were not abridged.


OUTCOME: The court denied the newspaper's motion to compel the judge to disclose the names and addresses of the sworn jurors in a criminal trial.

559 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Long Island Lighting Co. v. Assessor of Brookhaven


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


154 A.D.2d 188; 552 N.Y.S.2d 336; 1990 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2666



March 2, 1990 



DISPOSITION: Ordered that the order is affirmed, with one bill of costs payable by the appellants appearing separately and filing separate briefs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Intervenor school district and defendants, town, assessor, and board of assessment review, sought review of a judgment of the Supreme Court, Suffolk County (New York), which denied their motions for summary judgment, filed after enactment of the Long Island Power Authority Act of 1986, N.Y. Pub. Auth. Law §§ 1020 to 1020-hh. Plaintiff utility company filed the tax certiorari action for various tax years from 1976 to 1987.


OVERVIEW: The purpose of the Long Island Power Authority Act was to create a publicly owned power authority, to acquire any or all of plaintiff's stock or assets to assure the provision of an adequate supply of gas and electricity in a reliable, efficient, and economic manner to the residents. Plaintiff sought review of defendant town's assessments of its nuclear-power plant on the grounds that they are excessive, unequal, and unlawful. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which denied intervenor and defendants' motions for summary judgment. By enacting N.Y. Real Prop. Tax Law § 7, the legislature bestowed on taxpayers the right to seek judicial review of their tax assessments on various grounds. By enacting N.Y. Pub. Auth. Law § 1020-q(3), the legislature arbitrarily restricted plaintiff's right, as owner of the plant, to seek redress under that article. In doing so, it violated plaintiff's right to substantive due process, and, thus, ran afoul of both the state and federal constitutions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgement of the trial court, which denied intervenor and defendants' motions for summary judgment in the tax certiorari action.


HEADNOTES

Public Utilities -- Assessment of Real Property -- Curtailment of Judicial Review -- Equal Protection

1. Public Authorities Law § 1020-q (3), which provides that no governmental taxing entity shall be liable to the Long Island Power Authority (LIPA) or "any other entity" for a refund of property taxes originally assessed against certain real property formerly owned by petitioner public utility and currently owned by LIPA, that any judicial determination that the real property in question was overassessed shall not result in a refund of any taxes previously paid by petitioner, and that LIPA shall discontinue all proceedings brought "by its predecessor in interest" seeking repayment of any taxes assessed against the property in question, is unconstitutional as applied to petitioner since it violates the Equal Protection and Due Process Clauses of both the State and Federal Constitutions; the statute does not pass constitutional muster under the "rational basis" test. Although the State purpose served by the statute is the commendable goal of preserving the economic well-being of the residents of the relevant taxing district, no rational reason has been demonstrated for treating petitioner differently from other taxpayers owning real property within the taxing district. The statutory classification restricting petitioner's right to commence tax certiorari proceedings is palpably arbitrary and results in invidious discrimination with respect to petitioner, which has, thus, been denied equal protection.

Public Utilities -- Assessment of Real Property -- Curtailment of Judicial Review -- Long Island Lighting Company

2. The term "any other entity" in Public Authorities Law § 1020-q (3), which provides that no governmental taxing entity shall be liable to the Long Island Power Authority or "any other entity" for a refund of property taxes originally assessed against certain real property formerly owned by petitioner Long Island Lighting Company and currently owned by the Authority, that any judicial determination that the real property in question was overassessed shall not result in a refund of any taxes previously paid by petitioner, and that the Authority shall discontinue all proceedings brought "by its predecessor in interest" seeking repayment of any taxes assessed against the property in question for certain tax years, refers to petitioner; the portion of the statute barring any refund of taxes which might result from a tax certiorari proceeding erases all doubt as to whether the Legislature intended to preclude petitioner from recovering in such a proceeding where, coincidentally, petitioner is challenging tax assessments for the tax years specifically mentioned in the statute. Moreover, it was error to have concluded that in light of a related Federal court action finding that the statute was facially constitutional, respondents' motions for summary had to be denied.

Public Utilities -- Assessment of Real Property -- Curtailment of Judicial Review -- Due Process

3. Public Authorities Law § 1020-q (3), which provides that no governmental entity shall be liable to the Long Island Power Authority (LIPA) or "any other entity" for a refund of property taxes originally assessed against certain real property formerly owned by petitioner public utility and currently owned by LIPA, that any judicial determination that the real property in question was overassessed shall not result in a refund of any taxes previously paid by petitioner, and that LIPA shall discontinue all proceedings brought "by its predecessor in interest" seeking repayment of any taxes assessed against the subject property, is unconstitutional and a deprivation of petitioner's right to substantive due process; where the Legislature sees fit to grant a right to seek judicial review of tax assessments, it may not alter or restrict that right arbitrarily, which is precisely what it did by enacting the statute in issue.
