Greene v. Town Bd., Warrensburg


No. 59516


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


159 A.D.2d 781; 552 N.Y.S.2d 62; 1990 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2201



March 1, 1990 



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner resident challenged a judgment of the Supreme Court (New York), which granted the motion of respondent town board to dismiss the resident's petition to hold a referendum to enable the electorate to decide whether the town, at its expense, should install television translators.


OVERVIEW: Asserting that the requested action was illegal and unauthorized, the town board declined to submit the issue to the electorate. The resident them commenced an action to compel the board to present the proposition. The trial court dismissed the petition. In affirming the trial court's decision, the court held that whether the town board was required to submit the proposition to a referendum was an issue that had already been necessarily decided in a prior proceeding in which the resident was afforded a full and fair opportunity to contest the issue. The doctrine of collateral estoppel precluded the resident from relitigating the issue.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

 

561 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Alliance of American Insurers v. Chu


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


154 A.D.2d 82; 551 N.Y.S.2d 979; 1990 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2206



March 1, 1990 



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, insurers, trade associations, and policy holders (insurers), appealed an order of the Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), which granted defendant tax commissioner's motion for summary judgment and declared N.Y. Ins. Law § 7607(a) constitutional.


OVERVIEW: Section 7607(a) established an insurance security fund to protect against loss due to insurer insolvency. Section 7603(d) provided that investment income from the fund be credited to the state's general fund. Section 7603(e)(1)(A) directed that a portion of the fund be transferred to the general fund and appropriated each year in a budget bill. Such appropriation was deemed an asset of the fund and was payable to the fund in the event that the fund's other assets were depleted. Pursuant to N.Y. Ins. Law § 7603(b), (c)(1), the tax commissioner ordered insurers to made contributions to the fund when it fell below a certain level. In consolidated actions, the insurers then sought a declaratory judgment that § 7603 was unconstitutional in that it impaired the state's contract obligations and deprived the insurers of their property without due process of law. The trial court granted the tax commissioner's motion for summary judgment. On appeal, the court affirmed and held that the statutes were constitutional. Section 7607(a) did not create a contractual right in the insurers. Even assuming a contractual right, the insurers failed to show that § 7603 impaired contractual rights.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the award of summary judgment to the tax commissioner and affirmed that the statutes relating to the security fund were constitutional.


HEADNOTES

Insurance -- Property/Casualty Insurance Security Fund -- Contractual Rights of Insurers

1. Language in Insurance Law § 7607 (a), which established the Property/Casualty Insurance Security Fund, which in turn provides protection against loss owing to insurer insolvency, did not create a contract between the State and insurers doing business therein, since clear and precise language spelling out a covenant or agreement whereby the State becomes obligated to a third party is absent, and before a statute will be deemed a contract, its language and circumstances must manifest a legislative intent to create private rights of a contractual nature enforceable against the State. Language in the statute in question providing that the Fund be kept separate and apart from other State money and that it has the faith and credit of the State pledged for its safekeeping is declaratory of legislative policy and does not create a contract between the State and insurers which must contribute to the Fund.

Insurance -- Property/Casualty Insurance Security Fund -- Impairment of Contract -- Transfer of Money to State General Fund

2. Assuming that Insurance Law § 7607 (a), which provides that the Property/Casualty Insurance Security Fund, which in turn provides protection against loss owing to insurer insolvency, shall be separate and apart pledged for its safekeeping, created a contract between the State and insurers doing business therein, plaintiff insurers, which must contribute to the Fund, have failed to demonstrate beyond a reasonable doubt that the enactment of two amendments to the Insurance Law (see, L 1979, ch 503; L 1982, ch 55; Insurance Law § 7603 [d], [e] [1] [A]), which required that the excess of the Fund's annual investment income over $ 15 million be credited to the general fund of the State treasury, and that $ 87 million from the Fund be transferred to the general fund of the State treasury, with a corresponding "dry" appropriation to be made by the Governor in a budget bill and deemed an asset of the Fund, payable in the event that all of the Fund's other assets are depleted, substantially impaired that contract; the purpose of the Fund is to protect the policyholders of the State, not the insurers. There is no evidence that the State has failed to use the Fund for its intended purpose or that the transfers pursuant to the amendments in question threatened its solvency.

Insurance -- Property/Casualty Insurance Security Fund -- Deprivation of Insurers' Property without Due Process

3. Two amendments to the Insurance Law (see, L 1979, ch 503; L 1982, ch 55; Insurance Law § 7603 [d], [e] [1] [A]), which required that the excess of the annual investment income of the Property/Casualty Insurance Security Fund, which provides protection against loss owing to insurer insolvency, over $ 15 million be credited to the general fund of the State treasury, and that $ 87 million from the Fund be transferred to the general fund of the State treasury, with a corresponding "dry" appropriation to be made by the Governor in a budget bill and deemed an asset of the Fund, payable in the event that all of the Fund's other assets were depleted, did not deprive insurers doing business in New York, which must contribute to the Fund, of their property without just compensation or due process since the legislation establishing the Fund created no property interest in plaintiffs in either the assets or the income of the Fund; the contributions of the insurers to the Fund were mandated as a condition for doing business in the State, and to possess a property right, one must have more than an abstract need or unilateral expectation. Language in the legislation establishing the Fund (see, L 1969, ch 189, § 3) providing for the return of the interest to insurers did not create a vested property right in the Fund, and was no more than a declaration of the general legislative policy which was subject to alteration by the Legislature. No new liabilities are imposed by the amendments since the annual appropriation is to be included as an asset of the Fund, thereby compensating for any transfers to the State treasury, and the amendments have not allowed the State to raise revenues without incurring a corresponding detriment.  

 

562 of 992 DOCUMENTS


GE v. N.Y. State Dep't of Labor


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


154 A.D.2d 117; 551 N.Y.S.2d 966; 1990 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 1881; 29 Wage & Hour Cas. (BNA) 1284; 115 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P56,224



February 22, 1990 



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner potential bidder appealed from a judgment of the Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), which dismissed a petition to review a determination of respondent Department of Labor (department) establishing the prevailing wage rate on a public work project performed for the New York Power Authority (NYPA).


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed the department's establishment of the prevailing rate, refusing to modify the schedule to add an occupation requested for inclusion by the bidder. The court found that the position requested was not needed because the work to be provided under that occupation was done by electricians. The court also rejected the bidder's contention that NYPA, for which the project was planned, failed to file its own classification of workmen, mechanics, and laborers to be employed on the project, together with a statement of the work to be performed by each such classification and that N.Y. Lab. Law §220(3-a)(a), required annulment of the department's determination. The technical specification made by the NYPA were sufficient to meet the statutory requirements. Finally, the court found that the prevailing rate was properly established by collective bargaining agreement.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment dismissing the bidder's petition to review the department's establishment of the prevailing wage on a NYPA project.


HEADNOTES

Labor -- Prevailing Rate of Wages -- Expertise of Department of Labor

1. In a proceeding to review a determination of the Department of Labor which established the prevailing wage rate on a public work project performed for the New York Power Authority, petitioner failed to demonstrate that the Department's determination was arbitrary, capricious or without legal basis; the purpose of the prevailing wage law (Labor Law § 220) is to protect workers by ensuring that they have an effective remedy to secure the prevailing wage, which is that paid under collective bargaining agreements to at least 30% of those employed in the same trade or occupation in a given locality. "Trade or occupation" classifications for work embraced by the statute are a matter given to the expertise of the Department; upon a review of the record, it is concluded that the work envisaged by the project, the rewinding of off-line generators, is performed by electricians, and does not constitute work performed by the "trade or occupation" of "winder" which is so distinct therefrom as to render the Department's determination arbitrary or capricious.

Labor -- Prevailing Rate of Wages -- Failure of Project Owner to File Classification of Workers

2. In a proceeding to review a determination of the Department of Labor which established the prevailing wage rate on a public work project performed for the New York Power Authority, the failure of the Authority to file its own classification of workers to be employed on the project, together with a statement of the work to be performed by each such classification (see, Labor Law § 220 [3-a]), does not require annulment of the Department's determination. Although the Authority did not provide the Department with the specific classifications for the project, it did file the project's technical specifications, describing in detail the work to be done, and that sufficiently complied with the statute; any failure by the Authority to strictly comply with the statute had no material effect on the Department's duty to make a proper classification.

Labor -- Prevailing Rate of Wages -- Rule Promulgation 

3. The classification and prevailing wage determinations of the Department of Labor for a public work project performed for the New York Power Authority are not subject to the rule promulgation requirements of the State Administrative Procedure Act, since the Department's classifications are sufficiently established on a case-by-case analysis of the work to be performed such that they do not constitute a rule or regulation requiring filing (see, State Administrative Procedure Act § 102 [2]); moreover, Labor Law § 220 adequately outlines the Department's classification requirements so that rule promulgation of the process is unnecessary.

Labor -- Prevailing Rate of Wages -- Unconstitutional Delegation of Legislative Power 

4. In a proceeding to review a determination of the Department of Labor which established the prevailing wage rate on a public work project performed for the New York Power Authority, Labor Law § 220 (5) does not unconstitutionally delegate legislative power to private parties by allowing the Department to rely on collective bargaining agreements in determining wages; parties who negotiate the collective bargaining agreements with employers may establish the prevailing wage for public works in a locality, but only when that employer employs at least 30% of the workers engaged in that kind of work in the locality (see, Labor Law § 220 [5]), and although that may constitute some form of delegation of legislative power, the 30% requirement provides an ample safeguard so as to place any delegation well within constitutional limits. The statute itself clearly limits the use of collective bargaining agreements in setting prevailing wage rates, and the use thereof is reasonably necessary to accomplish the Legislature's mandate that workers receive that prevailing wage.

Labor -- Prevailing Rate of Wages -- Employee Retirement Income Security Act 

5. In a proceeding to review a determination of the Department of Labor which established the prevailing wage rate on a public work project performed for the New York Power Authority, any contention by petitioner that the Department's determination must be annulled to the extent it requires petitioner, a potential bidder on the project, to provide supplements under that part of Labor Law § 220 preempted by the Employee Retirement Income Security Act (see, 29 USC § 1001 et seq.) is premature; the determination in question was made prior to the designation of any contractual wage or supplement requirements attaching to the project.

Hauptman v. New York State Dep't of Motor Vehicles


No. 3384E


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


158 A.D.2d 600; 551 N.Y.S.2d 572; 1990 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 1982


Argued January 29, 1990  
February 20, 1990 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner appealed a judgment from the Supreme Court, Suffolk County (New York), which dismissed his proceeding seeking to review a determination of the Commissioner of Motor Vehicles denying his application for a driver's license.


OVERVIEW: When petitioner's application for a driver's license was denied, a suit was instituted against respondent asserting that the N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1193(2)(c) was unconstitutionally vague and interfered with his right to travel. The trial court rejected petitioner's assertions and dismissed the proceeding. On appeal, the court found that the statute was sufficiently precise to put reasonable people on notice and did not constitute interference with the right of travel. Although the trial court correctly determined the issues, the judgment entered merely provided for dismissal of the petition, therefore, the court modified the judgment by converting the proceeding to a declaratory judgment. As so modified, the judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court modified the judgment by deleting the provision dismissing the proceeding, and adding a provision converting the proceeding to an action for declaratory judgment. Further, a provision was added declaring that respondent's action was a valid exercise of delegated authority. As modified, the judgment was affirmed.

 

564 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Health Ins. Ass'n v. Corcoran


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


154 A.D.2d 61; 551 N.Y.S.2d 615; 1990 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 1836



February 15, 1990 



DISPOSITION: Judgment modified, on the law, with costs to petitioners, by converting the proceeding to an action for a declaratory judgment and declaring that 11 NYCRR 52.27 is invalid, and, as so modified, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a proceeding pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78, appellant state insurance commissioner sought review of an order from the Supreme Court of Albany County (New York), which granted a petition from respondent insurance companies and associations declaring that state insurance regulation preventing insurers from assessing an applicant's human immunodeficiency virus (HIV) status to be null and void.


OVERVIEW: In their petition, respondent insurance company trade associations and insurance companies challenged the validity of a state regulation promulgated by appellant insurance commissioner, claiming that N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit.11, § 52.27 was invalid because it prohibited small insurers from considering the results of human immunodeficiency virus (HIV) test results in underwriting. Appellant contended the regulation was valid where it sought to eliminate discriminatory practices. The trial court held the HIV testing ban regulation was invalid on the grounds it lacked a rational and factual basis in preventing insurers from identifying health risks. On appeal, the court reviewed the regulation and concluded that appellant had exceeded his powers in promulgating the regulation because it was the legislature's responsibility to enact laws limiting an insurer's right to assess and accept risks. Respondents' discriminatory action was proper considering it was related solely to assessing risks related to future health costs, which was a rational basis for respondents' underwriting process.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment declaring the regulation invalid.


HEADNOTES

Insurance -- Administrative Rulemaking -- Regulatory Authority of Superintendent of Insurance

1. The Superintendent of Insurance has wide authority to prescribe regulations, and in doing so may exercise broad power to interpret and implement legislative policy; however, his regulations must be consistent with and have a basis in the Insurance Law, which is the charter of the Superintendent's authority. Moreover, an additional check on administrative rulemaking is that an administrator may be required to justify the factual as well as statutory basis for the adoption of regulations.

Insurance -- Health and Accident Insurance -- Tests for Human Immunodeficiency Virus -- Regulation of Department of Insurance

2. The purpose and effect of a regulation of the Department of Insurance (11 NYCRR 52.27) barring individual and small group health insurance carriers from testing applicants for health insurance for the presence of the human immunodeficiency virus (HIV) or using such test results in determining an applicant's insurability are to mandate coverage for HIV seropositive persons in the general risk pool of applicants for the affected policies; however, the general rule is that an insurer is free to select its risks and may make inquiry of matters which it deems material to the risk (see, Insurance Law § 1113 [b]). It is noteworthy that where, for social policy reasons, the aforementioned general rule has been deviated from to require that certain coverages be provided, the Legislature itself has made specific provision therefor by statute.

Insurance -- Health and Accident Insurance -- Tests for Human Immunodeficiency Virus -- Valid Underwriting Practice

3. Insurance Law § 3201, which gives the Superintendent of Insurance the authority to approve or disapprove policy forms for health insurance, Insurance Law § 3217, which gives the Superintendent the authority to issue regulations to establish minimum standards for the form, content and sale of health insurance policies, and Insurance Law § 4224 (b) (1), which prohibits an insurer from unfairly discriminating between individuals of the same class with regard to health insurance, are intended to protect policyholders and the insurance-buying public from abuses by insurers, and the denial of coverage or differential premium rates on the basis of sound underwriting practices accurately assessing risks/future costs are not by nature misleading to the public or prejudicial to policyholders. Valid underwriting practices promote fairness to the policyholder in not requiring him to bear in premiums the costs of insuring others in higher risk categories, and the solvency of insurers. Moreover, provisions barring discrimination against insureds have been construed not to apply when differential treatment has a proper underwriting basis. Consequently, there is not a rational basis to conclude that using the results of tests for the presence of the human immunodeficiency virus (HIV) in determining the insurability of an applicant for health insurance is not a sound underwriting practice with regard for individual and small group policies in light of the formidable financial threat of the AIDS epidemic.

Insurance -- Health and Accident Insurance -- Tests for Human Immunodeficiency Virus -- Regulations Barring Use of Tests to Determine Insurability

4. There is not a rational basis to conclude that using the results of tests for the presence of the human immunodeficiency virus (HIV) in determining the insurability of an applicant for health insurance is not a sound underwriting practice with regard for individual and small group policies given the fact that the potential AIDS epidemic represents a formidable financial threat of disastrous proportions to health insurers and the public fisc; accordingly, insurance underwriters are unquestionably justified in attempting to identify persons having a much higher than normal statistical likelihood of contracting AIDS or ARC as a special risk category for purposes of determining insurability, and, hence, a regulation of the Department of Insurance (11 NYCRR 52.27) barring the use of such tests by health insurers is invalid unless there is no justification, from a valid underwriting standpoint, to use such tests for identifying the class to be placed in that extraordinary risk category.

Insurance -- Health and Accident Insurance -- Tests for Human Immunodeficiency Virus -- Actuarial Reliability of Tests

5. In a proceeding to challenge the validity of a regulation of the Department of Insurance (11 NYCRR 52.27) barring individual and small group health insurance carriers from testing applicants for health insurance for the presence of the human immunodeficiency virus (HIV) or using such test results in determining an applicant's insurability, the findings of the Superintendent of Insurance are inadequate to support the conclusion that the tests are invalid as an underwriting tool to identify the class of individuals significantly more at risk of getting AIDS or ARC; thus, a ban on testing for the reason of underwriting invalidity would lack a factual basis. Findings of the Commissioner of Health that it is not definitively known how many HIV seropositive persons will progress to AIDS or ARC do not render irrelevant the use of HIV test results for actuarial purposes, i.e., as a means of identifying a class substantially more prone to AIDS than the general population; the scientific literature unambiguously supports the validity for underwriting purposes of more thorough HIV testing procedures.

Insurance -- Health and Accident Insurance -- Tests for Human Immunodeficiency Virus -- Valid Underwriting Practices

6. In a proceeding to challenge the validity of a regulation of the Department of Insurance (11 NYCRR 52.27) barring individual and small group health insurance carriers from testing applicants for health insurance for the presence of the human immunodeficiency virus (HIV) or using such test results in determining an applicant's insurability, the Superintendent of Insurance may not, from an underwriting standpoint, rely upon the fact that there might be a lag of an indefinite number of years between the onset of HIV infection and progression to AIDS; because of statutory provisions limiting insurers regarding incontestibility and renewability after two years from the date of policy issuance (see, Insurance Law § 3216 [d] [1] [B] [ii]; [g]), any such delay would remain within an insurer's risk. Inasmuch as HIV testing, as a sound underwriting practice, is not unfair, inequitable, discriminatory or deceptive, Insurance Law §§ 3201 and 4224 do not afford the Superintendent authority to promulgate a regulation absolutely banning the tests.

Insurance -- Health and Accident Insurance -- Tests for Human Immunodeficiency Virus -- Validity of Regulation Banning Use of Tests to Determine Insurability

7. In a proceeding to challenge the validity of a regulation of the Department of Insurance (11 NYCRR 52.27) barring individual and small group

566 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Lower Manhattan Loft Tenants v. New York City Loft Bd.


No. 38283


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


157 A.D.2d 611; 550 N.Y.S.2d 630; 1990 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 840



January 30, 1990 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent New York City Loft Board (board) appealed the ruling of the Supreme Court of New York County (New York), which found for petitioner New York City loft tenants on their claim that the board's regulations allowing for termination of a harassment finding against a unit owner conflicted with N. Y. Mult. Dwell. Law § 286.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner New York City loft tenants challenged the validity of regulations enacted by respondent New York City Loft Board (board). N.Y. Mult. Dwell. Law § 286 (Loft Law) allowed the owner of a covered unit first right of purchase of tenant improvements to the unit. If the owner did so, the unit became exempt from rent control. The statute also provided that purchase of improvements was not available to an owner found guilty by the board of tenant harassment. The board subsequently enacted regulations allowing an owner found guilty of such harassment to apply for the termination of such a finding, thus enabling the owner to purchase tenant improvements. The tenants argued that allowing termination of a harassment finding conflicted with the Loft Law. The trial court agreed and declared the regulations annulled. On appeal, the court reversed. The board had discretion to construe the Loft Law in accord with its purpose; other rent regulatory statutes allowed termination of a harassment finding; and it was unclear whether the Loft Law intended for a harassment finding to be irrevocable. The board's construction of the Loft Law would therefore be accorded deference by the courts.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's annulment of the board's regulations.

 

567 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Malik v. Coughlin


No. 59351


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


157 A.D.2d 961; 550 N.Y.S.2d 219; 1990 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 407



January 18, 1990 



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate challenged a judgment from the Sullivan County Supreme Court (New York), which dismissed the inmate's application, in a proceeding pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78, to annul Directives 4910 and 4933 of respondent Department of Correctional Services. The inmate contended that the directives violated his constitutional rights.


OVERVIEW: The inmate commenced his proceeding to challenge certain conditions of his confinement in the special housing unit. The inmate claimed that Directives 4910 and 4933, under N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 7, §§ 302-305, constitutionally violated his right to a watch, a hairbrush, more than 10 family photographs, additional package purchases, more than one nonlegal visit per week, and not to have rectal searches. The trial court stated that the restrictions were not unconstitutional in light of the correctional facility's security needs. On review, the court opined that because the inmate did not claim that he was deprived of any rights guaranteed by statutes or regulations, his petition had to be considered as alleging only violations of the Eighth Amendment prohibiting cruel and inhuman treatment. The court noted that the conditions challenged were typical of confinement in maximum security and that the inmate was not physically injured by the items he lacked. The court pointed out that no due process rights were violated when considered against institutional security needs and concluded that rectal searches were not unreasonable but were required to maintain security and safety.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment, which dismissed the inmate's petition challenging conditions of his confinement.

Board of Educ. of Farmingdale Union Free School Dist. v. Gulotta


No. 3110E


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


157 A.D.2d 642; 549 N.Y.S.2d 740; 1990 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 257


Argued December 12, 1989  
January 8, 1990 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant County of Nassau sought review of a judgment of the Supreme Court, Nassau County (New York), which declared that some of the County's laws regarding respondents, Board of Education and Board of Educational Services, were unconstitutional and invalid.


OVERVIEW: The Boards initiated an action against the County contending that certain educational laws of the County were unconstitutional. The trial court granted judgment in favor of the Boards, and the County sought review. The court found that N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1604 provided that local authorities had no power to enact a law requiring from any owner or operator of a motor vehicle a tax or fee for the use of the public highways. The court held that the state statute constituted a broad and detailed legislative scheme for the regulation of the school buses and evinced an intention on the part of the state to preempt the field. The court held that although the home rule provision conferred broad police power on the local government powers concerning the welfare of its citizens, the County was not permitted to impose an additional layer of regulation where the state evidenced its intention to preempt the field, as required under N.Y. Const. art. IX, § 2(c). The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed a judgment of the trial court, which granted judgment in favor of the Boards and held that some of the County's educational laws were unconstitutional and invalid.

 

569 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Singer v. Eli Lilly & Co.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


153 A.D.2d 210; 549 N.Y.S.2d 654; 1990 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 50



January 4, 1990 



DISPOSITION: Order, Supreme Court, New York County, entered on September 7, 1988, unanimously affirmed, without costs and without disbursements.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, husband and wife, brought an action against appellees, manufacturers of the prescription drug, diethylstilbestrol (DES), seeking the recovery of damages for injuries allegedly sustained as a result of the wife's in utero exposure to the drug in 1949. The New York Supreme Court dismissed the action as time-barred because it was brought after the expiration of the one-year period of the Toxic Tort Revival Statute.


OVERVIEW: The trial court reasoned that the period could not be affected by the tolling provisions applicable to statutes of limitation even if New York courts were to adopt the Federal doctrine, which permitted tolling of a statute of limitations during the pendency of a class action. On appeal the court agreed. The court first held that the revival statute's one-year period was a condition precedent, rather than a statute of limitations. The revival statute revived extinguished claims and created a new right of action, but absent the revival statute, the husband and wife were without a remedy for their claims. The husband and wife claimed that the action was nonetheless timely by virtue of the commencement of two purported class actions within the one-year window period. The court disagreed. The court found that the courts had to construe revival statutes narrowly and that permitting tolling would expand the revival statute. In addition, the husband in wife did not belong to either of the two classes, so the class actions could not toll the revival statute if it was treated as a statute of limitations.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment dismissing the husband and wife's claims as untimely.


HEADNOTES

Limitation of Actions -- Condition Precedent -- Inapplicability of Tolling Provisions -- Action Pursuant to Toxic Tort Revival Statute

1. An action commenced pursuant to the toxic tort revival statute (L 1986, ch 682, § 4) to recover damages for the alleged effects of in utero exposure of plaintiff to the drug diethylstilbestrol (DES) is untimely where it was commenced beyond the one-year window period for bringing suit provided in the statute, since the one-year period is a condition precedent to suit, rather than a Statute of Limitations, and, thus, the tolling provisions applicable to Statutes of Limitation are inapplicable. Where a liability is created by a statute which limits the right of action thereon to a stated period of time, the effect is that at the termination of that time the liability expires, leaving nothing thereafter on which to predicate an action. Accordingly, compliance with the time period is a substantive element of the cause of action. Moreover, the literal language of the toxic tort revival statute, upon which any DES action based on a time-barred claim is dependent, supports the conclusion that commencement of the action within the window period is a condition precedent, since the statute clearly states that any claim is revived "provided such action is commenced within one year from the effective date of this act".

Limitation of Actions -- Condition Precedent -- Action Pursuant to Toxic Tort Revival Statute

2. An action commenced pursuant to the toxic tort revival statute (L 1986, ch 682, § 4) to recover damages for the alleged effects of in utero exposure to the drug diethylstilbestrol (DES) is untimely if commenced beyond the one-year window period for bringing suit provided in the statute, since the one-year period is a condition precedent to suit, rather than a Statute of Limitations and, thus, the tolling provisions applicable to Statutes of Limitation are inapplicable; the revival statute created a new right, and absent revival, plaintiffs were without a remedy. The fact that common-law tort remedies for plaintiffs' injuries predated the revival statute is beside the point; the public policy behind the statute was to promote uniformity; a goal which would be frustrated if tolling provisions were applicable. Moreover, revived actions are treated more restrictively, since revival is an extreme exercise of legislative power; uncertainties are resolved against consequences so drastic.

Limitation of Actions -- Condition Precedent -- Inapplicability of Tolling Provisions

3. An action commenced pursuant to the toxic tort revival statute (L 1986, ch 682, § 4) to recover damages for the alleged effects of in utero exposure of plaintiff wife to the drug diethylstilbestrol (DES) is untimely where it was commenced beyond the one-year window period for bringing suit provided in the statute, since the one-year period is a condition precedent to suit, rather than a Statute of Limitations and, thus, the tolling provisions applicable to Statutes of Limitation are inapplicable; even if the revival statute were a Statute of Limitations, the Federal class action tolling doctrine, which provides that in certain circumstances the commencement of a class action tolls the running of the applicable Statute of Limitations as to all putative members of the class, would be inapplicable to the instant plaintiffs who may not be considered members of the two class actions they rely upon since they seek recovery for different damages and seek different remedies. Inasmuch as plaintiffs herein are not members of the classes represented by the two class actions pending against the instant defendants, it cannot be said that the pendency of those class actions have put defendants on notice of the substance of plaintiffs' claims, which is the rationale for the Federal tolling doctrine.

 

570 of 992 DOCUMENTS


People v. Hankin


NO. 98-833 K CR


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE TERM, SECOND DEPARTMENT


182 Misc. 2d 1003; 701 N.Y.S.2d 778; 1999 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 567



September 3, 1999, Decided


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Solicitation of Law Clients - Sufficiency of Information

1. The count of an information charging defendant attorney with violating Judiciary Law § 482, which prohibits an attorney from employing any person for the purpose of soliciting legal business, is facially sufficient where it alleged that an informant referred cases to the defendant in the past for a fee, the informant told defendant he had a case to refer to him, the defendant told the informant to bring the client to his office, the informant escorted an undercover police officer to defendant's office, the officer signed a retainer, and the defendant paid the informant and discussed payment for future referrals. The information alleges both a prior arrangement, wherein the informant had been paid by defendant for past referrals, and the giving and receiving of compensation for the recommendation of the defendant to the officer. Thus, the information alleges affirmative acts to steer potential clients to defendant in exchange for payment. Also, if true, the allegations are sufficient to establish that defendant employed an informant to solicit business for him. The act of solicitation does not require the solicitor to convince the "client" of the need for an attorney.

Constitutional Law - Validity of Statute - Statute Penalizing Attorney for Solicitation of Clients

2. Judiciary Law § 482, which prohibits an attorney from employing any person for the purpose of soliciting legal business, is constitutional as applied to the defendant attorney, who allegedly paid an informant for referring clients. The United States Supreme Court has upheld the prohibition against in-person solicitation of legal business for remuneration by an attorney against a constitutional attack, based upon the important State interests which support the ban. Solicitation by a lawyer's agents or runners would present similar problems.

Bragston Realty Corp. v. Dixon


NO. 98-1690 K C


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE TERM, SECOND DEPARTMENT


180 Misc. 2d 1018; 694 N.Y.S.2d 878; 1999 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 344



June 28, 1999, Decided


HEADNOTES

Landlord and Tenant - Rent Regulation - Timeliness of Rent Overcharge Claim

The rent overcharge claim based on a 1987 rent increase, which was filed by respondent tenant in April 1998 after the annual rent registration statement for 1987 showing an increase in rent from $ 174.75 in 1986 to $ 450 in 1987 was finally filed on July 31, 1997, is time barred as it was not interposed within four years of the first overcharge alleged and a review of rent records is limited to the four years immediately preceding the interposition of an overcharge claim ( CPLR 213-a; Rent Stabilization Law [Administrative Code of City of NY] § 26-516 [a] [2]). While Rent Stabilization Law (Administrative Code) § 26-517 (e) would have barred petitioner's landlord from collecting rent in excess of $ 174.75 until the registration statement was finally filed on July 31, 1997, a rent overcharge does not occur when a registration statement is filed or late-filed, but rather, when an owner collects "an overcharge above the rent authorized for a housing accommodation" (Rent Stabilization Law [Administrative Code] § 26-516 [a]). The filing of a registration statement is relevant in overcharge cases only insofar as it is determinative of the legal regulated rent, and respondent does not claim that an overcharge occurred when the rent collected is measured against the legal regulated rent.

People v. Weinert


NO. 95-1042 RO CR


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE TERM, SECOND DEPARTMENT


178 Misc. 2d 675; 683 N.Y.S.2d 690; 1998 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 593



June 24, 1998, Decided


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Instructions - Verdict Sheet

1. In prosecuting defendant for, inter alia, driving while intoxicated (Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1192 [3]), the court committed reversible error where it presented to the jury the elements of each offense in its charge, as well as the lesser included offense of driving while ability impaired (Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1192 [1]), and gave the jury a written list of the offenses, but omitted from the list the lesser included offense of driving while ability impaired. CPL 310.20 (2) provides that upon retiring to deliberate, the jurors may take a written list prepared by the court containing the offenses submitted to the jury by the court in its charge and the possible verdicts thereon. Where more than one count is sent to the jury for verdict or where counts are submitted in the alternative and especially where there is a combination thereof, a verdict sheet is a virtual necessity. Based upon this "virtual necessity", it was incumbent upon the court to provide the lesser included offense.

Motor Vehicles - Operating Vehicle while Registration Suspended - Failing to Maintain Proper License Plates - Sufficiency of Evidence

2. The People sustained their burden of proof with regard to charges against defendant of operating a motor vehicle with a suspended registration (Vehicle and Traffic Law § 512) and failing to maintain proper license plates (Vehicle and Traffic Law § 402). The verdicts were not against the weight of the credible evidence and, viewing the evidence in a light most favorable to the People, defendant's guilt was proven beyond a reasonable doubt.

Motor Vehicles - Operating Uninsured Vehicle - Sufficiency of Evidence

3. The People failed to sustain their burden of proof with regard to charges against defendant of operating an uninsured vehicle (Vehicle and Traffic Law § 319 [1]) where they failed to offer proof that defendant, who did not own the subject vehicle, had knowledge that the owner lacked insurance.

Constitutional Law - Validity of Statute - Motor Vehicle Registration

4. Vehicle and Traffic Law § 402 (4), which mandates that each motor vehicle driven on a public highway display proper license plates is not unconstitutionally void for vagueness concerning the proper license plate type for pick-up trucks, notwithstanding that the statute makes no reference to a pick-up truck, or any other type of vehicle. 15 NYCRR 106.2 (g) defines a pick-up truck and requires such vehicles to have commercial license plates. Since the law concerning the proper plates for pick-up trucks is clear, the statute cannot be considered constitutionally void for vagueness.

Crimes - Information - Simplified Traffic Information - Sufficiency

5. The four simplified traffic informations charging defendant with violations of the Vehicle and Traffic Law are not jurisdictionally defective for failure to contain specific factual allegations with respect to each and every element of the offenses charged. The simplified traffic informations need only be in substantial compliance with the form prescribed by the Commissioner of Motor Vehicles ( CPL 100.25), and there is no allegation that the instruments fail to comply with the provision of the CPL.

Crimes - Unlawful Search and Seizure

6. A police officer's stop and seizure of defendant motorist was not contrary to the State and Federal Constitutions where the arresting officer's observations of defendant revealed that he was operating a motor vehicle with improper license plates, there were improper registration credentials and defendant exhibited physical manifestations of intoxication.

Bernard v. Scharf


97-132


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE TERM, FIRST DEPARTMENT


170 Misc. 2d 909; 656 N.Y.S.2d 583; 1997 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 71



January 9, 1997, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] EDITED FOR PUBLICATION



DISPOSITION: Appellants' claim that they cannot be held liable after their replacement at the helm of the bankrupt corporate debtor in possession by a Trustee lacks merit. The Scharf family owns the majority of the Corporation's shares, and the evidence adduced at trial indicates that appellants are still de facto in control of it. Accordingly, as "owners" of the Premises under the HMC, appellants are obligated to undertake and finance the restoration of the Premises under the lower court's order of September 26, 1995.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and Tenant - Housing Code Violations - Restoration of Fire Damaged Building - Applicability of Housing Maintenance Code The Housing Maintenance Code (HMC) (Administrative Code of City of NY, tit 27, ch 2), which provides for enforcement of minimum housing standards and requires the owner to keep the premises in good repair and to be responsible for compliance with the requirements of the code, is controlling in a dispute arising out of the restoration of a building that was destroyed by fire where the trial court found the premises salvageable, although at considerable cost, since the fundamental purpose of the HMC is to correct any condition leading to the deterioration of salvageable housing. Although in poor condition, most of the building exists.

Landlord and Tenant - Rent Regulation - Restoration of Fire Damaged Building - Economic Infeasibility Defense - Proof of Cost In a Housing Part proceeding where petitioners, rent-stabilized tenants and proprietary lessees, seek restoration of a building that was severely damaged by fire, respondent shareholders and managing agents of respondent cooperative corporation failed to establish the affirmative defense of economic infeasibility. Although the cost of repairs would most likely exceed the market value of the restored premises, the respondents presented no proof of the costs they would incur if the building is not restored, which is necessary for comparison. If not restored, respondents will have to bear the cost of demolition, or putting the building in compliance with the applicable statutes, as well as the cost for relocating displaced rent-stabilized tenants. In addition, the corporation and respondents may be liable to the tenant shareholders for the destruction of their proprietary leaseholds. Furthermore, respondents failed to show that the market value of the restored premises will not equal the difference between the cost of restoration and the cost of demolishing along with all other expenses ancillary to the cost of demolition. Finally, no proof was presented that, once restored, the premises would not generate enough income to recover the cost discrepancy in a reasonable time.

Landlord and Tenant - Rent Regulation - Restoration of Fire Damaged Building - Applicability of Marine Rule The marine rule, which provides that if the cost of restoration of a building exceeds one half the value of the building before the damage, then it is deemed a total destruction and does not require repair, is inapplicable to residential buildings, especially where there are protected tenancies and cooperative apartments. To apply the marine rule to residential property would thwart the public policy that housing be maintained in safe and habitable condition.

Landlord and Tenant - Rent Regulation - Restoration of Fire Damaged Building - Economic Infeasibility Defense - Self-Inflicted Hardship In a Housing Part proceeding where petitioners, rent-stabilized tenants and proprietary lessees, seek restoration of a building that was severely damaged by fire, respondent shareholders and managing agents of respondent cooperative corporation may not rely on the affirmative defense of economic infeasibility, where one respondent, acting as president of the Board of Directors, deliberately reduced the original $ 3 million replacement cost insurance policy and did not report the reduction to the corporations' Board of Directors for three months. Other members of the cooperative board and the shareholders were not given any serious input into the significant reduction in insurance coverage. The underinsurance amounts to a bad business decision for whose consequences they are responsible. This underinsurance also breached respondents' statutory duty and contractual obligations to the petitioner proprietary lessees. The corporations' offering plan provided that the sponsors would carry at least $ 3 million in replacement cost insurance and respondents never amended the offering plan to disclose the reduced coverage in violation of 13 NYCRR 18.3 (w) (11). Inasmuch as respondents and the other sponsors still held two thirds of the shares and controlled the corporation, respondents' failure to amend the offering plan to disclose the underinsurance breached a duty both to the proprietary lessees and the public at large.

Constitutional Law - Taking of Property - Restoration of Fire Damaged Residential Apartment Building - Physical Taking In a Housing Part proceeding, an order mandating respondent shareholders and managing agents of respondent cooperative corporation to restore a building that was severely damaged by fire and was formerly occupied by petitioners, rent-stabilized tenants and proprietary lessees, does not constitute an unconstitutional physical taking. The statutory requirement that a landlord keep residential property in good repair is a legitimate exercise of the police power. Respondents purchased the premises and operated it as residential property subject to the Housing Maintenance Code (Administrative Code of City of NY, tit 27, ch 2) and other housing laws. This order protects the existing tenancies and leaseholds.

Constitutional Law - Taking of Property - Restoration of Fire Damaged Residential Apartment Building - Regulatory Taking In a Housing Part proceeding, an order mandating respondent shareholders and managing agents of respondent cooperative corporation to restore a building that was severely damaged by fire and was formerly occupied by petitioners, rent-stabilized tenants and proprietary lessees, does not constitute an unconstitutional regulatory taking. Government regulation may be deemed a regulatory taking if it denies an owner economically viable use of his property or if it does not substantially advance legitimate State interests. Respondents made no showing that the premises, once restored, will not generate sufficient income to offset the restoration expenses over time. Moreover, the State has a legitimate interest in keeping residential properties habitable, even after fire damage, and the Housing Maintenance Code (Administrative Code of City of NY, tit 27, ch 2) and other statutes and regulations advance these interests.

Landlord and Tenant - Rent Regulation - "Owner" Defined In a Housing Part proceeding, respondents, a cooperative corporation's president who is directly in control of the premises, and the registered managing agent for the premises who is a partner in the management company and secretary of the corporation, are "owners" under the Housing Maintenance Code (Administrative Code of City of NY, tit 27, ch 2) inasmuch as they are directly or indirectly in control of the premises. The Housing Maintenance Code will pierce the corporate veil and hold the true owners rather than holders of title responsible for violations. Here, along with other family members under their control, respondents were the majority shareholders of the corporations' shares, controlled the Board of Directors and made and implemented all policy decisions, including the relinquishment of full replacement cost casualty insurance, thus causing the premises to be underinsured at the time of the fire. They also acted as landlords to the nonshareholder tenants and caused the corporation to seek protection of the Bankruptcy Court. The corporation was the alter ego of the respondents. Despite the existence of a bankruptcy trustee, the family still owns the majority of the corporation's shares and respondents are still de facto in control.

People v. Conrad


97-029


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE TERM, SECOND DEPARTMENT


169 Misc. 2d 1066; 654 N.Y.S.2d 226; 1996 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 525



September 26, 1996, Decided


NOTICE:   [***1]  EDITED FOR PUBLICATION



DISPOSITION: Reversed on the law, accusatory instruments charging defendants with violation of Vehicle and Traffic Law section 1192(2) reinstated and matters remanded to the court below for further proceedings.


HEADNOTES

Motor Vehicles - Operating Vehicle while Under Influence of Alcohol or Drugs - Former Jeopardy - License Suspension Pending Prosecution A preliminary license suspension pursuant to Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1193 (2) (e) (7) imposed during the course of the prosecution of a Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1192 (2) violation does not violate the Double Jeopardy Clause (NY Const, art I, § 6; US Const 5th Amend), even though section 1192 (2) and section 1193 (2) (e) (7) constitute the same offense for the purposes of double jeopardy, since section 1193 (2) (e) (7) is a remedial sanction, and the two sections do not constitute separate prosecutions. The Legislature intended to impose cumulative sanctions under these two statutes for a single course of conduct. Although the license suspension imposes some deterrence, the deterrence is not so punitive as to be unconstitutional. The public safety is enhanced by this judicially ordered suspension in the context of the already-existing criminal proceeding. Also, to the extent that the temporary suspension lasts only until the conclusion of the section 1192 (2) prosecution, it is not a prosecution within the meaning of CPL 40.30 (1) and does not constitute double jeopardy under CPL 40.20 (2). Finally, the statute is only viable as part of a section 1192 (2) or (3) prosecution.

People v. Perkins

Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Term, Second Department


150 Misc. 2d 543; 576 N.Y.S.2d 750; 1991 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 632



July 3, 1991 



DISPOSITION: Order unanimously reversed upon the law, information reinstated and case remanded for further proceedings.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Disorderly Conduct

Penal Law § 240.20 (3), which provides that a person is guilty of disorderly conduct when, "in a public place, he uses abusive or obscene language, or makes an obscene gesture", with intent to cause public inconvenience, annoyance or alarm, or recklessly creating a risk thereof, is constitutional on its face. This provision, directed toward the public in general, seeks to proscribe words which, by their utterance alone, inflict injury or tend naturally to evoke immediate violence or other breach of the peace and, therefore, the statute is constitutionally valid.
