People v. Santoriello


Docket No: 99SN067932


CRIMINAL COURT OF THE CITY OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


183 Misc. 2d 54; 702 N.Y.S.2d 539; 1999 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 568



December 30, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected February 7, 2000.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant brought motion to dismiss charge that defendant violated New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § 10-126(d)(1).


OVERVIEW: Defendant, who was an officer and employee of a company in the aerial advertising business, para-sailed an advertising banner over the Hudson River and was charged with violating New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § 10-126(d)(1), prohibiting any person to use, suffer, or permit to be used advertising in the form of towing banners from or upon an aircraft over the limits of New York City. Defendant's company displayed banners in navigable airspace under the authority of a Certificate of Waiver or Authorization issued by the Federal Aviation Administration, which specifically authorized defendant to conduct his business in the navigable airspace where he sailed his banners. The court granted defendant's motion to dismiss charge, finding that however salutary and well intentioned the New York Administrative Code might have been, it prohibited, not regulated, what the federal government had authorized. Thus, § 10-126(d)(1) was unconstitutional on its face.


OUTCOME: Motion to dismiss granted; charge that defendant violated municipal law prohibiting person to advertise in form of towing banners from or upon aircraft over city limits was unconstitutional, as defendant operated under authority of Certificate of Waiver or Authorization issued by the Federal Aviation Administration.


HEADNOTES

Statutes - Federal Preemption - Flying Advertisement Banners from Para-sail over Hudson River

 Administrative Code of the City of New York § 10-126 (d) (1), which makes it unlawful for a person to advertise by towing banners from or upon an aircraft over the City limits, is preempted by Federal law. Accordingly, the summons charging defendant with a violation of this statute arising from his business activities of towing advertisements from a para-sail pulled by boats in the navigable airspace over the Hudson River is dismissed. Where the Federal Government has enacted laws dealing with a particular subject matter, State or local regulation in that field is not proscribed unless national uniformity is essential. If the local ordinance entirely proscribes and prohibits that which is permitted by the Federal law, it must be uniformly stricken down. 49 USC § 40103 (a) (1) grants the United States Government exclusive sovereignty of airspace of the United States, and the Administrator of the Federal Aviation Administration (FAA) has broad authority to regulate the use of the Nation's navigable airspace (49 USC § 40103 [b] [2]). Above all else, a citizen of the United States has a public right of transit through the navigable airspace (49 USC § 40103 [a] [2]). The defendant applied for and received a Certificate of Waiver or Authorization from the FAA which allows him to operate a moored kite (para-sail) from a vessel for advertising purposes over the Hudson River. Further, the FAA defines a para-sail as a moored kite, not an aircraft. However salutary and well intentioned the Code may be, it entirely prohibits, not regulates, what the Federal Government has authorized. Thus, Administrative Code § 10-126 (d) (1) is unconstitutional on its face.

 

578 of 992 DOCUMENTS


People v. Carlson


Docket No. 99N041062


CRIMINAL COURT OF THE CITY OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


183 Misc. 2d 630; 705 N.Y.S.2d 830; 1999 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 627



December 14, 1999, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Defendant's motion granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant moved to dismiss the charges of aggravated harassment in the second degree, N.Y. Penal Law § 240.30(3), harassment in the second degree, N.Y. Penal Law § 240.26(1), and menacing in the third degree, N.Y. Penal Law § 120.15, for facial insufficiency.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that the charge of aggravated harassment had to be dismissed because the accusatory instrument failed to allege that defendant intentionally subjected complainant to physical contact, or attempted or threatened to do so. Defendant argued that merely spitting in complainant's face was insufficient to establish intentional conduct or physical contact. The court held that spitting on the complainant was offensive physical contact. For the purposes of facial sufficiency, the allegation that defendant spit in complainant's face coupled with racial epithets was adequate and defendant's motion to dismiss aggravated harassment count was denied. Speech accompanied by spitting was sufficient to charge harassment in the second degree. The physical act of spitting could be offensive physical contact but the physical menace had to place complainant in fear of imminent physical injury. Spitting in complainant's face could not result in serious physical injury or physical injury. Therefore, the menacing charge was dismissed.


OUTCOME: Defendant's motion granted in part; the court dismissed the menacing charge because spitting in complainant's face could not result in serious physical injury or physical injury. Spitting was an offensive physical contact and when accompanied by racial epithets the evidence was adequate to charge defendant with aggravated harassment and harassment.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Aggravated Harassment - Spitting in Complainant's Face while Using Racial Epithets - Facial Sufficiency

1. For purposes of facial sufficiency, spitting in the face of the complainant while using racial epithets constitutes offensive "physical contact" within the meaning of the aggravated harassment statute (Penal Law § 240.30 [3]). The statute provides that a person is guilty of the offense "when, with intent to harass, annoy, threaten or alarm another person, he or she ... [s]trikes, shoves, kicks, or otherwise subjects another person to physical contact, or attempts or threatens to do the same because of the race, color, religion or national origin of such person." The intent of the statute is to prohibit violence and physical intimidation based upon bigotry. Violent conduct and not speech is what is being regulated. Defendant's alleged conduct is precisely the type of physical intimidation based on bigotry that the statute was intended to target. Racial epithets create a viable inference that the conduct was racially motivated. Accordingly, defendant's motion to dismiss the aggravated harassment charge for facial insufficiency is denied.

Crimes - Harassment - Spitting in Complainant's Face while Using Racial Epithets - Facial Sufficiency

2. An information charging defendant with harassment in the second degree (Penal Law § 240.26 [1]) for having allegedly spit in the face of the complainant while using racial epithets is facially sufficient. The statute provides that a person is guilty of the offense "when, with intent to harass, annoy, threaten or alarm another person, [h]e or she ... strikes, shoves, kicks, or otherwise subjects another person to physical contact, or attempts or threatens to do the same." Speech accompanied by "spitting" on the victim constitutes offensive "physical contact" within the meaning of the statute for purposes of facial sufficiency. The determination of whether words which are sufficient for pleading purposes are ultimately punishable must await a trial. Accordingly, defendant's motion to dismiss the harassment charge for facial insufficiency is denied.

Crimes - Menacing - Spitting in Complainant's Face while Using Racial Epithets - Facial Sufficiency

3. A count in an information charging defendant with menacing in the third degree (Penal Law § 120.15) for having allegedly spit in the face of the complainant while using racial epithets must be dismissed for facial insufficiency. The statute provides that a person is guilty of the offense "when, by physical menace, he or she intentionally places or attempts to place another person in fear of death, imminent serious physical injury or physical injury." Spitting in complainant's face, although sufficient to constitute offensive "physical contact" for purposes of the harassment charges against defendant (see, Penal Law § 240.26 [1]; § 240.30 [3]), cannot alone constitute the type of physical menace that would place complainant in fear of imminent physical injury within the meaning of the menacing statute. The facts alleged in the information are insufficient to determine defendant's intent. Some other act must transpire before complainant could reasonably fear physical injury.

 

579 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Metropolitan Funeral Dirs. Ass'n v. City of New York


Index No. 117790/99


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


182 Misc. 2d 977; 702 N.Y.S.2d 526; 1999 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 545



December 10, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected February 22, 2000. As Corrected April 10, 2000.



DISPOSITION: Denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, a funeral directors association and its president, moved for a preliminary injunction enjoining defendants, City of New York (City), New York City Department of Consumer Affairs (DCA), and DCA's Commissioner, from enforcing newly amended local rules regulating the funeral home industry.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, a funeral directors association and its president, sought a declaratory judgment against defendants, City of New York (City), New York City Department of Consumer Affairs (DCA), and DCA's Commissioner, declaring invalid N.Y. City Rules 5-162, 5-164 ,5-165 and 5-166 as amended. Plaintiffs moved for a preliminary injunction. In order to show entitlement to a preliminary injunction, plaintiffs needed to establish (1) the likelihood of success on the merits; (2) irreparable injury absent granting the preliminary injunction; and (3) a balancing of the equities. The court denied plaintiffs' motion, because plaintiffs did not demonstrate that the equities balanced in their favor. Plaintiffs' argument against enforcement of the City's rules was that the State had preempted the field of funeral home regulation. This argument was unavailing. The court determined that regulation of the same subject matter by both the State and a locality did not give rise to an express conflict.


OUTCOME: Motion denied; plaintiffs did not show that they were entitled to a preliminary injunction. The court rejected plaintiffs argument that local regulations pertaining to the funeral home industry where preempted by State regulations.


HEADNOTES

Local Laws - Preemption by State - Regulation of Funeral Home Business

1. Rules promulgated by defendant Commissioner of the New York City Department of Consumer Affairs regarding disclosure by funeral homes of price and other information are not preempted by Public Health Law article 34 and its implementing regulations, which regulate the funeral home industry. Local laws may be invalidated as inconsistent with State law where there is an express conflict and where the State has evinced a desire to preempt an entire field, thereby precluding further local regulation. The fact that the State and City both seek to regulate the same subject matter does not in and of itself give rise to an express conflict. Although the State's laws are detailed, the State Legislature has not expressed a desire to take a centralized or comprehensive approach to regulating this industry. In fact, article 34 implicitly permits local legislation which is not inconsistent with State law.

Municipal Corporations - Regulation of Funeral Home Business - Rules Disclosing Price Information - State Preemption

2. Rules promulgated by defendant Commissioner of the New York City Department of Consumer Affairs, which require funeral homes to display price lists in the immediate vicinity of the establishment's main public entrance (6 RCNY 5-162 [c] [2]) and provide certain price information over the telephone (6 RCNY 5-164 [a], [b]), are not invalid as inconsistent with State law and regulations. The State has not preempted the field of funeral home regulation. Also, the State does not specifically permit any conduct prescribed by the rule, nor are the two sets of laws otherwise inconsistent. Public Health Law § 3440-a mandates funeral directors to furnish a written statement showing the prices, and 10 NYCRR 79.4 requires that the funeral home present a printed or typewritten price list for individuals inquiring about arrangements or prices. Section 5-162 (c) (2) merely goes beyond the State law by requiring that price lists be displayed. This additional protection is not inconsistent with State law. Similarly, section 5-164 is not inconsistent with 10 NYCRR 79.1 which regulates disclosure of pricing information over the telephone, as it simply imposes additional requirements on providers when giving price information over the telephone.

Municipal Corporations - Regulation of Funeral Home Business - Rules Disclosing Ownership Interest in Funeral Homes - State Preemption

3. A rule promulgated by defendant New York City Department of Consumer Affairs, which requires providers of funeral services to display the names of each licensed funeral director-owner and ownership entity with a 10% or greater ownership interest in the funeral home on a sign located either immediately outside or inside the main entrance, and requires the name, address and telephone number of such entity to be listed on all advertising material (6 RCNY 5-165), does not conflict with State law and regulations prohibiting unlicensed and unregistered funeral firms and directors from displaying their names upon or in the establishment or in advertising (Public Health Law § 3443 [1] [a]; 10 NYCRR 77.5 [c] [4], [5]). The rule does not conflict with or undermine the State law and regulations designed to prevent unlicensed and unregistered funeral establishments and individuals from giving the impression that they are licensed or registered, and/or doing business as such. The rule supplements the State's regulations by providing the consumer with further identifying information. Furthermore, the State regulations are not intended to prohibit additional local regulation, as 10 NYCRR 77.5 (c) (4) provides "[s]igns shall conform with all local laws, ordinances and regulations." Moreover, Public Health Law § 3443 prohibits the display of the names of unlicensed individuals and the rule requires the display of licensed individuals.

Municipal Corporations - Regulation of Funeral Home Business - Authority of Commissioner of Department of Consumer Affairs to Promulgate Rules

4. The Commissioner of the New York City Department of Consumer Affairs (DCA) acted within his authority when adopting rules which require providers of funeral services to disclose price and other information. New York City Charter § 2203 (d) and (e) give the Commissioner the power to enforce all laws relating to the sale of goods and services and to promulgate, amend and modify rules and regulations necessary to carry out the powers of the DCA. Under Administrative Code of the City of New York § 20-737, the Commissioner is broadly mandated to promulgate rules and regulations reasonably necessary to effectuate the purposes of protecting consumers from excessive funeral costs and providing for the disclosure of pricing and related information. Although the Administrative Code does not directly refer to the sale of monuments, the Commissioner's authority to regulate this area can be implied from the Commissioner's broad mandate.

Municipal Corporations - Regulation of Funeral Home Business - Rules Disclosing Price Information

5. A rule promulgated by defendant Commissioner of the New York City Department of Consumer Affairs, which requires providers of funeral services to publicly display price lists in the immediate vicinity of an establishment's main public entrance (6 RCNY 5-162), does not conflict with Administrative Code of the City of New York § 20-732, which only requires a funeral home to display pricing information prominently at the funeral home premises and to provide such information upon request. Merely because the rule provides an additional layer of protection to consumers does not mean that the rule is contrary to the Administrative Code provision or that it was enacted outside the scope of the Commissioner's authority.

Municipal Corporations - Regulation of Funeral Home Business - Rules Disclosing Price Information and Ownership Interest

6. Rules promulgated by defendant Commissioner of the New York City Department of Consumer Affairs, which require providers of funeral services to publicly display price lists in the immediate vicinity of the establishment's main public entrance (6 RCNY 5-162), disclose an owner or entity which has a 10% or greater interest in the business (6 RCNY 5-164), and disclose a separate price list for monuments are rationally related to a legitimate government purpose and, thus, are valid. Judicial review of administrative rule making is confined to determining whether rules have a rational basis and are not arbitrary, capricious, or unreasonable. The rules are rationally related to defendants' legitimate goal of protecting consumers. They also provide consumers with complete and easy access to pricing information regarding funeral costs and to ownership information. The rule regarding the sale of monuments has a rational basis in that having a separate contract for this part of the process allows consumers to evaluate needs when they are less emotionally involved.

Municipal Corporations - Regulation of Funeral Home Business - Rule Disclosing Ownership Interest in Advertising Material

7. The term "advertising material" in a rule promulgated by defendant Commissioner of the New York City Department of Consumer Affairs, which requires a funeral business to list names, addresses and phone numbers of business entities having a 10% or greater ownership interest in the funeral home on all "advertising material" (6 RCNY 5-165 [b]), is not unconstitutionally vague. Due process requires only a reasonable degree of certainty so that individuals of ordinary intelligence are not forced to guess at the meaning of statutory terms. Plaintiff entities in the funeral home business can easily interpret the term themselves in order to determine what material they use for advertising purposes.

Injunctions - Preliminary Injunction - Staying Enforcement of Rules Regulating Funeral Home Business

8. Plaintiffs are not entitled to a preliminary injunction in their action challenging rules promulgated by the New York City Department of Consumer Affairs regulating the funeral home business where they failed to file a statement pursuant to CPLR 6001, which mandates that a plaintiff seeking a provisional remedy must state whether any other such remedy has been secured or sought in the same action against the same defendants. This statement enables a court to determine whether it should exercise its discretion to require a plaintiff to elect between any provisional remedy previously obtained and that or those now sought.

 

580 of 992 DOCUMENTS


People v. Carr


20-035


JUSTICE COURT OF NEW YORK, VILLAGE OF ORCHARD PARK, ERIE COUNTY


183 Misc. 2d 94; 703 N.Y.S.2d 868; 1999 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 586



November 26, 1999, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The defendant's Motion to Dismiss is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged under N.Y. Agric. & Mkts. Law § 353 of cruelly beating his dog, defendant moved for dismissal, arguing that the statute was unconstitutionally vague in that it did not give fair notice that certain conduct was forbidden.


OVERVIEW: The court found to interpret N.Y. Agric. & Mkts. Law § 353 in such a manner that would make the defendant's alleged conduct criminal was not rational, as pursuant to N.Y. Penal Law § 240.26(1) the shoving, striking, or kicking of a person by another was not a crime, but constituted harassment, a non-criminal violation. The court found that the Legislature's intent could not have been to hold a person to a higher standard of conduct with respect to an animal than toward a fellow human being. The court found that "physical injury" had to be inferred as an element that had to be alleged and proven to sustain a violation under the statute as it applied to defendant. The court found no allegations that the defendant attempted to cause or actually caused physical injury to his dog.


OUTCOME: The defendant's motion to dismiss was denied; the court found no allegation that the defendant attempted to cause or actually caused physical injury to his dog as the intent could not have been to hold a person to a higher standard with respect to an animal.


HEADNOTES

Animals - Cruelty to Animals - Requirement of Physical Injury to Animal

In a prosecution in which it was alleged that defendant kicked his dog two or three times and threw it to the ground, an information charging defendant with cruelly beating his dog in violation of Agriculture and Markets Law § 353 must be dismissed for facial insufficiency where there are no allegations that defendant attempted to cause or actually caused physical injury to his dog. In light of the fact that one cannot legally "assault" a human being without there being "physical injury" to the victim, to interpret section 353 in such a manner as to make defendant's alleged conduct criminal is not rational. The Legislature's intent could not have been to hold a person to a higher standard of conduct with respect to an animal than toward a fellow human being. Thus, "physical injury" must necessarily be inferred to be an element which must be alleged and proven to sustain a violation under section 353.

 

581 of 992 DOCUMENTS


People v. Attawwab


INDICTMENT NO. 2047/99


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, KINGS COUNTY


182 Misc. 2d 824; 700 N.Y.S.2d 395; 1999 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 530



November 16, 1999, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Defendant's motion to preclude the state from seeking the death penalty denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Hearing on defendant's motion to preclude the plaintiff state from seeking the death penalty in a criminal prosecution for murder in the second degree and criminal possession of a weapon in the second and third degrees.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with murder in the second degree (two counts) and criminal possession of a weapon in the second and third degrees. Defendant filed a motion to preclude the plaintiff state from filing a notice of intent to seek the death penalty in his case. The motion was denied because defendant's request for an injunction was premature where the harm to the defendant was entirely speculative and contingent upon two events that had not yet occurred: the filing of a first degree murder indictment against him, and the filing of a notice of intent to seek the death penalty.


OUTCOME: Motion denied because defendant's request for an injunction was premature where the harm to the defendant was entirely speculative and contingent upon two events that had not yet occurred.


HEADNOTES

Courts - Ripeness Doctrine - Motion to Preclude State from Seeking Death Penalty

A criminal defendant's motion to enjoin the State from seeking the death penalty is denied where defendant was arraigned on an indictment charging him with second degree murder and other crimes, and the People reserved the right to file a capital murder indictment should evidence become available supporting such charges, since the issue is not ripe for judicial review. For the death penalty to be a potential punishment, the People must file a notice of intent to seek the death penalty within 120 days following defendant's arraignment upon an indictment charging first degree murder. A request for an injunction or declaratory judgment is premature if the harm to the petitioner is contingent upon events that may never occur. Here, the harm to defendant is contingent upon two events that may never occur: filing a first degree murder indictment against him and filing a notice of intent to seek the death penalty.

 

582 of 992 DOCUMENTS


People v. Santos


Docket No. 99N070057


CRIMINAL COURT OF THE CITY OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


182 Misc. 2d 764; 700 N.Y.S.2d 381; 1999 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 512



November 10, 1999, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant moved to dismiss charges of trespass and obstruction of governmental administration on the ground that the accusatory instrument was facially insufficient and to dismiss the charge of obstruction of governmental administration on the ground that the statute defining the crime was unconstitutionally vague.


OVERVIEW: Defendant returned to her former employer and sat in a chair and refused to leave the premises after repeatedly being told to leave. After arrest, a police officer attempted to take the defendant to the fingerprint station. The defendant repeatedly pulled away from the officer and finally sat in a chair and refused to get up. Defendant was charged with criminal trespass and obstruction of governmental administration. Defendant moved to dismiss her charges on the ground that the accusatory instrument was facially insufficient. Defendant also unsuccessfully argued that the statute defining the crime of obstruction was unconstitutionally vague. The court dismissed the trespass charge, because the accusatory instrument did not set forth any facts establishing that the office was fenced or otherwise enclosed in a manner designed to exclude intruders and therefore failed to establish every element of the crime of trespass and the defendant's commission of that crime.


OUTCOME: Defendant's motion for order declaring N.Y. Penal Law § 195.05 void for vagueness was denied. Defendant's motion to dismiss charge of obstruction of governmental administration on the ground that accusatory instrument was facially insufficient was denied. The motion to dismiss count charging trespass in the third degree was granted.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Criminal Trespass - Sufficiency of Accusatory Instrument

1. Under Penal Law § 140.10 (a), which provides that a person is guilty of trespass in the third degree when he or she knowingly enters or remains unlawfully in a building or upon real property which is fenced or otherwise enclosed in a manner designed to exclude intruders, the phrase "which is fenced or otherwise enclosed in a manner designed to exclude intruders," modifies both the words "building" and "upon real property." Accordingly, the accusatory instrument charging defendant with trespass in the third degree for refusing to leave a dental office, which failed to set forth that the office was enclosed in a manner to exclude intruders, is facially insufficient.

Crimes - Obstructing Governmental Administration - Interfering with Fingerprinting Process

2. Defendant was appropriately charged with obstructing governmental administration (Penal Law § 195.05) when she prevented a police officer from fingerprinting her upon her arrest. Penal Law § 195.05 prohibits a person from intentionally preventing or attempting to prevent a public servant from performing an official function by means of physical force or interference. Under CPL 160.10 (1), the arresting officer was required to fingerprint the defendant following her arrest. Thus, the officer was attempting to perform an official function when he sought to fingerprint the defendant. Also, by pulling away from the officer and sitting in a chair during the fingerprint attempt, the defendant affirmatively resisted the officer's attempt to fingerprint her and these actions on her part constitute physical force as well as physically interfering with the officer's attempt to perform an official function.

Crimes - Obstructing Governmental Administration - Constitutionality of Statute 

3. Penal Law § 195.05, which prohibits a person from intentionally obstructing, impairing, or perverting the administration of law or other governmental function by preventing or attempting to prevent a public servant from performing an official function by means of intimidation, physical force or interference, or by means of any independently unlawful act, is not unconstitutionally vague. A legislative enactment must inform the average person of fair notice that his or her action is forbidden by law and may not engender arrests, prosecutions and convictions that are arbitrary and erratic. What is and what is not the administration of law or other governmental function is not beyond the understanding of the average person. The prohibition of preventing the performance of an official function by means of intimidation, physical force or interference, or by means of any independently unlawful act is also clear and not unconstitutionally vague in defining the type of activities which are prohibited, and is not subject to arbitrary and erratic arrests, prosecutions and convictions. That portion of the statute which sets forth what constitutes preventing or attempting to prevent the performance of an official function is also clear, unambiguous and not subject to interpretation. It prohibits an individual from intentionally and affirmatively engaging in conduct which is designed to prevent the orderly administration of government.

 

583 of 992 DOCUMENTS


In re Dormitory Auth. of New York


Index No. 403257/95


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


182 Misc. 2d 565; 699 N.Y.S.2d 645; 1999 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 504



November 1, 1999, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Condemnor's motion for summary judgment is granted and claimant's claims for compensation are dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Condemnor, pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3212, sought a motion for summary judgment dismissing claimant's compensation claim.


OVERVIEW: The Rent Stabilization Laws, N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 9, § 2500.1 et seq., protected claimant's month-to-month occupancy. Claimant individually filed a claim for compensation, wherein claimant sought damages for the loss of his claimed leasehold based upon the difference between his former rent, and the rent on the premises to which he relocated, times a multiple of two to compensate for the downsize in space for the period of his remaining life expectancy of 25 years. While claimant's, month-to-month single room occupancy was protected by the rent laws against removal in the ordinary relationship of landlord and tenant, the statutes did not in themselves create any further property interest, let alone the life estate claimant asserted. Claimant failed to demonstrate a compensable property interest arising out of his former occupancy of a room. Therefore, condemnor's motion for summary judgment was granted and claimant's compensation claims were dismissed.


OUTCOME: Motion for summary judgment granted; claimant's compensation claims were dismissed; the court held that the Rent Stabilization Laws protected against his removal in the ordinary landlord/tenant relationship, but the laws did not create any further property interest especially not the life estate that claimant asserted.


HEADNOTES

Eminent Domain - Award - Month-to-Month Tenant

Although a month-to-month tenant in a single room occupancy (SRO) residence is protected by rent laws against removal, such statutes do not create any further property interest. Accordingly, inasmuch as a month-to-month tenant has no property interest that entitles him to compensation in an eminent domain proceeding, the tenant's claims for compensation based on an asserted life estate are dismissed. In condemnation, the compensation awarded an owner is based upon an unencumbered fee value. Any award made to a tenant for his leasehold interest would then be carved out of the award the owner received. If every SRO tenant in a condemned SRO hotel had his interest valued as claimant contends, then the sum total of their "leasehold claims" would leave nothing for the fee owner.
