Int'l Indosuez Fin. v. Nat'l Reserve Bank


Index No. 601467/99, Motion Sequence No. 003


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


1999 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 476



October 18, 1999, Decided  
October 22, 1999, Filed


NOTICE: NOT APPROVED BY REPORTER OF DECISIONS FOR REPORTING IN STATE REPORTS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff moved for summary judgment against defendant bank, based on defendant's allegedly admitted default under a global agreement governing a series of 14 foreign currency exchange forward transactions entered into by the parties; defendant cross-moved for dismissal of the complaint.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff a corporation of the Netherlands commenced this action to recover past due amounts arising out of defendant's default under a series of non-deliverable forward currency exchange transactions it entered into with plaintiff from September 1997 through July 1998. Defendant was organized under the laws of Russia and was not qualified to do business in the state of New York. The court ruled that each transaction was reflected in a confirmation signed on behalf of defendant, by a Deputy Chairman of the Board of defendant. New York law governed 10 of the 14 transactions at issue. The remaining four transactions were governed by English law. The court determined that the plaintiff's motion for summary judgment as to liability only should have been granted because on the papers presented to the court, the amount the plaintiff was entitled to was uncertain. The issue of damages was referred to a Special Referee.


OUTCOME: Plaintiff's motion for summary judgment as to liability only should have been granted because amount plaintiff was entitled to was uncertain; issue of damages was referred to a Special Referee.

 

585 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Marylou L. v. Tenecha L.


Index No. V05631/99


FAMILY COURT OF NEW YORK, KINGS COUNTY


182 Misc. 2d 457; 698 N.Y.S.2d 827; 1999 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 474



October 15, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected December 13, 1999.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, the grandmother of a child placed in temporary, kinship foster care under Fam. Ct. Act §1055(a), filed a petition seeking permanent custody of the child.


OVERVIEW: This proceeding for custody of a child in foster care was brought by petitioner, the child's grandmother. Respondent mother argued that the petitioner lacked standing to seek permanent custody of the child who was placed in temporary custody, pursuant to Fam. Ct. Act §1055(a). Petitioner argued that the policy reflected in recent amendments to the Fam. Ct. Act and N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law conferred standing on petitioner to seek custody. The court dismissed the petition for custody as unauthorized by the statute under which the child was placed in foster care. Moreover, treating the request as a petition for a permanency hearing under Fam. Ct. Act §1055(b)(ii), petitioner failed to allege facts sufficient to establish standing to seek permanent custody of the child. The child had not remained in the petitioner's care for a 15-month period as required by N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law §384-b (3)(1)(i)(A).


OUTCOME: Petition for custody must be dismissed as unauthorized by the statute under which the child was placed in foster care. The petition failed to allege facts sufficient to establish standing or to give rise to a cause of action for permanent neglect.


HEADNOTES

Social Services - Foster Care - Custody of Child Not Freed for Adoption - Standing of Kinship Foster Parent to Seek Permanent Custody

Petitioner, the paternal grandmother and foster mother of a child who had been placed under Family Court article 10 in the temporary custody of the Administration for Children's Services upon a finding that the child was neglected by her biological mother, lacks standing to seek permanent custody of the child over the mother's objection. A foster parent does not have standing to seek custody of a child in temporary foster care, and under the specific terms of Family Court Act article 10 this principle applies equally to kinship foster parents where a parent appears in the proceeding and objects to the relative's intervention. Where a parent withholds consent, the court may only place the child temporarily in the custody of a relative, and even where there has been a termination of parental rights the appropriate means for a relative to obtain permanent custody of the child is to file a petition for adoption. Although Family Court Act § 1055 (b), as recently amended, permits placement of a child out of foster care and permanently in the care of a fit and willing relative, such petitions are limited to circumstances in which the parents' right to custody and guardianship of the child may otherwise be terminated.

 

586 of 992 DOCUMENTS


People v. Mohamadou


Docket No: 99N022538


CRIMINAL COURT OF THE CITY OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


182 Misc. 2d 77; 698 N.Y.S.2d 445; 1999 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 443



October 14, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected November 22, 1999.



DISPOSITION: Motion to Dismiss Denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant moved to dismiss charge of resisting arrest, on the basis that, subsequent to the arrest the underlying statute was deemed unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: A police officer observed defendant hand another person a NYC Transit Authority token in exchange for cash. When defendant was apprehended by the arresting officer, he allegedly flailed his arms which prevented the officer from effecting the authorized arrest. Defendant was charged with illegal access to transit authority services, and resisting arrest. A judge later declared that Transit Authority Rule § 1050.4(c) was vague and unconstitutional on its face. Thus, the court had to determine whether the charge of resisting arrest is valid when a police officer made an authorized arrest even though subsequent to the arrest the underlying statute has been deemed unconstitutional. The court held that that the charge of resisting arrest is valid since the police effected an "authorized arrest" at the time of the arrest. Review of case law indicated that dismissal of the underlying crime did not necessarily warrant preclusion of crime of resisting arrest.


OUTCOME: Motion to dismiss denied. Court held that the charge of resisting arrest was valid since the police effected an "authorized arrest" at the time of the arrest.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Resisting Arrest - Unconstitutionality of Underlying Offense

Defendant's motion to dismiss a charge of resisting arrest (Penal Law § 205.30) is denied where the offense for which defendant was arrested, exchanging a transit token for cash, was subsequently declared unconstitutional, since the arresting officer was effecting an "authorized arrest", which is an essential element of the crime of resisting arrest. Where the underlying charge is dismissed for facial insufficiency, the arrest is unauthorized; however, a disposition favorable to the defendant on the underlying charge will not automatically render invalid a resisting arrest charge arising out of the same incident. Accordingly, since the officer who arrested defendant made an authorized arrest for conduct reasonably believed to constitute an offense, the resisting arrest charge arose from subsequent independent unlawful actions of the defendant and, therefore, may be prosecuted independently of the underlying charge.

 

587 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Moran Towing Corp. v. Urbach


Index No. 3543-98, (RJI No. 0198-ST8909)


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, ALBANY COUNTY


182 Misc. 2d 756; 699 N.Y.S.2d 252; 1999 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 509



October 7, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reversed by, Motion denied by Moran Towing Corp. v. Urbach, 283 A.D.2d 78, 726 N.Y.S.2d 748, 2001 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6005 (N.Y. App. Div. 3d Dep't, 2001)
Affirmed by, Appeal after remand at Moran Towing Corp. v. Urbach, 2003 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11736 (N.Y. App. Div. 3d Dep't, Nov. 13, 2003)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Both the petitioner and the intervenor's challenges to the legislative amendments and Tax Law Article 13-A are dismissed with $ 100.00 costs for failure to exhaust administrative remedies.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner taxpayers commenced proceedings challenging a tax obligation imposed by respondent Commissioner of the New York State Department of Taxation and Finance on the grounds that N.Y. Tax Law § 301-a violated the commerce clause, U.S. Const. art. I, § 8, cl. 3.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner taxpayers claimed they were entitled to refunds going back to the inception of N.Y. Tax Law § 301-a and sought a declaration that N.Y. Tax Law § 301-a violated the federal commerce clause, U.S. Const. art I, § 8, cl. 3. Petitioners conceded that they failed to exhaust their administrative remedies before instituting this proceeding; however, petitioners claimed that administrative exhaustion was unnecessary, because the statute was facially unconstitutional and exhaustion would be futile. The court held that futility would only apply here if petitioners demonstrated that the statutes are facially unconstitutional and therefore there would be no point in developing the facts through the administrative process applying those unconstitutional statutes. Here petitioners failed to meet their heavy burden of demonstrating that the challenged statutes were facially unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: Petition denied; petitioner taxpayers' action challenging legislative amendments to tax laws was dismissed because petitioners failed to exhaust administrative remedies.


HEADNOTES

Administrative Law - Failure to Exhaust Administrative Remedies - Challenge to Tax on Fuel Oil - Facial Unconstitutionality - Futility

1. In a challenge to the facial constitutionality of recent statutory amendments to Tax Law article 13-A, which imposes a tax on fuel oil imported into New York by vessels for consumption by the importer within New York while engaged in interstate commerce, the claim of petitioners, who have concededly not exhausted their administrative remedies, that exhaustion of administrative remedies would be futile, is superfluous and misleading, adding nothing but pointless circularity to petitioners' argument. Futility would only apply if petitioners demonstrate that the statutes are facially unconstitutional and, therefore, there would be no point in developing the facts through the administrative process applying those unconstitutional statutes. Of course, if the statute is facially unconstitutional, it is unnecessary to claim futility because petitioners would be entitled to avoid administrative exhaustion based on facial unconstitutionality alone.

Taxation - Motor Fuel Taxes - Constitutionality of Tax on Imported Fuel Oil

2. In a challenge to the facial constitutionality of recent statutory amendments to Tax Law article 13-A, which imposes a tax on fuel oil imported into New York by vessels for consumption by the importer within New York while engaged in interstate commerce, Matter of Tug Buster Bouchard Corp. v Wetzler (217 AD2d 192), which prompted the amendments in question, does not prohibit all efforts to tax fuel that is purchased in other States and used in New York. Tug Buster did not hold that imposing a tax on fuel purchased outside of New York and consumed within New York would necessarily violate the Commerce Clause; the Court recognized that there would be no violation so long as the tax imposed did not interfere with interstate commerce by favoring the purchase of fuel in New York. Tug Buster may not be used to invalidate Tax Law § 301-a, which has already been judicially upheld as facially constitutional, since that decision was specifically limited to Tax Law § 301 (a) (1) (ii), a very different statute. Petitioners' attempt to point out differences between themselves and the petitioner in the case which has upheld section 301-a is an "as applied" argument. Moreover, petitioners' attempt to circumvent any inquiry into whether the statute has any actual impact on interstate commerce by relying on a 1929 United States Supreme Court case is unavailing, since the Court's simplistic analysis bears little or no resemblance to its present-day Commerce Clause analysis.

Administrative Law - Failure to Exhaust Administrative Remedies - Challenge to Tax on Fuel Oil - Failure to Establish Facial Unconstitutionality 

3. In a challenge to the facial constitutionality of recent statutory amendments to Tax Law article 13-A, which imposes a tax on fuel oil imported into New York by vessels for consumption by the importer within New York while engaged in interstate commerce, petitioners have failed to establish that Tax Law § 301-a is facially unconstitutional and, therefore, their challenge to it must be dismissed for their failure to exhaust their administrative remedies and present their arguments and facts for review at the administrative level.

Taxation - Motor Fuel Taxes - Constitutionality of Tax on Imported Fuel Oil

4. In a challenge to the constitutionality of recent statutory amendments to Tax Law article 13-A, which imposes a tax on fuel oil imported into New York by vessels for consumption by the importer within New York while engaged in interstate commerce, the State may retroactively adjust Tax Law § 301 (a) (1) (ii). Retroactive taxation is not prohibited, and the type of adjustment in question does not constitute imposition of any new or greater tax upon petitioners, does not change petitioners' expectations during almost all of the years they seek refunds for, and does not require the time restrictions normally placed on retroactive taxes. There is no unfairness worth mentioning because petitioners would be in essentially the same position they would have been had the court denied a prior facial unconstitutionality argument and required the prior petitioner to proceed through the administrative process and establish that the statute actually does burden interstate commerce.

Taxation - Motor Fuel Taxes - Constitutionality of Tax on Imported Fuel Oil

5. In a challenge to the constitutionality of recent statutory amendments to Tax Law article 13-A, which imposes a tax on fuel oil imported into New York by vessels for consumption by the importer within New York while engaged in interstate commerce, petitioners have no valid due process argument, since the judicial decision which prompted the amendments in question did not bestow any property interest or expectation on petitioners. Although petitioners allege that the amendments violate NY Constitution, article III, § 16, they have failed to establish that the incorporation of any Tax Law sections by reference has resulted in the creation of substantive obligations or requirements, or that there is any reason to believe that the Legislature was unaware of what it was doing. Notwithstanding the possibility that, even with the latest legislative enactments, the challenged tax was not passed through to in-State fuel purchasers, the recent statutes, when given the favorable benefit of every intention, eliminate the former facial unconstitutionality of Tax Law § 301 (a) (1) (ii). Accordingly, petitioners may not challenge the statute's constitutionality as applied in court, unless they have first exhausted their administrative remedies, raising all of their arguments and presenting any evidence that establishes that in fact the statute's costs are not passed through to consumers or that the statute otherwise contravenes the Commerce Clause by favoring the purchase of fuel in New York, as well as the actual extent of the alleged preferential effect so as to determine what if any tax refund is warranted.

 

588 of 992 DOCUMENTS


People v. Schroedel


Indict. # 115-99


COUNTY COURT OF NEW YORK, SULLIVAN COUNTY


182 Misc. 2d 154; 697 N.Y.S.2d 904; 1999 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 450



October 4, 1999, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Motion to plea to entire indictment 115-99 denied in its entirety.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant moved to plead guilty to an entire indictment, charging him, inter alia, with first degree murder, without the consent of plaintiff People nor permission of trial court, during the pendency of N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 250.40 120-day period for filing of notice of intent to seek death penalty.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was indicted on charges of first degree murder, among other things. Before plaintiff People decided whether it would seek the death penalty, defendant pled guilty to the entire indictment. The issue on appeal was whether a defendant arraigned upon an indictment charging murder in the first degree under N.Y. Penal Law § 125.27 may plead to the entire indictment and avoid the possibility of a death penalty verdict without the permission of the court and the consent of plaintiff when a notice of intent to seek the death penalty was not pending, but the 120-day period for such filing under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 250.40 has not yet run. The court held defendant could not plead under these circumstances until plaintiff had either declined to file his § 250.40 notice on the record, consented to such plea, or the 120-day period with any court ordered extensions had expired.


OUTCOME: Defendant's motion to plea entire indictment was denied; defense tactics invoked to avoid the possibility of a death penalty verdict violated the rights of the People to seek the death penalty for certain specifically enumerated criminal conduct.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Murder - Capital Murder - Guilty Plea

Defendant may not plead guilty to first degree murder during the pendency of the CPL 250.40 120-day period for the filing of a notice of intent to seek the death penalty without the consent of the District Attorney and the permission of the court.

 

589 of 992 DOCUMENTS


LaValle v. Hayden


INDEX NO. 05029/1998


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, SUFFOLK COUNTY


182 Misc. 2d 409; 696 N.Y.S.2d 782; 1999 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 438



September 30, 1999, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs brought an action for declaratory and injunctive relief, taking issue with the manner in which defendant members of the Board of Regents of the University of the State of New York were elected pursuant to the alternative joint ballot provision of N.Y. Educ. Law §§ 202(1) and (2).


OVERVIEW: In their action for declaratory and injunctive relief, plaintiffs challenged the manner in which defendant members of the Board of Regents of the University of the State of New York were elected pursuant to the alternative joint ballot provision of N.Y. Educ. Law §§ 202(1) and (2). Plaintiffs claimed the manner of election violated their right to a bicameral legislature, their right to be heard and to participate in the formulation and effectuation of state educational policy through elected representatives to the New York Senate, their right to one-person, one-vote and to proportional representation, and their right to a republican form of government. The court upheld the contested legislation as constitutional, holding that whether sitting bicamerally or unicamerally, the New York Legislature was the Legislature under N.Y. Const. art. 11, § 2. Further, the "one man, one vote" principle was not applicable to the election of regents.


OUTCOME: The court upheld the constitutionality of the contested legislation since plaintiffs, the parties challenging the constitutionality of the legislation, failed in their burden of overcoming the legislation's strong presumption of constitutionality.


HEADNOTES

Legislature - Election of Regents - Constitutionality of Joint Ballot Alternative

The statutory provision providing for the election of members of the Board of Regents by joint ballot of both houses of the Legislature meeting in joint session in the event that the Regents cannot be elected by concurrent resolution of the Legislature (Education Law § 202 [1], [2]) does not unconstitutionally delegate a legislative power or function to the unicameral Legislature. Although the unicameral Legislature cannot exercise legislative power and pass a bill into law (NY Const, art III, §§ 1, 14), the Senate and Assembly, sitting unicamerally as a single body, still continues to constitute the Legislature, and as such, may constitutionally elect Regents. Furthermore, the joint ballot alternative for the election of Regents does not result in an unconstitutional delegation of the Legislature's fundamental law-making powers and functions. There is no constitutional prohibition against the legislative delegation of power, with reasonable safeguards and standards, to an agency or commission to administer the law as enacted by the Legislature. The "objects" of the University of the State of New York, the limitation on those persons who could be Regents, and the powers to be exercised by Regents (see, Education Law §§ 201, 202 [4]; § 207) sufficiently set forth over-all policy and purpose as well as general standards and guidelines to withstand constitutional scrutiny. The joint ballot mechanism is necessary to break legislative deadlocks over appointments and thereby ensure that seats on the Board of Regents do not remain empty. Such procedure neither violates the equal protection principle of "one person, one vote" nor the constitutional guarantee of a republican form of government (US Const, art IV, § 4).

 

590 of 992 DOCUMENTS


People v. Brignoni


Docket No. 98X047773


CRIMINAL COURT OF THE CITY OF NEW YORK, BRONX COUNTY


182 Misc. 2d 779; 701 N.Y.S.2d 253; 1999 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 521



September 23, 1999, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The defendant's motion to dismiss on the ground that VTL 511(d) is unconstitutional is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: During trial, defendant moved for dismissal of the charge against him for aggravated failure to answer appearance tickets or pay fines imposed pursuant to N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 511-d on the grounds that the statute was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Defendant had in effect twenty or more license suspensions imposed for failure to answer, appear, or pay a fine and was charged with aggravated failure to answer appearance tickets or pay fines imposed pursuant to N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 511-d. Defendant contended that the statute was unconstitutional because it violated his constitutional right against double jeopardy, U.S. Const. amend. V, and N.Y. Const. art. I, § 6. Under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 40.20(1), a person cannot be twice prosecuted for the same offense. The court found that defendant's previous license suspension did not constitute a prosecution because it neither terminated in a conviction upon a plea of guilty nor proceeded to the trial stage as codified in N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 40.30(1). Additionally, there were no equal protection or due process concerns because N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 420.10(5) provided a mechanism protecting defendant against incarceration as an alternative to payment due to defendant's inability to pay.


OUTCOME: Defendant's motion to dismiss the charges was dismissed because the statute did not violate his constitutional right against double jeopardy.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Former Jeopardy - Aggravated Failure to Answer Appearance Tickets or Pay Fines

1. Vehicle and Traffic Law § 511-d, which makes it a misdemeanor to have in effect 20 or more license suspensions imposed for failure to answer, appear or pay a fine under Vehicle and Traffic Law § 226 (3) and/or § 510 (4) (a), does not violate double jeopardy principles, which protect a person from being separately prosecuted for two offenses based upon the same act or criminal transaction ( CPL 40.20 [1]). A suspension of a license under Vehicle and Traffic Law § 226 (3) or § 510 (4) (a) does not constitute a prosecution (see, CPL 40.30 [1]). Even if it does, further prosecution under section 511-d is not barred, since double jeopardy is not violated if one of the offenses requires proof of an additional fact, and CPL 40.20 (2) (b) permits sequential prosecutions for offenses arising from the same criminal transaction if each offense contains an element which is not an element of the other and the offenses are designed to prevent different kinds of harm. Prosecution under section 511-d requires different elements than section 226 (3) and section 510 (4) (a), its legislative purpose is to prosecute persons who show contempt for our system of driver regulation, and the statute is designed to prevent different kinds of harm than the underlying statutes. The legislative purpose of the underlying statutes is to suspend or revoke driving privileges where a person fails to answer, appear or pay a fine.

Constitutional Law - Validity of Statute - Aggravated Failure to Answer Appearance Tickets or Pay Fines

2. Vehicle and Traffic Law § 511-d, which makes it a misdemeanor to have in effect 20 or more license suspensions imposed for failure to answer, appear or pay a fine, does not constitute cruel and unusual punishment or violate due process to the extent it is based on a status or condition, not an act. To meet due process standards a statute must make it reasonably clear what conduct constitutes a violation and must provide sufficient enforcement standards to prevent arbitrary and discriminatory enforcement, which is not apparent here. Also section 511-d establishes criminal liability for an omission, i.e., failure to respond to appearance tickets or to pay fines. Penal Law § 15.10 provides that the minimal requirement for criminal liability is the omission to perform an act.

Constitutional Law - Validity of Statute - Aggravated Failure to Answer Appearance Tickets or Pay Fines

3. Vehicle and Traffic Law § 511-d, which makes it a misdemeanor to have in effect 20 or more license suspensions imposed for failure to answer, appear or pay a fine, does not violate due process or equal protection. An equal protection violation occurs when similarly situated people are denied equal protection or access to the same resources due to classifications. The defense arguments do not show that the statute violates due process or equal protection principles.

Constitutional Law - Validity of Statute - Aggravated Failure to Answer Appearance Tickets or Pay Fines

4. Vehicle and Traffic Law § 511-d, which makes it a misdemeanor to have in effect 20 or more license suspensions imposed for failure to answer, appear or pay a fine, is not unconstitutional for failure to have a Statute of Limitations. The violation of the statute constitutes a misdemeanor. Thus, the statute is subject to the Statute of Limitations in CPL 30.10 (2) (c), which mandates that a prosecution for a misdemeanor must be commenced within two years after commission of the crime.

Constitutional Law - Validity of Statute - Aggravated Failure to Answer Appearance Tickets or Pay Fines

5. Vehicle and Traffic Law § 511-d, which makes it a misdemeanor to have in effect 20 or more license suspensions imposed for failure to answer, appear or pay a fine, does not violate due process or equal protection principles to the extent that it authorizes imprisonment for nonpayment of a fine, even if nonpayment results from inability to pay. Constitutional principles are violated when an individual is incarcerated, without a proper hearing, as an alternative to payment when such person is unable to pay through no fault of his or her own. CPL 420.10 (5) provides for a hearing and, thus, protects against incarceration as an alternative to payment for a defendant unable to pay.

Constitutional Law - Ex Post Facto Law

6. Vehicle and Traffic Law § 511-d, which makes it a misdemeanor to have in effect 20 or more license suspensions imposed for failure to answer, appear or pay a fine, is not unconstitutional as an ex post facto law, since only suspensions imposed after the effective date of the statute may be the basis for prosecution.

 

591 of 992 DOCUMENTS


In re Adoption of Jonee


Docket Nos. A-25547-50/98


FAMILY COURT OF NEW YORK, KINGS COUNTY


181 Misc. 2d 822; 695 N.Y.S.2d 920; 1999 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 409



September 14, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected November 15, 1999.



DISPOSITION: Granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, who had been foster parent to her nieces for several years, applied to adopt her nieces. Petitioner claimed that she was entitled to adopt them despite her manslaughter conviction because the New York State Adoption and Safe Families Act, N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law §§ 378-a(2)(e)(1) and 378-a(2)(h), which prohibited foster parenting or adoption by someone with a felony conviction for homicide, was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner, foster parent to her four nieces, applied to adopt her nieces after their parents executed judicial surrenders. However, the New York State Adoption and Safe Families Act, N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law §§ 378-a(2)(e)(1) and 378-a(2)(h) prohibited foster parenting or adoption by someone with a felony conviction for homicide. Petitioner claimed that she was entitled to adopt her nieces despite her manslaughter conviction because §§ 378-a(2)(e)(1) and 378-a(2)(h) were unconstitutional. Family court agreed, holding that the sections established an irrebuttable presumption that a person with a criminal history was unfit to raise children. Irrebuttable presumptions were prohibited by the due process clause if they were not necessarily or universally true in fact and the state had reasonable alternative means of making the crucial determination. In petitioner's case, the state was able to determine that petitioner was fit to adopt her nieces. Several expert witnesses testified that she was a fit parent, had a strong commitment to her nieces, and that adoption by petitioner was in the girls' best interests. As a result, family court granted petitioner's application for adoption.


OUTCOME: Family court granted petitioner's application to adopt her nieces despite petitioner's conviction for manslaughter. The court held that the provisions of the New York State Adoption and Safe Families Act that prohibited adoption by someone with a manslaughter conviction violated due process because they created an irrebuttable presumption that a person convicted of manslaughter was unfit to raise children.


HEADNOTES

Adoption - Agency Adoption - Adoption and Safe Families Act Prohibition against Adoption by Certain Convicted Criminals

 Social Services Law § 378-a (2) (e) (1) and (2) (h), enacted by New York's Adoption and Safe Families Act (L 1999, ch 7 [ASFA]) so as to require a court to summarily deny pending adoption petitions and immediately remove children from a foster or preadoptive home whenever the petitioner has been convicted of specified criminal offenses, creates an irrebuttable presumption that fails to satisfy the due process requirements of the State and Federal Constitutions. Accordingly, in an adoption proceeding where petitioner seeks to adopt the four nieces who have been living with her for the past seven years and the unequivocal evidence establishes that it is in the children's best interests for petitioner to adopt them, the petitions are granted despite the fact that 21 years ago petitioner pleaded guilty to manslaughter and served three years' probation for recklessly killing her abusive paramour, which conviction would otherwise bar petitioner from adopting the children under ASFA. Permanent irrebuttable presumptions are disfavored under the Due Process Clause, and Family Court can readily provide the individualized determination that due process requires to protect the liberty interests of petitioner and her nieces. Absent such an individualized determination, petitioner's nieces and thousands of other children in State-supervised foster care are subject to an arbitrary procedure that leaves their fate to happenstance.

 

592 of 992 DOCUMENTS


People v. Smelefsky


Indictment No. 3262/98, Indictment No. 3263/98


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, QUEENS COUNTY


182 Misc. 2d 11; 695 N.Y.S.2d 689; 1999 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 396



September 8, 1999, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Defendant's plea accepted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court reviewed whether the plea made by defendant to first degree murder was valid under the plea procedure followed before the court when a notice of intent to seek the death penalty was not withdrawn by plaintiff prior to start of the plea proceedings.


OVERVIEW: Defendant suspect was charged with murder. Plaintiff state filed a notice of intent to seek the death penalty. Defendant and plaintiff agreed that defendant would plead guilty to first degree murder in exchange for life without parole. Plaintiff was reluctant to withdraw the notice of intent prior to defendant's plea because plaintiff was unable to refile the notice if defendant failed to plead. Plaintiff was not allowed to conditionally withdraw the plea. In court, plaintiff stated the agreement and its intention to withdraw the notice if defendant entered a guilty plea and received the agreed sentence. The court then questioned defendant regarding the plea. Plaintiff consented to the plea and withdrew the notice. The court granted the plea application. After a review of the proceedings, the court found that the plea was valid because plaintiff withdrew the notice before the court consented to the plea. The plea was not valid until the court accepted it. Therefore, plaintiff's withdrawal of the notice after defendant proffered his plea did not violate the prohibition on pleas while a defendant faced the death penalty.


OUTCOME: The court found that defendant's plea to first degree murder was valid and held that the plea proceedings did not violate the prohibition on accepting guilty pleas while a notice of intent to seek the death penalty was pending.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Murder - Capital Murder - Plea Bargaining Procedure

In a capital murder case, defendant did not plead guilty to first degree murder while a notice of intent to seek the death penalty was pending in violation of Matter of Hynes v Tomei (92 NY2d 613) when the parties engaged in the following plea bargaining process: the defendant proffered a guilty plea to first degree murder and asked the District Attorney to consent to it and to withdraw the notice of intent; the District Attorney expressed a willingness to do so, provided that an agreed-upon sentence would be imposed and defendant gave a full and truthful allocution; the defendant made a full allocution; the District Attorney consented to the plea, withdrew the notice of intent, and joined in the defendant's motion that the plea be accepted; and the court granted the motion, accepted the plea, and ordered it entered. If the prosecutor withdraws a notice of intent prior to the plea, defendant might renounce the agreement once the notice is withdrawn and the People would be barred from refiling by CPL 250.40 (4). Accordingly, the prosecutor was permitted to withhold consent and withdrawal until after the defendant proffered his plea and underwent allocution. Since defendant claimed to be seeking the disposition, he could have no legitimate complaint that the sequence unfairly infringed upon his right to renege. Moreover, since a guilty plea carrying a promised sentence cannot be entered without the permission of the court, the acceptance by the court, not the proffer by defendant, makes a guilty plea cognizable; thus, a guilty plea to murder in the first degree is valid so long as no notice of intent is pending when the court accepts and enters the plea.

 

593 of 992 DOCUMENTS


In re Diurno


INDEX NO. 25952-I-99


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NASSAU COUNTY


182 Misc. 2d 205; 696 N.Y.S.2d 769; 1999 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 395



September 2, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected November 22, 1999.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought appointment as guardian for incapacitated person, including power to authorize the administration of antipsychotic drugs to that person.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was appointed guardian for mentally incapacitated person pursuant to N.Y. Mental Health Law § 81.01 et seq., with power to authorize use of antipsychotic drugs. Trial court, mindful that sister court had held that Mental Health Law did not authorize grant of such power to a guardian, issued an opinion explaining its rationale. Court opined that (a) while guardian must take into account incapacitated person's wishes, preferences, and desires in making decisions for him or her, that concern must also take into account the person's ability to comprehend his or her situation and act in his or her own best interest; (b) while incompetence does not trigger loss of privacy rights, exercise of such rights must be balanced against state's interest in protecting those incapable of caring for themselves or of rational thought; and (c) sufficient procedural protections exist to ensure that guardian's powers are not abused.


OUTCOME: Appointment ordered, with powers sought, with provisio that guardian take into account incapacitated person's wishes in making decisions (to the extent person had capacity to judge); right to privacy must be balanced with state's need to protect incapacitated persons, and procedural protections adequate to ensure no abuse of power.


HEADNOTES

Insane and Other Incompetent Persons - Involuntary Administration of Drug - Power of Guardian to Consent to Administration of Antipsychotic Medication over Objection of Incapacitated Person

The court-appointed guardian for an incapacitated person who has been granted the power by the court to consent to or to refuse accepted routine or major medical treatment on behalf of the incapacitated person (Mental Hygiene Law § 81.22 [a] [8]) may consent to the administration of antipsychotic medication for schizophrenia, a treatment specifically included within a guardian's treatment powers (Mental Hygiene Law § 81.03 [i]), over the objection of the incapacitated person without the necessity of a further hearing. A guardian appointed under adequate due process procedures, properly informed in accordance with accepted medical standards, having a superior familiarity with the incapacitated person and acting as a fiduciary under mandatory, specific standards (see, Mental Hygiene Law § 81.20 [a] [1]-[3]), is equally if not more qualified than a court to make such treatment decisions. The incapacitated person's due process rights are protected by Mental Hygiene Law § 81.36, which grants the incapacitated person or anyone concerned with his or her welfare the right to request a hearing on the continued need for treatment powers in which the burden of proof will be on the guardian to show by clear and convincing evidence that the incapacitated person is still incapable of making reasoned treatment decisions and the guardian's powers are still necessary with respect thereto. The Mental Hygiene Law article 81 provisions concerning the authorization and exercise of the power to make decisions as to the administration of psychotropic drugs presumptively meet constitutional due process requirements and therefore medical facilities and providers may give such drugs on the basis of a guardian's consent without the need for further judicial action.

 

594 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Mayor of City of New York v. Council of City of New York


Index No. 401042/98


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


182 Misc. 2d 330; 696 N.Y.S.2d 761; 1999 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 426



August 31, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reversed by, Summary judgment denied by, Judgment entered by Mayor, City of New York v. Council, City of New York, 280 A.D.2d 380, 721 N.Y.S.2d 39, 2001 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 1734 (N.Y. App. Div. 1st Dep't, 2001)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Parties filed cross motions of summary judgment in plaintiff city mayor's claim that statute enacted by defendant city council was invalid.


OVERVIEW: Defendant city council passed a law creating an independent board to oversee the police department. Plaintiffs city mayor and district attorney sued for declaratory and injunctive relief to have the law declared invalid. Defendant filed motion to dismiss and both sides filed motions for summary judgment. The court held that local governments have the power to enact local laws not inconsistent with the state constitution. Plaintiffs argued that only plaintiff mayor had the authority to appoint members of a board and that the law impermissibly allowed defendant to name members to the board. The court held that the law required that appointments be made by plaintiff mayor. Although defendant council could nominate members, only plaintiff mayor could appoint them. The court further held that defendant had the authority to create investigatory bodies and grant them subpoena powers. The court also held that the board did not illegally infringe on plaintiff mayor's powers to implement systems of internal control or plaintiff district attorney's right to investigate and prosecute crimes.


OUTCOME: The court granted summary judgment to defendant and refused to order implementation of the law as there was no counterclaim requesting such relief.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations - Home Rule Powers - Creation of Independent Police Corruption Investigatory Body - Local Laws Subject to Mandatory Referendum - Mayor's Appointment Powers

1. Local Laws, 1997, No. 91 of the City of New York, which created the Independent Police Investigation and Audit Board (NY City Charter § 450 et seq.), does not unlawfully transfer governmental functions of the Mayor to members of the Board, who are not solely appointed by the Mayor, in violation of the Mayor's statutory right to appoint all nonelected officers of City government (NY City Charter § 6 [a]; Municipal Home Rule Law § 23 [2] [f]). The law clearly vests in the Mayor the power to appoint all five members of the Board, thereby obviating the need for a referendum which would have been required if the law had attempted to transfer or curtail the Mayor's appointment powers. The law's appointment process, which grants defendant City Council the right to designate, but not appoint, two members of the Board, and further requires the Mayor to only consult with the Council's Speaker as to the appointment of the chairperson of the Board, does not deprive the Mayor of the ultimate right to appoint all of the Board members.

Municipal Corporations - Home Rule Powers - Creation of Independent Police Corruption Investigatory Body - No Interference with Mayor's Executive Powers

2. Local Laws, 1997, No. 91 of the City of New York, which created the Independent Police Investigation and Audit Board (NY City Charter § 450 et seq.), does not unlawfully diminish or interfere with the executive powers of the Mayor or any mayoral appointees regarding City government operations (see, NY City Charter § 8 [a]; § 389 [a]). The Board's power to issue subpoenas in support of its investigations of possible police corruption does not interfere with the functions of the Police Department. The Board is only vested with the power to investigate and make recommendations.

Municipal Corporations - Home Rule Powers - Creation of Independent Police Corruption Investigatory Body - No Infringement on Powers of District Attorney

3. Local Laws, 1997, No. 91 of the City of New York, which created the Independent Police Investigation and Audit Board (NY City Charter § 450 et seq.), does not unlawfully infringe upon the power of the District Attorney to investigate and prosecute crimes. The Board has no prosecutorial powers, only investigative and advisory ones, nor does the Board's investigative function in any way usurp the powers of the Grand Jury. The Board is required to make referrals of certain discovered wrongful behavior to the Police Commissioner and to the appropriate prosecuting authority. An investigation conducted by the Board would not affect the power of the District Attorney to conduct a concurrent or independent investigation.

Municipal Corporations - Home Rule Powers - Creation of Independent Police Corruption Investigatory Body - No Unlawful Delegation of Legislative Powers

4. Local Laws, 1997, No. 91 of the City of New York, which created the Independent Police Investigation and Audit Board (NY City Charter § 450 et seq.), does not constitute an unlawful delegation by defendant City Council of any of its law-making powers to the Board. The Council, as the legislative body of New York City, may legally delegate powers to an administrative investigatory body in the executive branch of City government. Moreover, the Council still maintains its own powers of investigation and oversight (see, NY City Charter § 29 [a]) and can exercise them in areas that the Board would be investigating.

 

595 of 992 DOCUMENTS


People v. Davidson


Ind. No. 47


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, MONROE COUNTY


181 Misc. 2d 999; 696 N.Y.S.2d 640; 1999 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 414



August 23, 1999, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Pursuant to N.Y. Penal Law § 330.30(1), defendant filed an application to vacate his conviction of loitering for the purpose of gambling with dice, under § 240.35(2), based on the ground that the loitering statute was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to N.Y. Penal Law § 240.35(2), defendant was convicted of loitering for the purpose of gambling with dice. Defendant filed an application to vacate the guilty verdict, pursuant to § 330.30(1), arguing that § 240.35(2) was unconstitutional and, therefore, reversal of the conviction was required as a matter of law. The court stated that, under §§ 225.00(4) and (5), gambling was only illegal if a person advanced or profited from the gambling activity when acting other than a player. Therefore, persons who participated in gambling with dice as casual players did not commit a crime. The court stated that loitering for the purpose of gambling with dice did not constitute criminal behavior because gambling with dice, as a player, did not amount to criminal behavior. Consequently, the court held that § 240.35(2) was unconstitutional because it prohibited loitering for the purpose of gambling with dice. The charge against defendant for loitering was dismissed.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed defendant's conviction of loitering for the purpose of gambling with dice because the criminal statute was unconstitutional in that it prohibited an act based on a purpose that did not constitute a criminal behavior.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Loitering - Gambling - Unconstitutionality of Statute 

 Penal Law § 240.35 (2), which provides that a person is guilty of loitering when he remains in a public place for the purpose of gambling with cards, dice or other gambling paraphernalia, is unconstitutional since gambling is not per se illegal; rather, gambling only becomes illegal if a person advances or profits from gambling activity, and does so other than as a player. Individuals who participate in gambling as casual players are not committing a crime. Accordingly, inasmuch as casual gambling does not amount to criminal behavior, loitering for that purpose does not amount to criminal behavior, and although there is a strong presumption that a statute is constitutional and a nisi prius court should not ordinarily find a penal statute unconstitutional, this is a situation where such a conclusion is inescapable and the invalidity of the act is apparent on its face.

 

596 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Kenmar Surgical Aids, Inc. v. New York State Dep't of Health


Index No. 1534-99, (RJI No. 0199-ST9713)


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, ALBANY COUNTY


182 Misc. 2d 247; 697 N.Y.S.2d 468; 1999 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 462



August 16, 1999, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner-plaintiff commenced a declaratory judgment action and asserted that N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 18, § 518.7(d)(3) was facially unconstitutional to the extent that it authorized respondent-defendant New York State Department of Health to act on requests by the other state respondents-defendants to withhold Medicaid reimbursement from providers.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner-plaintiff filed an action for declaratory judgment and commenced a proceeding asserting that N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 18, § 518.7(d)(3) was facially unconstitutional because respondent-defendant New York State Department of Health (DOH) withheld payments for Medicaid reimbursement to it. The DOH based its action on alleged fraudulent filings by petitioner-plaintiff and, pending the outcome of its investigation, it did not pay petitioner-plaintiff's claims. After the filing of petitioner-plaintiff's action, it was indicted for Medicaid fraud, but the extent of its fraud was not determined. The court dismissed petitioner-plaintiff's action and vacated the restraining order against the DOH, which prohibited it from withholding more than 15 percent of the weekly Medicaid reimbursement payments to petitioner-plaintiff. The court found that the statute permitting withholding by the DOH of payments to a suspected fraudulent provider was not unconstitutional and the action of the DOH under that statute was not arbitrary, capricious or illegal as to petitioner but was proper under the circumstances.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed petitioner-plaintiff's action and held that the statute under which respondent-defendant withheld Medicaid payments to petitioner-plaintiff pending the outcome of an investigation for Medicaid fraud was not unconstitutional, and the action of respondent-defendant was proper under the circumstances.


HEADNOTES

Health - Medicaid Reimbursement Payments - Withholding of Reimbursement Following Request by State Agency - Facial Constitutionality

1. Pursuant to 18 NYCRR 518.7 (d) (3), the Department of Health (DOH) may constitutionally withhold Medicaid reimbursement from Medicaid providers suspected of fraud where such withholding is requested by another State agency. Although there is no express regulatory requirement that the outside agency render its investigatory determination within a specific time period, the mere absence of such time period does not render the regulation unconstitutional. A Medicaid provider has no constitutionally protected property interest in being immediately paid for items that it bills, and it is not constitutionally entitled to full notice of the extent of its alleged fraud, an opportunity to respond to the charges, and a final administrative determination regarding the extent of its fraud before DOH may withhold payments pending a final determination. Constitutional due process will be met by a hearing at such time as the investigations are complete and the provider is given notice of the charges against it and an opportunity to respond, so long as that is within a reasonable period of time. The withholding of payments pending completion of a criminal investigation is a constitutionally permissible and eminently reasonable means of assuring some recoupment of losses resulting from fraudulent diversion of funds by Medicaid providers.

Health - Medicaid Reimbursement Payments - Withholding of Reimbursement Following Request by State Agency - Constitutionality of Regulation as Applied

2. The Department of Health (DOH), acting pursuant to 18 NYCRR 518.7 (d) (3), did not unconstitutionally withhold Medicaid reimbursement from petitioner, a Medicaid provider suspected of fraud, where such withholding was requested by another State agency, and at the time the present proceeding challenging the constitutionality of the above regulation was commenced DOH had withheld a portion of the money that petitioner had expected for fewer than four months. Petitioner does not allege that respondents had completed their investigation, exonerated it or found that there was no basis for believing that it was guilty of fraud. Although petitioner has alleged that respondents failed to give notice of the withhold within five days or describe the reasons for the withhold, failed to state that the withholding is for a temporary period only, and failed to advise petitioner of the right to submit written arguments and documentation in opposition, petitioner has not demonstrated that any initial regulatory violation invalidates DOH's withholding and requires that the monies be returned to petitioner. Petitioner clearly is aware of its rights now and has adequate notice of the basis for the preliminary administrative action. Accordingly, petitioner is not entitled to return of the withheld money.

Health - Medicaid Reimbursement Payments - Withholding of Reimbursement Following Request by State Agency - Arbitrary and Capricious Regulatory Action

3. Pursuant to 18 NYCRR 518.7 (d) (3), the Department of Health (DOH) constitutionally withheld Medicaid reimbursement from Medicaid providers suspected of fraud where such withholding was requested by another State agency. Although petitioner claims that respondents' actions are arbitrary and capricious, DOH is acting pursuant to the other respondents' request, and the other respondents are entities authorized to make such requests. The other respondents have rendered no final determination and their actions are intended merely to ensure that there is some money available at such time as a final determination is rendered. Petitioner has not demonstrated that there is no evidence of fraud or that respondents were acting for improper reasons, and therefore has failed to meet its burden to establish that respondents' determination was arbitrary, capricious, or illegal.

 

597 of 992 DOCUMENTS


People v. Machado


DOCKET NUMBER 99X043176


CRIMINAL COURT OF THE CITY OF NEW YORK, BRONX COUNTY


182 Misc. 2d 194; 698 N.Y.S.2d 416; 1999 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 391



August 11, 1999, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: At criminal defendant's arraignment, defendant's counsel orally requested that the accusatory instrument be dismissed pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. §§ 140.20, 140.25, 140.40, and 140.45, because the instrument was not based on a warrant and was facially insufficient. The people asserted that the instrument could not be dismissed without a written motion.


OVERVIEW: Defendant and another person were arrested on drug charges without a warrant. At the arraignment, pursuant to an oral request by defendant's counsel, the court found the accusatory instrument did not connect defendant with the drugs found near the other person, or even with the other person. When the people could not provide additional evidence to connect defendant to the crimes after being given an opportunity to do so, the court found that the accusatory instrument was insufficient on its face, and dismissed it pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. §§ 140.40 and 140.45. The court held that because defendant was arrested without a warrant, the court had a duty to examine the instrument to determine its sufficiency at the arraignment. Because the statute did not require that a motion be filed in order to dismiss a warrantless accusatory instrument for insufficiency, because the people had an opportunity to present more evidence, and because the written motion procedures would have involved an unconstitutional delay of defendant's right to be released from custody, defendant did not need to file a motion in order to have the accusatory instrument dismissed.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the accusatory instrument as insufficient, because the people failed to provide a warrant showing probable cause, failed to allege sufficient facts either on the instrument, or at the arraignment, which could have provided a reasonable basis to find that defendant committed the crimes for which he was charged, and because the court could not delay defendant's release by requiring defendant to file a motion to dismiss.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Arrest - Arrest without Warrant - Dismissal of Accusatory Instrument for Facial Insufficiency at Arraignment Following Warrantless Arrest

A misdemeanor complaint filed against defendant based on a drug purchase by an undercover police officer from a codefendant must be dismissed, sua sponte, for facial insufficiency at defendant's arraignment pursuant to CPL 140.45 in the absence of available evidence tending to connect defendant to the actual sale beyond the allegation that defendant directed the undercover officer to "[g]o see" the codefendant "in the basketball court" if he wanted some marihuana. Where, as here, an accusatory instrument is filed in a local criminal court based upon a warrantless arrest, the court is required to inspect the accusatory instrument for facial sufficiency during the defendant's arraignment ( CPL 100.40) and since the court must inspect the instrument promptly after a warrantless arrest and exercise its dismissal power under CPL 140.45 in the event the pleading is defective, the People are not entitled to demand motion papers from the defendant or an opportunity to prepare a written response. The People may present only such facts or evidence as may be available to them at the arraignment. CPL 140.45 does not, by its express terms, require any application by the defendant. The statute simply commands the court to dismiss when an instrument has been filed that is facially insufficient and the available facts reveal no possibility that the pleading can be redeemed.

 

598 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Hutterian Brethren in N.Y., Inc. v. Town of Hunter


Index Nos.: 95-647, 96-858, 97-587, 98-566


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, GREENE COUNTY


181 Misc. 2d 776; 695 N.Y.S.2d 500; 1999 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 365



August 10, 1999, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Petitioner is determined to be entitled to the relief requested in its pleadings, in conformity with this decision.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner challenged a decision of respondent town that its real property was not entitled to a tax exemption.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was organized for exclusively religious, charitable, or other such purposes under N.Y. Real Prop. Tax Law § 420-a(1)(a). Petitioner owned 479 acres of land, which respondent town determined was taxable. Some 300 persons lived on the premises in a classless society, neither owning property nor receiving wages. Respondent denied petitioner a tax exemption, contending that its provision of food, shelter, and other necessities constituted a profit inuring to the members, contrary to N.Y. Real. Prop. Tax Law § 420-a (1)(b). The court held that petitioner's provision of necessities to its members was not inurement of profits to disqualify it for a tax exemption for its real property used to further its articulated purposes. To hold otherwise would be inconsistent with the relevant statute's intent. The fact that petitioner generated substantial profits through its manufacturing operations did not disqualify it for the tax exemption because all the profits were used for statutorily permitted purposes.


OUTCOME: Petitioner was entitled to a tax exemption under state real property laws because its provision of life necessities to its members to further its religious and charitable purposes was not the inurement of pecuniary of other profit as to disqualify it for the exemption.


HEADNOTES

Taxation - Exemptions - Religious Organizations - Pecuniary Profit Inuring to Benefit of Members

Petitioner, a religious organization whose approximately 300 members all live together in one community and work without remuneration at petitioner's factory where furniture as well as educational and rehabilitative products are manufactured, is entitled to a real property tax exemption pursuant to RPTL 420-a for its real property. The provision by petitioner to its members of food, clothing, shelter, medical care and other necessities and ordinaries of life does not constitute a pecuniary profit inuring to petitioner's members such as to result in petitioner's disqualification for a tax exemption under RPTL 420-a (1) (b) in view of petitioner's commitment to a communal and classless way of life for its members as a matter of articulated religious principle rather than a de facto arrangement of convenience or mere social utility. Likewise, the fact that petitioner generates substantial profits through its manufacturing operations does not result in petitioner's disqualification to receive a tax exemption since all such profits are used in conformity with statutorily permitted purposes. Moreover, the statute would be unconstitutional if applied to deny a tax exemption to petitioner for its real property used in furtherance of its religious purposes since such an application would be an invalid discrimination by government with respect to the exercise of religion.

 

599 of 992 DOCUMENTS


People v. World Interactive Gaming Corp.


Index No. 404428/98


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


185 Misc. 2d 852; 714 N.Y.S.2d 844; 1999 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 425



July 22, 1999, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, attorney general for the State of New York, sued respondents, a corporation and its subsidiaries and officers, under N.Y. Exec. Law § 63(12) to enjoin them from offering Internet gambling to residents of New York. Respondents moved to dismiss petitioner's case, claiming that petitioner did not have personal jurisdiction over them because the gambling transactions occurred offshore.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner sued respondents, a corporation and its subsidiaries and officers, under N.Y. Exec. Law § 63(12) to enjoin them from offering Internet gambling to residents of New York. Respondents moved to dismiss petitioner's case, claiming that petitioner did not have personal jurisdiction over them because the gambling transactions occurred offshore and were authorized by the government of Antigua. The trial court ruled for petitioner, explaining that respondents were subject to personal jurisdiction in New York because they engaged in significant activities such that they availed themselves of the privilege of conducting business in New York. Respondents maintained offices in New York and used the Internet to conduct business with New York residents. Respondents worked in New York with a New York company to design the graphics for their Internet gambling site. Additionally, under N.Y. Penal Law § 225.00(2), if a person engaged in gambling was in New York, then New York was the location where the gambling occurred. As a result, respondents were subject to personal jurisdiction in New York and the trial court denied their motion to dismiss.


OUTCOME: The trial court denied respondents' motion to dismiss an illegal gambling claim against them for lack of personal jurisdiction. The trial court held that respondents were subject to personal jurisdiction in New York because they had offices there, used the Internet to conduct business with New York residents, and worked there with a New York company to design graphics for their Internet gambling site.


HEADNOTES

Attorney General - Enjoining Fraudulent or Illegal Acts - Internet Gambling - Jurisdiction

1. The Attorney General may maintain a special proceeding pursuant to Executive Law § 63 (12) to enjoin respondents, a foreign corporation legally licensed to operate a casino offshore and its subsidiaries and officers, from offering gambling to Internet users in New York in violation of New York's prohibition against unauthorized gambling activity (NY Const, art I, § 9 [1]; Penal Law § 225.05). Respondents are subject to personal jurisdiction in New York for purposes of this proceeding since they purposefully engaged in significant activities such that they availed themselves of the privilege of conducting business in this State. Respondents maintained offices in New York and used the Internet to conduct business with New York residents. Respondents also worked with a New York company to design the graphics for their Internet gambling site. Furthermore, the court has subject matter jurisdiction over this proceeding. Internet gambling falls within the scope of New York's gambling prohibitions. Even though gambling is legal where the bet was accepted, the act of entering the bet and transmitting the information from New York via the Internet is adequate to constitute illegal gambling activity within New York. A computer server cannot be permitted to function as a shield against liability, particularly where respondents actively targeted New York as the location where they conducted many of their allegedly illegal activities.

Attorney General - Enjoining Fraudulent or Illegal Acts - Internet Gambling

2. In a special proceeding brought by the Attorney General pursuant to Executive Law § 63 (12), respondents, a foreign corporation legally licensed to operate a casino offshore, and its subsidiaries and officers, are enjoined from offering gambling to Internet users in New York. Even though gambling is legal where the bet was accepted, the act of entering the bet and transmitting the information from New York via the Internet is adequate to constitute illegal gambling activity within New York. By having established the gambling enterprise and solicited users in New York to gamble through their gambling web site, respondents have illegally promoted gambling in New York in violation of Penal Law § 225.05. In addition, respondents' interstate use of the Internet to conduct their illegal gambling business also violates Federal laws proscribing gambling, such as the Wire Act (18 USC § 1084 [a]), the Travel Act (18 USC § 1952), and the Wagering and Paraphernalia Act (18 USC § 1953). The Federal statutes apply to the operation of an offshore casino. The Interstate Commerce Clause gives Congress the plenary power to regulate gambling conducted between a location in the United States and a foreign location.

Attorney General - Enjoining Fraudulent or Illegal Acts - Securities Fraud - Sales of Stock in Internet Gambling Business

3. In an action brought by the Attorney General pursuant to Executive Law § 63 (12), respondents, a foreign corporation legally licensed to operate a casino offshore, and its subsidiaries and officers, are enjoined from continuing their fraudulent and illegal sales of stock in their Internet gambling business to prospective investors in New York. Respondents are operating illegally in New York by failing to have applied to the Department of State for approval to do business in New York despite operating out of offices located in New York. In addition, respondents violated the Martin Act (General Business Law § 359-e [1] [a]) by selling units of stock from their New York offices without registering with the Attorney General. Respondents also violated the Martin Act's prohibition against the use of deception, misrepresentations, or concealment in the sale of securities (see, General Business Law § 352 [1]) by failing to disclose that approximately 46% of the investors' funds were used to pay respondents' commissions, salaries, and consulting fees. Moreover, each individual respondent is liable for the actions conducted by the corporate entities. As part of the injunctive relief, respondents will be required to post a bond to assure future proper behavior. Furthermore, an award for restitution, penalties and costs is warranted in an amount to be fixed at a hearing.

 

600 of 992 DOCUMENTS


In re Jennifer G.


Docket No. S-05583/98, Docket No. D-5771/99


FAMILY COURT OF NEW YORK, QUEENS COUNTY


182 Misc. 2d 278; 695 N.Y.S.2d 871; 1999 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 370



July 21, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected November 29, 1999.
Later proceeding at In re Jennifer G., 2003 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 754 (N.Y. Fam. Ct., May 30, 2003)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner father filed a motion under N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 720(2) (1962) to have respondent, his daughter, adjudged a person in need of supervision. After 10 warrants were issued for respondent's arrest, the city filed a motion under N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act §§ 304.1(2), (3) (1962) to have respondent detained in a secure facility because she committed an act that constituted criminal contempt under N.Y. Penal Law § 215.50(3).


OVERVIEW: Petitioner father filed a motion under N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 720(2) (1962), to have respondent, his daughter, adjudged a person in need of supervision (PINS). Petitioner claimed that respondent continually absconded from the custody of the Commissioner of Social Services in violation of court orders. After 10 warrants were issued for respondent's arrest, the city filed a motion under N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act §§ 304.1(2), (3) (1962) to have respondent detained in a secure facility. The city alleged that detention was warranted because respondent committed an act that, if committed by an adult, would constitute criminal contempt in the second degree under N.Y. Penal Law § 215.50(3). The court granted the city's motion and adjudged respondent a juvenile delinquent under N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 352.1, holding that due to her continued disregard for the court's orders that she appear, respondent required supervision, treatment, or confinement. The court dismissed petitioner's motion as a result of its decision to grant the city's motion, and because N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 720(2) specifically proscribed detention of juveniles determined to be PINS.


OUTCOME: Family court dismissed petitioner's motion under the New York Family Court Act to have respondent, his daughter, adjudicated a person in need of supervision (PINS). The court explained that it granted the city's motion to have respondent detained instead because respondent continually ignored the court's orders that she appear and required supervision and confinement, and the law under which petitioner filed his motion proscribed detention.


HEADNOTES

Infants - Persons in Need of Supervision - Need for Secure Detention

In a person in need of supervision (PINS) proceeding in which respondent juvenile has continually absconded from the custody of the Commissioner of Social Services in violation of Family Court's remand orders, respondent is adjudged to be a juvenile delinquent for having engaged in intentional disobedience to lawful process or other court mandates (see, Penal Law § 215.50 [3]), and is remanded to secure detention, since the statutory scheme does not provide for any secure detention in PINS proceedings (Family Ct Act § 720). Family Court Act § 720, which, for pecuniary reasons only, prohibits Family Court from ordering secure detention in a PINS proceeding under any and all circumstances whether by way of preventive detention or dispositional order, may unconstitutionally deprive needy children of equal protection of the laws by depriving the court of the power to remand to an appropriate setting when necessary to accomplish the rehabilitative and nonpunitive policy underlying PINS proceedings. Although the statutory prohibition against secure detention in PINS proceedings was primarily intended to insure continued Federal funding under provisions of the Federal Juvenile Justice and Delinquency Prevention Act of 1974 (Pub L 93-415) regarding preventive detention and dispositional orders in PINS proceedings, the Federal law specifically authorizes secure detention for PINS children who violate a valid order of the court and does not demand a total prohibition in order for the State to obtain Federal funding (see, 42 USC § 5633 [a] [12] [A]). In addition, the statutory prohibition is violative of the inherent powers doctrine. The Legislature, having granted Family Court exclusive original subject matter jurisdiction over PINS proceedings, cannot deprive Family Court of the power to enforce, implement and effect the policy and goals inherent in that subject matter jurisdiction.
